THE water of the WONDROUS ISLES 




 the first PART : of the HOUSE of CAPTIVITY 




 CHAPTER I. CATCH AT UTTERHAY 



 Whilom , as tell the tale , be a walled cheaping - town hight Utterhay , 
 which be build in a bight of the land a little off the great 
 highway which go from over the mountain to the sea . 

 the say town be hard on the border of a wood , which man hold to be 
 mighty great , or maybe measureless ; though few indeed have enter it , 
 and they that have , bring back tale wild and confused thereof . 

 therein be neither highway nor byway , nor wood - reeve nor way - warden ; 
 never come chapman thence into Utterhay ; no man of Utterhay be so 
 poor or so bold that he durst raise the hunt therein ; no outlaw durst 
 flee thereto ; no man of God have such trust in the saint that he 
 durst build he a cell in that wood . 

 for all man deem it more than perilous ; and some say that there 
 walk the bad of the dead ; othersome that the Goddesses of the 
 Gentiles haunt there ; other again that it be the faery rather , 
 but they full of malice and guile . but most commonly it be deem 
 that the devil swarm amidst of its thicket , and that wheresoever 
 a man seek to , who be once environ by it , ever it be the Gate 
 of Hell whereto he come . and the say wood be call Evilshaw . 

 nevertheless the cheape - town throve not ill ; for whatso evil thing 
 haunt Evilshaw , never come they into Utterhay in such guise that 
 man know they , neither wotte they of any hurt that they have of the 
 Devils of Evilshaw . 

 now in the said cheaping - town , on a day , it be market and high noon , 
 and in the market - place be much people throng ; and amidst of they 
 go a woman , tall , and strong of aspect , of some thirty winter by 
 seem , black - haired , hook - nosed and hawk - eye , not so fair to look 
 on as masterful and proud . she lead a great grey ass betwixt two 
 pannier , wherein she lade her marketing . but now she have do she 
 chaffer , and be look about she as if to note the folk for her 
 disport ; but when she come across a child , whether it be bear in 
 arm or lead by its kinswoman , or be go alone , as be some , she 
 seem more heedful of it , and eye it more closely than aught else . 

 so she stroll about till she be come to the outskirt of the 
 throng , and there she happen on a babe of some two winter , which 
 be crawl about on its hand and knee , with scarce a rag upon its 
 little body . she watch it , and look whereto it be go , and 
 see a woman sit on a stone , with none anigh she , her face bow 
 over her knee as if she be weary or sorry . unto she creep the 
 little one , murmur and merry , and put its arm about the woman 's 
 leg , and bury its face in the fold of her gown : she look up 
 therewith , and show a face which have once be full fair , but be 
 now grow bony and haggard , though she be scarce past five and 
 twenty year . she take the child and strain it to her bosom , and 
 kiss it , face and hand , and make it great cheer , but ever 
 woefully . the tall stranger stand look down on she , and note how 
 evilly she be clothe , and how she seem to have nought to do with 
 that throng of thriving cheapener , and she smile somewhat sourly . 

 at last she spake , and her voice be not so harsh as might have be 
 look for from her face : Dame , she say , thou seemest to be less 
 busy than most folk here ; might I crave of thee to tell an alien who 
 have but some hour to dwell in this good town where she may find she a 
 chamber wherein to rest and eat a morsel , and be untroubled of 
 ribald and ill company ? say the poor - wife : short shall be my 
 tale ; I be over poor to know of hostelry and ale - house that I may 
 tell thee aught thereof . say the other : maybe some neighbour of 
 thine would take I in for thy sake ? say the mother : what 
 neighbour have I since my man die ; and I die of hunger , and in 
 this town of thrift and abundance ? 

 the leader of the ass be silent a while , then she say : poor woman ! 
 I begin to have pity on thee ; and I tell thee that luck hath come to 
 thee to - day . 

 now the poor - wife have stand up with the babe in her arm and be 
 turn to go her way ; but the alien put forth a hand to she , and 
 say : stand a while and hearken good tiding . and she put her hand 
 to her girdle - pouch , and draw thereout a good golden piece , a noble , 
 and say : when I be sit down in thine house thou wilt have 
 earn this , and when I take my sol out thereof there will be three 
 more of like countenance , if I be content with thee meanwhile . 

 the woman look on the gold , and tear come into her eye ; but she 
 laugh and say : houseroom may I give thee for an hour truly , and 
 therewithal water of the well , and a mouse 's meal of bread . if thou 
 deem that worth three noble , how may I say thee nay , when they may 
 save the life of my little one . but what else wouldst thou of I ? 
 little enough , say the alien ; so lead I straight to thine house . 

 so go they forth of the market - place , and the woman lead they , the 
 alien and the ass , out of the street through the west gate of 
 Utterhay , that , to wit , which look on Evilshaw , and so into a 
 scatter street without the wall , the end of which near a corner 
 of the wood aforesaid : the house there be nought so evil of 
 fashion , but whereas they be so nigh unto the Devil 's Park , rich 
 man might no long away with they , and they be become ware for 
 poor folk . 

 now the townswoman lay her hand on the latch of the door that be 
 her , and throw the door open ; then she put forth her palm to the 
 other , and say : wilt thou give I the first gold now , since rest be 
 make sure for thee , as long as thou wilt ? the ass - leader put it into 
 her hand , and she take it and lay it on her baby 's cheek , and then 
 kiss both gold and child together ; then she turn to the alien and 
 say : as for thy way - beast , I have nought for he , neither hay nor 
 corn : thou wert well to leave he in the street . the stranger 
 nod a yeasay , and the three go in together , the mother , the 
 child , and the alien . 

 not right small be the chamber ; but there be little therein ; one 
 stool to wit , a yew - chair , a little table , and a coffer : there be 
 no fire on the hearth , nought save white ash of small wood ; but it 
 be June , so that be of no account . 

 the guest sit down in the yew - chair , and the poor - wife lay her child 
 down gently on the floor and come and stand before the stranger , as 
 if abide her bidding . 

 spake the alien : Nought so uncomely or strait be thy chamber ; and 
 thy child , which I see be a woman , and therefore belike shall long 
 abide with thee , be lovely of shape , and fair of flesh . now also 
 thou shalt have well day , as I deem , and I pray they on thine 
 head . 

 she spake in a kind wheedle voice , and the poor - wife 's face grow 
 soft , and presently tear fall down on to the table from she , but 
 she spake no word . the guest now draw forth , not three noble , but 
 four , and lay they on the table , and say : Lo , my friend , the three 
 noble which I behight thee ! now be they thine ; but this other thou 
 shalt take and spend for I . go up into the town , and buy for I 
 white bread of the good ; and right good flesh , or poulaine if it may 
 be , already cook and dight ; and , withal , the good wine that thou 
 mayst get , and sweetmeat for thy baby ; and when thou comest back , we 
 will sit together and dine here . and thereafter , when we be full of 
 meat and drink , we shall devise something more for thy good speed . 

 the woman kneel before her weeping , but might speak no word because 
 of the fullness of her heart . she kiss the guest 's hand , and take 
 the money , and then arise and catch up her child , and kiss she 
 bare flesh eagerly many time , and then hasten out of the house and 
 up the street and through the gate ; and the guest sit hearken to 
 the sound of her footstep till it die out , and there be nought to 
 be hear save the far - off murmur of the market , and the chirrup of 
 the little one on the floor . 

 then arise the guest and take up the child from the floor , who kick 
 and scream , and crave her mother as her broken speech might ; but 
 the alien spake softly to she , and say : Hush , dear one , and be 
 good , and we will go and find she ; and she give she therewith a 
 sugar - plum from out of her scrip . then she come out of door , and 
 spake sweetly to the little one : see now this pretty way - beast . we 
 will ride merrily on he to find thy mother . 

 then she lay the child in the pannier with a soft cushion under , and 
 a silk cloth over she , so that she lie there happily . then she take 
 her ass 's rein and go her way over the waste toward Evilshaw ; for , 
 as ye may deem , where the house and the street end , the beat way 
 end also . 

 quietly and speedily she go , and meet but three man on the way ; and 
 when these see she , and that she be make for Evilshaw , they turn 
 their head away , each one , and bless themselves , and go past 
 swiftly . not one seek to stay she , or hold any converse with she , 
 and no foot she hear follow after she . so in scarce more than 
 the saying of a low mass she be in amongst the tree , with her ass 
 and her ware and her prey . 

 no stay she make there , but hold forward at she good before the night 
 should fall upon she . and whatsoever might be tell concern the 
 creature that other folk have meet in Evilshaw , of she it must need 
 be say that therein she happen on nought worse than herself . 



 CHAPTER II . now shall be tell of the HOUSE by the WATER - SIDE 



 four day they wend the wood , and nought befell to tell of . the 
 witch - wife   for even such be she   feed the steal child well and 
 duly , and while caress she and spake sweetly unto she ; while also 
 she would take she out of the pannier , and set she on the ass 's back 
 and hold her thereon heedfully ; or , otherwhile , when they come upon 
 grassy and flowery place , she would set she down on the ground and 
 let she roam about , and pluck the flower and the strawberry . and 
 whoso might be sorry , the child be glad , so many thing new and fair 
 as she come upon . 

 at last , when the fifth day be wan , and they have be a long 
 while wend a wood set thick with tree , it begin to grow grey 
 betwixt the distant bole , and then from grey to white , and it be as 
 if a new world of light lie before they . Thitherward go they , and 
 in a little , and before the sun be set , come they to the shore of a 
 great water , and thence be no more land to be see before they than 
 if it have be the main sea itself , though this be a sweet water . 
 albeit , less than a half mile from the shore lie two eyot , as it 
 might have be on the salt sea ; but one of these sit low down on the 
 water , and be green and well bush , but the other , which lie east 
 of it , and be nigh to the shore , be high , rocky , and barren . 

 now the ending of the wood leave a fair green plain betwixt it and the 
 water , while more than a furlong across , while much less ; and 
 while the tree come down close to the water - side . but the place 
 whereas they come from out the wood be of the wide , and there it 
 be a broad bight of greensward of the fashion of the moon seven 
 night old , and a close hedge of thicket there be at the back of it ; 
 and the lake lie south , and the wood north . some deal of this 
 greensward be break by close of acre - land , and the tall green 
 wheat stand blossom therein ; but the most be sweet meadow , and 
 there as now be a gallant flock of goat feed down it ; five kine 
 withal , and a tethered bull . through the widest of this meadow run a 
 clear stream wind down to the lake , and on a little knoll beside a 
 lap of the say stream , two bow - shot from the water , be a knoll , 
 whereon stand , amidst of a potherb garden , a little house strongly 
 frame of timber . before it the steep bank of the lake break down 
 into a slowly - shelve beach , whose honey - colour sand thrust up a 
 tongue in amongst the grass of the mead . 

 go the witch - wife straight to the door of the say house as if she 
 be at home , as be sooth indeed . she throw the door open , and 
 unlade the ass of all his ware , and first of the youngling , whom 
 she shake awake , and bear into the house , and lay safely on the 
 floor of the chamber ; nor do she wait on her wailing , but set about 
 what be to be do to kindle fire , and milk a she - goat , and get meat 
 upon the board . that do she , and feed both herself and the child 
 plenteously : neither do she stint she of meat ever , from that time 
 forward , however else she deal with she . 



 CHAPTER III . of skin - change 



 one thing must here be tell : Whenas the say dame stand forth clothe 
 amidst of the chamber the next morning , the child run up to she to 
 greet she or what not , but straightway when she see she close , drew 
 aback , and stand gasp with affright ; for verily she deem this 
 be nowise she who have bring she last night into the fair chamber , 
 and give bread and milk to she and put she to bed , but someone else . 
 for this one have not dark hair , and hooked nose , and eyen hawk- 
 bright ; stark and tall be she indeed , as that other one , and by 
 seeming of the same - like age ; but there come to an end all her 
 likeness to last night 's housewife . this one have golden - red hair 
 flow down from her head ; eye of hazel colour , long and not well- 
 open , but narrow and sly . high of cheekbone she be , long - chin 
 and thin - lipped ; her skin be fine and white , but without ruddiness ; 
 flat - breasted she be , and narrow - hippe . 

 now she laugh at the babe 's terror , and say , but in her old voice 
 at least : thou foolish little beast ! I know what scar thee , to 
 wit , that thou deemest I change : now I tell thee that I be the one 
 who bring thee here last night , and feed thee ; neither be my 
 change a matter of thine , since at least I be the one who shall 
 keep thee from hunger and weather henceforward ; that be enough for 
 thee to know as now . now thou hast to eat and sleep and play and cry 
 out , that thou mayest the soon wax , and grow into the doing of my 
 will . 

 Therewith she lead she out into the sunshine , and tether she to an 
 ash sapling which grow anigh the door , that the child might be safe 
 the while she go about her work in acre and mead . 

 but as for that matter of change of aspect , the maiden come to know 
 thereafter that the witch durst not go into the wood in the same skin 
 as that which she wear at home , wherefore she have change it for the 
 journey to Utterhay , and change back again in the night - tide before 
 she arise . 



 CHAPTER IV . of the waxing of the STOLEN child 



 this little one , who be henceforth call Birdalone , though the witch 
 call she but seldom so , nor indeed by any name , dwelt there betwixt 
 the water and the wood , and see none save the say witch - wife , who , 
 as aforesaid , feed she well , but scarce meddle with she else for a 
 long while ; so she wander well - nigh as she have will , and much in 
 the wood ; for she have no fear thereof , nor indeed of aught else save 
 of the dame . she learn of the way and the will not of all the 
 creature round about she , and the very grass and flower be 
 friend to she , and she make tale of they in her mind ; and the wild 
 thing fear she in no wise , and the fowl would come to her hand , 
 and play with she and love she . a lovely child she be , rosy and 
 strong , and as merry as the bird on the bough ; and have she trouble , 
 for while she come across some ugly mood of the witch - wife , she bear 
 it all as lightly as they . 

 wear the year thus , till now she be grow tall and thin , and have 
 see twelve winter , and be far strong and handy than at first 
 sight she look to be . that find her mistress , and would not 
 forego the using of her deftness . for indeed the maiden know all 
 matter of wood and field full well , and somewhat of the water also 
  though no boat have she ever see there , for she learn herself 
 swimming , as the duck do belike . 

 but now her mistress would learn her swinking , and hard be the 
 lesson , for with twiggen rod and switch be she learn , and be 
 somewhat stubborn with this woman , who she deem love she not ; and , 
 however it be , there begin to grow in she an inkling that all be 
 not well with the dame , and howsoever she might fear she , she trust 
 she not , nor worship she ; otherwise she have learn her lesson 
 speedily ; for she be not slack nor a sluggard , and hat not the 
 toil , even when it pain and weary she , but against the anger and 
 malice she harden her heart . 

 it be to be say , that though there she dwelt alone with the witch- 
 wife , she have somehow get to know that they two be not alone in the 
 world , and she know of male and female , and young and old . thereof 
 doubtless the witch herself have learn she , would she , would she 
 not ; for though she be mostly few - speak , yet while the tongue of 
 she would loosen , and she would tell Birdalone tale of man and 
 woman , and king and warrior and thrall , and the folk of the world 
 beyond they , if it be but to scare the child . yea , and when she 
 rat Birdalone , or gird at she , word would come forth which the 
 maiden store up , and by lay two and two together gat wisdom howso 
 it be . moreover , she be of the race of Adam , and her heart 
 conceive of diverse matter from her mother 's milk and her father 's 
 blood , and her heart and her mind grow up along with her body . 
 Herein also be she wise , to wit , how to give wrath the go - by , so 
 that she oft find the wood a well home than the house : for now 
 she know that the witch - wife would enter it never ; wherefore she 
 love it much , and haunt it daily if she might . 

 amidst all this she live not unmerrily ; for the earth be her 
 friend , and solace she when she have suffer aught : withal she be 
 soon grow hardy as well as strong ; and evil she could thole , nor let 
 it burden she with misery . 



 CHAPTER V. of BIRDALONE , and how she be grow into maidenhood 



 wear the year and the year amidst such day as these , and now be 
 birdalone grow a dear maiden of seventeen summer ; and yet be her 
 life not unhappy ; though the mirth of her childhood be somewhat 
 chasten in she , and she walk the earth soberly and measurely , as 
 though deep thought be ever in her head : though , forsooth , it be 
 not all so sure that her serious face and solemn eye be but a part 
 of the beauty which be grow with the come forth of childhood 
 into youth and maidenhood . but this at least be sure , that about 
 this time those foreboding which have show she that she have no call 
 to love and honour her mistress take clear shape , and become a 
 burden on she , which she might never wholly shake off . for this she 
 see , that she be not her own , but a chattel and a tool of one who 
 not only use she as a thrall in the pass day , but have it in her 
 mind to make of she a thing accurse like to herself , and to bait the 
 trap with she for the taking of the son of Adam . Forsooth she see , 
 though dimly , that her mistress be indeed wicked , and that in the 
 bond of that wickedness be she bind . 

 one thing , moreover , have she note now this long while , that once and 
 again , it might be once every two moon , the witch - wife would arise 
 in the dead of night and go forth from the house , and be away for a 
 day , or two or three , or while more , and come back again weary and 
 fordone ; but never say she any word to Birdalone hereof . yet oft 
 when she arise to go this errand , before she leave the chamber would 
 she come to Birdalone 's truckle - bed , and stand over she to note if 
 she be asleep or not ; and ever at such time do Birdalone feign 
 slumber amidst of sicken dread . Forsooth in these latter day it 
 while enter the maiden 's head that when the dame be go she 
 would rise and follow she and see whither she go , and what she do ; 
 but terror constrain she that she go not . 

 now from amidst all these imagining arise a hope in she that she 
 might one day escape from her thralldom : and while , when she be 
 lonely and safe in the wood , to this hope she yield herself ; but 
 thereof come such tumult of her soul for joy of the hope , that she 
 might not master her passion ; the earth would seem to rise beneath 
 she , and the wood to whirl about before her eye , so that she might 
 not keep her foot , but would sink adown to earth , and lie there 
 weeping . then most oft would come the cold fit after the hot , and 
 the terror would take she that some day the witch would surprise the 
 joy of that hope in her eye , and would know what it mean , or that 
 some light word might bewray she ; and therewith come imagining of 
 what would then befall she , nor be that hard to picture , and it 
 would come before she over and over again till she become weary and 
 wear out therewith . 

 but though they abode ever with she , these troubling thought prick 
 not so oft at the keen , but be as the dull ache of little import 
 that come after pain overcome : for in sooth busy and toilsome day 
 do she wear , which irk she in nowise , since it ease she of the 
 torment of those hope and fear aforesaid , and bring her sound 
 sleep and sweet awaking . the kine and the goat must she milk , and 
 plough and sow and reap the acre - land accord to the season , and 
 lead the beast to the woodland pasture when their own be flood 
 or burn ; she must gather the fruit of the orchard , and the hazel 
 nut up the woodland , and beat the walnut - tree in September . she 
 must make the butter and the cheese , grind the wheat in the quern , 
 make and bake the bread , and in all way earn her livelihood hard 
 enough . moreover , the bowman 's craft have she learn , and at the 
 dame 's bidding must fare alone into the wood now and again to slay 
 big deer and little , and win venison : but neither do that irk she 
 at all , for rest and peace be in the wood for she . 

 true it be , that as she wend thicket or glade or wood - lawn , she 
 would at while grow timorous , and tread light and heedfully , lest 
 rustle leaf or crackling stick should arouse some strange 
 creature in human shape , devil , or god now damn , or woman of the 
 faery . but if such be there , either they be wise and would not 
 be see , or kind and have no will to scare the simple maiden ; or else 
 maybe there be none such in those day . anyhow , nought evil come 
 to she out of Evilshaw . 



 CHAPTER VI . HEREIN be tell of BIRDALONE 's RAIMENT 



 Lank and long be Birdalone the sweet , with leg that come forth bare 
 and brown from under her scant grey coat and scanty smock 
 beneath , which be all her raiment save when the time be bitter , and 
 then , forsooth , it be a cloak of goat - skin that eke her attire : 
 for the dame heed little the clothing of she ; nor do Birdalone 
 give so much heed thereto that she care to risk the anger of her 
 mistress by ask she for aught . 

 but on a day of this same spring , when the witch - wife be of sweet 
 temper than she will not be , and the day be very warm and kindly , 
 though it be but one of the last of February day , Birdalone , 
 blushing and shameface , crave timidly some more womanly attire . 
 but the dame turn gruffly on she and say : tush , child ! what 
 needeth it ? here be no man to behold thee . I shall see to it , that 
 when due time come thou shalt be whiten and sleek to the very 
 utmost . but look thou ! thou art a handy wench ; take the deer - skin 
 that hang up yonder and make thee brogue for thy foot , if so thou 
 wilt . 

 even so do Birdalone , and shape the skin to her foot ; but as she 
 be sew they a fancy come into her head ; for she have just come 
 across some thread of silk of diver colour ; so she take they and 
 her shoon and her needle up into the wood , and there sit down happily 
 under a great spread oak which much she haunt , and fall to 
 broider the kindly deer - skin . and she get to be long about it , 
 and come back to it the next day and the next , and many day , whenso 
 her servitude would suffer it , and yet the shoon be scarce do . 

 so on a morning the dame look on her foot as she move about the 
 chamber , and cry out at she : what ! art thou barefoot as an hen 
 yet ? Hast thou spoil the good deer - skin and art yet but shoeless ? 
 Nay , our lady , say Birdalone , but the shoon be not altogether do . 
 show they to I , say the dame . 

 birdalone go to her little coffer to fetch they , and bring they 
 somewhat timorously , for she know not how her mistress would take she 
 work on they so long , if perchance she would blame she , or it 
 might be chastise she , for even in those day the witch - wife 's hand 
 be while raise against she . but now when the dame take the shoe 
 and look on they , and see how there be oak - leaf do into they , 
 and flower , and coney , and squirrel , she but smile somewhat 
 grimly on Birdalone , and say : well , belike thou art a fool to waste 
 thy time and mine in such toy ; and to give thee thy due would be to 
 give thee stripe . but thou doest herein after the nature of earthly 
 woman , to adorn thy body , whatsoever else be toward . and well be 
 that , since I would have thee a woman so soon as may be ; and I will 
 help thy mind for finery , since thou art so deft with thy needle . 

 Therewith she go to the big coffer and draw forth thence a piece of 
 fine green cloth , and another of fine linen , and say to Birdalone : 
 this mayest thou take , and make thee a gown thereof and a new smock , 
 and make they if thou wilt as gay as thy new shoon be get to be ; 
 and here be wherewithal . and therewith she give she two handful of 
 silken thread and gold , and say : now I suppose that I must do the 
 more part of thy work , while thou art make thee these gaudy 
 garment . but maybe someone may be come this way ere long , who 
 will deem the bird the finer for her fine feather . now depart from 
 I ; for I would both work for thee and I , and ponder weighty 
 matter . 

 who be glad now but birdalone ; she grow red with new pleasure , and 
 kneel down and kiss the witch 's hand , and then go her way to the 
 wood with her precious lading , and work there under her oak - tree 
 day after day , and all day , either there , or in the house when the 
 weather be foul . that be in the middle of March , when all bird 
 be sing , and the young leaf show on the hawthorn , so that 
 there be pale green clouds , as it be , betwixt the great grey 
 bole of oak and sweet - chestnut ; and by the lake the meadow - saffron 
 new - thrust - up be open its blossom ; and March wear and April , and 
 still she be at work happily when now it be later May , and the 
 hare - bell be in full bloom down the bent before she . 

 all this while the witch have meddle little with Birdalone , and have 
 bid she to no work afield or in the stead which be anywise 
 grievous , but have do all herself ; yet be she few - speak with she , 
 and would oft behold she gloomily . and one evening when Birdalone 
 come in from the wood , the witch come close up to she and star she 
 in the face , and say suddenly : be it in thine heart to flee away 
 from I and leave I ? 

 a sharp pang of fear shoot through Birdalone 's heart at that word , and 
 she turn very red , and then pale to the lip , but stammer out : 
 no , lady , it be not in mine heart . the dame look grimly on she and 
 say : if thou try it and fail , thou shalt rue it once only , to wit , 
 lifelong ; and thou canst but fail . she be silent a while , and then 
 spake in a milder voice : be content here a while with I , and 
 thereafter thou shalt be more content , and that before long . 

 she say no more at that time ; but her word clave to Birdalone 's 
 heart , and for some time thereafter she be sorely oppress with a 
 burden of fear , and know not how to hold herself before the witch- 
 wife . but the day wear , and nought betid , and the maiden 's heart 
 grow light , and still she work on at her gown and her smock , and 
 it be well - nigh do . she have broider the say gown with rose 
 and lily , and a tall tree spring up from amidmost the hem of the 
 skirt , and a hart on either side thereof , face to face of each other . 
 and the smock she have sew daintily at the hem and the bosom with 
 fair knot and bud . it be now past the middle of June , hot and 
 bright weather . 



 CHAPTER VII . birdalone HATH an adventure in the wood 



 on a day she go to the wood , and sit down under her oak - tree , and 
 it be far and far out of sight of anyone stand in the meadow by 
 the lake ; and in the wood Birdalone look to see nought at all save 
 the rabbit and squirrel , who be , forsooth , familiar enough with 
 she , and fearless , so that they would come to her hand and sport with 
 she when she hail they . wherefore , as the day be exceed hot , 
 she put off from her her simple raiment , that she might feel all the 
 pleasure of the cool shadow and what air be stir , and the 
 kindness of the greensward upon her very body . so she sit sewing , 
 cover but by a lap of the green gown which her needle be paint . 

 but as she sit there intent on her work , and her head bend over it , 
 and it be now at the point of high noon , she hear as if some 
 creature be go anigh to she ; she heed it not , deem that it 
 would be but some wander hind . but even therewith she hear one 
 say her name in a soft voice , and she leapt up tremble , deeming at 
 first that it would be the witch come to fetch she : but yet more 
 scar she be , when she see stand before she the shape of a young 
 woman as naked as herself , save that she have an oak wreath round 
 about her loin . 

 the new - comer , who be now close to she , smile on she , and say in a 
 kind and sweet voice : fear nought , Birdalone , for I deem thou wilt 
 find I a friend , and it be not unlike that thou wilt need one ere 
 long . and furthermore , I will say it , say she smile , that since I 
 be not afraid of thee , thou needest not be afraid of I . say 
 Birdalone , she also smile : true it be that thou art nought 
 fearsome to look on . the new - comer laugh outright , and say : be 
 we not well meet then in the wildwood ? and we both as two child 
 whom the earth loveth . so play we at a game . at what game ? say 
 Birdalone . spake she of the oak - wreath : this ; thou shalt tell I 
 what I be like in thine eye first , because thou wert afraid of I ; 
 and then when thou art do , I will tell thee what thou seemest to 
 I . 

 Quoth birdalone : for I that will be hard ; for I have nought to 
 liken thee to , whereas save this sight of thee I have see nought 
 save she that dwelleth in the House by the Water , and whom I serve . 
 Nay , say the other , then will I begin , and tell thee first whatlike 
 thou art , so that thou wilt know the well how to frame thy word 
 concern I . but tell I , hast thou ever see thyself in a mirror ? 
 what thing be that ? say Birdalone . it be a polished round of steel 
 or some other white metal , say the wood - maiden , which giveth back in 
 all truth the image of whatso cometh before it . say Birdalone and 
 reddened therewith : we have at home a broad latten dish , which it be 
 my work , amongst other thing , to brighten and keep bright ; yet may I 
 not make it so bright that I may see much of mine image therein ; and 
 yet . what wouldst thou ? say the wood - woman . say birdalone : I 
 shall tell thee presently when thy part of the play be do . 

 laugh the new - comer , and say : it be well ; now be I to be thy 
 mirror . thus it be with thee : thou stand before I a tall and 
 slim maiden , somewhat thin , as befitteth thy seventeen summer ; where 
 thy flesh be bare of will not , as thy throat and thine arm and thy leg 
 from the middle down , it be tan a beauteous colour , but otherwhere 
 it be even as fair a white , wholesome and clean , and as if the golden 
 sunlight , which fulfilleth the promise of the earth , be play 
 therein . Fairer and rounder shall be thine arm and thy shoulder 
 when thou hast see five more summer , yet scarce more lovesome , so 
 strong and fine as now they be . Low be thy breast , as be meet for 
 so young a maiden , yet be there no lack in they ; nor ever shall they 
 be fair than now they be . in goodly fashion sit thine head upon 
 thy shoulder , uphold by a long and most well - work neck , that the 
 sun hath tan as aforesaid . the hair of thee be simple brown , yet 
 somewhat more golden than dark ; and ah ! now thou lett it loose it 
 waveth softly past thy fair smooth forehead and on to thy shoulder , 
 and be not stay by thy girdlestead , but hideth nought of thy knee , 
 and thy leg shapely thin , and thy strong and clean - wrought ankle 
 and foot , which be with thee as full of thine heart and thy soul and 
 as wise and deft as be thy wrist and thine hand , and their very 
 fellow . now as to thy face : under that smooth forehead be thy 
 nose , which be of measure , neither small nor great , straight , and 
 lovely carven at the nostril : thine eyen be as grey as a hawk 's , 
 but kind and serious , and nothing fierce nor shift . Nay , now thou 
 lett thine eyelid fall , it be as fair with thy face as if they 
 be open , so smooth and simple be they and with their long full 
 lash . but well be thine eyen set in thine head , wide apart , well 
 open , and so as none shall say thou mayst not look in the face of 
 they . Thy cheek shall one day be a snare for the unwary , yet be 
 they not fully round , as some would have they ; but not I , for most 
 pitiful kind be they forsooth . delicate and clear - make be the 
 little trench that goeth from thy nose to thy lip , and sweet it be , 
 and there be more might in it than in sweet word speak . Thy lip , 
 they be of the fine fashion , yet rather thin than full ; and some 
 would not have it so ; but I would , whereas I see therein a sign of 
 thy valiancy and friendliness . surely he who do thy carven chin have 
 a mind to a master - work and do no less . Great be the deftness of 
 thine imaginer , and he would have all folk that see thee wonder at 
 thy deep thinking and thy carefulness and thy kindness . ah maiden ! 
 be it so that thy thought be ever deep and solemn ? yet at least I 
 know it of thee that they be hale and true and sweet . 

 my friend , when thou hast a mirror , some of all this shalt thou see , 
 but not all ; and when thou hast a lover some deal wilt thou hear , but 
 not all . but now thy she - friend may tell it thee all , if she have 
 eye to see it , as have I ; whereas no man could say so much of thee 
 before the mere love should overtake he , and turn his speech into 
 the folly of love and the madness of desire . so now I have play 
 the play , and tell thee of thee ; tell I now of I , and play thy 
 play . 

 for a while stand Birdalone silent , blush and confuse , but while 
 cast shy glance at her own body , what she might see of it . at 
 last she spake : Fair friend , I would do thy will , but I be not deft 
 of speech ; for I speak but little , save with the fowl and wild 
 thing , and they may not learn I the speech of man . yet I will say 
 that I wonder to hear thee call I fair and beauteous ; for my dame 
 tell I that never , nor sayeth aught of my aspect save in her anger , 
 and then it be : rag ! and bag - of - bone ! and when wilt thou be a 
 woman , thou lank elf thou ? the new - comer laugh well - favouredly 
 hereat , and put forth a hand , and stroke her friend 's cheek . 
 birdalone look piteous kind on she and say : but now I must need 
 believe thy word , thou who art so kind to I , and withal thyself so 
 beauteous . and I will tell thee that it fill my heart with joy to

 to I as thou art ; nay , not the fritillary nodding at our brook 's 
 mouth , nor the willow - bough wave on Green Eyot ; nor the wild - cat 
 sporting on the little woodlawn , when she see I not ; nor the white 
 doe rise up from the grass to look to her fawn ; nor aught that 
 move and grow . yet there be another thing which I must tell thee , 
 to wit , that what thou hast say about the fashion of any part of I , 
 that same , set aside thy lovely word , which make the tear come 
 into the eye of I , would I say of thee . look thou ! I take thine 
 hair and lie the tress amongst mine , and thou mayst not tell which be 
 which ; and amidst the soft wave of it thy forehead be nestling 
 smooth as thou saidst of mine : hawk - grey and wide apart be thine 
 eyen , and deep thought and all tenderness be in they , as of I thou 
 sayest : fine be thy nose and of due measure ; and thy cheek a little 
 hollow , and somewhat thin thy lovely lip ; and thy round chin so 
 goodly carven , as it might not be well do . and of thy body else 
 I will say as thou sayst of mine , though I deem these hand have do 
 more work than thine . but see thou ! thy leg and mine as they stand 
 together ; and thine arm , as if it be of my body . slim and slender 
 thou art , or it may be lank ; and I deem our dame would call thee also 
 bag - of - bone . now be this strange . who art thou ? art thou my very 
 own sister ? I would thou wert . 

 Spake then to Birdalone that image of she , and say , smile kindly 
 on she : as to our likeness , thou hast it now ; so alike be we , as if 
 we be cast in one mould . but thy sister of blood I be not ; nay , I 
 will tell thee at once that I be not of the child of Adam . as to 
 what I be , that be a long story , and I may not tell it as now ; but

 true that to everyone I show not myself in this fair shape of thee ; 
 but be not aghast thereat , or deem I like unto thy mistress herein , 
 for as now I be , so ever shall I be unto thee . 

 Quoth Birdalone , look on her anxiously : yea , and I shall see thee 
 again , shall I not ? else should I grieve , and wish that I have never 
 see thee at all . yea , forsooth , say Habundia , for I myself be 
 most fain to see thee oft . but now must thou presently get thee back 
 home , for evil as now be the mood of thy mistress , and she be rue 
 the gift of the green gown , and hath in her mind to seek occasion to 
 chastise thee . 

 now be birdalone half weeping , as she do on her raiment while her 
 friend look on she kindly . she say presently : Habundia , thou 
 seest I be hard bestead ; give I some good rede thereto . 

 that will I , say the wood - wife . when thou goest home to the house , 
 be glad of countenance , and joyous that thy gown be nigh do ; and 
 therewith be exceed wary . for I deem it most like that she will 
 ask thee what thou hast see in the wood , and then if thou falter , or 
 thy face change , then she will have an inkling of what hath befall , 
 to wit , that thou hast see someone ; and then will she be minded to 
 question thy skin . but if thou keep countenance valiantly , then 
 presently will she doubt run off she , and she will cease grudging , 
 and will grow mild with thee and meddle not . this be the first rede , 
 and be for to - day ; and now for the second , which be for day yet 
 unborn . thou hast in thy mind to flee away from she ; and even so 
 shalt thou do one day , though it may be by way of Weeping Cross ; for 
 she be sly and wise and grim , though sooth it be that she hateth thee 
 not utterly . now thou must note that nowise she hindereth thee from 
 faring in this wood , and that be because she wotteth , as I do , that 
 by this way there be no outgoing for thee . wherefore look thou to it 
 that it be by the way of the water that thou shalt fare to the land 
 of man - folk . belike this may seem marvellous to thee ; but so it be ; 
 and belike I may tell thee more hereof when time serveth . now cometh 
 the last word of my rede . maybe if thou come often to the wood , we 
 shall while happen on each other ; but if thou have occasion for I , 
 and wouldst see I at once , come hither , and make fire , and burn a 
 hair of my head therein , and I will be with thee : here be for thee a 
 tress of mine hair ; now thou art clothe , thou mayst take a knife from 
 thy pouch and shear it from off I . 

 even so do Birdalone , and set the tress in her pouch ; and therewith 
 they kiss and embrace each other , and Birdalone go her way home 
 to the house , but Habundia go back into the wood as she have come . 



 CHAPTER VIII . of birdalone and the WITCH - WIFE 



 it go with Birdalone as Habundia have foretell , for she come home to 
 the house glad of semblance , flushed and light - foot , so that she be 
 lovely and graceful beyond she will not . the dame look on she 
 doubtfully and grimly a while , and then she say : what ail thee , my 
 servant , that thou look so masterful ? Nought ail I , lady , say 
 Birdalone , save that I be gay because of the summer season , and 
 chiefly because of thy kindness and thy gift , and that I have well- 
 nigh do my work thereon , and that soon now I shall feel these 
 dainty thing beat about my ankle . and she hold up and spread 
 abroad the skirt with her two hand , and it be indeed goodly to look 
 on . 

 the witch - wife snort scornfully and scowl on she , and say : 
 thine ankle forsooth ! Bag - o'-bones ! thou wisp ! forsooth , thou art 
 in love with thy look , though thou know not what like a fair 
 woman be . Forsooth , I begin to think that thou wilt never grow into 
 a woman at all , but will abide a skinny elf thy life long . belike I 
 do myself wrong to suffer thee to waste these three or four month 
 of thy thrall 's work , since for nought but thrall 's work shalt thou 
 ever be meet . 

 birdalone hang her head adown , and blush , but smile a little , and 
 sway her body gently , as a willow - bough be sway when a light air 
 arise in the morning . but the witch stand so scowl on she , and 
 with so sour a look , that Birdalone , glance at she , find her heart 
 sink so within she , that she scarce keep countenance ; yet she lose it 
 not . 

 then say the witch sharply : Wert thou in the wood to - day ? yea , 
 lady , say the maiden . then say the dame fiercely : and what saw 
 thou ? Quoth Birdalone , look up with an innocent face somewhat 
 scared : Lady , I see a bear , one of the big one , cross a glade . 
 and thou without bow and arrow or wood - knife , I warrant I , say the 
 witch . thou shalt be whip , to keep thee in mind that thy life be 
 mine and not thine . Nay , nay , I pray thee be not wroth ! say the 
 maid ; he be a long way down the glade , and would not have follow 
 I if he have see I : there be no peril therein . say the witch- 
 wife : didst thou see aught else ? Yea , say Birdalone , and be 
 weep somewhat now ; which forsooth be not hard for she to do , 
 over - wrought as she be betwixt hope and fear : yea , I see my white 
 doe and her fawn , and they pass close by I ; and two heron fly 
 over my head toward the water ; and . . . but the witch turn 
 sharply and say : Thrall ! hast thou see a woman to - day in the wood ? 
 a woman ? say Birdalone , and what woman , my lady , say Birdalone . 
 hath any woman come to the house , and pass forth into the wood ? 

 the dame look on she carefully , and remember how she have falter 
 and change countenance that other day , when she have charge she with 
 be minded to flee ; and now she see she with wonder face , and in 
 no wise confused or afraid of guilt , as it seem ; so she believe 
 her tale , and be the more at ease thereby , her wrath run off she , 
 and she spake altogether pleasantly to Birdalone , and say : now I 
 have have my gird at thee , my servant , I must tell thee that in sooth 
 it be not all for nothing that thou hast have these month of rest ; 
 for verily thou hast grow more of a woman thereby , and hast 
 sleekene and round much . albeit , the haysel will wait no long 
 for we , and the day after to - morrow we must fall to on it . but when

 wilt , till wheat harvest be toward ; and thereafter we shall see to 
 it . or what sayest thou ? 

 birdalone wonder somewhat at this so gracious word , but not much ; 
 for in her heart now be some guile bear to meet the witch 's guile ; 
 so she kneel down and take the dame 's hand and kiss they , and 
 say : I say nought , lady , save that I thank thee over and over again 
 that thou art become so good to I ; and that I will full merrily work 
 for thee in the hay - field , or at whatsoever else thou wilt . 

 and indeed she be so light - hearted that she have so escape from the 
 hand of the witch for that time , and above all , that she have get a 
 friend so kind and dear as the wood - woman , that her heart go out 
 even toward her mistress , so that she go nigh to love she . 



 CHAPTER IX . of birdalone 's SWIMMING 



 full fair be the morrow morn , and Birdalone arise betime before the 
 sun be up , and she think she would make of this a holiday before 
 the swink afield begin again , since the witch be grow good toward 
 she . so she do on her fair shoe , and her new raiment , though the 
 green gown be not fully do , and say to herself that she would 
 consider what she would do with her holiday when she be amidst of 
 her bathing . 

 so she go down to the water - side , and when she be stand knee- 
 deep in the little sandy bight aforesaid , she look over to Green 
 eyot , and be minded to swim over thither , as oft she do . and it 
 be a windless dawn after a hot night , and a light mist lie upon the 
 face of the water , and above it rise the greenery of the eyot . 

 she push off into the deep and swam strongly through the still 
 water , and the sun rise while she be on the way , and by then she have 
 lay a hand on the willow - twig of the eyot , be send a long beam 
 across the water ; and her wet shoulder rise up into the path of it 
 and be turn into ruddy gold . she hoist herself up , and 
 climb the low bank , be stand amongst the meadow - sweet , and 
 dripping on to its fragrance . then she turn about to the green 
 plain and the house and the hedge of woodland beyond , and sigh , and 
 say softly : a pity of it , to leave it ! if it be no well 
 otherwhere , and not so fair ? 

 then she turn inward to the eyot , which have do her nought but 
 good , and which she love ; and she unbound her hair , and let it fall 
 till the end of the tress mingle with the head of the meadow- 
 sweet , and thereafter walk quietly up into the grassy middle of the 
 isle . 

 she be will not to go to a knoll there where the grass be fine , and 
 flowery at this time with white clover and dog violet , and lie down 
 under the shade of a big thorn with a much - twisted bole : but to - day 
 some thought come across she , and she turn before she come to the 
 thorn , and go straight over the eyot   which be but a furlong over 
 at that place   and down to the southward - look shore thereof . 
 there she let herself softly down into the water and thrust off 
 without more ado , and swam on and on till she have go a long way . 
 then she commune with herself , and find that she be think : if 
 I might only swim all the water and be free . 

 and still she swam on : and now a light wind have be draw up from 
 the west , and be drive a little ripple athwart the lake , and she 
 swam the swiftli for it awhile , but then turn over on her back 
 and float southward still . till on a sudden , as she lie look up 
 toward the far - away blue sky , and she so little and low on the face 
 of the water , and the lake so deep beneath she , and the wind come 
 ever fresher from the west , and the ripple rise higher against she , 
 a terror fall upon she , and she long for the green earth and its 
 well - work little blossom and leaf and grass ; then she turn 
 over again and swam straight for the eyot , which now be but a little 
 green heap far away before she . 

 long she be ere she make land there , and the sun be high in the 
 heaven when she come , all spend and weary , to the shadow of the 
 hawthorn - tree ; and she cast herself down there and fall asleep 
 straightway . Forsooth her swim be about as much as she have might 
 for . 

 when she awake it lack but an hour of noon - tide , and she feel the 
 life in she and be happy , but have no will to rise up for a while ; 
 for it be ajoy to she to turn her head this way and that to the dear 
 and dainty flower , that make the wide , grey , empty lake seem so far 
 away , and no more to be deal with than the very sky itself . 

 at last she arise , and when she have pluck and eat some handful 
 of the strawberry which grow plenteously on the sweet ground of the 
 eyot , she go down to the landward - look shore , and take the 
 water , and swam slowly across the warm ripple till she come once more 
 to the strand and her raiment . she clothe herself , and set her hand to 
 her pouch and draw forth bread , and sit eat it on the bank above 
 the smooth sand . then she look around , and stand up with her face 
 toward the house , to see if the dame would call to she . but she see 
 the witch come out of the porch and stand there look under the 
 sharp of her hand toward she , and thereafter she go back again into 
 the house without give any sign . Wherefore Birdalone deem that 
 she have leave that day , and that she might take yet more holiday ; so 
 she step lightly down from her place of vantage , turn her face 
 toward the east , and go quietly along the very lip of the water . 



 CHAPTER X. birdalone come on new tiding 



 soon she have cover up the house from she , for on that eastern end , 
 both a tongue of the woodland shove out west into the meadow , and , 
 withal , the whole body of the wood there draw down to the water , and 
 presently cut off all the greensward save a narrow strip along by the 
 lake , off the narrow whereof lie the rocky eyot aforesaid , nigh 
 unto the shore than lay Green Eyot . 

 now never have Birdalone go so far east as to be over against Rock 
 Eyot . in her childish day the witch have let she know that she might 
 go where she would , but therewith have tell she a tale of a huge 
 serpent which dwelt in the dark wood over against Rock Eyot , whose 
 will not it be to lap his fold round and round live thing that go 
 there , and devour they ; and many an evil dream have that evil serpent 
 bring to Birdalone . in after day belike she scarce trowe in the 
 tale , yet the terror of it abode with she . moreover the wildwood 
 toward that side , as it draw toward the water , be dark and dreary 
 and forbidding , run into black thicket stand amidst 
 quagmire , all unlike to the sweet , clean upland ridge , oak begrown 
 and greenswarde , of the part which lie toward the north , and which 
 she mostly haunt . 

 but this summer day , which be so bright and hot , Birdalone deem 
 she might harden her heart to try the adventure ; and she have a mind 
 to enter the wood thereby , and win her way up into the oakland 
 whereas she have meet Habundia , and perchance she might happen on she ; 
 for she would not dare to summon she so soon after their first 
 meeting . and if she meet she , there would be the holiday worthily 
 bring to an end ! 

 on go Birdalone , and be soon at the narrow of the greensward , 
 and have the wood black on her left hand , for the tree of it be 
 mostly alder . but when she be come just over against Rock Eyot , she 
 find a straight creek or inlet of the water across her way ; and the 
 say creek run right up into the alder thicket ; and , indeed , be much 
 overhung by huge ancient alder , gnarl , riven , mossy , and fall 
 low over the water . but close on the mouth of the creek , on 
 Birdalone 's side thereof , lie a thing float on the dull water , 
 which she know not how to call a boat , for such have she never see , 
 nor hear of , but which be indeed a boat , oarless and sailless . 

 she look on it all about , and wonder ; yet she see at once that it 
 be for wend the water , and she think , might she but have a long 
 pole , she might push it about the shallow part of the lake , and 
 belike take much fish . she try to shove it somewhat toward the 
 lake , but with her little might could make nothing of the work ; for 
 the craft be heavy , like a barge , if there be nothing else that 
 withstand she . 

 about this new thing she hang a long while , wonder that she have 
 never hear thereof , or be set to toil therewith . she note that 
 it be mostly pale grey of hue , as if it have be bleach by sun and 
 water , but at the stem and stern be smear of dark colour , as 
 though someone have be try the tint of stain there . 

 now so much do this new matter take up all her mind , that she 
 think no more of go up into the wood ; but though she have fain 
 abide there long to see whatever might be see , she deem it would 
 go ill with she do the witch happen on she there ; wherefore she 
 turn about , and go back the way she have come , go very slowly 
 and ponder the tiding . and ever she call to mind what Habundia 
 have say to she , that it be by water she must flee , and wonder if 
 she have send she this thing that she might escape therein ; so 
 different as her go would be thereby to swim the lake with her 
 wet body . then again she think , that before she might let herself 
 hope this , it be good , if she might , to find out from the witch 
 what be the thing , and if she know thereof . yet at last she call 
 to mind how little patient of question be her mistress , and that if 
 she be unheedful she might come to raise an evil storm about she . 
 wherefore she take this rede at the last , that she would keep all 
 hide in her own breast till she should see Habundia again ; and 
 meanwhile she might steal down thither from time to time to see if 
 the thing still abode there ; which she might the easy do by 
 swim if she choose her time heedfully , and go thither from Rock 
 Eyot , which now and again she visit . 



 CHAPTER XI . of BIRDALONE 's GUILT and the chastisement THEREOF 



 by this she be come back to the sandy bight , and the sun be 
 westere ; and she look up toward the house and see that it be the 
 time of their evening meal , for the blue smoke of the cooking fire 
 be go up into the air . so she go thither speedily , and enter 
 gay of seem . the witch look on she doubtfully , but presently 
 fall to speak with she graciously as yesterday , and Birdalone be 
 glad and easy of mind , and go about the serving of she ; for always 
 she eat after the dame ; and the mistress ask she of many matter 
 concern the house , and the gathering of stuff . 

 so come the talk on the fishing of the brook that run before their 
 door , and how the trout therein be but little , and not seldom none 
 at all ; and even therewith come these word into Birdalone 's mouth , 
 she scarce know how : my lady , why do we not fish the lake , whereas 
 there be shoal place betwixt we and the eyot where lie many and 
 great fish , as I have see when I have be swim thereover ? and 
 now in that same creek whereas the serpent use to lurk when I be 
 little , we have a thing come , which be make to swim on the water ; and 
 I , could I have a long pole to shove withal . 

 but no time she have to make an end , ere the witch - wife spring up and 
 turn on she with a snarl as of an evil dog , and her face change 
 horribly : her tooth show grinning , her eye goggle in her head , 
 her brow be all to - furrow , and her hand clench like iron 
 spring . 

 birdalone shudder back from she and cringe in mere terror , but have 
 no might to cry out . the witch haul she up by the hair , and 
 drag her head back so that her throat lie bare before she all 
 along . then draw the witch a sharp knife from her girdle , and raise 
 her hand over she , growling and snarl like a wolf . but suddenly 
 she drop the knife , her hand fall to her side , and she fall in a 
 heap on the floor and lie there hush . 

 birdalone stand gaze on she , and tremble in every limb ; too 
 confuse be she to think or do aught , though some image off light 
 through the open door pass before she : but her foot seem of 
 lead , and , as in an evil dream , she have no might to move her limb , 
 and the minute go by as she stand there half dead with fear . 

 at last ,   and belike it be no long while   the witch - wife come to 
 herself again , and sit up on the floor , and look all about the 
 chamber , and when her eye fall upon Birdalone , she say in a weak 
 voice , yet joyfully ; Hah ! thou art there still , my good servant ! 
 then she say : a sickness fall upon I suddenly , as while it be 
 will not ; but now be I myself again ; and presently I have a word for 
 thee . 

 Therewith she rise up slowly , Birdalone help she , and sit in her 
 big chair silent awhile , and then she spake : my servant , thou hast 
 for the more part serve I well : but this time thou hast do ill , 
 whereas thou hast be spy on my way ; whereof may come heavy 
 trouble but if we look to it . well be it for thee that thou hast 
 none unto whom thou might babble ; for then must I need have slay 
 thee here and now . but for this first time I pardon thee , and thou 
 hast escape the wrath . 

 her voice be soft and wheedling ; but for Birdalone the terror have 
 enter into her soul , and yet abode with she . 

 the witch - wife sit a while , and then arise and go about the 
 chamber , and come to a certain aumbry and open it , and draw forth a 
 little flasket of lead and a golden cup score over with strange 
 sign , and lay they on the board beside her chair , wherein she now 
 sit down again , and spake once more , still in the same soft and 
 wheedle voice : yet , my servant , thy guilt would be require of I , 
 if I let this pass as if to - day be the same as yesterday ; yea , and 
 of thee also would it be require ; therefore it be a part of the 
 pardon that thou be correct : and the correction must be terrible 
 to thee , that thou mayst remember never again to thrust thyself into 
 the jaw of death . and what may I do to correct thee ? it shall be 
 in a strange way , such as thou hast never dream of . yet the 
 anguish thereof shall go to thine heart 's root ; but this must thou 
 need bear , for my good and thine , so that both we may live and be 
 merry hereafter . go now , fill this cup with water from the spring 
 and come back with it . birdalone take the cup with a sink heart , 
 and fill it , and bring it back , and stand before the witch more 
 dead than alive . 

 then the witch - wife take up the flasket and pull out the stopple 
 and betook it to Birdalone , and say : drink of this now , a little 
 sip , no more . and the maiden do so , and the liquor be no soon 
 down her gullet than the witch - wife and the chamber , and all thing 
 about she , become somewhat dim to she ; but yet not so much so as that 
 she could not see they . but when she stretch out her arm she could 
 see it not at all , nor her limb nor any other part of she which her 
 eye might fall upon . then would she have utter a lamentable wail , 
 but the voice be seal up in she and no sound come from her voice . 
 then she hear the witch - wife how she say   and yet she hear it as 
 if her voice come from afar , Nay , thou canst not speak , and thou 
 canst not see thyself , nor may any other , save I , and I but dimly . 
 but this be but part of what I must lay upon thee ; for next I must 
 give thee a new shape , and that both thyself and all other may see . 
 but , before I do that , I must speak a word to thee , which thy new 
 shape would not suffer the sense thereof to reach to thine heart . 
 Hearken ! 



 CHAPTER XII . the word of the WITCH - WIFE to BIRDALONE 



 say the witch - wife : when thou comest to thyself   for it be not my 
 will that thou should never have thine own shape again , doubtless 
 the first thing which thou shalt do with thy new - gain voice and thy 
 new - gain wit shall be to curse I , and curse I again . do as thou 
 wilt herein ; but I charge thee , disobey I not , for that shall bring 
 thee to thy bane . for if thou do not my bidding , and if thou pry 
 into my matter , and lie bare that which I will have hide , then 
 will it be impute unto thee for guilt , and will I , will I not , I 
 must be avenge on thee even to slay : and then be undo all the 
 toil and pain I have have in rear thee into a deft and lovely 
 maiden . deem thou , then , this present anguish kind to thee , to keep 
 thee that thou come not to nought . 

 now since I have begin speak , I will go on ; for little heretofore 
 have I speak to thee what be in mine heart . well I wot that thou 
 think of I but as of an evil dream , whereof none can aught but 
 long to awake from it . yet I would have thee look to this at least ; 
 that I take thee from poverty and pinching , and have rear thee as 
 faithfully as ever mother do to child ; clem thee never , smite 
 thee not so oft , and but seldom cruelly . moreover , I have suffer 
 thee to go whereso thou would , and have compel thee to toil for 
 nought but what be needful for our two livelihood . and I have not 
 stay thy swimming in the lake , nor thy wandering in the wood , and 
 thou hast learn bowshot there , till thou art now a past - master in 
 the craft : and , moreover , thou art swift - foot as the good of the 
 deer , and mayest over - run any one of they whom thou wilt . 

 soothly a merry life hast thou have as a child , and merry now would be 
 thy life , save for thine hatred of I . into a lovely lily - lass hast 
 thou grow . that I tell thee now , though my will not have be to gird at 
 thee for the fashion of thy body ; that be but the word of the 
 mistress to the thrall . and now what awaiteth thee ? for thou mayst 
 say : I be lonely here , and there be no man to look on I . of what 
 avail , therefore , be my goodliness and shapeliness ? child , I answer 
 thee that the time be come when thou shalt see here a many of the 
 fairest of man , and then shalt thou be rather rise than lily , and 
 fully come to womanhood ; and all those shall love and worship thee , 
 and thou mayst gladden whom thou wilt , and whom thou wilt mayst 
 sadden ; and no lack soever shalt thou have of the sweetness of love , 
 or the glory of dominion . 

 think of it then ! all this be for thee if thou dwell here quietly 
 with I , do my will till thy womanhood hath blossom . wherefore 
 I beseech and pray thee put out of thy mind the thought of flee 
 from I . for if thou try it , one of two thing shall be : either I 
 shall bring thee back and slay thee , or make thee live in misery of 
 torment ; or else thou wilt escape , and then what will it be ? dost 
 thou know how it shall go with thee , come poor and nameless , an 
 outcast , into the world of man ? lust shalt thou draw unto thee , but 
 scarce love . I say an outcast shalt thou be , without worship or 
 dominion ; thy body shall be a prey to ribald , and when the fine 
 flower thereof hath fade , thou shalt find that the word of thy 
 lover be but mockery . that no man shall love thee , and no woman 
 aid thee . then shall Eld come to thee and find thee at home with 
 Hell ; and death shall come and mock thee for thy life cast away for 
 nought , for nought . this be my word to thee : and now I have nought 
 to do to thee save to change thee thy skin , and therein must thou do 
 as thou canst , but it shall be no ugly or evil shape at least . but 
 another time maybe I shall not be so kind as to give thee a new 
 shape , but shall let thee wander about see by none but I . then she 
 take the cup and take water in the hollow of her hand and cast it 
 into Birdalone 's face , and mutter word withal ; and presently she 
 see herself indeed , that she be become a milk - white hind ; and she 
 hear and see again , but not as she , the maiden , be will not to hear and 
 see ; for both her hearing and seeing and her thought be of a beast 
 and not of a maiden . 

 say the witch - wife : it be do now , till I give thee grace again ; 
 and now be off into the field ; but if thou stray more than half a 
 bowshot from the brook , it shall be the bad for thee . and now the 
 day be do and night be come . 



 CHAPTER XIII . birdalone meeteth the wood - woman again 



 it be fifteen day thereafter that birdalone awake lie in her bed 
 on a bright morning , as if all this have be but a dream . but the

 slug - a - bed , this fair - weather day , and the grass all spoil for 
 lack of the scythe . off ! and down to the meadow with thee . 

 birdalone wait not for more word , but spring out of bed , and have 
 her work - a - day raiment on in a twinkling , and stay but to wash she

 swathe fall before she . as she work she think , and could 
 scarce tell whether joy at her present deliverance , or terror of the 
 witch - wife , be the great . Sore be her longing to go see she 
 friend in the wood , but the haysel last more than a week , and when 
 that be do , whether it be of set purpose or no , the dame forgat 
 her other promise , to give Birdalone more holiday , and keep she close 
 to her work about meadow and acre . otherwise her mistress nowise 
 mishandle or threaten she , though she have go back to the

 quiver , and green gown truss up till her knee be naked .

 would weep for thee if I might shed tear . but thou mayest tell I

 thereof . Hast thou happen on the witch 's ferry ? 

 even so it be , sister , quoth Birdalone . and therewith she pluck 
 up heart , and tell she all the tale of the vanishing of her body and 
 the skin - change . and Habundia answer : well then , there be this 
 to be say , that soon or later this must have happen , for thereby 
 lieth thy road of escape ; wherefore it be well soon than later . 
 but tell I again : be she fierce and rough in word with thee ? for

 nought but wolfish cry . but thereafter she spake unto I 
 strangely , yet neither fiercely nor roughly ; nay , it seem to I as 
 if almost she love I . and more than almost she besought I rather 
 than command I not to flee from she . and wert thou beguile by 
 her soft speech ? say Habundia . Nowise to cast aside my hope of 
 escape , nay , not even in that hour , say Birdalone ; but amidst all 
 the confusion and terror somewhat be I move to compassion on she . 

 Spake Habundia , look anxiously on she : dost thou deem that thou 
 art somewhat cow by what she hath do to thee ? say Birdalone , 
 and flush very red : oh no , no ! nought save death or bond shall 
 come betwixt I and my utmost strive for escape . that be well 
 than well , say Habundia ; but again , canst thou have patience a 
 little , and be wary and wise the while ? so meseemeth , say the 
 maiden . say Habundia : again it be well . now be the summer 
 beginning to wane , and by my rede thou shalt not try the flight until 
 May be come again and well - nigh wear into June ; for thou wilt be 
 big then , little sister , and tiding be wax that shall get 
 matter ready for thy departure : moreover , thou must yet learn what 
 thou hast to do meanwhile , and thereof shall I tell thee somewhat as 
 now . for that boat , the thing which thou didst find , and for which 
 thou didst suffer , be call the Sending Boat , and therein thy 
 mistress fareth time and again , I deem to seek to some other of she 
 kind , but I know not unto whom , or whereto . Hast thou note of she 
 that while she goeth away privily by night and cloud ? yea , verily , 
 say Birdalone , and this be one of the thing which heretofore hath 
 make I most afraid . say Habundia : well now , that she wendeth 
 somewhither in this ferry I wot ; but as I wot not whither , so also I 
 know not what she doth with the Sending Boat to make it obey she ; 
 whereas , though I know all thing of the wood , I know but little of 
 the lake . wherefore , though there be peril to thee therein , follow 
 she twice or thrice when she riseth up for this faring , and note 
 closely what be her manner of deal with the say Sending Boat , so 
 that thou mayst do in like wise . wilt thou risk the smart and the 
 skin - change , or even if it be the stroke of the knife , to gather 
 this wisdom ? and thereafter thou shalt come hither and tell I how 
 thou hast speed . with a good heart will I , dear sister , say 
 Birdalone . 

 then Habundia kiss she and say : it be a joy to I to see thee so 
 valiant , but herein may I help thee somewhat ; here be a gold finger- 
 ring , see thou ! fashion as a serpent hold his tail in his mouth ; 
 whenso thou goest on this quest , set thou this same ring on the 
 middle finger of thy left hand , and say thou above thy breath at 
 least : 


 to leave and right , 
 before , behind , 
 of I be sight 
 as of the wind ! 


 and nought then shall be see of thee even by one who standeth close 
 beside . but wear not the ring openly save at such time , or let the 
 witch have sight thereof ever , or she will know that thou hast meet 
 I . dost thou understand , and canst thou remember ? 

 Laughed Birdalone , and take the ring and set it on her finger , and 
 spake aloud even as Habundia have give she the word . then quoth 
 Habundia , laugh : now have I lose my friend and sister , for thou 
 art go , Birdalone . take off the ring , sweetling , and get thee to 
 thine hunting , for if thou come home empty - handed there will be 
 flyting await thee , or bad . 

 so Birdalone take off the ring and come back to sight again laugh ;

 presently have a brace of hare , wherewith she go back home to the 
 dame ; who indeed gird at she for her sloth , and her little catch in 
 so long a while ; but there it end . 



 CHAPTER XIV . of BIRDALONE 's fishing 



 now be the day wear toward wheat - harvest , and nought befel to 
 tell of , save that on a morn the witch - wife call Birdalone to she , 
 and say : now be little to be do till the wheat be ready for the 
 hook , and thy day be idle ; or what be that word that fall from thee 
 that other day , that there be good swim for fish about the eyot ? 
 Canst thou swim across bear thine angle , and back again therewith , 
 and thy catch withal ? Yea , certe , say Birdalone gaily ; with one 
 hand I may swim gallantly , or with my leg alone , if I stir mine arm 
 ever so little . I will go straightway if thou wilt , lady ; but give 
 I a length of twine so that I may tie my catch about my middle when 
 I swim back again . 

 Therewith she go forth lightly to fetch her angle , which be in a 
 shed without ; but just as she take it in her hand , a sudden thought 
 come to she , so wary as she be grow . she undo the bosom of her 
 gown , and take forth her serpent - ring ; for she bear it next to her 
 skin , make fast to the bosom of her smock ; but now she hide it 
 carefully in the thickest of her brow - hair , which be very thick and 
 soft . withal the tress of Habundia 's hair she bear ever mingle with 
 her own . 

 no soon have she do it , but she be glad ; for she hear the dame 
 call she , who , when she come to the house - door , spake and say : 
 now shall I fare with thee down to the water , and look to thy 
 garment lest they be foul by some stray beast . and therewith 
 she look curiously on Birdalone , and knit her brow when she see 
 that the maiden change countenance in nowise . 

 down to the water go they , and the witch sit down close to where 
 birdalone should take the water , and watch she do off her raiment , 
 and eye she keenly when she be bare , but say nought . birdalone 
 turn her head as she stand knee - deep , and say : how long shall I 
 abide , lady , if I have luck ? as long as thou wilt , say the dame : 
 most like I shall be go by then thou comest back , even if thou be 
 away no long while . 

 Fell birdalone to swim then , and when she be more than half 
 over , the witch , stir no more than need be , get hold of her 
 raiment , which be but the old grey coat over a smock , and ransack 
 it , but find nought , as well ye may wot . and when she have do , she 
 sit down again in heavy mood as it seem , and watch Birdalone 
 swimming , and when she behold her body come forth out of the water , 
 and pass out of sight amongst the flower of the eyot , she arise and 
 go her way home . 

 birdalone look through the willow - bough , and see her turn away ; 
 then she fare to her fish with a smile , and soon have plenteous 
 catch from under the willow - bough . then , whereas the day be very 
 calm and fair , and the dame have give her holiday , she wander about 
 the eyot , and most in a little wood of berry - tree , as quicken and 
 whitebeam and dog - wood , and sport with the bird , who fear she 
 not , but come and sit on her shoulder , and creep about her foot . 
 she go also and stand a while on the southern shore , and look on 
 the wide water dim in the offing under the hot - weather haze , and 
 long to be go beyond it . then she turn away , and to the other 
 shore , and gat her fish and string they on the string , and make they 
 fast to her middle , and so take the water back again to the yellow 
 strand , where now be no one await she . but before she do on her 
 garment , she look on they , and see that they lay not as she have 
 leave they , whereby she know well that the witch - wife have handle 
 they . 

 amidst all this the day be wear to an end , and again she see the 
 smoke of the cooking - fire go up into the air from the chimney of 
 the house ; and she smile ruefully , think that the witch might yet 
 find an occasion for ransack her raiment . but she pluck up 
 heart , and come home with her catch , and the dame meet she with a glum 
 face , and neither praise she nor blame she , but take the fish 
 silently . such ending have that day . 



 CHAPTER XV . birdalone weareth her SERPENT - RING 



 after this she go once and again fish on to Green Eyot by the 
 bidding of the dame , who go not again to the shore with she . these 
 time she have half a mind to go see the Sending Boat , but durst not , 
 lest the thing itself might have life enough to tell of she . 

 and now be come the time of wheat - harvest , and Birdalone must wear 
 her day swinke in the acre - land , clothe but in smock and shoe ; and 
 the toil be hard , and brown her skin and harden her hand , but 
 it irk she not , for the witch let she work all alone , and it be 
 holiday unto the maiden if her mistress be not anigh , despite those 
 word which have somewhat touch her heart that other day . 

 but when wheat - getting be do , there be again rest for her body , 
 and swim withal and fish from the eyot by the witch 's leave . 
 and again by her own leave she go to seek Habundia in the wood , and 
 spend a happy hour with she , and come back with a fawn which she have 
 shoot , and so but barely save her skin from the twig - shower . then 
 yet again she go into the wood on the witch 's errand as well as she 
 own , and be pay by her friend 's sweet converse , and by nought else 
 save the grudging girding of her mistress . 

 but on a night when September be well in , and the sky be moonless 
 and overcast , somewhat before midnight the dame come and hang over 
 birdalone as she lie abed , and watch to see if she wake ; forsooth 
 the witch 's coming have wake she ; but even so she be wary , and lay 
 still , nor change her breathing . so the witch turn away , but even 
 therewith Birdalone make a shift to get a glimpse of she , and this 
 she see thereby , that the semblance of she be change , and that she 
 bear the self - same skin wherewith she have come to Utterhay , and which 
 she have wear twice or thrice afterwards when she have an errand 
 thither . 

 the witch now glide swiftly to the door , and out into the night . 
 birdalone lie still a little , lest she should fall into a trap , and 
 then arise very quietly and do on her smock , which lie ever under 
 her pillow with the ring sew thereto again , and so go out adoor 
 also , and deem she see the witch some way on ahead ; but it be 
 nothing for her light foot to overtake she . so she stay to take 
 the ring from her smock , and set it on her finger ; then in a low 
 voice she say : 


 to leave and right , 
 before , behind , 
 of I be sight 
 as of the wind ! 


 then boldly she speed on , and be soon close on the heel of the 
 witch , who make her way to the edge of the lake , and then turn 
 east , and go even as Birdalone have go when she come across the 
 Sending Boat . 

 so fare the witch - wife straight to the creek - side , and birdalone 
 must need stick close to she , or she have know nought , so black be 
 the night amongst the alder - bough . but the witch - wife fumble about 
 a while when she be stay by the creek , and presently draw somewhat 
 from under her cloak , and the maiden see that she be about strike 
 flint upon steel , and quake somewhat , lest her charm have play she 
 false . presently the tinder quicken , and the dame have light a 
 lantern , which she hold up , peer all about ; and full she look on 
 the place whereas be Birdalone , and make no show of see she , 
 though well - nigh the maiden look for it to see her drop the lantern 
 and spring on she . 

 now the witch , hold the lantern aloft , step over the gunwale of 
 the boat , and sit down on the thwart ; and it be a near thing but 
 that birdalone follow she into the boat , but she fear the getting 
 forth again , so she but hang over it as close as she might . then she 
 see the witch draw out of her girdle that sharp little knife which 
 Birdalone have see raise against her own throat ; and then the witch 
 bar her arm , and prick it till the blood spring from that barren 
 white skin ; thereat she stand up , and go to the bow of the craft 
 and hang over they , and draw her arm to and fro over the stem to 
 bloody it ; and go thereafter to the stern , and take blood into her 
 right hand and pass it over the place of the steerage   for there 
 be no rudder   and come back and sit down on the thwart again ; and , 
 so far as Birdalone might see , busy herself in staunch the 
 little wound on her arm . then deem Birdalone that she know what 
 manner of paint be that which have make the rusty smear which she 
 have see on the boat by daylight . 

 but now as the witch sit there , a harsh voice begin to stir in her 
 throat , and then word come out of she , and she sing in a crow 's 
 croak : 


 the red raven - wine now 
 Hast thou drunk , stern and bow ; 
 then wake and awake 
 and the wonte way take ! 
 the way of the Wender forth over the flood , 
 for the will of the Sender be blent with the blood . 


 Therewithal begin the boat to stir , and anon it glide forth out of 
 the creek into the water of the lake , and the light of the lantern 
 die , and it be but a minute ere Birdalone lose all sight of it . 
 she abode a little long , lest perchance boat and witch might come 
 back on her hand , and then turn and go swiftly back again . she 
 would have draw off her ring straightway , but the thought come on 
 she , that she have see the witch depart in her second semblance ; how 
 if she be abide she at home in her wonted skin ? so she come to 
 the house even as she be , and open the door , and look in , 
 quake ; but there be no image of a child of Adam therein , and no
