 himself against her leg , and she be fain of he . 

 thereafter she make her ring fast to her smock again , and set the 
 smock under her pillow as she will not be , and betook herself to bed , 
 and fall asleep sweetly , leave all troublous thought for the 
 morrow ; and that the more as she be free of the witch - wife for that 
 night at least . 



 CHAPTER XVI . BIRDALONE meeteth habundia again ; and LEARNETH she 
 first wisdom of she 



 when morning be , Birdalone arise , and long sore to go into the 
 wood to seek Habundia again , but durst not , lest the witch - wife 
 should come to hand again early than might be look for . so she 
 abode quiet and do what be toward near about the house . all that 
 day the witch come not back , nor the next ; but the morrow thereafter , 
 when Birdalone arise , she find the wonte aspect of her mistress in 
 the wonted place , who , when she see the maiden , greet she , and be 
 somewhat blithe with she ; and Birdalone would have ask her leave to 
 go to the wood , but she trust little in her unwonted soft mood ; 
 which yet last so long that on the third day she herself bade 
 birdalone go take her pleasure in the wood , and bear back with she 
 what of venison she might . 

 Forthwith go Birdalone as glad as might be , and meet her friend at 
 the Oak of Tryst , and tell she closely how all have betid ; and 
 Habundia say : here , then , thou hast learn how to sail the lake . 
 but hast thou learn enough to try the adventure and not to fail ? 
 even so I deem , say Birdalone ; but this I would say , that meseemeth 
 it well that I follow the witch down to the boat one more time at 
 least ; for this first time it be dark ; and moreover shall I not be 
 surer of the spell if I hear it say oftener , lest it be not ever the 
 same word ? what sayest thou ? she say : thou art right herein , 
 and , since the adventure may not be try till next June be at hand , 
 there be time enough and to spare . and now for this hour that be we 
 need talk no more of it . only , my sweet , I beseech thee be wary ; and 
 above all suffer not the witch - wife to set eye or hand on the ring . 
 truly mine heart oft ache sorely for thy peril ; for therein the 
 image of thee abideth rather as of my daughter than my friend . yea , 
 now thou laugh , but kindly , so that the sound of thy laughter be 
 as sweet music . but know that though thou art but a young maiden , 
 and I in all wise like unto thee of aspect , yet have I dwelt many and 
 many a year upon the earth , and much wisdom have learn . trow 
 thou I ? 

 Yea , yea , say Birdalone , with all my heart . then she hang her head 
 a while and keep silence , and thereafter look up and spake : I 
 would ask thee a thing and crave somewhat of thee , as if thou wert 
 verily my mother ; wilt thou grant it I ? yea , surely , child , say 
 Habundia . say birdalone : this it be then , that thou wilt learn I 
 of thy wisdom . Habundia smile full kindly on she , and say : this 
 of all thing I would have have thee ask ; and this day and now shall 
 we begin to open the book of the earth before thee . for therein be 
 mine heritage and my dominion . sit by I , child , and hearken ! 

 so the maiden sit down by her likeness under the oak , and begin to 
 learn her lesson . Forsooth forget be the wisdom , though the tale 
 of its learn abideth , wherefore nought may we tell thereof . 

 when it be do , Birdalone kiss her wood - mother and say : this be 
 now the good day of my life , this and the day when first I see thee . 
 I will come hither now many time before the day of my departure . 
 yea , but , sweet child , say Habundia , beware of the witch and her 
 cruelty ; I fear I she shall yet be grim toward thee . so will I be 
 wary , say Birdalone , but I will venture some little peril of pain 
 but if thou forbid I , mother . and I pray thee by thy love to forbid 
 I not . and this I pray thee the more , because after one of these 
 grim time then mostly doth she meddle the less with I for a while , 
 wherefore I shall be the free to come hither . Habundia kiss she 
 and embrace she , and say : valiant art thou for a young maiden , my 
 child , and I would not refrain thee more than a father would refrain 
 his young son from the stroke of the tilt - yard . but I pray thee to 
 forget not my love , and my sorrow for thy grief . 

 Therewith they sunder , and it be draw toward evening . 
 birdalone seek catch , and bring home venison to the dame , who be 
 yet blithe with she , and spake that evening as she eye she : I 
 can not tell how it be , but thou seemest change unto I , and look 
 more towards thy womanhood than even yesterday . I mean the face of 
 thee , for wert thou strip , lean enough I should see thee , 
 doubtless . but now look to it , I beseech thee , to be both deft and 
 obedient , so that I may be as kind to thee as I would be , and kind 
 than I have be heretofore . 



 CHAPTER XVII . the passing of the year into WINTER 



 wear the day now , till on a night of October , toward the end 
 thereof , the witch go a - night - tide to the Sending Boat , and 
 birdalone follow she as erst . this time the night be wild and 
 windy , but the moon be high aloft and big , and all cloud save a few 
 fleck be blow from off the heaven ; so that the night be as light 
 as could be ; and even at the tree - hang creek it be easy to see all 
 that be do . and so it be that the witch do and spake in all 
 wise as she do before . 

 another time , when November be well - nigh out , the dame arise for her 
 lake - fare ; but this night the snow lie deep betwixt house and 
 water , and Birdalone think that it would scarce do to follow . 
 Forsooth she know not whether her foot would the less leave their 
 print in the snow because they be not to be see . when she ask 
 Habundia thereof , she laugh and say : once more thou hast be 
 wise , my child , for though it have be no hard to put this might 
 into thy ring , that whoso wear it should not touch the ground , yet it 
 hath not be do . 

 it must be tell , that in this while Birdalone go oft to the 
 Trysting Tree , and call on her mother   as now she call she   to 
 come to she , and ever more and more of wisdom she win thereby . 
 though the witch be oft surly with she , and spar not she gird , 
 yet , the needful work do , she meddle little with she . but on a 
 day she straightly ban she the wood , and Birdalone go 
 notwithstanding , and when she be there with the wood - mother nought 
 she tell she thereof , but be blithe and merry beyond she will not . she 
 come back home thereafter empty hand , and step into the chamber 
 proudly and with bright eye and flushed cheek , though she look 
 for nought save chastisement ; yea , it might be even the skin- 
 change . Forsooth the witch be sit crouch in her chair with 
 her hand on the elbow and her head thrust forward , like a wild 
 beast at point to spring ; but when her eye fall on Birdalone , she 
 falter and draw back into herself again , and mutter somewhat 
 unheard ; but to Birdalone spake nought of good or bad . 

 now be winter - tide upon they , when there be nought to do in field 
 and acre , and but a little in the byre . in year bygone , and even in 
 the last one , the witch have not spar Birdalone toil any the more , 
 but have make errand for she amidst the snow and bite wind , or 
 over the lake when it be lay with ice . but now she bid she to 
 nought save what she have a will to ; whereby she lose but little , 
 whereas birdalone be well willing to strive against wind and weather 
 and the roughness of the winter earth , and overcome if she might , so 
 that all be well do that have to be do about the stead . 

 still do the witch give her hard word and rail at she for the most 
 part , but from the tooth outward only , and because she be will not 
 thereto . inwardly indeed she begin to fear Birdalone , and deem 
 that she would one day have the mastery ; and this lead she into fierce 
 and restless mood ; so that she would sit star at the maiden 's 
 beauty handle her knife withal , and scarce able to forbear she . 
 and in such a mood she once make occasion to chastise she as she will not 
 have be erst , and look to see Birdalone rebel against she ; but it 
 fall out otherwise , for Birdalone submit herself to her meekly and 
 with a cheerful countenance . and this also be a terror to the 
 witch , who deem , as indeed it be , that the purpose be grow in 
 her thrall . so from that time she meddle with she no more . all 
 this while , as may be think , Birdalone go yet oftener to the Oak 
 of Tryst , despite frost and snow and wind , and gat much lore of her 
 wood - mother , and learn wisdom abundantly . and her day be happy . 



 CHAPTER XVIII . of SPRING - TIDE and the MIND of BIRDALONE 



 now be the winter go and the spring - tide come again , and with the 
 blossoming of the earth blossom Birdalone also . Nought sweet of 
 flesh might she be than erst , but there be now a new majesty grow 
 into her beauty ; her limb be rounded , her body fulfil , her skin 
 sleek and whiten ; and if any mother 's son have behold her foot as 
 they trod the meadow besprinkle with saffron and daffodil , ill have 
 it go with he be he gainsay the kiss of they , though for the 
 kissing have he fare the bad belike . 

 that spring - tide , amidst of April , she follow the witch - wife down 
 to the Sending Boat for the third time ; and there go everything as 
 erst , and she deem now that the lesson be well learn , and that 
 she be well - nigh as wise as the witch herself therein . 

 but the day after she go about somewhat pensive , as though a 
 troublous thought be on she ; and when , three day thereafter , she 
 meet the wood - mother , she spake to she even as they part , and say : 
 Mother , much wisdom hast thou learn I , and now this at the last 
 withal , that hitherto there have be shame in my life ; and now fain 
 be I to be do with it . fair child , say Habundia , little be the 
 shame though this woman hath have the upper hand of thee and hath use 
 thee cruelly : how might thou , a child , strive with she ? but now 
 I see and know that there be an end of that ; that she feareth thee 
 now , and will never again raise a hand against thee save thou fall 
 wholly into her power ; as thou shalt not , my child . be comfort 
 then for what be go by ! Nay , mother , say Birdalone , it be not 
 that which troubleth I ; for , as thou sayest , what else might I do ? 
 but thy wisdom which thou hast set in my heart hath learn I that 
 for these last month I have be meet guile with guile and lie 
 with lie . and now will I do so no more , lest I become a guileful 
 woman , with nought good in I save the fairness of my body . 
 wherefore hearken , sweet mother ! what be do , be do ; but when it 
 cometh to the day , which be speedily draw nigh , that I must part 
 from thee , it may be for a long while , then will I not fare to the 
 Sending Boat by night and cloud and with hidden head , but will walk 
 thither in broad day , and let that befall which must befall . 

 change then Habundia 's face and become haggard and woeful , and she 
 cry out : o if I could but weep , as ye child of Adam ! o my 
 grief and sorrow ! child , child ! then will betide that fall into 
 her hand which I spake of e'en now ; and then shall this wretch , this 
 servant of evil , assuredly slay thee there and then , or will keep 
 thee to torment thee till thy life be but a slow death . Nay , nay , do 
 as I should do , and fare with hidden head , and my ring on thy finger . 
 or else , o child , how wilt thou hurt I ! 

 birdalone weep ; but presently she fall to caress the mother 's 
 hand , and say : this be thy doing , wherein thou hast make I wise . 
 yet fear not : for I deem that the witch - wife will not slay I , 
 whereas she looketh to have some gain of I ; moreover , in the evil of 
 her heart be mingle some love toward I , whereof , as erst I tell 
 thee , I have a morsel of compassion . mother , she will not slay I ; 
 and I say that she shall not torment I , for I will compel she to 
 slay I else . it be my mind that she will let I go . say the 
 mother : yea , mayhappen , yet but as a bird with a string to its leg . 
 if it be so , say Birdalone , then let my luck prevail over her guile ; 
 as well it may be , since I have know thee , o wise mother ! 

 the wood - wife hang her head and spake nought for a while ; then she 
 say : I see that thou wilt have it so , and that there be something 
 in thine heart which we , who be not child of Adam , may not 
 understand ; yet once wert thou more like unto we . now all I may say 
 be , that thou must rule in this matter , and that I be sad . 

 then she look down again and presently raise a bright face , and 
 say : belike all shall be well than I think . then she kiss 
 birdalone and they part for that time . 



 CHAPTER XIX . THEY BID FAREWELL , BIRDALONE and the wood - mother 



 now April be go , and May be come with the thorn a - blossoming , and 
 there be birdalone wax still in loveliness . and now the witch 
 have leave all gird at she even , and spake to she but little , save 
 when she need must . but to Birdalone it seem that she watch she 
 exceed closely . 

 birdalone go oft to the wood , and learn yet more of lore : but of 
 the matter of the departure , how it be to be go about they spake 
 no more , and great be the love betwixt they . 

 at last when May be wear nigh to June come Birdalone to the Oak of 
 Tryst , and find the wood - mother there ; and when they have talk a 
 while , but ever from the tooth out , spake Habundia : though thou be 
 now the wiser of we two maybe , yet have I wisdom to wot that this be 
 the hour of our sundering , and that to - morrow thou wilt try the 
 adventure of the Sending Boat : be it not so ? yea , mother , say 
 birdalone ; I bid thee farewell now : woe be I therefor ! say 
 Habundia : and thou wilt deliver thyself into the hand of the witch , 
 wilt thou , as thou saidst that other day ? Quoth Birdalone : be it 
 not wisdom , dear mother , if I trust in my goodhap ? alas , say the 
 mother , it may be so when all be say . but o my sad heart ! and how I 
 fear for thee ! 

 my mother , my mother ! say Birdalone , that I should make the day 
 grievous unto thee ! and thou who hast make my day so joyous ! but 
 now canst thou not say of thy wisdom that we shall meet again ? 

 the wood - woman sit down , and let her head fall over her knee , and 
 be silent a long while ; then she rise up and stand before Birdalone , 
 and say : yea , we shall meet again , howsoever it may be . let we 
 depart with that sweet word in the air between we . yet first thou 
 shalt give I a tress of thine hair , as I do to thee when first we 
 meet ; for by mean of it may I know to - morrow how thou hast speed . 

 even so do Birdalone , and this be the end of their talk , save 
 break word of lamentation as they say farewell . and therewith for 
 that while they sunder . 



 CHAPTER XX . of birdalone and the SENDING BOAT 



 birdalone wake up in the morning , and arise and clothe herself , and she 
 see not the witch - wife in the chamber , though her bed look as if it 
 have be sleep in . birdalone account little thereof , whereas the 
 dame would oft go on one errand or another much betime in the 
 morning . yet be she somewhat glad , for she be nowise wishful for a 
 wrangle with she . Withal , despite her valiancy , as may well be 
 think , she be all a - flutter with hope and fear , and must need 
 refrain her body from overmuch quaking and restlessness if she might . 

 now she mingle the tress of the wood - mother 's hair with her own 
 hair , but deem it nought perilous to leave the ring yet sew to her 
 smock : she set some deal of bread and flesh in her scrip , lest she 
 voyage should be long , and then all simply step over the threshold 
 of the House of her Captivity . 

 she go straight to the strand aforesaid , see nought of the 
 witch - wife by the way ; and when she come there , be about to turn 
 straightway to her left hand down to the creek , when it come into her 
 mind that she would first swim over to Green Eyot for this last of 
 time . for the eyot indeed she love , and deem it her own , since 
 never have her evil dream , the witch , set foot thereon . moreover , she 
 say to herself that the cool lake would allay the fever of her 
 blood , and make her flesh firm and less timorous for the adventure . 
 and again , that if the witch should see she from afar , as she could 
 scarce fail to do , she would deem the maiden be about she wonte 
 morning swimming , and would be the less like to spy on she . 

 so now , when she have let her garment slip from off she on to the 
 sand close to the water 's edge , she stand a while , with her foot 
 scarce cover by the little ripple of the bight , to be a token of 
 safety to her mistress . to say sooth , now it be come so nigh to the 
 deed , she shrink aback a little , and be fain to dally with the time , 
 and , if it might be , thrust something of no import betwixt she and 
 the terror of the last moment . 

 now she take the water , and row strongly with her lovely limb till 
 she come to the eyot , and there she go aland , and visit every 
 place which have be kind to she ; and kiss the tree and flower 
 that have solace she , and once more draw the bird and rabbit to 
 sport with she ; till suddenly it come into her head that the time be 
 wear overfast . then she run down to the water and plunge in , and 
 swam over to the strand as fast as she might , and come aland there , 
 thinking of nothing less than what have befall . 

 for lo ! when she look around for her raiment and her scrip , it be 
 nowhere to be see ; straightway then it come into her mind , as in one 
 flash , that this be the witch 's work ; that she have divine this deed 
 of the flight , and have watch she , and take the occasion of her 
 nakedness and absence that she might draw she back to the House of 
 Captivity . and this the more as the precious ring be sew to 
 Birdalone 's smock , and the witch would have find it there when she 
 handle the raiment . 

 birdalone waste no time in seek for the lost ; she look down on 
 to the smooth sand , and see there footprint which be not her own , 
 and all those go straight back home to the house . then she turn , 
 and for one moment of time look up toward the house , and see 
 plainly the witch come out adoor , and the sun flash from something 
 bright in her hand . 

 then indeed she make no stay , but set off run at her swift 
 along the water - side toward the creek and the Sending Boat . as be 
 aforesaid she be as fleet - foot as a deer , so but in a little space 
 of time she have come to the creek , and leapt into the boat , pant 
 and breathless . she turn and look hastily along the path her 
 foot have just wear , and deem she see a flutter and flash 
 come along it , but some way off ; yet be not sure , for her eye 
 be dizzy with the swiftness of her flight and the hot sun and the 
 hurry of her heart . then she look about a moment confusedly , for 
 she call to mind that in her nakedness she have neither knife , nor 
 scissor , nor bodkin to let her blood withal . but even therewith 
 close to hand she see hang down a stem of half - dead briar - rose 
 with big thorn upon it ; she hastily tear off a length thereof and 
 scratch her left arm till the blood flow , and step lightly 
 first to stem and then to stern , and besmear they therewith . then 
 she sit down on the thwart and cry aloud : 


 the red raven - wine now 
 Hast thou drunk , stern and bow ; 
 then wake and awake 
 and the wonte way take ! 
 the way of the Wender forth over the flood , 
 for the will of the Sender be blent with the blood . 


 Scarce have she time to wonder if the boat would obey her spell ere it 
 begin to stir beneath she , and then glide out into the lake and take 
 its way over the summer ripple , go betwixt Green Eyot and the 
 mainland , as if to weather the western ness of the eyot : and it go 
 not a stonecast from the shore of the said mainland . 

 Hither to meet it now cometh the witch , run along the bank , her 
 skirt fly wild about she , and a heavy short - sword gleaming in her 
 hand . her furious running she stay over against the boat , and 
 cry out in a voice break for lack of breath : 


 back over the flood 
 to the house by the wood ! 
 back unto thy rest 
 in the alder nest ! 
 for the blood of the Sender lie warm on thy bow , 
 and the heart of the Wender be weary as now . 


 but she see that the Sending Boat heed her word nothing , whereas 
 it be not her blood that have awaken it , but Birdalone 's . then 
 cry out the witch : o child , child ! say the spell and come back to 
 I ! to I , who have rear thee and love thee and hop in thee ! O 
 come back ! 

 but how should Birdalone heed her prayer ? she see the sax ; and 
 withal have her heart forget , her flesh might well remember . she 
 sit still , nor so much as turn her head toward the witch - wife . 

 then come wild yelling word from the witch 's mouth , and she cry : 
 go then , naked and outcast ! go then , naked fool ! and come back 
 hither after thou hast be under the hand of the pitiless ! ah , it 
 have be well for thee have I slain thee ! and therewith she whirl 
 the sax over her head and cast it at Birdalone . but now have the boat 
 turn its head toward the ness of Green Eyot and be swiftly 
 departing , so that Birdalone but half hear the last word of the 
 witch - wife , and the sax fall flash into the water far astern . 

 there the witch stand toss her arm and screaming , wordless ; but 
 no more of her see Birdalone , for the boat come round about the ness 
 of Green Eyot , and there lie the Great Water under the summer heaven 
 all wide and landless before she . and it be now noon of day . 


 here end the First Part of the Water of the Wondrous Isles , which be 
 call of the House of Captivity . and now begin the Second Part , 
 which be call of the Wondrous Isles . 




 the second part : of the WONDROUS ISLES 




 CHAPTER I. the first ISLE 



 so glide Birdalone over the lake and be come forth from the House 
 of Captivity ; it might well be that she be but swim unto death ; 
 naked as she be , fireless , foodless , and helpless , at the mercy of 
 mere sorcery . yet she call to mind the word of the wood - mother 
 that they should meet again , and take heart thereby ; and she be glad 
 in that she have have her will , and shake off the guile and thraldom 
 of the witch . much she think of the wood - mother , and love she , 
 and wonder have she yet seek into and see her welfare by the 
 burning of a hair of that tress of her ; and therewith she look on 
 that tress of Habundia 's hair and kiss it . 

 all day the Sending Boat speed on , and she see no land and nought to 
 tell of . it be but wave and sky and the familiar fowl of the lake , 
 as coot , and mallard , and heron , and now and then a swift wood - dove 
 go her way from shore to shore ; two gerfalcon she see also , an 
 osprey , and a great ern on his errand high up aloft . 

 birdalone wake in her loneliness till the day be spend , and 
 somewhat worn of the night ; then she fall asleep for weariness ; but 
 so it be , that before dusk she have deem that a blue cloud lie 
 before she in the offing which move not . 

 she sleep the short night through , and be awaken by the boat 
 smite against something , and when her eye open she see that she 
 be come aland and that the sun be just risen . she stand up , and 
 for the first minute wonder where she be , and she behold her 
 nakedness and know not what it mean ; then she loosen her hair , and 
 shake its abundance all about she , and thereafter she turn her eye 
 on this new land and see that it be fair and goodly . the flowery 
 grass come down to the very water , and first be a fair meadow - land 
 besprinkle with big ancient tree ; thence arise slope of vineyard , 
 and orchard and garden ; and , look down on all , be a great White 
 House , carven and glorious . a little air of wind have awaken with 
 the sunrise , and bear the garden sweetness down to she ; and warm it 
 be after the chill of the wide water . no other land could she see 
 when she look lakeward thence . 

 she step ashore , and stand ankle - deep in the sweet grass , and 
 look about she for a while , and see no shape of man astir . she be 
 yet weary , and stiff with abide so long amongst the hard rib of 
 the boat , so she lay herself down on the grass , and its softness 
 solace she ; and presently she fall asleep again . 



 CHAPTER II . birdalone falleth in with new friend 



 when she next awoke , the sun be not yet high , and the morning young , 
 yet she stand upon her foot much refresh by that short slumber . 
 she turn toward the hill and the gay house , and see one come over 
 the meadow to she , a woman to wit , in a shin golden gown , and as 
 she draw nigh Birdalone could see that she be young and fair , tall , 
 white - skinned and hazel - eye , with long red hair dance all about 
 she as she trip lightly and merrily over the greensward . 

 now she come up to Birdalone with wonder in her eye , and greet she 
 kindly , and ask she of her name , and Birdalone tell it all simply ; 
 and the new - comer say : what errand hast thou hither , that thou art 
 come thus naked and alone in this ill - omen ferry ? birdalone 
 tremble at her word , though she spake kindly to she , and she say : 
 it be a long story , but fate drave I thereto , and misery , and I know 
 not whither I be bind . but be there no welcome for I in this 
 lovely land ? I lack not deftness wholly ; and I will be a servant of 
 servant , and ask no well if it must be so . say the new - comer : 
 unto that mayst thou come , but sore will be thy servitude . I fear I 
 thy welcome here may be but evil . say birdalone : wilt thou not 
 tell I how so ? Quoth that lady : we know thy ferry here , that it be 
 the craft wherein cometh hither now and again the sister of our lady 
 the Queen , into whose realm thou art now come , and who liveth up in 
 the white palace yonder , and whom we serve . and meseem thou wilt 
 not have come hither by she leave , or thou wouldst be in other guise 
 than this ; so that belike thou wilt be the runaway of thy mistress . 
 wherefore I fear that thou wilt be send back to thy say mistress 
 after a while , and that that while will be grievous to thee , body and 
 soul . 

 Birdalone 's heart sink , and she be pale and trembling ; but she say : 
 o dear lady , might I then depart as I have come hither , without the 
 wotting of this Queen ! after thou hast give I a morsel of bread , 
 for I be hungry . say the gold - clothe one , look on her pitifully : 
 Nay , maiden , I can not choose but bring thee before our mistress , 
 whereas most like she hath already see thee from above there . for 
 she be far - sight beyond the will not of folk who be more manlike . but 
 as for the bread , see thou ! I have bring a manchet in my pouch , 
 and cheese withal , as I come hurry ; for I think , she will be 
 hungry . and she reach the victual out to she . and Birdalone take 
 it and kiss the golden lady 's hand , and she might not refrain her 
 tear , but weep as she eat . 

 meanwhile the golden lady spake unto she and say : nevertheless , 
 thou poor maiden , somewhat may be do for thine helping , and I will 
 presently speak to my sister thereon , who be , both of they , wise 
 than I. Sisters by blood be we not , but by love and fellowship . 
 and I doubt not but that as we go up into the house we shall happen 
 upon they in the garden . but now I look upon thee , how fair a woman 
 art thou ! 

 thou art kind and friendly , say Birdalone , smile amidst of her 
 tear , might I know by what name to call so dear a woman ? thou shalt 
 call I Aurea , say the other ; and my next sister be Viridis , and the 
 third , Atra ; for that be accord to the hue of our raiment , and 
 other name we have not now . and lo ! here cometh Viridis over the 
 meadow . 

 birdalone look , and see a woman come toward they clothe all in 
 green , with a rose - wreath on her head . and she draw nigh , and 
 greet Birdalone kindly , and she also be a very beauteous woman ; 
 not great of body , whereas Aurea be tall and big - make , though 
 excellently shapen . light brown and goodly wave of hair be 
 Viridis , her eye brown , and rather long than great ; her lip full 
 and ruddy , her cheek soft and sweet and smooth , and as rosy - tint 
 pearl ; her hand small and delicate of fashion ; her whole body soft- 
 shapen as an egg ; a kind , wheedle look her face bore . 

 when she have look a while on Birdalone , she kiss she , and say : 
 I would thou wert happier , for thou art beauteous , and all but the 
 evil must love thee . Therewith she draw a cate from her pouch , and 
 say : eat somewhat , for thou wilt be hungry ; and let we go meet our 
 other sister , who be wise than we . 

 so they go , all three of they , and come from off the meadow on to 
 the garden - slope , and at the entry thereof be come Atra to meet 
 they ; she be clothe all in black , a tall , slim woman , with the grace 
 of the willow - bough in the wind , with dark plenteous hair and grey 
 hawk - eye ; her skin privet - white , with but little red in her cheek . 
 she also greet Birdalone kindly , but sadly withal . she give she 
 strawberry to eat lay on a big kale - blade ; and she say : sister , 
 here be we hide by the tree , and can not be see from the house ; 
 therefore we may sit here for a minute or two , while we talk together 
 as to what may perchance be do for the helping of this unhappy 
 maiden , who be so fair and lovely , and hath stray into so ugly a 
 trap . then she say to Birdalone : Thou must know , poor wanderer , 
 that this Queen , our mistress , who be sister to the Witch under the 
 Wood , be big and strong , well - make , and white - skinned , so that she 
 deem herself a queen of all beauty : keen - eyed be she to see a fly 
 where other would see nought small than a coney ; fine - eare 
 withal ; wise in wizardry ; not altogether dull - witted , though she be 
 proud , and crueller than the cruell . but herein she faileth , that 
 her memory be of the short for matter of the pass hour , albeit 
 she remember her spell and witch - song over well . but other 
 matter will scarce abide in her head for four and twenty hour . 
 wherefore , sister , if we may keep this maiden out of her sight 
  after she hath see she and give doom upon she   till the dead of 
 to - morrow night , we may perchance do some good for she ; and it be in 
 my mind that then she may do good for we also . 

 now they rejoice in this word of Atra the wise ; and Atra pray 
 birdalone to tell they somewhat more of her story ; and she tell they 
 much ; but , whyso it be , she say nought concern the wood - wife , 
 whose outward semblance be the same as her . then they pity she , 
 and caress she ; but Atra say : we must tarry here no more , but go 
 straight up to the lady , or maybe we shall lose all . 

 so they go their way and come into the pleasance , and trod the 
 sweet greensward betwixt the garland flower and the beauteous tree ; 
 which now indeed , though Birdalone see they all clear and over - clear , 
 be become nought to she . those three also spake gently to she , and 
 now and then ask she somewhat , as if to show she that she be one 
 of themselves ; but she spake not , or answer at random , and to say 
 sooth scarce hear their word : forsooth she be now become heart- 
 sick , and be half dead for fear ; and her nakedness , which would have 
 trouble she little across the water , be now grow a shame and a 
 terror unto she , and every deal of her body quiver with the anguish 
 thereof . 



 CHAPTER III . birdalone be BROUGHT before the WITCH - WIFE 's SISTER 



 so come they at last to the very house , and whereas it stand high on 
 the bent , a great stair or perron of stone go up to it , and be of 
 much majesty . they go through the porch , which be pillare and 
 lovely , and into a great hall most nobly build , and at the other 
 end thereof , on a golden throne raise upon a dais , sit a big woman 
 clothe in red scarlet . the three damsel lead Birdalone to some four 
 pace of the great lady , and then stand away from she , and leave she 
 stand there alone , the scarlet - clothe woman before she ; on the right 
 and the leave the tall pillar go up gleam toward the roof , and 
 about her foot the dark polished pavement , with the wallowing of 
 strange beast and great serpent and dragon all do on the coal- 
 blue ground . 

 when she be so left alone , at first she totter , and go nigh to 
 fall ; but then come back some little heart to she , as she say to 
 herself that now she should verily die once for all , and that no long 
 while would be the passing from life into death . she look up and 
 behold the lady - witch , that she be somewhat like to her sister , 
 white - skinned and of plenteous golden - hair as be she , but young of 
 aspect , and nowise so ill - look as that other have now become ; for 
 somewhat well - shapen of body she be ; but her face forbid ; her 
 low lip thrust out , her cheek flaggy and droop , her eye little 
 more than half open ; to be short , a face both proud , foolish , and 
 cruel ; terrible indeed , sit in judgment in that place on a 
 shrink naked creature . 

 now she spake ; and if there be no majesty or solemnity in the 
 voice , there be ugly glee and malice therein ; but she say to those 
 damsel : be this the woman that my keen eye behold come aland from 
 my sister 's Sending Boat e'en now ? Aurea kneel on one knee , and 
 say : yea , so please you , my lady . 

 then say the witch : Ho thou ! wilt thou plead some errand hither 
 from my sister ? dost thou deem I so witless as not to know that if 
 she have send thee hither thou wouldst not have come in this plight ? 
 Nay , I know ; thou hast steal thyself from she : thou art a thief , 
 and as a thief shalt thou be deal with . 

 Spake Birdalone in a clear voice : no errand do I feign from thy 
 sister , lady : when I could bear my life there no long , I take 
 occasion to flee from she : this be all the tale . yet once and again 
 it hath be in my mind that it be thy sister who steal I from they 
 that love I . 

 Hah , thrall ! say the lady , thou art bold ; thou art over - bold , thou 
 naked wretch , to bandy word with I . what hee I thy tale now thou 
 art under my hand ? her voice be cold rather than fierce , yet be 
 there the poison of malice therein . but birdalone spake : if I be 
 bold , lady , it be because I see that I have come into the House of 
 Death . the dying may well be bold . 

 the House of Death ! cry the stupid lady ; and wilt thou call my 
 noble house the House of Death ? now art thou no long bold , 
 strip thrall , but impudent . 

 Scorn rise into Birdalone 's heart at this word , but she refrain 
 she , and spake : I mean that I have stir the wrath in thee , and 
 that thou wilt slay I therefor ; and that it availeth not to crave 
 mercy of thee . 

 laugh the lady : thou art a fool , thrall , say she ; if a sparrow 
 flee hither from my sister , I should not wring its neck , but keep it 
 for she . so shall I do with thee . I shall not slay thee , and so 
 destroy my sister 's chattel ; nor shall I spoil thee , and spoil her 
 possession . I shall send thee back unto she , the steal thrall in 
 the steal boat , when I have learn thee a lesson here . Forsooth it 
 be for that cause meseemeth that she let thee slip through her 
 finger , for she be wise enough to have stay thee from this holiday 
 have she will it . but she be tender - hearted , and kind , and soft , 
 and might well deem that if thy chastisement be do to her hand 
 here , it be well do than by her mercy . now , thrall , I have 
 speak enough to thee , or more than enough : get thee back out of 
 earshot ! 



 CHAPTER IV . of the WITCH 's prison in the WAILING - TOWER 



 Birdalone do as she be bid , and the witch call unto her Atra , 
 who come and stand humbly on the footpace beside she , and hold 
 converse with her mistress a while . then she go backward from she 
 a little , and then come to Birdalone , and in a somewhat harsh voice 
 bade she come with she . birdalone follow she , quaking , and they 
 come out of the hall and into a long passage , which lead to a wide 
 stair wind round a newel ; and all be build exceed fair , have 
 Birdalone 's heart suffer her eye to see it ; but her flesh be 
 weak , and quake before the torment to come , so that her knee well- 
 nigh fail she . 

 but now Atra lay a hand kindly on her shoulder and stay she , and 
 say : now meseem the wall of the Wailing - Tower , for so it hight , 
 have no ear to hear , and we may talk together . wottest thou why I 
 have bring thee hither ? say birdalone in a faint voice : Hast 
 thou be bid to whip I ? and if I have be so bidden , dear 
 maiden , say Atra laughing , nowise would I do it . hold up thine 
 heart ! for all hath go well so far , and now meseem betwixt we 
 three we shall save thee . 

 Birdalone 's spirit come back to she at that word , and she put she 
 hand to her face and fall a - weeping . but Atra be kind to she and 
 make much of she ; and she kiss she and wipe her tear , and 
 birdalone smile again amidst her sob , and she thank Atra ; who 
 say to she : first of all I must tell thee that I be take thee to 
 prison by the witch 's bidding . yea , say Birdalone , and what be 
 prison ? say Atra : a prison be a grim place where poor folk who 
 have do that which pleaseth not rich folk be shut up , that they 
 may be grieve and torment by not be able to fare abroad , or go 
 where they would ; and by suffer whatsoever their master may lay 
 upon they , as darkness , and cold , and hunger , and stripe . somewhat 
 so , or bad , our lady would have it for thee ; but so would not we . 
 therefore for thee shall this prison be a place where thou shalt be 
 safe till we may bring thee forth when the night hath wear towards 
 its ending . for she will have forget thee by to - morrow ; and this 
 she knoweth ; wherefore just now , when thou stoodest out of earshot , 
 she be bid I , amongst other matter , to bring thee before she 
 to - morrow morning , and tell she the tale of thee , that she might call 
 it to mind then what she have will to this morning . 

 Yea , say Birdalone , but will she not remember that she hath give 
 thee a charge concern I ? but little thereof , say Atra , and with 
 a few word I may easily confuse her memory so that speech thereon 
 will fail she . keep up thine heart , sweetle ; but let we up this 
 stair now forthwith , for I be fain to have thee hide away in this 
 prison , and then will I down to she and tell she that thou art lie 
 therein in all misery and terror , lest it come into her head to send 
 for thee ere her memory be grow dim . 

 again do Birdalone take heart , and they hasten a long way up the 
 stair , till Atra stay at last at a door all do with iron , endlong 
 and over - thwart . then she take a leash of key from her girdle , one 
 big and two little , and set the big one in the lock and turn it , 
 and shove the heavy door and enter thereby a chamber four - square 
 and vault ; and the vault be uphold by a pillar of red marble , 
 wherein , somewhat high than a man 's head , be set stanchion of 
 latten , that could be clasp and unclasped . this chamber be in a 
 way goodly , but yet grim to look on ; for the wall be all of black 
 ashlar stone close - jointed , and the floor black also , but of marble 
 polished so wholly that it be as dark water , and give back the image 
 of Birdalone 's dear foot and leg as she go thereon . the window 
 be not small , and the chamber be light in every corner because of 
 they , but they be so high up under the vaulting that none might see 
 thereout aught save the heaven . there be nought in the chamber 
 save a narrow bench of oak and three stool of the same , a great and 
 stately carven chair dight with cushion of purple and gold , and in 
 one corner a big oaken coffer . 

 now spake Atra : this be our lady 's prison , and I fear I we can not 
 make it soft for thee , dear stranger . yea , I must tell thee   and she 
 redden therewith   that it be part of my charge to set thee in 
 iron . birdalone smile on she , and be over weary to ask what that 
 mean , though she know not . but Atra go to the big coffer and 
 open it and thrust in her hand , and there be a jangling 
 therewith , and when she turn about to Birdalone again she have iron 
 chain in her hand , and she say : this shameth I , dear friend ; yet 
 if thou wouldst wear they it might be well , for she may have a mind 
 to go visit her prison , and if she find thee there unshackle she 
 shall be wroth , and oftenest her wrath hath a whip in its hand . and 
 these be the light that I might find . 

 birdalone smile again , and spake not , for she be very weary , and 
 Atra do the iron on her wrist and her ankle ; and say thereafter : 
 yet bear in mind that it be a friend that hath the key of these 
 thing . and now I will go away for a little , but I shall be on thine 
 errand ; for first I shall tell the mistress that thou art lie here 
 shackle and in all wanhope ; and next , by the will and command of 
 she , I be to see that thou be well feed and nourish to - day that thou 
 mayst be the strong for to - morrow . now if I may give thee rede , it 
 be that thou forbear to open the coffer yonder ; for ugly thing shalt 
 thou find there , and that may dishearten thee again . 

 Therewith she kiss she kindly on the cheek and go her way , and 
 the great key turn in the lock behind she . 

 there then be birdalone leave to herself ; and she be over weary even 
 to weep ; true it be that she make a step or two towards the coffer , 
 but reframe she , and take two of the pillow from the great chair 
 and turn aside into the other corner , her chain jingle as she 
 go . there she lay herself down , and nestle into the very wall- 
 nook , and presently fall asleep , and slumber dreamlessly and 
 sweetly a long while . 



 CHAPTER V. THEY feast in the WITCH 'S prison 



 birdalone be awaken by the sound of the key in the lock , and the 
 door open , and there be Atra bear dish and platter , and 
 behind her Viridis with the like gear , and beaker and a flagon to 
 boot , and both they be smile and merry . 

 Birdalone 's heart leapt up to meet they , and in especial be she 
 gladden by the coming of Viridis , who have seem to be the kind 
 of they all . 

 Viridis spake : now be come the meat for the dear sister , and it be 
 time , for surely thou art famish , and it be now long past high 
 noon . do off her iron , Atra . say Atra : maybe it be well to let 
 the fetter abide on her ankle , lest the mistress should come ; but 
 for the wrist , reach out thine hand , wayfarer . so do Birdalone , 
 and Atra lay her thing on the ground , and unlock the hand- 
 shackle , and do they off : and meanwhile Viridis spread forth the 
 banquet , partly on the floor , and partly on that ill - omen coffer . 
 then she go up to Birdalone and kiss she , and say : now shalt 
 thou sit in our lady 's throne , and we shall serve thee , and thou 
 shalt deem thee a great one . 

 Nought else would they have , and Birdalone lay her nakedness on the 
 purple cushion , and then they fall all three to the feast . the 
 victual be both plenteous and dainty , of venison and fowl , and cream 
 and fruit and sweetmeat , and good wine they have withal : never have 
 birdalone feast in like manner , and the heart come back unto she , 
 and her cheek grow rosy and her eye glitter . but she say : how 
 if your lady be to come upon we here , and we so merry ? say Atra : 
 out of the chair must thou when thou hear the key in the lock , and 
 then be all well , and she would have nought against we ; for she 
 herself bid we , and I in special , to keep thee company here , and 
 talk with thee ; and Aurea also would have be here , but that she be 
 serve the lady as now . hath she then some pity on I , say 
 Birdalone , that she hath bid thee do by I what be most to my 
 pleasure ? 

 laugh Viridis thereat , and Atra say : she hath no pity , nor ever 
 shall have ; but so hard of heart be she , that she may not deem that 
 we could love thee , a stranger , and unhappy , who can serve we in 
 nowise ; so she feareth not the abatement of thy grief from any 
 compassion of we . rather she hath send we , and I in especial , not 
 to comfort thee , but to grieve thee by word ; for she biddeth I tell 
 thee fair tale , forsooth , of what to - morrow shall be to thee , and 
 the day after ; and of how she shall begin on thee , and what shall 
 follow the beginning , and what thou mayst look for after that . for 
 by all this she deemeth to lower thy pride and abate thy valour , and 
 to make every moment of to - day a terror to thy flesh and thy soul , so 
 that thereby thou mayest thole the bitterness twice over . such be 
 her pity for thee ! and yet belike this cruelty hath save thee , for 
 but for that she have not refrain she from thee to - day , and to- 
 morrow thou shalt be far away from she . 

 meanwhile , say Viridis , in her soft sweet voice , none of all these 
 thing will we talk over with thee , but thing comfortable and kind ; 
 and we will tell each to each of our story . will we not , Atra ? yea , 
 verily , say she . 

 birdalone look upon they and say : wondrous be your compassion and 
 love - kindness unto I , and scarce do I know how to bear the burden 
 thereof . but tell I one thing truly ; will ye not suffer in my place 
 when this witch cometh to know that ye have steal I away from she ? 

 Nay , say Atra , I have tell thee that by to - morrow she will have 
 altogether , or at least almost , forget thee and thy come hither . 
 moreover , she be foresee , and hath come to know that if she raise 
 a hand against any of we three , it will lead she to her bane , save it 
 be for heavy guilt clearly prove against we . Forsooth , in the 
 early day of our captivity such a guilt we fall into , and do not 
 wholly escape , as Viridis can bear I witness . but we be now grow 
 wiser , and know our mistress well , and will give she no such joy . 

 Viridis cast her eye down at those word and Atra 's smile , and 
 turn red and then pale , and Birdalone look on she wonder what 
 ail she ; then she say : do ye sister work in the field and the 
 garden ? I mean at milk the kine and the goat , and dig the 
 earth , and sow and reaping , and the like . Nay , say Atra ; either 
 our mistress or someone else who be of marvellous might , hath so 
 ordain , that here everything waxeth of itself without tillage , or 
 sowing or reaping , or any kind of tending ; and whatso we need of 
 other matter the mistress taketh it for we from out of her wonder- 
 coffer , or suffereth we to take it for ourselves . for thou must know 
 that this land be one of the Isles of the Lake , and be call the 
 Isle of Increase Unsought . 

 Meseemeth then , say Birdalone , be the mistress of you to gainsay 
 you the gift of the Wonder - coffer , ye be undo . yea , verily , 
 say Atra ; then would be but the fruit of the earth and the wild 
 creature for our avail , and these , we have not learn how to turn 
 they into dinner and supper . and they all laugh thereat ; but 
 birdalone say : see ye then how I be right to offer myself unto you 
 as a servant , for in all matter of the house and the byre and the 
 field have I skill . but since ye would not or could not have I , I 
 wonder not that ye be ill at ease here , and long to be go , for as 
 plenteous and lovely as the isle be , and though ye live here without 
 present mishandling or pin . for , sooth to say , ye have over you a 
 tyrant and a fool . 

 Viridis answer : yet be there something else , dear friend , that 
 whet our longing to depart . tell she thereof , Atra . 

 Atra smile and say : simple it be : there be they who long for we 
 and for whom we long , and we would be together . say birdalone : be 
 these kinsfolk of your , as father , mother , sister , brother , or 
 the like ? 

 reddened Viridis again ; but Atra spake , and she also blush 
 somewhat , though she smile : those whom we love , and who love we , be 
 not quean , but carle ; neither be they of our blood , but alien , 
 till love overcometh they and causeth they to long to be of one flesh 
 with we ; and their longing be beyond measure , and they desire our 
 body , which they deem far fair than belike they be . and they 
 would bed we , and beget child on we . and all this we let they do 
 with a good will , because we love they for their might , and their 
 truth , and the hotness of their love toward we . 

 look up Viridis thereat , and her eye gleam amidst the flushing 
 of her cheek , and she say : Sister , sister ! even in such wise , and 
 no other , as they desire we do we desire they ; it be no mere good 
 will toward they from we , but longing and hot love . 

 now must atra blush no less than Viridis ; yet she but say : I have 
 tell thee hereof , Birdalone , because I deem that thou hast live 
 simply and without the sight of man ; but it be what all know in the 
 world of the son of Adam . say birdalone : thou say sooth 
 concern I . yet about this love have I learn somewhat even ere 
 to - day , and now , as ye speak and I , meseem the lore of it come 
 pour in on I and fill my heart with its sweetness . and o , to 
 have such love from any , and with such love to be love withal ! 

 dear sister , say little Viridis , fear not ; such as thou shall not 
 fail of the love of some man whom thou must need love . be it not 
 so , sister Atra ? say Atra : yea ; such love shall come unto she as 
 surely as death . 

 they be silent now a little , and it be as if some sweet incense 
 have be burn within the chamber . for Birdalone the colour come 
 and go in her cheek , her flesh quake , her heart beat quick , and 
 she be oppress by the sweetness of longing . more daintily she 
 move her limb , and lay foot to foot and feel the sleekness of her 
 side ; and tender she be of her body as of that which should one day 
 be so sorely love . 

 now she spake timidly to the other , and say : each one of you then 
 have a man who love she , and long for she and for none else ? so it 
 be , say Viridis . how sweet that shall be ! say Birdalone ; and now 
 all the more I wonder that ye could trouble yourselves over I , or 
 think of I once ; and the kind I think it of you . 

 say Atra smile on she : Nay , now must the cat be out of the bag , 
 and I must tell thee that thou art to think of we as chapman who with 
 our kindness would buy something of thee , to wit , that thou wouldst 
 do an errand for we to those three lover of our . surely , say 
 Birdalone , it be a little payment to set against your saving of my 
 life and my soul ; and have I to go barefoot over red gleed I would do 
 it . and yet , if I may go hence to your lover , why not all three of 
 you along with I ? 

 say Atra : for this reason ; thy ferry , the Sending Boat , wherein ye 
 come hither , be even somewhat akin to thy mistress and our ; and the 
 mistress here hath ban it against bear we ; and now , be we so 
 much as to touch it , such sore turmoil would arise , and such hideous 
 noise as if earth and heaven be fall together ; and the lady 
 would be on we straightway , and we should be undo ; and , as thou 
 shalt hear presently , this hath be prove . but thou , thou art free 
 of the say ferry . Forsooth I wot not why thy mistress ban it not 
 against thee ; maybe because she deem not that thou wouldst dare to 
 use it or even go anigh it . 

 birdalone consider , and think that even so it be ; that the witch 
 deem that she would not dare use the Sending Boat , nor know how to , 
 even if she come upon it , and that if she do so find it , she would 
 sicken she of the road thereto . so now she tell her friend the 
 whole tale thereof more closely than she have afore , save again what 
 pertain to Habundia ; withal she tell every word of what her 
 mistress have say to she at that time when she change she into a 
 hind . and Viridis hear and wonder , and pity she . but Atra sit 
 somewhat downcast a while . then she say : however this may be , we 
 will send thee forth to - morrow in the dawn , and take the risk of what 
 may befall thereafter ; and thou shalt bear a token for each of those 
 three that love we . for we deem that they have not forget aught , 
 but be still seek we . 

 birdalone say : Whatsoever ye bid I ; that will I do , and deem I 
 your debtor still . but now I pray you , pleasure a poor captive 
 somewhat more . wherein ? say they both ; we be all ready thereto . 
 say the maiden : would ye do so much as to tell I the tale of how 
 ye come hither , and then how it hath be with you from your first 
 come until now ? with a good will , say Atra ; hearken ! 



 CHAPTER VI . ATRA tell of how they three come unto the ISLE of 
 INCREASE UNSOUGHT 



 we be bear and breed in the land that lie south - west along this 
 Great Water , and we wax happily , and become fellow when we be 
 yet but child , and thus grow up dear friend into maidenhood and 
 womanhood . we be woo by many man , but our heart turn to none 
 of they save unto three , who be goodly , kind , and valiant ; and thou 
 mayst call they the Golden Knight , who be Aurea 's man ; the Green 
 Knight , who be man of Viridis ; and my man , the Black Squire . but in 
 this be unhap , that because of certain feud which have endure from 
 old time , this love be perilous unto they and we ; so that we live 
 in doubt and unrest . 

 come a day , now three year ago , when the king of the whole land 
 bring his folk into our lakeside country , and there hold a court 
 and a mote in a fair great meadow anigh to the water . but even as 
 the mote be hallow , and the Peace of God proclaim at the blast 
 of the war - horn , come we three woeful lady clothe in black and knelt 
 before the lord king , and pray he hearken we . and he deem that 
 we be fair , so he have compassion on we , and raise we up , and bade 
 us speak . 

 so we tell our tale , how that strife and wound and death stand 
 betwixt we and love ; and we weep , and bewail it , that our love must 
 be slay because man be wroth with each other and not with we . 

 the king look on we kindly , and say : who be the swain for whom 
 these lovely damsel make such a piece of work ? so we name they , 
 and say that they be there in the mote ; and the king know they for 
 valiant man who have do he good service ; and he cry out their 
 name , and bid they stand forth out of the throng . so forth they 
 stand , the Golden Knight , the Green Knight , and the Black Squire   and 
 he also be now a knight ; but now be they all three clothe in black , 
 and they be unarmed , save for their sword girt to their side , 
 without which no man amongst we may come to the mote , be he baron or 
 earl or duke , or the very lord king himself . 

 so the king look upon we and they , and laugh and say : fair 
 lady , ye have get I by the nose , so need must my body follow . do 
 ye three knight , whom I know for valiant man and true , take each his 
 love by the hand , and let the wedding be to - morrow . who then be 
 joyful but we ? but even at the word the king spake arise great 
 turmoil in the mote , for they smite the feud and contention awake , 
 and man throng forward against each other , and sword be draw 
 and brandish . but the king arise in his place and spake long and 
 deftly , and wax exceed wroth , while none heed he nor 
 hearken . and there stand our three man , who lay no hand to hilt , 
 but abode heart - whole by seem amid the tumult . and lovely they 
 be to look on . at last the wise man and old baron go between , 
 and by fair word appease the trouble , and the mote grow hush . 
 then spake the king : what be this , my thane ? I have deem that my 
 foeman be far away , and that ye that here be be all friend unto 
 I and unto each other . but now must we try another rede . Therewith 
 he turn unto our man and say : ye champion , be ye so much in 
 love with Love that ye will fight for he ? they all yeasaid that , 
 and then the king say : then do I declare that these three will hold 
 the field against all comer from matin till high noon , and that he 
 who vanquisheth any one of they shall have his lady and we d she if he 
 will , and , if he will , shall ransom she . and this field shall be 
 foughten after two month ' frist in these fair meadow , when I return 
 from the outermost march of the south , whereto I be now wend . 
 but when the battle be do , then let all man bow to the judgment of 
 God , whether he be well content or not , and this on peril of life and 
 limb . and now let there be deep peace between all man meanwhile ; and 
 if any break the peace , be he high or low , rich or unrich , churl or 
 earl , I swear it by the soul of my father that he shall lose nought 
 save his life therefor . 

 at these word be there a rumour of yeasay , and all man be 
 content , save we three poor maiden , into whose heart have now 
 enter fear of loss and death . 

 but our kindred on both side be glad and proud , and they be not 
 so bitter against we as they have be ; they put hand to pouch , and 
 let rear for we a fair pavilion of paint timber , all hang with silk 
 and picture cloth and saracen tapestry , by the very lake - side ; and 
 gay boat gaily bedight lie off the say pavilion for our pleasure ; 
 and when all be do , it yet lack a half month of the day of 
 battle , and thither be we bring in triumph by the kindred on a 
 fair day of May , and there be not a sword or a spear amongst the 
 whole company , and peaceful and merry be all by seem . but we 
 be not suffer to meet our lover all this while , from the time 
 when the mote be . 

 now on a day come a messenger on the spur , and do we to wit that the 
 king would be with we on the morrow , and that the day after , the 
 fateful field should be foughten . then , though the coming of this 
 day have be so long for by we , yet now it be at hand it cast we 
 into all unrest and trouble , so that we scarce know whether to go , or 
 stand , or sit , or what to do with our body . our folk , and all 
 other man withal , be so busy make ready for the morrow of to- 
 morrow , that they leave we alone to wear through the day as we might . 

 now it be afternoon , and the day hot and hazy , and we stand on the 
 very lip of the land weary with hope and fear , and strive to keep 
 good countenance to each other ; and there come a boat unto the shore 
 gaily paint and gild , and bedight with silken cloth and 
 cushion ; and the steerer thereof be a woman , not young , by seem 
 of fifty winter ; red - haired she be , thin - lipped and narrow - eye , 
 flat - breasted and strait - hippe ; an ungoodly woman , though her skin 
 be white and smooth as for her age . Hast thou ever see such an 
 one , guest ? Said Birdalone , smile : Forsooth that have I ; for such 
 an one be my mistress to behold . 

 well , say Atra , this dame stretch out her hand to we , and say : 
 will not the pretty lady , the dear lady , who have nought on hand 
 this afternoon , come into my boat and look on the face of the water , 
 so calm and fair as it be , and let their lovely hand go over the 
 gunwale and play with the ripple , and so beguile this heavy time for 
 a two hour ; and then give a little gift of a piece or two of silver 
 to a poor carline , who loveth all fair lady and bright warrior , 
 and who needeth a little livelihood ? 

 now the woman seem nought lovely unto we , and to I forsooth she 
 seem hateful ; but we look on each other , and we find that we 
 be utterly weary of go up and down on the meadow , and lie 
 about in the pavilion , and it seem as if this would give we a 
 little rest ; withal we see not that the woman could do we any hurt , 
 whereas we be three , and strong enough as woman go ; nor be we 
 mariner so evil but that we might sail or steer a boat at a pinch . 
 so we step into the boat straightway , and the woman sit aft and 
 paddle deftly with the steering oar , and we glide away from the 
 land . 

 soon we be come so far that we could but just see our pavilion 
 through the haze , which have somewhat thicken , and we say to the 
 woman that she should go about and make for the shore , and that then 
 we would go to and fro a while along by our stead . she nod 
 yeasay , and begin by seem to dight the craft for return . but 
 therewith the haze be grow suddenly into a low cloud , which come 
 down upon we from the south - west in the arm of a cold breeze , that 
 grow strong every minute , so no wonder it be though the steerer 
 might not keep head to wind ; and then who be afraid and ashamed save 
 ourselves ? 

 but the woman say , and there seem to be a mock in her voice : Ill 
 luck , pretty lady ! now be there nought for it but to drive , if we 
 would not drown . but belike this duskiness will clear presently , and 
 then at least we shall know whither we be go ; and we may either 
 turn back , or seek some other shelter , for I know the lake well ; I 
 know , I know . 

 we be too terror - stricken to speak , for we feel that still the wind 
 grow strong , and the lake begin to rise into wave , and the craft 
 to wallow ; but well - nigh therewith be the dusk and the mist go ; 
 the sky be bright blue overhead , and the westere sun shine 
 cloudless ; but on no land it shine , or on aught save the blue water 
 and the white wave - crest . 

 then weep Aurea , and this Viridis here , but as to I , I grow wroth 
 and cry out to the steerer : Accursed carline ! thou hast betray 
 we ; never now may we get back to our pavilion till the fight be 
 foughten , and our lover will deem that we have forsake they , and we 
 be sham for ever . well , well , say the carline , what remedy save 
 patience for the wind and wave ? and she laugh mockingly . Quoth 
 I : there be this remedy , that we three arise and lie hand on thee , 
 and cast thee outboard , save thou straightway turn the boat 's head 
 and back to the main . Forsooth I doubt not but that as thou hast 
 raise this foul wind against we , thou canst raise a fair wind for 
 we . 

 Hearken to the lovely lady ! quoth the carline , how she deemeth I to 
 be none other than the great God himself , to hold the wind in the 
 hollow of my hand , and still the wave with a word ! what ! be I 
 work somewhat after his image , kind lady ? and she grin 
 horribly therewith . then she say again : as to thy remedy , 
 sweetling , meseemeth it nought . for how shall ye sail this stormy 
 water when your captain be go , and ye but holiday sailor belike ? 

 as she spake , a great wave come up from the windward , and brake over 
 we , and half fill the craft , and lift she bow up tower , and 
 then down we go into the trough ; and I sit cow and quaking , and 
 spake never another word . 

 now begin the sun to sink , and the wind abate , and the sea go 
 down , but the boat speed on as swift as ever over the landless water . 

 now the sun be down , and dusk be at hand , and the carline spake , 
 and draw a bright - gleam sax from under her raiment : damsel , I 
 warn you that now it be good that ye obey I in all thing ; for 
 though ye be three and I one , yet whereas I have here an edge - friend , 
 I may take the life of any one of you , or of all three , as simply as 
 I could cut a lamb 's throat . moreover it will serve you well in 
 the house whereto ye be wend , that I make a good tale of you 
 rather than a bad . for the mistress of that house be of all might ; 
 and I must say it of she , though she be my very sister , yet she be 
 not so sweet - temper and kind of heart as I be , but somewhat rough 
 and unyielding of mood , so that it be good to please she . wherefore , 
 maiden , I rede you be sage . 

 our unhappy heart be now so sink in wan - hope , that we have no 
 word wherewith to answer she , and she spake : now obey ye my bidding 
 and eat and drink , that ye may come hale and sound to your journey 's 
 end , for I would not give starveling to my dear sister . Therewith 
 she bring forth victual for we , and that nought evil , of flesh and 
 bread , and cheese and cake , and good wine withal ; and we be 
 hunger - weary as well as sorrow - weary ; and hunger do at that moment 
 overcome sorrow , so we eat and drank , and , would we , would we not , 
 something of heart come back to we thereby . then again spake the 
 carline : now my will be that ye sleep ; and ye have cushion and 
 cloth enough to dight you a fair bed ; and this bidding be easy for 
 you to obey . Forsooth , so weary be we with sorrow , and our hunger 
 be now quench , that we lay we down and sleep at once , and forgat 
 our trouble . 

 when we awake it be after the first dawn , and we be come aland 
 even where thou didst this morning , guest . and thou mayst deem it 
 wondrous , but so it be , that close to where our boat take land lay 
 the ferry which bring thee hither . 

 now the carline bade we get ashore , and we do so , and find the land 
 wondrous fair , little as that solace we then . but she say unto we : 
 Hearken ! now be ye come home ; and long shall ye dwell here , for 
 never shall ye depart hence save by the will of my sister and I , 
 wherefore , once more , I rede you be good , for it will be well for 
 you . go forth now unto yonder house , and on the way ye shall meet 
 the queen of this land , and ye have nought to do but to say to she 
 that ye be the Gift ; and then shall she see to your matter . 

 Therewith she gat into her own craft , the Sending Boat , and therein 
 do the deed and spake the word ye wot of , and be go north - away ; 
 and when we turn to seek for our boat wherein we have come hither , 
 it be go . 

 we stand miserably for a while on the lip of the land , and then I 
 say that we might as well go meet our fate as die there of grief and 
 hunger . so we go , and come into those fair garden , and as we go 
 slowly up toward the house come on we a woman clothe in red scarlet and 
 grandly dight . a big woman she be , and like to she that beguile 
 we , but far young and fair of favour , foolish and proud of 
 visage . she star on we , and seem half afeard of we at first , but 
 ask we what we be , and I answer that we be the Gift . the 
 Gift ? say she , what meaneth that ? will ye obey I in all thing ? 
 if ye gainsay it , ye will perish , unless ye can eat grass ; for on 
 this isle everything cometh from my hand . 

 what might we do ? we all knelt down before she , and swear to do she 
 will . then she say , after she have star on we a while : now I 
 know : ye be they of whom my sister spake , that she would fetch I a 
 gift of a leash of damsel for my service . now I take the Gift and 
 thank her good heart . but if ye would do my will , then . . . but 
 she break off here and star at we a long while , and then she say : 
 now I know ; she bid I treat you well , and hold my hand from you , or 
 evil would come of it , belike at last my bane . so go ye home to the 
 house , and I will give you meat and drink , and show you my store and 
 the wonder - coffer , and ye shall serve I in honour . 

 even so do we ; and we eat and drank and rest , and nought we 
 lack , save leave to depart home to our lover , and some mistress 
 well than this stupid and proud lump of flesh . but the next 
 morning when we come before the lady , she know nought of what we 
 be ; and again we have to tell she that we be the Gift , and again 
 she glare at we balefully , and again she call to mind her sister 
 and her rede concern we . and this go on for many day , till at 
 last she get to know what we be ; and she follow her sister 's rede 
 in that she never mishandle we , though we could see that it irk 
 she to forbear , nor do she speak to we more roughly than her fool 's 
 will not be ; and we have in our hand all that be need for our 
 sustenance , and live easily enough . 

 now our come hither betid three year ago , and a month thereafter 
 come thy witch hither in her ferry , and she greet we when we meet , 
 and ask we , grin , have she not be kind to win we such good 
 day ? yea , and over kind , say she , ye would deem I , know ye what 
 would have betid you save for my good word . Forsooth we deem it no 
 kind deed to steal we from our lover ; but we keep good tongue in 
 our head , for thrall must need kiss the rod . 

 she go away in two day , but come again many time thereafter , till 
 we win the secret of the Sending Boat , and her spell therewith ; but 
 we know not that be ban against we . wherefore on a day in the 
 grey of the morning , when we have be on this isle somewhat less than 
 a year , we go down to it and step in , and reddened stem and 
 stern and say the spell - word . but straightway arise an hideous 
 braying and clatter , and thunder come therewith , and tremble of the 
 earth , and the water of the lake arise in huge wave ; nor might we 
 move from our seat in the boat till the two witch come run 
 down to we , and hale we out ashore , and have we up into the house ,

 sister , wert send hither by heaven for our helping . 

 now what become of the king 's court , and the hazelled field of our

 thee ; but if they be alive , it be to they that we would have thee do 
 our errand , and thereof will we tell thee closely to - morrow . and so , 
 sweetling , an end of my tale . 



 CHAPTER VII . the three damsel take birdalone out of the WITCH 'S 
 prison 



 birdalone thank Atra much for her tale , and strange it be to she 
 to hear of such new thing and the deed of folk ; but the dealing of 
 the witch with those three be familiar to she and be of her 
 world . 

 now they talk merrily , till there come a footstep to the door and 
 one without knocked . Viridis pal thereat , and a pang of fear smote

 Therewith she take her hand , and lead she forth of the prison , and

 CHAPTER VIII . in what WISE birdalone be clad , and how SHE go she 
 way from the ISLE of INCREASE UNSOUGHT 



 when they be all clear of the orchard tree the three damsel keep

 I .

 the red raven - wine now 
 Hast thou drunk , stern and bow ; 
 awake then , awake ! 
 and the southward way take : 
 the way of the Wender forth over the flood ,

 CHAPTER IX . how birdalone come to the ISLE of the YOUNG and the OLD

 wherefore she lay he so that he be easy , and then stand up and
