 compel they . but forsooth , go thither , and come back again , 
 some peril there may be therein . and yet for week past there have 
 be no word of any unpeace ; and the Red Knight it be say for 
 certain be not rid . 

 birdalone be silent a while ; then she say : fair and kind friend , I 
 be eat my heart out in longing for the come back of my friend , 
 and it be like , that unless I take to some remedy , I shall fall sick 
 thereby , and then when they come back there shall be in I but sorry 
 cheer for they . now the remedy I know , and it be that I betake I 
 alone to this adventure of the Black Valley ; for meseemeth that I 
 shall gain health and strength by my go thither . wherefore , to be 
 short , if thou wilt help I , I will go to - morrow . what sayest thou , 
 wilt thou help I ? 

 he turn very red and spake : Lady , why shouldest thou go , as thy 
 name be , birdalone ? Thou hast call I just now thy kind friend , so 
 kind as it be of thee ; now therefore why should not thy friend go 
 with thee ? 

 kindly indeed she smile on he , but shake her head : I call thee 
 trusty and dear friend again , say she ; but what I would do I must do 
 myself . moreover to what end shouldst thou go ? if I fall in with 
 ghost , a score of man would help I nought ; and if I happen on 
 weapone man who would do I scathe , of what avail be one man 
 against they ? and look thou , Sir Leonard , there be this avail in 
 thine abide behind ; if I come not back in two day ' space , or three 
 at the most , thou wilt wot that I have fare amiss , and then mayst 
 thou let it be know whither I go , and man will seek I and deliver 
 I maybe . 

 Therewith she stay her word suddenly , and turn very pale , and 
 lay her hand on her bosom , and say faintly : but o my heart , my 
 heart ! if they should come while I be away ! and she seem like to 
 swoon . 

 Leonard be afraid thereat , and know not what to do ; but presently 
 the colour come into her face again , and in a little while she 
 smile , and say : Seest thou not , friend , how weak I be get to 
 be , and that I must now beyond doubt have the remedy ? wilt thou not 
 help I do it ? 

 yea verily , say he ; but in what wise wilt thou have it ? he spake as 
 a man distraught and redeless ; but she smile on he pleasantly , and

 await I somewhat anigh the gate , so that I may not have to go 
 afoot : for I be become soft and feeble with all this house - life . 

 Leonard seem to wake up with that word , and say : I have the key 
 of the priest 's door of the chapel , and the postern beyond it ; that 
 shall be thine out - gate , lady . I will come and scratch at thy 
 chamber - door much betime , and I will see to it that thy palfrey be 
 bestow in the bower wherein thou didst rest the first night thou 
 camest amongst we . she say : I trust thee , friend . and she thank 
 he sweetly , and then rise up and fall to pace the hall up and 
 down . Leonard hang about watch she a while , she nought forbid 
 he , for her thought be elsewhere , and she have forget he ; and 
 at last he go his way to set about do what she would . 



 CHAPTER VIII . birdalone FARES on her adventure 



 Dawn be but just begin when Birdalone awake , and though she have 
 not hear Leonard at the door , she spring out of bed and clothe 
 herself , do on her black gown ; and she have a scrip with some bread 
 therein , and a sharp knife at her girdle . then even as she have do 
 she hear the priest 's nail on the door , and she turn thereto ; but 
 as she go , her eye catch her bow and quiver of arrow where they 
 hang on the wall , so she take the bow in her hand and sling the 
 quiver over her shoulder ere she open the door and find Leonard 
 stand there . neither of they spake aught , but they stole 
 downstair , and so to the chapel and out by the priest 's door and the 
 postern in the wall - nook , and be presently out in the fresh morning 
 air ; and Birdalone be joyous and lightfoot , and scarce feel the 
 earth beneath her sol for pleasure of her hope , whereas she deem 
 she have a thing to crave of the Stony Folk , if they should come alive 
 before she . fain be she , if she might withal , to give a joy to 
 some other ; so that when they be go but a little way from the 
 castle she reach out her hand to Leonard and take his , and say : 
 Hand in hand we walk when first I go this way , and I deem thee 
 kind and friendly then , and even so hast thou be sithence . 

 he be dumbfoundere at first for joy of the touch of her hand and 
 the sweetness of her word ; but presently he spake to her confused 
 and stammering , and praise she that she have think to take her bow 
 and arrow ; for , say he , that they might stand she in stead for 
 defence or for get of food , or for an excuse for wend the 
 wood . she nod yeasay unto he , and bid he again to bide three 
 day for she , and if she come not again in that time , to make a clean 
 breast of it to Sir Aymeris . 

 Yea , say the priest , and then . . . why , what then ? he can but 
 shove I out by the shoulder , and then I can seek to the little 
 house of canon that be at Gate Cross on the road to Greenford . 

 ah , my friend ! say Birdalone , how we woman think of nothing at all 
 but ourselves ! and wilt thou be thrust out of thine home for help 
 I herein ? why do I not look to my palfrey myself ? and the key I 
 might have steal from thee , always with thy good will . but now I 
 see that I have do thee a hurt . 

 say Sir Leonard : Lady , a priest hath a home wheresoever be an house 
 of religion . there be no harm do , save Sir Aymeris bethink he of 
 hang I over the battlement ; as I doubt he will not with a 
 priest . moreover , I pray thee believe , that wert thou go from the 
 castle , house and home be none for I there . and he look upon 
 she piteously , as if he be beseech . 

 but she know not what to say , and hang her head adown ; and presently 
 they be come to the bower in the copse , which this time be a 
 stable for Birdalone 's palfrey instead of a chamber for herself . so

 with he just in the cover of the copse wait to put her foot in

 stand there as if he be strive with his word . 

 so she say : now be need of haste to be go . yet one word , my 
 friend : be there aught betwixt we wherein I have do thee wrong ? 
 if so it be , I pray thee to say out what it be ; for it may be   though 
 I think it not   thou shalt not see I again from henceforth . 

 he catch his breath , as if he have much ado to refrain the sobbing ;

 not again , I hee not what shall befall I . Thou hast do I no 
 wrong . there be this only betwixt we , that I love thee , and thou 
 lov not I . 

 she look on he sweetly and pitifully , and say : I may not choose 
 but understand thy word , to wit , that thy love for I be the desire 
 of a man toward a woman ; and that be unhappy ; for I love thee indeed , 
 but not as a woman loveth a man . it be good to say thus much to thee 
 downright . but I feel in my heart that when I have say it , it be as

 he stand before she abash , but he say at last : now art thou so 
 sweet , and so kind , and so true , that I must perforce love thee yet 
 more ; and this maketh I bold to say that thou mayst help I a 
 little , or so meseemeth . how so ? say Birdalone . Quoth he : if thou

 and therewith she put up her face to he , and he kiss she cheek 
 without touch she otherwise , and then he kiss her mouth ; and she

 ashamed ; so she but say : Farewell , friend , till to - morrow at least . 

 and therewith her foot be in the stirrup , and anon she sit in the 
 saddle , and her palfrey be amble briskly on the way she would . 



 CHAPTER IX . birdalone come to the BLACK VALLEY 



 Little be to tell of Birdalone 's journey unto the knoll above the 
 Black Valley of the Greywethers . it be about noon when she come 
 there , and have meet but few folk on the way , and those few be 
 husbandman , or carline , or maidens wending afield betime not far 
 from the Castle of the Quest . 

 now she sit on her horse and look down into the dale and its stony 
 people once more , and see nought stir save three raven who , not 
 far off , be flap about from stone to stone of the Greywethers , 
 and croak loud to each other as if some tiding be toward . she 
 watch their play for a little , and then gat off her horse , and sit 
 down on the grass of the knoll , and draw forth her victual , and eat 
 and drank ; for she deem it happy to eat and drink there than in 
 the very jaw of the Black Valley . 

 soon be her dinner do , and then she get to her saddle again , and 
 ride slowly down to the little stream , and along it toward the valley 
 and the gate of the mountain , which she have be fain to pass 
 through ; but now , as have happe with she that morning when she be 
 boun for the Sending Boat , somewhat she hang back from the adventure , 
 and when she lack but some five score yard from the very dale 
 itself , she light down again , and let her way - beast bite the grass , 
 while she sit down and watch the ripple water . 

 in a while she draw off shoon and hosen , and stand in the shallow 
 ripple , and bath her hand and face withal , and stoop up - stream 
 and drink from the hollow of her hand , and so step ashore and be 
 waxen hardier ; then she string her bow and look to the shaft in 
 her quiver , and do on her foot - gear , and mount once more , and so 
 ride a brisk amble right on into the dale , and be soon come amongst 
 the Greywethers ; and she see that they be a many , and that all the 
 bottom of the dale be besprinkle with they on either side of the 
 stream , and some stand in the very stream itself , the ground whereof 
 be black even as the rest of the valley , although the water run over 
 it as clear as glass . 

 as for the dale , now she be fairly within it , she could see but a 
 little way up it , for it wind much , and at first away from her left 
 hand , and the side of it go up in somewhat steep scree on either 
 side , which be top with mere upright stave and burg of black 
 rock ; and these be specially big and outthruste on the right hand 
 of she ; and but a furlong ahead of where she be , one of these burg 
 thrust out past the scree and come down sheer into the dale , and 
 straiten it so much that there be but little way save by the 
 stream itself , which run swift indeed , but not deep , even there where 
 it be straiten by the sheer rock . 

 but up the dale would she go , whatever be before she ; and now she 
 tell herself her very purpose , as forsooth she scarce have heretofore ; 
 to wit , that she would abide in the dale the night over and see what 
 should betide , and if those wight should chance to come alive , then 
 she look to have valiance enough to face they and crave the 
 fulfilment of her desire . 

 so she take the water and ride the stream till she be past the say 
 sheer rock , and then the valley widen again , and presently be 
 wide than it be in the beginning ; and here again be the 
 greywether grow many more and close together , and , as she deem , 
 be set in ring round about one very big one , which , forsooth , be 
 somewhat in the shape of a man sit down with his hand lay on 
 his knee . 

 birdalone rein up for a minute , and look about she , and then go 
 up on to the grass , and ride straight to the say big stone , and 
 there light down from off her horse again , and stand by the stone 
 and ponder . presently she deem that she see something dark 
 move just beyond the stone , but if it be so , it be go in a 
 twinkling ; nevertheless she stand affright , and star before she 
 long , and see no more , but yet for a while durst not move hand nor 
 foot . 

 at last her courage come again , and she think : yet how if this 
 great chieftain be inwardly stir and will come awake ? shall I 
 say the word now , lest hereafter it be of no avail ? Therewith she 
 stretch out her right hand and lay it on the stone , and spake 
 aloud : o Earth , thou and thy first child , I crave of you that he 
 may come back now at once and love I . and her voice sound 
 strange and unkent to she in that solitude , and she rue it that she 
 have speak . 



 CHAPTER X. how birdalone fall in with a man in the BLACK VALLEY of 
 the greywether 



 come new tiding therewithal ; for the moment after she have speak , a

 shame that be now , and her fear that have be , she yet have no might

 now the new - comer bow before her smile , and say : I ask thy

 here ; and no harm be do thereby I trow ; for now I see that the

 betray . only this I will say , that if he loveth thee not , he be

 now Birdalone find speech and say : Knight , for such thou seemest

 he spake these last word rather as one give a command than make

 he laugh and say : Nay , now thou art angry . I compel thee not , I 
 but say that it will not do for thee to compel I to leave thee . go

 one unto I , so long as I be with thee . Forsooth , damsel , I have

 themselves compel . 

 she pal but answer nought ; then she mount her palfrey , and the

 CHAPTER XI . birdalone be lead up the BLACK VALLEY 



 as they go , the knight fall a - talk to Birdalone , and that 
 without any of the covert jeering which he have use erewhile ; and he 
 show her place in the dale , as cavern under the burg , and little 
 eyot in the stream , and certain stone amongst the greywether

 folk , and in especial how they of the plain , when he be scarce more

 the fight be ; whereas the mountain - man be fight for a life of 
 desire accomplish , which hitherto have be but a dream unto they ;

 that make it aught save death in life . wherefore up and down the

 or black sand there which have not its shower of blood ; and the stream 
 be choke with the dead , and run red out of the dale ; till at last

 the victory . 

 all this he tell she deftly and well , and though he say not so right

 goodly of fashion ; she think his eye be deep , and his face sober

 she redden at his word , and yeasaid he shortly . then say he : be

 she look on his face steadily , to wot if there be no half - hide

 now they go thus , and Birdalone not without pleasure , since she

 come they then to a level space of greensward clear of the grey

 CHAPTER XII . how those TWAIN get they from OUT of the BLACK VALLEY 
 of the greywether 



 when they have din , and have sit a while talk , the knight say : I

 the knight lay his hand upon her shoulder , and thrust she on toward 
 her palfrey , and spake fiercely , but not loud : Thee I pray not to 
 fool now ! there be not a minute to spare . if thou deem I evil , 
 as I think thou dost , there be worse than I , I tell thee , there be 
 worser . but we will talk of it when we be in the saddle , and clear 
 of this accurse dale . 

 birdalone know not what to do save obey he , so she lightly gat into 
 her saddle , and follow he , for he be mount in a twinkling , and 
 rid on . he lead out of the ring , and fall to thread the maze of 
 the greywether , keep ever toward the steep side of the dale , 
 which be on that hand that look toward the Castle of the Quest , 
 that be to say , the eastern bent . birdalone wonder at this 
 leading , and when she be come up with the knight she spake to he 
 breathlessly , and say : but , fair sir , why wend we not down the 
 dale ? he answer : first , lady , because we must hide we from they 
 straightway ; and next because they be more than we , many more , and 
 their horse be fresh , while thine at least be somewhat spend ; and if 
 they be to spur down the dale in chase , they would soon be upon we ; 
 for think not that I would escape and leave thee behind . 

 say birdalone : but thou know they , then , what they be ? since 
 thou wottest of their number and their riding . Hearken now ! upon 
 thy soul and thy salvation , be they more friend unto thee than unto 
 I ? 

 he say , as be ride on a little slow than erst : upon my soul and 
 my salvation I swear it , that the man yonder be of the bad 
 unfriend to thee that may be in the world . and now , lady , I promise 
 thee that I will unravel thee the riddle , and tell thee the whole 
 truth of these hap , whatsoever may come of my word , when we be in a 
 safe place than this ; and meantime I beseech thee to trust in I 
 thus far , as to believe that I be lead thee out of the very bad 
 peril that might befall thee . Nay , thou must need trust I ; for I 
 tell thee , that though I now love thee well than all the world and 
 all that be in it , I would slay thee here in this dale rather than 
 suffer thee to fall into the hand of these man . 

 birdalone hear he with a sick heart ; but such passion go with his 
 word that she believe what he say ; and she spake softly : Sir , I 
 will trust thee thus far ; but I beseech thee to have pity upon a poor 
 maiden who hath have but little pity show unto she until these latter 
 day ; and then : o woe 's I , to have fall out of the kindness and 
 love once more ! 

 the Black Knight spake to she in a little while , and say : what pity 
 I can to thee , that I will . once more I tell thee , that if thou but 
 know it thou wouldst thank I indeed for what I have do for thee in 
 this hour ; and henceforth I will do and forbear with thee to the 
 uttermost that love will suffer I . but lo thou ! here be we safe 
 for this present ; but we must nowise tarry . 

 birdalone look and see that they be come to the wall of the dale , 
 and that there it go down sheer to the plain thereof , and that 
 before they be a cleft that narrow speedily , and over which the 
 rock well - nigh meet , so that it be indeed almost a cave . they ride 
 into it straightway , and when that they have go but a little , and 
 because it have wind somewhat , they could but see the main valley as 
 a star of light behind they , then it narrow no more , but be as a 
 dismal street of the strait , while light and while dark , 
 accord as the rock roof it in overhead or draw away from it . 
 long they ride , and while come trickle of water from out the rock 
 on one hand or the other ; and now and again they meet a stream which 
 cover all the ground of the pass from side to side for the depth of 
 a foot or more . great rock also be strew over their path every 
 here and there , so that while must they need dismount and toil 
 afoot over the rugged stone ; and in most place the way be toilsome 
 and difficult . the knight spake little to Birdalone , save to tell 
 she of the way , and warn she where it be perilous ; and she , for she 
 part , be silent , partly for fear of the strange man , or , it might 
 be , even for hatred of he , who have thus bring she into such sore 
 trouble , and partly for grief . for , with all torment of sorrow , she 
 keep turn over and over in her mind whether her friend have yet 
 come home to the Castle of the Quest , and whether they would go seek 
 she to deliver she . and such shame take hold of she when she think 
 of their grief and confusion of soul when they should come home and 
 find she go , that she set her mind to ask if it have not be 
 well have she never meet they . yet in good sooth her mind would not 
 shape the thought , howsoever she bid it . 



 CHAPTER XIII . now they rest for the night in the STRAIT pass 



 at last , when they have be go a long while , it might be some six 
 hour , and it have long be night in the world without , but moon - light , 
 and they have rest but seldom , and then but for short while , the 
 knight draw rein and spake to Birdalone , and ask she be she not 
 weary . o yea , she say ; I be at point to pray thee suffer I to get 
 off and lie down on the bare rock . to say sooth , I be now too weary 
 to think of any peril , or what thou art , or whither we be go . he 
 say : by my deeming we be now half through this mountain highway , 
 and belike there be little peril in our resting ; for I think not that 
 any one of they knoweth of this pass , or would dare it if he do ; and 
 they doubtless come into the dale by the upper pass , which be strait 
 enough , but light and open . 

 as he speak , Birdalone bow forward on her horse 's neck , and would 
 have fall but that he stay she . then he lift she off her 
 horse , and lay she down in the seemly place he might find ; and 
 the pass there be much widen , and such light as there be in the 
 outer world come down freely into it , though it be but of the moon 
 and the star ; and the ground be rather sandy than rocky . so he 
 dight Birdalone 's bed as well as he might , and do off his surcoat 
 and lay it over she ; and then stand aloof , and gaze on she ; and he 
 mutter : it be an evil chance ; yet the pleasure of it , the pleasure 
 of it ! yea , say he again , she might well be weary ; I myself be 
 ready to drop , and I be not the least tough of the band . and 
 therewith he lay he down on the further side of the pass , and fall 
 asleep straightway . 



 CHAPTER XIV . the BLACK KNIGHT tell the truth of HIMSELF 



 when the morning be come down into the straitness of their secret 
 road , Birdalone open her eye and see the Black Knight busy over 
 dighte their horse : so she arise and thrust her grief back into 
 her heart , and give her fellow - farer the sele of the day , and he 
 bring her victual , and they eat a morsel , and gat to horse 
 thereafter and depart ; and the way become smooth , and it be 
 light overhead everywhere now , and the rock never again meet 
 overhead athwart the way ; and it seem to Birdalone that now they 
 be wend somewhat downward . 

 the knight be courteous unto Birdalone , and no long for the 
 present thrust his love upon she , so that now she have some solace of 
 his fellowship , though he be but few - speak to she . 

 it be betime when they arise , and they ride all the morning till it 
 be noon , which they might well wot of , because the way be much 
 wide , and the cliff - wall of the pass much low , so that the sun 
 shine in upon they and cheer they . 

 now the Black Knight draw rein and say : shall we rest , lady , and 
 eat ? and thereafter , if thou wilt , I shall tell thee my tale . or 
 rather , if thou wilt suffer I , I shall speak first and eat 
 afterwards , or else the morsel might stick in my throat . Knight , 
 say Birdalone , smile , I hope thou hast no lie to swallow down 
 before the meat . Nay , lady , say he ; no lie that be of moment at 
 least . 

 so they light down , and Birdalone sit on the wayside under a birch- 
 bush that come thrust out from the rock , and the knight stand 
 before she , hang his head , as though he be one accuse who would 
 plead his cause ; and he begin : 

 Lady , I must tell thee first of all , that to - day I have do as an 
 unfaithful servant and a traitor to my lord . say Birdalone simply : 
 shall I tell thee the truth , and say that from the first I seem to 
 see in thee that thou wert scarce trusty ? he say : well , that mind 
 I see in thee , and it go to my heart that thou shouldest think it , 
 and that it should be no less than true . but now I must tell thee , 
 that it be for thy sake that I have be untrusty to my lord . how 
 so ? say she . Quoth he : Heardest thou ever of the Red Knight ? yea , 
 say Birdalone , I have hear of he ever as a tyrant and oppressor . 
 then she grow pale , and say : art thou he ? Nay , say the knight , I 
 be but a kinsman of his , and his well - trust man ; nor have I fail 
 he ever till yesterday . 

 he keep silence a while , and then say : this be the true tale : that 
 we have have tiding of thee and of thy riding abroad with that old 
 fool , Sir Aymeris , and how thou hadst be twice to look into the 
 Black Valley . this I say hath the Red One heard , and the heart of 
 he be touch by the mere hearsay of thee ; and moreover ' tis 
 bless bread to he the doing of any grief to the knight of Quest 
 Castle ; wherefore he hath send I to hang about the dale , to lay 
 hand on thee if I might for ; he know be wise , that thou wouldst 
 hanker after it ; and moreover he let one of his wise woman sit out in 
 spell on thee . so I espy , and happen on thee all alone ; and 
 mine errand it be , since I come upon thee thus , to draw thee till I 
 have thee safe at home in the Red Hold . Forsooth I begin mine errand 
 duly , and fall to beguile thee , so that thou mayst well have see 
 the traitor in I . but then , and then my heart fail I , because I 
 fall , not to desire thee as covet my master 's chattel , but to 
 love thee and longing for thee as my fellow and speech - friend . and 
 I say to myself : into the Red Hold she shall not go if I may hinder 
 it . 

 birdalone be very pale , but she refrain she from grief and fear , 
 and say : but those horse and weapone man up the dale , who be 
 they ? he say : I will not lie now , not even a little ; they come 
 into the dale by that upper pass whereof I tell thee ; they be of 
 our man ; I bring they . I be never all alone in the dale ; I be to 
 have fetch thee to they , so that thou might not see a rout of 
 folk and flee away ; and then would we all have go home together by 
 the upper pass . but we two must have go on unto they in the dale 's 
 head , whereas for all that I could say I might not bring they down 
 into that doom - ring where we eat and talk yesterday . we two have 
 be valianter than thou mayst have deem , to have do the deed of 
 dining there ; for all man fear it . but as for I , I have be there 
 more than twice or thrice , and thence have I wander , and find this 
 pass wherein now we be ; concern which I have hold my tongue , 
 deeming that it might one day serve my turn ; as it hath do now 
 abundantly , since it hath be a refuge unto thee . 

 Yea , but whither be we go now ? say birdalone ; be it perchance to 
 the Red Hold ? Nay , never , say the knight , so help I God and All- 
 hallow ! 

 Whither then ? say birdalone ; tell I , that I may at least trust 
 thee , even though I owe thee for all the pain and grief which thou 
 hast work I . he redden and say : wait a while ; I bring thee 
 to no ill place ; there shall no harm befall thee . and he fret and 
 fume , and be confuse of speech and look , and then he say : when 
 we come there I shall belike crave a boon of thee . 

 O , but I crave a boon of thee here and now , say Birdalone . wipe 
 away thine offence to I and take I back to my friend and the 
 Castle of the Quest ! so mayst thou yet be dear unto I , though maybe 
 not wholly as thou wouldst have it . and she reach out her two 
 hand toward he . 

 his breast heave , and he seem nigh to weeping ; but he say : Nay , 
 lady , ask I not here and now , but there and to - morrow . but again I 
 swear to thee by thine hand that to the Red Hold I will not bring 
 thee , nor suffer thee to be bring , if I may hinder it ; nay , not 
 though I give my life therefor . 

 birdalone be silent awhile ; then she say : and what shall befall I 
 if I come to the Red Hold ? what be the Red Knight , and what would he 
 do with I ? say he : the Red Knight be terrible and fierce and 
 wise ; and I fear he , I. he hold his peace , and say : I must need 
 say it , that to thee he would have be as death and the Devil . he 
 would have bed thee first . she break in : Nay , never ! and flush 
 very red . but the knight go on : and after , I wot not ; that be 
 accord to his mood . and as to thy never , lady , thou wott not 
 the like of he or of the folk he hath about he . such as thou ? she 
 say angrily . Nay , he say , far bad than I ; man who fare little 
 afield , and be not sweeten by adventure and war - peril ; and woman 
 worse yet ; and far worse be they deal with a woman . she be 
 silent again awhile , and pal once more ; then her colour come back 
 to she , and she hold out her hand to he and say kindly : Thou be 
 what thou art , I thank thee for thy dealing with I ; and now until 
 to - morrow , when I shall ask thee of that again , I be friend with 
 thee ; so come now , and let we eat and drink together . 

 he take her hand and kiss it , and then come and sit down meekly 
 beside she , and they eat and drink in that wild place as though they 
 have be friend of long acquaintance . 



 CHAPTER XV . the black KNIGHT bring birdalone to the bower in the 
 DALE 



 when they have make an end of their meal , they gat to horse again and 
 ride on their way ; and every mile now be their road the easy , the 
 pass wide , and its wall lower and now also more broken ; till at 
 last they begin to go down hill swiftly , and after a little their 
 road seem to be swallow in a great thicket of hornbeam and holly ; 
 but the knight ride on and enter the said thicket , and ever find 
 some way amidst the branch , though they be presently in the very 
 thick of the tree , and see no daylight between the trunk for well- 
 nigh an hour , whereas the wood be thick and tangle , and they have to 
 thread their way betwixt its maze . 

 at last the wood begin to grow thin before they , and the white 
 light to show between the trunk ; and Birdalone deem that she hear 
 the sound of fall water , and presently be sure thereof ; and the 
 knight spake to she : Patience , my lady ; now be we near home for to- 
 day . she nod kindly to he , and therewith they ride on to open 
 ground , and be on the side of a steep bent , break on their right 
 hand into a sheer cliff as Birdalone see when the knight lead she to 
 the edge and bade her look over . then she see down into a fair dale 
 lie far below they , through the which run a little river , clear and 
 swift , but not riotous , after it have fall over a force at the upper 
 end of the dale , and make the sound of water which she have hear . 
 the say dale be so , that whatsoever be on the other side thereof 
 be hide by tall and great tree , that stand close together some 
 twenty yard aloof from the stream , and betwixt they and it be fair 
 greensward with a few bush and thorn - tree thereon . 

 Quoth the knight : down there shall we rest till to - morrow , if it 
 please thee , lady ; and since the sun will set in an hour , we be 
 good on our way at once . it please I well , say Birdalone , and I 
 long to tread the turf by the river - side , for I be weary as weary may 
 be of the saddle and the pass . 

 so down the bent they ride , and it be but a little ere they have 
 ride it to an end , and have meet the river as it sweep round the 
 cliff - wall of the valley ; and they ride through it , and come on to 
 the pleasant greensward aforesaid under the tree ; and in a bight of 
 the wood be a bower build of turf and thatch with reed ; and 
 there , by the bidding of the knight , they alight ; and the knight 
 say : this be thine house for to - night , my lady ; and thou may lie 
 there in all safety after thou hast sup , and mayst have my weapon 
 by thy side if thou wilt , while I lie under the tree yonder . and if 
 thou wilt bathe thee in the cool water , to comfort thee after the 
 long ride and the weariness , I swear by thy hand that I will take 
 myself out of eye - shot and abide aloof till thou call I . 

 Said Birdalone , smile somewhat : Fair sir , I will not have my watch 
 and ward unarmed ; keep thou thy weapon ; and thou wilt not forget , 
 perchance , that I be not wholly unarmed , whereas I have my bow and 
 arrow and my knife here . and as to my bathing , I will take thee at 
 thy word , and bid thee go aloof a while now at once ; for I will go 
 down to the water ; and if thou spy upon I , then will it be thy shame 
 and not mine . 

 the knight go his way therewith , and Birdalone go down to the 
 water and unclad she ; but ere she step into the river , she lay 
 her bow and three shaft on the lip thereof . then she take the 
 water , and disport she merrily therein ; and now , forsooth , she be 
 nowise downcast , for she say to herself , this man be not all evil 
 and he lovest I well , and I look for it that to - morrow he will bring 
 I on my way toward the Castle of the Quest , for mere love of I ; and 
 then shall he be a dear friend to I , and I will comfort he what I 
 can for as long as we both live . 

 so she come out of the water and clothe she , and then call aloud for 
 the knight , and he come speedily unto she , as if he have be not 
 exceed far away , though he swear with a great oath that he have 
 nowise espy she . she answer he nought , and they go side by 
 side to the bower ; and there the knight dight the victual , and they 
 sit together and eat their meat like old friend ; and Birdalone ask 
 the knight concern this valley and the bower , if he have know it 
 for long , and he answer : yea , lady , I be but a stripling when I 
 first happen on the dale ; and I deem that few know thereof save I ; 
 at least none of our flock knoweth thereof , and I be fain thereof , 
 and keep they unknowing , for if my lord be to hear of my have a 
 haunt privy unto I he would like it but ill . 

 Birdalone turn pale when she hear he speak of his lord ; for fear 
 of the Red Knight have enter into her soul , so that now the flesh 
 creep upon her bone . but she enforce she to smile , and say : yea , 
 and what would he do to thee be he ill - content with thy way ? 
 Forsooth , lady , say he , if he could spare I he would make an end of 
 I in some miserable way ; nay , if he be exceed ill - content , he 
 would do as much for I whether he could spare I or not ; otherwise 
 he would watch his occasion , and so grieve I that what he do would 
 go to my very heart . Woe 's I ! say Birdalone , thou serv an evil 
 master . the knight answer not , and Birdalone go on speak 
 earnestly : it be a shame to thee to follow this fiend ; why dost thou 
 not sunder thee from he , and become wholly an honest man ? say he 
 gruffly : it be of no use talk of this , I may not ; to boot , I fear 
 he . then do Birdalone hold her peace , and the knight say : Thou 
 dost not know ; when I part from thee I must need go straight to he , 
 and then must that befall which will befall . speak we no more of 
 these matter . 

 birdalone flush with hope and joy as he spake thus , for she take 
 he to mean that he would lead she , on the morrow , on her way to the 
 Castle of the Quest . but the knight spake in a voice grow cheerful 
 again : as to this bower , lady , the tale thereof be soon tell ; for 
 with mine own hand I build it some fifteen year ago ; and I have 
 come to this place time and again when my heart be overmuch 
 oppress with black burden of evil and turmoil , and have while 
 prevail against the evil , and while not . Mayst thou prevail this 
 time , then ! say she . he answer she not , but presently fall to 
 talk with she of other matter , and the two be frank and 
 friendly together , till the August night grow dark about they ; and 
 then spake birdalone : now would I rest , for I can no long keep 
 mine eye open . abide aloof from I to - morrow morning till I call to 
 thee , as thou didst this evening ; and then , before we eat together 
 again , thou shalt tell I what thou wilt do with I . he stand up to 
 depart , and she reach out her hand to he in the glimmer , and he 
 see it , but say : Nay , if I take thine hand , I shall take thine 
 whole body . and therewith he depart , and she lay she down in her 
 smock alone , and sleep anon , and be dreamless and forget 
 everything till the sun be up in the morning . 



 CHAPTER XVI . yet a day and a night they tarry in the DALE 



 Birdalone awake when the sun come into the bower to she , and stand up 
 at once , and go down to the river and wash the night off she ; and 
 then , when she be clothe , call on the knight to come to she ; and he 
 come , look downcast and troubled ; so that Birdalone think within 
 herself : it be well , he will do my will . 

 she stand before he , and give he the sele of the day , and he look 
 on she sorrowfully . then she say : now be come the time when I be 
 to ask thee to take I back to the Castle of the Quest and my own 
 people . he be not hasty to answer she , and she spake again : this 
 must thou do , or else take I to the Red Hold and deliver I to the 
 tyrant there ; and I have hear it from thine own mouth that will be 
 nought else than cast I into shame and torment and death . and I 
 deem thou canst not do it . Nay , she say , stay the word that 
 be come from his mouth , I wot that thou canst do it if thine 
 heart can suffer it ; for thou art strong than I , and thou mayst 
 break my bow , and wrest this knife out of mine hand ; and thou canst 
 bind I and make I fast to the saddle , and so lead my helpless body 
 into thraldom and death . but thou hast say that thou lov I , and 
 I believe thee herein . therefore I know that thou canst not will to 
 do this . 

 he answer in his surly voice : thou art right , lady , I can not . 
 Nay , hearken thou this time . I have be turn over night - long 
 what thou didst say about leave my lord , that is , betray he , 
 for it come to that ; and now I have make up my mind to do it , and I 
 will betray he for thy sake . wherefore there be a third way to take 
 which thou hast not see ; we will ride out of this dale in an hour 's 
 time , and I will bring thee to they who be only less the mortal foe 
 of the Red Knight than be thy fellow of the Quest , to wit , to the 
 captain and burgess of the good town of Greenford by the Water ; and 
 I will do they to wit that I have rescue thee from the hand of the 
 Red Knight , and be become his foe ; and will show they all his 
 incoming and outgoing , and every whit of rede , and entrap he , so 
 that he fall into their hand . now , though be I to be take in 
 battle by they , I should be speedily bring to the halter , or may be 
 to the bale - fire   for we be wizard all in the Red Hold ; yet with 
 this word in my mouth , if they trow in it , I shall be make their 
 captain , and presently their master . Trow in my tale they will , if 
 thou bear I out therein , and they will honour thee , and suffer thee 
 to give thyself to I in marriage ; and then I know thee , and myself 
 also , and that ere long we shall be both mighty and wealthy and 
 beloved , and fair will be the day before we . 

 his voice have grow soft as he spake , and toward the end of his 
 word he falter , and at last brake out a - weeping , and cast himself 
 wordless on the grass before she . 

 she be pale , and her brow be knit , and her face quiver ; but 
 she spake coldly to he and say : this way I can not take ; and I 
 wonder at thee that thou hast show it unto I , for thyself thou 
 know that I can not go with thee . I will go nowhere hence save to 
 the Castle of the Quest . if thou wilt not lead I thereto , or put I 
 on the road , I ask thee straight , Wilt thou stay I if I go seek the 
 way thither myself ? 

 he rise up from the ground with a pale face full of anger as well as 
 grief , and catch she by the wrist and say , scowl the while : 
 tell I now which of they it be ; be it the stupid oaf Baudoin , or the 
 light fool Hugh , or the dull pedant Arthur ? but it matter not ; for 
 I know , and all the country - side know , that they be vow , each man 
 of they , to his own woman ; and if they find not the woman themselves , 
 such dolt they be , that they will ever be worship the mere 
 shadow of they , and turn away from flesh and blood , be it the 
 fairest in the world , as thou art , as thou art . 

 she shrink away from he what she might , but he still hold she 
 wrist ; then she spake in a quivering voice , her very lip pale with 
 fear and wrath : it be well see that thou art a man of the Red 
 Knight ; and belike thou wouldst do with I as he would . but one 
 thing I crave of thee , if there be any grain of mercy in thee , that 
 thou wilt draw thy sword and thrust I through ; thou mayst leave 
 thine hold of I to get at the blade , I will not stir from where I 
 stand . o ! to think that I deem thee well - nigh a true man . 

 he drop her hand now and stand aloof from she , star at she , 
 and presently cast himself on the ground , roll about and tear 
 at the grass . she look on he a moment or two , and then step 
 forward and stoop to he , and touch his shoulder and say : rise 
 up , I bid thee , and be a man and not a wild beast . 

 so in a while he arise , and stand before her hang - dog - like ; then she 
 look on he pitifully , and say : Fair sir and valiant knight , thou 
 hast go out of thy mind for a while , and thus hast thou sham both 
 I and thyself ; and now thou wert well forget it , and therewithal my 
 last word to thee . 

 Therewith she hold out her hand to he , and he go on his knee and 
 take it , sobbing , and kiss it . but she say , and smile on he : 
 now I see that thou wilt do what I pray of thee , and lead I hence 
 and put I on the road to the Castle of the Quest . he say : I will 
 lead thee to the Castle of the Quest . 

 say birdalone : then shall it be as I promise , that I will be thy 
 dear friend while both we live . and now , if thou canst , be a little 
 merry , and come and sit with I , and let we eat our meat , for I 
 hunger . 

 he smile , but woefully , and presently they sit down to their meat ; 
 and he strove to be somewhat merry of mood , and to eat as one at a 
 feast ; but while his heart fail he , and he set his tooth and tore 
 at the grass , and his face be fierce and terrible to look on ; but 
 birdalone make as if she heed it nought , and be blithe and 
 debonair with he . and when they have do their meat he sit look 
 at she a while , and at last he say : Lady , dost thou deem that , when 
 all be say , I have do somewhat for thee since first we meet the day 
 before yesterday at the low end of the Black Valley ? Yea , she 
 say , as erst I spake , all thing consider I deem that thou hast 
 do much . and now , say he , I be to do more yet ; for I be to lead 
 thee to where henceforth I shall have no more part or lot in thee 
 than if thou wert in heaven and I in hell . I pray thee say not so , 
 say Birdalone ; have I not say that I will be thy friend ? Lady , 
 say the knight , I wot well that accord to the sweetness of thine 
 heart wilt thou do what thou canst do . and therewith he be silent a 
 while and she also . 

 then he say : I would ask thee a grace if I durst . ask it , say 
 she , and I will grant it if I may ; I have gainsay thee enough 
 meseemeth . 

 Lady , he say , I will ask this as a reward of the way - leader , to wit , 
 that thou abide with I here in this dale , in all honour holden , till 
 to - morrow morning ; and let this place , which have help I aforetime , 
 be hallow by thy dwelling here ; and I , I shall have have one happy 
 day at least , if never another . Canst thou grant I this ? if thou 
 canst not , we will depart in an hour . 

 her countenance fall at his word , and she be silent a while ; for 
 sore she long to be speedily whereas her friend should find she if 
 they come back to the castle . but she think within herself how 
 wild and fierce the man be , and doubt if he might not go stark mad 
 on her hand and destroy she if she thwart overmuch ; and , moreover , 
 frankly she pity he , and would do what she might to ease his pain 
 and solace his grief of heart . wherefore she clear her face of its 
 trouble and let it be vex no long , but smile upon the knight and 
 say : Fair sir , this meseemeth but a little thing for I to do , and 
 I grant it thee with a good will , and this shall now be the first day 
 of the friendship if so thou wilt take it ; and may it solace thee . 

 who then be gleeful but the knight , and strange it be to see all 
 his sorrow run off he ; and he become glad and gamesome as a youth , 
 and yet withal exceed courteous and kind with she , as though he 
 be serve a mighty queen . 

 so then they wear the day together in all good fellowship ; and first 
 they go up the dale together and right to the foot of that great 
 force , where the stream come thunder down from the sheer rock ; 
 and long Birdalone stand to look thereon , and much she marvel at 
 it , for no such thing have she see before . 

 thereafter they go afoot into the wood behind the green bower , and 
 when they have go some way therein for their pleasure , they fall to 
 seek venison for their dinner ; and the knight take Birdalone 's bow 
 and shaft to strike the quarry withal , but he would have her gird 
 his sword to she , that she might not be weaponless . so they gat they 
 a roe and come back therewith to the bower , and the knight dight it 
 and cook it , and again they eat in fellowship and kindness ; and 
 Birdalone have be to the river and fetched thence store of blue- 
 flower mouse - ear , and of meadow - sweet , whereof be still some left 
 from the early day of summer , and have make her garland for her head 
 and her loin ; and the knight sit and worship she , yet he would 
 not so much as touch her hand , sorely as he hunger for the beauty 
 of her body . 

 next , when dinner be do , and they lie in the shadow of the tree , 
 and hearken the moor - hen cry from the water , and the moaning of 
 the wood - dove in the high tree , she turn to he and bid he tell 
 she somewhat of the tale of his life and deed ; but he say : Nay , 
 lady , I pray thee pardon I , for little have I to tell thee that be 
 good , and I would not have thee know of I aught bad than thou 
 know of I already . rather be thou kind to I , and tell I of thy 
 day that have be , wherein I know full surely shall be nought but 
 good . 

 she smile and blush , but without more ado fall to tell he of 
 her life in the House under the Wood , and spar not even to tell he 
 somewhat of the wood - mother . and he say no word to her thereover , 
 save thank and praise for the kindness of her story . 

 at last the day wear to its ending , and then the knight 's grief 
 strode over he again , and he be moody and few - spoken ; and birdalone 
 be blithe with he still , and would have solace his grief ; but he 
 say : let it be ; as for thee , thou shalt be happy to - morrow , but 
 this happy day of mine be well - nigh wear , and it be as the wearing of 
 my life . and the dark night come , and he bid her good - night 
 sorrowfully , and depart to his lair in the wood . birdalone lie in 
 the bower , and might not sleep a long while for her joy of the 
 morrow , which should bring she back to the Castle of the Quest . 

 but when morning be , and the sun be but just risen , Birdalone 
 awake , and stand up and do on her raiment , and call her servant 
 the knight , and he come at once lead the two horse , and say : 
 now go we to the Castle of the Quest . and he be sober and 
 sorrowful , but nought fierce or wild . 

 so Birdalone thank he kindly and praise he , and he change 
 countenance no whit therefor . 

 then they mount and set forth , and the knight lead straight into the 
 wood , and by road that he wotte of , so that they go nowise slowly 
 for wender through the thick woodland . thus go they on their way 
 together , he sorry and she glad . 

 but now leave the tale to tell of Birdalone and the knight on whom 
 she happen in the Black Valley of the Greywethers , and turn to the 
 Castle of the Quest and the folk thereof , and what they do in this 
 while and thereafter . 


 here end the Fourth Part of the Water of the Wondrous Isles , which 
 be call of the day of Abiding , and the Fifth Part now begin , 
 which be call the Tale of the Quest 's Ending . 




 the FIFTH PART : the tale of the QUEST 's ENDING 



 CHAPTER I. of SIR LEONARD 's TROUBLE and the coming of the quest 



 tell the tale that when the chaplain have depart from Birdalone at 
 the bower in the copse , he go home to the castle sadly enough , 
 because of his love and longing for she , which well he wotte might 
 never be satisfied . moreover when he be come into the castle again , 
 there fall fear upon he for what might betide she , and he rue it 
 that he have do she will in get she forth of the castle ; and in 
 vain now he set before himself all the reason for deeming that she 
 peril herein be little or nothing , even as he have lay they before 
 she , and which he then believe in utterly , whereas now himseeme 
 there be an answer to every one of they . so he sigh heavily and 
 go into the chapel , wherein be an altar of St. Leonard ; and he 
 kneel thereat , and pray the saint , as he have erst deliver folk 
 from captivity , now to deliver both he and Birdalone from peril and 
 bond ; but though he be long a - pray and make many word , it 
 lighten his heart little or nothing ; so that when he rise up again , 
 that if anything evil happen to this pearl of woman , he wish 
 heartily that some one might take his life and he be do with it . 

 now be the house astir , and the chaplain come from out the chapel , 
 and think all thing over , he think he would go straight to Sir 
 Aymeris and make a clean breast of it , so that weapone man might be 
 send at once to seek Birdalone . and he say to himself : what matter 
 if he slay I or cast I into prison , if birdalone be lose ? 

 so he go his way to the high tower , which look landward and 
 hight the Open Eye , deeming to find Sir Aymeris ; but when he get to 
 the topmost , he find neither captain nor carle there : wherefore he 
 stay a little and look forth betwixt the battlement , if 
 perchance there be some wild chance of see Birdalone 's come 
 home again ; but his keen eye beheld nothing more than he look to 
 see , as sheep and neat , and the field - folk of thereabout . so he 
 turn away and go by the swale toward the next tall tower , 
 which look lakeward , and be call Hearts ' hope ; and as he go 
 he fall to frame in his mind the word which he should say to the 
 castellan . 

 thus come he , haggard and hapless , on the lead of the tower , which 
 be nought small ; and there gather together in a knot , and all 
 gaze eagerly out over the lake , he find a dozen of man - at - arm and 
 the castellan amongst they . they take no heed of he as he come up , 
 though he stumble as he cross the threshold and come clatter 
 over the lead floor , and he see at once that there be something 
 unwonted toward ; but he have but one thought in his mind , to wit , the 
 rescuing of Birdalone . 

 he go up now behind where the castellan be lean over the 
 battlement , and pull his skirt , and when Sir Aymeris turn round , 
 he say : Lord , I have a word for thine ear . but the old knight do 
 but half turn round , and then spake peevishly : tush , man ! another 
 time ! se thou not I have get no eye for aught save what we see on 
 the lake ? yea , but what then ? say the priest . there cometh a boat , 
 say Sir Aymeris , not look back at he , and our thought be that 
 therein be our lord . 

 when the priest hear that word , it be to he as if hell have open 
 underneath his foot ; and he have no might to speak for a minute ; then 
 he cry out : Sir Aymeris , hearken , I pray thee . but the old knight 
 but thrust he back with his hand , and even therewith one of the men- 
 at - arm cry out : I hear the voice of their horn ! then shout Sir 
 Aymeris : where art thou , Noise ? blow , man , blow , if ever thou 
 blewest in all thy life ! and therewithal come the blare of the 
 brass , and Sir Aymeris nod to the trumpeter , who blow blast after 
 blast with all his might , so that the priest might as well have be 
 dumb for any hearing he might get ; and all the while to Leonard the 
 minute seem hour , and he be well - nigh distraught . 

 and then when the knight hold up his hand for the Noise to stay his 
 blowing , and Leonard strove to speak , the castellan turn on he and 
 say : Peace , Sir Leonard ; dost thou not know that now we would 
 listen with our ear to heed if they answer we ? not a word any one 
 man of you , learn or lewd , or ye shall rue it ! 

 even therewith come clearly the sound of the horn from the water , and 
 again and yet again ; and no man spake but the chaplain , who cry 
 out : Hearken , knight , it be of Birdalone . but Sir Aymeris lay his 
 hand on his shoulder and say in an angry whisper : Thou shalt be put 
 downstair , priest , if thou hold not thy peace . 

 Leonard draw aback scowling , and go out of the door , and so slowly 
 down the stair , and withdraw he into the cover of the door of the 
 first chamber down from the tower - top , with the mind to waylay Sir 
 Aymeris as he come down ; and meanwhile he curse he for a fool and a 
 dull - wit , and himself yet more , as be but right , for a fool and a 
 licorous traitor . 

 but he have not tarry there more than a score of minute , ere he 
 hear a great shout from those up above : they be come ! they be 
 come ! and next thereafter come all the man clatter down the stair 
 past he , scarce refrain they from shove each his neighbour on 
 to the next one ; Leonard follow on they , and presently arise great 
 shouting and tumult through all the house , and all folk , man and 
 woman , hurried flock - meal toward the water - gate , and with they go 
 Leonard perforce ; and sick of heart he be , call to mind the first 
 come thither of Birdalone . 

 but now when they come to the water - gate , there verily be the 
 send Boat just come to hand ; and in the stern stand the three 
 knight together , all clothe in their armour , and before they sit three 
 lovely lady , clothe one in gold , one in green , and one in black : and 
 lo , there be the Quest come home . 



 CHAPTER II . now ask THEY of BIRDALONE , and SIR LEONARD SPEAKS 



 now the prow touch the stone of the stair , and folk be busy to 
 lay hold of it that the wayfarer might land , but Sir Baudoin cry 
 out in a great voice : let none be so hardy as to touch this ferry , 
 either now or hereafter ; for there be peril therein . and therewith 
 he take Aurea by the hand , and lead she out of the boat and up the 
 stair , and she all joyous and wonder ; and thereafter come Hugh and 
 his darling , and last of all Arthur and Atra , and she alone of the 
 three woman look downcast , and her eye wander about the throng 
 that be before they there , as though she seek something , yet 
 fear to see it . 

 but when they be all stand together on the landing - plain , and 
 the folk be all about they in a ring , Sir Baudoin spake to the 
 castellan and say : Sir Aymeris , thee and other folk I see here , the 
 sight of whom doth I great joy ; but where , I pray thee , be the lady , 
 our friend Birdalone , by whom it be that all we be come happily 
 hither ? and he look around with an anxious face ; but Arthur be as 
 pale as ash , yet he spake nought , and Atra let her hand fall away 
 from his . 

 then spake the castellan , and say : no harm hath befall the Lady 
 Birdalone ; but while she hath be somewhat ailing of late , and it 
 be like that she wotteth not what be toward , and keepeth her chamber 
 now , for it be yet betime in the morning . 

 as he spake , come thrust a man through the throng , eager and pale- 
 face ; who but the chaplain ; and he say : he would not let I speak , 
 this fool ; I can not choose my time . lord , I bear evil tiding and 
 an ugly welcome home . the Lady Birdalone be in peril , and she be not 
 in the castle ; I wot not where she be . Ye must send armed man to 
 seek she out . 

 Thereat fall the silence of woe upon the throng ; but Arthur run 
 forward on the priest with draw sword , and cry out : I misdoubt I 
 that thou art a traitor ; speak ! or I will slay thee here and now . if 
 I be a traitor , quoth Leonard , I shall tell thee in little while what 
 ye must do to undo my treason , if there be yet time thereto ; so slay 
 I not till ye have hear , and then do what ye will with I . 

 but Baudoin put Arthur aside , and say : refrain thee a little , fair 
 brother , else shall word tumble over each other and we shall know 
 nothing clear . Sir Aymeris , bring our dear lady to the fair 
 chamber , and do all honour and courtesy to they . and ye , 
 sweetling , ye will not begrudge we that we go to seek your friend . 
 Thou priest , come with we a little apart , and tell thy tale as 
 shortly as thou mayst , and fear nought ; we be not God 's dastard , as 
 the Red Knight and his man . 

 Viridis weep and kiss her love before all folk , and bid he go and 
 do his good to find her friend , or never come back to she else . much 
 move , even to tear , be Aurea withal , and reach her hand to 
 Baudoin , and say : if any man on earth can help we it be thou . go 
 thou . but Atra weep not , and but say to Arthur : go thou , it be 
 meet . 

 Therewith be the lady bring to fair chamber ; but the three 
 knight go with the priest and Sir Aymeris into the solar , and set 
 a guard at the door that their talk should be privy . 



 CHAPTER III . how they follow the slot of BIRDALONE and the black 
 KNIGHT 



 it be but five minute ere the priest have tell they all that need 
 be ; so they let he abide alone there , though sooth to say there be 
 none of they but have good will to break his neck ; and the same rede 
 have all three , that there be nought for it but to go their way with 
 all speed to the Black Valley of the Greywethers , and follow up the 
 slot of birdalone if it might yet be find ; wherefore they bid 
 saddle their horse straightway ; and while that be a - do they eat 
 a morsel , and bade farewell to their loveling . and they dight they 
 to go , they three together , with but one squire and a sergeant , who 
 be both of they keen tracker and fell woodsman . but ere they 
 go , by the rede of Arthur they bid Sir Aymeris to arm a two score 
 of man and ride toward the Red Hold , and beset the way ' twixt that 
 and the Castle of the Quest ; for one and all they deem that if any 
 harm befell Birdalone , the Red Knight would be at the bottom of it . 

 so ride those fellow , and come unto the dale but some four hour 
 after Birdalone have happen on the stranger knight ; and they take up 
 the slot of she , but not easily , whereas the ground be hard and 
 stony ; howbeit , they find token of the knight also , find here 
 and there what they deem the footprint of a tall man . and this 
 be grievous to those fellow , since now they could not but deem that 
 somewhat untoward have befall Birdalone . but they go on make 
 out the slot , and they follow it with much toil until they come to 
 the doom - ring in the head of the dale , whereas Birdalone and the 
 stranger have sit down to meat ; but by that time , so toilsome have be 
 their go , it be somewhat more than dusk , and there be nought for 
 it but to abide there night - long . so a while they sit talk , all 
 of they , and the squire and the sergeant aforesaid be not a little 
 timorous of the adventure of make that stead unkenned their 
 sleeping chamber ; and to while away the time , their lord make they 
 tell tale such as they know concern that place ; and both they 
 say that they have never erst come into the dale but a very little 
 way , and say that they have do so then but trust in their lord ' 
 bidding and the luck of the Quest . thereafter turn the talk as to 
 what have befall Birdalone , and the chance of come on she ; and , 
 as folk will in such a plight , they talk the matter over and over 
 again till they be weary and could say no more . 

 then they go to sleep , and nought befell they till they awake in 
 the broad daylight ; but they have little inkling of what hour it be , 
 for all the dale be full of thick white mist that come roll down 
 from the mountain , so that they could scarce see their hand before 
 they , and there they have to tarry still , would they , would they not ; 
 and the sergeant fall to tell tale of folk who have be lose in 
 that stony maze ; and all of they deem , more or less , that this be 
 the work either of evil wight , or it might be of the wizardry of the 
 Red Knight ; and , to be short , they all deem that he it be who have 
 wield it , save the sergeant , who say that the mountain wight be 
 the master and not the servant of he of the Red Hold . 

 thus , then , it betide ; but when the say mist have be hang upon 
 they for some six hour , it roll up like a curtain , and lo the blue 
 sky and the sun , and the mountain as clear blue as in a picture ; and 
 they see by the sun that it be but a little after high noon . 

 but as they rejoice herein , and betook they once more to track 
 out the slot of Birdalone and the other , the sky become suddenly 
 overcast , and down from the jaw of the mountain come a storm of wind 
 and rain , and thunder and lightning , so great that they might scarce 
 see each other 's face , and when it clear off , in about an hour and 
 a half , and go down the wind to the south - east , the stream be 
 waxen great , and run brown and furious down the dale , so that it be 
 fordable only here and there ; and as for track the slot of those 
 twain , there be no need to talk thereof , for the fury of the driving 
 rain have wash all away . 

 thus fare they the whole day betwixt fog and clear weather , and they 
 lay they down to rest at night sore dishearten . when the day 
 break they talk together as to what be good to do ; and the 
 sergeant aforesaid spake : Lords , say he , meseemeth I be more at 
 home in the Black Valley than ye be ; heed ye not wherefore . now so 
 it be that if we tarry here till night come we wot not what of evil 
 may betide we , or at the least we do nought . or if we turn back and 
 go southward out of the dale we shall be safe indeed ; but safe should 
 we have be at your house , lord , and should have do no less . but 
 now I shall tell you that , if ye will , lord , I shall guide you to a 
 pass that goeth out of the head of the dale to our right hand , and 
 so turneth the flank of the mountain , and cometh out into the 
 country which lieth about the Red Hold ; and meseemeth it be 
 thitherward that we must seek if we would hear any tiding of the 
 lady ; for there may we lay in ambush and beset the way that lead up 
 to the Hold , by which she must have be bring if she hath not be 
 carry through the air . how say ye , lord ? soothly there be peril 
 therein ; yet meseemeth peril no more than in our abide another 
 night in the Black Valley . 

 Said Arthur : we hee not the peril if there be aught to be do ; 
 wherefore let we be stir straightway . and so say they all . 
 wherefore they gat to horse , and ride up to the very head of the 
 valley , and the weather be now calm and bright . 

 but the sergeant bring they to the pass whereof the stranger knight 
 have speak to Birdalone , which lead into the Red Knight 's country , and 
 without more ado they enter it when it be now about three hour 
 after noon . but the way be both steep and rough , so that they have 
 much toil , and go not very far ere night fall upon they , and the 
 moon be not yet up . so when they have stumble on another two hour , 
 and their horse be much spend and they themselves not a little 
 weary , they lay they down to sleep , after they have eat such meat 
 as they have with they , in a place where be a little grass for the 
 horse to bite ; for all the road hitherto have be mere grim stone 
 and big rock , wall on either side by stony scree , above which 
 rise steep and beetle crag . 

 in the dawn they arise again , and make no ado till they be in the 
 saddle , and ride till they come to the crest of the pass , and come 
 out thence after a while on to the swell flank of a huge mountain 
  as it might be the side of the mountain of Plinlimmon in Wales , 
 which be grass and nought craggy , but utterly treeless . 

 now the sergeant lead they somewhat athwart the say mountain till 
 they begin to go down , and see below they a country of little hill 
 much cover with wood , and in a while , and ere it be noon , they 
 be among the say wood , which be grow mostly with big tree , as 
 oak here and beech there , and the going be good for they . 



 CHAPTER IV . of the slaying of FRIEND and FOE 



 so come they , three hour after noon , to where be a clearing in the 
 woodland , and a long narrow plain some furlong over lie before they , 
 with a river run along it , and the wood rise on the other side 
 high and thick , so that the say plain look even as a wide green 
 highway lead from somewhence to somewhither . 

 at the edge hereof their way - leader , the sergeant , bade draw rein , 
 and say : lord , we be now in the land of the Red Hold , and 
 therein be mickle peril and dread to any save stout heart as ye be ; 
 but meseem we be so steade , that whatever may come out of the 
 Black Valley of the Greywethers to the Red Hold , ye now may scarce 
 miss . yonder along this plain to the north lie the way to the say 
 hold , and any man come from the head of the valley be sure to come 
 by the way we have come , and will pass we not many yard at the bad 
 from where we now be . on the other hand , if any come to the hold 
 from the mouth of the Black Valley , then along this green road must 
 they need pass under your very eye . lastly , if we do what we be 
 come to do , to wit , to deliver the lady from the Red Knight , then , 
 the deed do , we have to take the green road southward , and ride it 
 for a league and then turn east , and we shall have our head turn 
 toward the Castle of the Quest , and shall speedily fall in with Sir 
 Aymeris and our man who be guard the out - gate of the Red Knight 's 
 country toward our house . so now , by my rede , ye shall lay in covert 
 here and abide a while what may befall ; if nought come hereby ere two 
 hour be lack of sunset , then may we seek far . 

 they all yeasaid this , and gat off their horse , and lie quiet on the 
 grass , not even speak save softly . and when they have abide thus 
 scarce an hour 's space , the squire , who be a man of very fine ear , 
 hold up his hand as though to bid utter silence , and all hearken 
 eagerly . presently he say : hear ye not ? Said Arthur : Meseemeth I 
 hear a faint tinkle as of a sheep - bell . say the squire : ' tis the 
 clashing of sword down the plain to the south , and meseemeth ' tis 
 but of two : ride we thither ? 

 Quoth Baudoin : Nay , not by my rede ; for if we can hear they they can 
 hear we ; let we quietly edge along afoot somewhat nigh their way , 
 ever keep the cover of the wood betwixt we and the open plain . 
 now then to it ; and let each man keep his weapon ready . 

 even so do they , and spread out in a line as they go , in such wise 
 that there be some six pace betwixt each man of they , and they go 
 softly forward ; Baudoin go first , Hugh second , then Arthur ; then 
 the squire and the sergeant last of all . 

 now when they have go but a quarter of an hour , the squire catch up 
 with Arthur , and spake to he softly , and say : the voice of the 
 sword have be silent now a while , and I hear a voice cry out 
 e'en now , a woman 's voice . and now again I could well - nigh deem that 
 I hear horse - hoof . 

 Arthur nod to he , and they go but a little furth ere he say : 
 Lo , lo ! ' tis the time of the eye now ! here come folk . and 
 therewithal they stay they . for the wood turn somewhat here , so 
 as to hide all but a little of the plain , and round the wood neb the 
 new - comer hove in sight , and be close on they at once , so that 
 they might see they clearly , to wit , a knight weapone , clothe all in 
 red , a very big man , rid on a great bay horse , and behind he a 
 woman go afoot in very piteous plight ; for she be tethered to the 
 horse 's crupper by a thong that bind her wrist together , so that 
 she have but just room leave ' twixt she and the horse that she might 
 walk , and round about her neck be hang a man 's head newly hew off . 

 this sight they all see at once , and be out of the wood in a trice 
 with weapon aloft , for they know both the man and the woman , that 
 they be the Red Knight and Birdalone . 

 so swift and sudden have they be , that he have no time either to spur 
 or even to draw his sword ; but he have a heavy steel axe in his hand 
 as the first man come up to he , which be the tall Baudoin ; and 
 therewith he smite down on Baudoin so fierce and huge a stroke , that 
 come on he betwixt neck and shoulder , that all give way before it , 
 and the Golden Knight fall to earth all carven and stark dead : but 
 even therewith fall Hugh , the squire , and the sergeant on the Red 
 Knight ; for Arthur have run to Birdalone and shear she loose from 
 her tether . the sergeant smite he on the right arm with a maul , so 
 that the axe fall to the ground ; the squire 's sword come on the side 
 of his head , and , as it be cast back beneath the stroke , Hugh thrust 
 his sword through the throat of he , and down he fall unto the earth 
 and be dead in less than a minute . 

 then gather the other round about Baudoin , and see at once that he 
 be dead ; and Birdalone come thrust through the press of they , and 
 kneel down beside he , and when she see her friend so piteously 
 dight , she weep and wail over he as one who might not be 
 comfort ; and Hugh stand over she and let his tear fall down upon 
 the dead man ; and withal the squire and the sergeant do not refrain 
 their lamentation , for sore beloved be Sir Baudoin the Golden 
 Knight . 

 but Arthur spake dry - eye , though there be grief in his countenance , 
 and he say : fellow , and thou , lady , let we lament afterwards , but 
 now be time for we to get we go hence as speedily as may be . yet I 
 will ask , doth any know whose be this head that the slain tyrant here 
 have hang about the lady 's neck ? may the fiend curse he therefor ! 

 say the sergeant : yea , lord , that wot I ; this be the head of the 
 Red Knight 's captain and head man , Sir Thomas of Estcliffe ; one of 
 the hardy of knight he be while he be alive , as ye surely wot , 
 lord ; neither , as I have hear say , be he as cruel a tyrant as his 
 lord that lieth there ready for the raven . 

 now have Birdalone arise and be stand face Arthur ; her face be 
 pale and full of anguish , and she be dabble with blood from the 
 dead man 's neck ; but there be nought of shame in her face as she 
 stand there and speak : o my living friend , who have but now save 
 I , ye and my dead friend , from what shame and death I know not , the 
 tale of this woeful hap be over long to tell if there be peril at 
 hand , and I scarce alive from dread and sorrow ; but shortly thus it 
 be : this man , whose head here lieth , entrap I as I foolishly 
 wander in the Black Valley , and afterwards deliver I , and be 
 lead I to your castle , my friend , when this other one , his 
 master , the tyrant of the Red Hold , come upon he , and fall upon he 
 and slay he as a traitor , and dighte I as ye see . and woe 's I ! 
 I be the fool whose folly have slay your friend and mine . wherefore 
 I be not worthy of your fellowship , and ye shall cast I forth of it ; 
 or to slay I be well . 

 so she spake , gaze earnestly on Arthur ; and so troubled and 
 grieve , that she might well have die but for her woodland breeding , 
 and the toil of the day she have win through in the House under the 
 Wood . 

 but Hugh spake gently to she and say : keep up thine heart yet , 
 maiden ; for the hand of Fate it be that lead thee , and none doeth 
 grievously amiss but if he mean wrong - doing in his heart ; and we know 
 thee for true ; and thou hast be our helper , and bring our 
 loveling unto we to make we happy . 

 but she brake out weeping afresh , and say : o no , no ! it be but woe 
 and weariness I have bring unto my friend ; and to myself woe and 
 weariness yet more . 

 and she look piteously into Arthur 's face , and hard and stern it 
 seem unto she ; and she writhe and wrung her hand for anguish . 
 but he spake and say : this will we look into when we be safe behind 
 our wall , and see what she hath do amiss and what not amiss . but 
 now be there but one thing to do , and that be to get we speedily on 
 our way to the Castle of the Quest , and bind our fellow 's body on his 
 horse that he also may ride with we , and the lady shall ride the 
 horse of the accursed thief . then they turn to go toward their 
 horse ; but therewith Birdalone smite her foot against the slain 
 knight 's head , and shrank aback from it , and point down toward it 
 and spake no word ; and Hugh say : friend , the lady be right , this 
 at least we will cover with earth . do ye go fetch hither our horse , 
 since we be on the road , and I will do here what need be meanwhile . 

 so they go on that errand , and then Hugh and Birdalone between they 
 dig a hole with the sword and lay the head of the captain of the 
 Red Knight therein . and forsooth , somewhat would Birdalone have weep 
 for he have she have a tear to spare . 

 then they fall to and bind the dead Baudoin on the Red Knight 's 
 mighty bay steed , so that no time might be waste ; and when that be 
 do , and the other have not come back with their horse , Hugh take 
 Birdalone 's hand and lead she down to the stream and wash the gore 
 off her bosom , and she wash her face and her hand and let he lead 
 she back again in such wise that now she could hearken to the word 
 of comfort he spake to she , and piteous kind he seem unto she ; so 
 that at last she pluck up heart , and ask he how Viridis do . 
 Quoth he : they be all safe at home in the castle , and Viridis be 
 well and loveth thee well . and Aurea be well , woe worth the while 
 for she now ! as for Atra , she have not be so glad as the other 
 twain , I wot not wherefore . 

 even as he spake be the other come up with the horse , and Arthur 
 nod yeasay when he see what have be do with Baudoin dead ; and 
 so they gat to horse , and birdalone it be that rode Baudoin 's steed . 
 then they go their way , cross the river into the wood ; and the 
 sergeant be ever way - leader , but the squire lead the horse which bore 
 the sorrowful burden of the dead Knight of the Quest . 



 CHAPTER V. THEY come home to the CASTLE of the quest 



 now they have go but some three hour , rid dreary and nigh 
 speechless all of they , ere they begin to know the land they be in , 
 and that they be come to the place where they might look 
 presently to fall in with Sir Aymeris and his company ; and even so 
 the meeting betid , that they see man stand and go about their 
 horse beside a little wood , and know they presently for their folk , 
 who mount at once and spur forward to meet they , spear aloft .

 lady be now accomplish , we should turn what be leave of the 
 fellowship into a war against the Red Hold and its evil thing ; and

 force and go thither in arm to live or die in the quarrel , and so 
 sweeten the earth , as do the man of ancient day when they slay the 
 dragon and the giant , and the child of hell , and the son of 
 Cain . 

 his cheek flush as he speak , and he look around till his eye

 but Aurea spake and say : I thank thee for thy word , Black Squire ,

 CHAPTER VIII . ATRA and birdalone talk together while the lords SIT 
 AT the MURDER - COUNCIL 



 meanwhile of their absence , Viridis sit sad and silent and downcast ,

 bitter winter , burn summer , never more shall waste and wear ;

 o for the hope of it , that I might hope it ! o for the day to be and

 CHAPTER IX . HUGH tell the STORY of the QUEST 's ending . 



 when this be say , and there have be silence a while , Birdalone

 now on this last day betid somewhat of new tiding ; for scarce be

 when I fare to bed I feel confused in my head and sick of heart . 

 the night of the next day   the sixth , as we go to our chamber , 
 and the witch - wife and Arthur hand - in - hand , she stay he a while , 
 and spake eagerly to he in a soft voice ; and as he come up to I 
 afterwards he say : to - night I have escape it , but there will not 
 be escape for long . from what ? say I. he say : from bed she ; 
 for now it have come to this , that presently we must slay she at once 
 and have no knowledge of our sweetling , or I must do she will . 

 in such wise pass four more day , and it be the twelfth morning of 
 our sojourn there , and we go forth on our search of every mead and 
 every covert of the isle , and all day we find nought to our purpose ; 
 but as it grow toward sunset , and there grow great cloud in the 
 eastern ort , pile up and copper - colour , we come over a bent on to 
 a little green dale water by a clear brook , and as we look down 
 into it we see something shine amongst its tree ; so we hasten 
 toward that gleam , and lo , amidst the dale , with the brook run 
 through it , a strange garth we see . for there be a pavilion do of 
 timber and board , and gaily paint and gild , and out from that 
 house be , as it be , a great cage of thin gild bar , both wall 
 and roof , just so wide apart as no one full - grow , carl or quean , 
 could thrust through . 

 Thitherward then run we , shout , for we see at once that in the 
 say cage be three woman whose aspect be that of our sweetling , 
 and presently we be stand by the say herse , reach our hand 
 out to they to come to we and tell we their tale , and that we would 
 deliver they . but they stand together in the midst of the say cage , 
 and though they gaze piteously on we thence , and reach out their 
 hand to we , they neither spake nor come to the herse to we ; so we 
 deem that they be bewitch , and our joy be dash . 

 then we go all about the cage and the pavilion to find ingate , and 
 find it not ; and then the three of we together strove with the bar 
 of the herse , and shake and sway they , but it be all to no 
 purpose . 

 moreover , while we be at this work the sun seem to go out , and 
 there come a heavy black mist roll into the dale , and wrap we 
 about so that we see not each other 's face , and the bar of the 
 herse be go from our hand as we stand there . then come rain and 
 thunder and lightning on to the black night , and by the glare of the 
 lightning we could see the leaf and grass of the dale , but neither 
 herse nor house nor woman . so we abode there in the dark night , and 
 the storm all bewilder we , till the rain and cloud draw off and it 
 be calm fair starlight again , but clean go be the golden cage and 
 they that stand therein ; and we turn sadly , and go our way 
 toward the witch - house . 

 on the way say Arthur : Brethren , this meseemeth be but a - go on 
 with the show which be play we on the water as we come hither ; 
 but whether she doth this but for to mock and torment we , or that she 
 would beguile we into deeming that our friend be verily here , I wot 
 not ; but to - morrow , meseemeth , I shall can to tell you . 

 now come we to the perron of the house , and there stand the witch- 
 wife under the star to meet we . and when she see we , she take hold 
 of Arthur by the hand and the arm to caress he , and find that he 
 and we be drench with the rain and the storm , as might well be 
 deem ; then she bid we up to our chamber to do on raiment which 
 she have dight for we , and we go thither , and find our garment 
 rich and dainty indeed ; but when we come down into the hall where the 
 witch abode we , we see that Arthur 's raiment be far the rich and 
 daintiest . but the witch run to he and cast her arm about he , and 
 clip and kiss he before the other , and he suffer it . so 
 speed the feast again . 

 but when they go to bed , the say witch take Arthur 's hand and 
 spake a word unto he , and lead he away , and he go with she as one 
 nought loth ; but we twain be afraid lest she should destroy he
