 when she have have her will of he . wherefore we wake through the 
 more part of the night with our sword ready to hand . 

 but when we be clothe in the morn he come unto we , he also clothe , and 
 be downcast and shameface indeed , but safe and sound ; and he say : 
 speak no word about our matter till we be out in the open air , for I 
 fear all thing about we . 

 so when we have go forth and be under the apple - tree once more , 
 spake Arthur : now , lord , be I sham for ever , for I have become 
 the leman of this evil creature ; but I pray ye not to mock I ; and 
 that the more as the same lot may happen on you both , or either ; for 
 I can see for sure that the wretch will weary of I and desire one of 
 you two . let it pass . somewhat have I find out from she , but not 
 much ; first , that she have forget her first lie , to wit , how she 
 send our lady to the sister - witch ; for I tell she of the golden 
 cage , and how we have miss it in the storm ; and she say : though I 
 deem it a folly that ye should seek these thrall , yet would I help 
 you if I might , since ye be now become my dear friend . though , 
 forsooth , when ye meet they I deem that ye will find they sore 
 change to you . for , as I tell you , they flee away from I , after I 
 have chastise they for a treason , into the hidden place of the isle , 
 whereas they have no keel to sail away hence . and I care not to 
 follow they , as I myself be queen and lady of all thing here , and be 
 enough for myself , save when love constraineth I , dear lord . now , 
 my rede be that ye seek the golden cage again and yet again , because 
 I deem that these thrall have somehow learn some wisdom , and they 
 have enchant the say cage for a defence against I , from whom they 
 might not hide as they do from you ; for of I have they steal their 
 wizardry , and I be their mistress therein . 

 this , therefore , be the new lie of she , and my rede be that we hee 
 it nought . for my mind be that she it be that hath make the 
 appearance of the cage and the woman therein , and that she hath our 
 poor friend somewhere underneath her hand . 

 now this we deem most like ; yet whereas we have nought to do with 
 the time , which , now that we have search the isle throughly , hung 
 heavy on hand , we deem it good to go to the dale of the golden cage 
 again , though we look not to find the cage there any more . but 
 this betid , that we find the little dale easily enough , and there 
 stand the cage as we have see it yesterday , but nought be there 
 within its bright bar save the grass and the flower , and the water 
 of the brook a - running . 

 we loiter about that place a while , and go back to the house in 
 due time ; and to shorten the tale , I shall tell that for many day it 
 betid that we go every day to seek the golden cage , but after the 
 first three day we see it no more . 

 now begin sadness and weariness to overcome we as the day and week 
 wear , and belike the witch - wife note it that we be bad company 
 than heretofore . 

 and now on a day Arthur bade we note that the say witch be grow 
 weary of he , and he bid I look to it ; for , say he , she be turn 
 her face toward thee , brother . my heart burn with rage at that 
 word ; I say nought , but make up my mind that I would try to bring 
 the matter to an end . 

 that same night befell what Arthur have threaten ; for the feast 
 be do in the evening , the witch draw I aside while the music 
 be a - playing , and caress my hand and my shoulder , and say : I be 
 yet wonder at you Champions , that ye must need follow after those 
 three wretched thrall , whom never will ye find , for they . need ye 
 not , but will flee from you if ye have sight of they , as they do 
 that other day ; and therein they be scarce in the wrong , whereas 
 they may well think that if ye find they they should fall into my 
 hand ; for easily may I take they any day that I will , and then I 
 have a case against they , and may lawfully chastise they accord to 
 the law that have be give unto I ; and then shall they be in 
 grievous plight . wherefore the rede we give unto you three now be 
 the rede of friendliness that ye make yourself happy in our Island , 
 and then will we do everything we may for your pleasure and delight ; 
 and if ye will that we make ourselves even fair than now we be , 
 that may be do , and shall be a reward unto you for your yielding 
 and obedience . and if ye will woman thrall for your pleasure , that 
 also may be get for you ; for we be not wholly without power in 
 these water , though we have no keel or ferry upon they . and now , 
 thou fair lad , we give thee this last word : Ye Champions have be 
 dwell in our house a long while , and that while have ever strive 
 to thwart we . we now counsel you to make an end of it , and it shall 
 be well for you . 

 she seem to my eye prouder and stupider than ever erst , despite 
 her golden hair and white skin and lovely limb ; and I say to myself 
 that now must we destroy the evil of that house even if we die for 
 it , or else we be all undo ; withal I see somewhat of truth 
 thrust up through her much lying , and I deem , even as Arthur 
 do , that she have our friend under her band somewhere . 

 Nought else betid that night ; but on the morrow we go forth and 
 stray on till we be come into the southernmost quarter of the 
 isle , and not very far from the water we come upon a wood or big 
 thicket which be new to we . so we enter it , and as we go and 
 note the wild thing of the wood go hither and thither , we espy 
 afar off the shape of a man go amidst the thicket ; wherefore we 
 go warily towards he , lest he should see we and flee from we ; and 
 when we draw a little nigh we see it be a woman , though she be 
 clothe as a hunter , with leg naked to above the knee . she have a 
 quiver at she back and a bow in her hand , and her coat be black of 
 hue . belike now she hear our go amongst the dry leaf , for she 
 turn her face to we , and lo ! it be the face of Atra . 

 when she see we , she give a shrill cry , and fall to run at she 
 swiftest away from we , and we follow all we might , but we could not 
 over - run she , though we keep she in sight ever , till we have run all 
 through the wood , and before we be the sheer side of a rocky hill 
 and the mouth of a cave therein , and by the say mouth who should 
 there be but Aurea and Viridis , as we think , clothe in gold and in 
 green , but the fashion of their raiment not otherwise than Atra 's . 
 their bow be bended and they have shaft in their hand , and as we 
 come out of the thicket into the open lawn before the cave , Viridis 
 nock a shaft and aim at we and draw , and the shaft fly over my 
 head ; therewith mocking laughter come from they , and they run into 
 the cave . speedily we run up to it , but when we come home thither , 
 there be the sheer hillside , but never a cave nor an opening . 

 Dismayed be we thereat ; but more dismayed have we be but that we 
 deem that all this be but a cheat and a paint show put upon we 
 by the witch to back up her lying . nevertheless we fare the next 
 day to seek the wood and the cave in the sheer rock , but nowise might 
 we find either wood or cave . 

 now it be the night of the day hereafter , as we go to our 
 chamber , that the witch - wife take I by the hand and lead I apart , 
 and say I many soft thing of her accursed lust , whereof I will not 
 say one again . but the upshot of it all be that she would bring I 
 to her chamber and her bed . and whereas I be determine what to do , 
 and have my war - sword by my side , I naysaid she not , but make her good 
 countenance . and when we come to her chamber , which be full 
 gloriously dight , and fragrant as with the scent of the rose and 
 lily of mid - June , she bid I to lie in her bed of gold and ivory 
 and she would be with I anon . so I unclad myself and lay I down , 
 but I draw forth my sword , and lay the ancient naked blade betwixt 
 my side and her place . 

 Anon she cometh back again unclad , and would step into the bed ; but 
 she see the sword and say : what be this , Champion ? say I : these 
 edge be the token of sunder between we , for there be a spell on 
 I , that with no woman may I deal , save with mine only love , but I 
 shall do her mortal scathe ; so beware by the token of the grey edge 
 of battle . she draw aback , and be as a spiteful and angry cat , and 
 there be no loveliness in she ; and she say : thou liest , and thou 
 hatest I ; see thou to it , both for thyself and thy loveling . and 
 she turn about and stride out of the chamber ; but I arise and clothe 
 myself in haste , and take my naked sword in my hand . but before I 
 go , I look around , and espy an ambry fashion in the wall of 
 the bed - lane , and the door be half open ; and the say ambry be 
 wrought of the daintiest , all of gold and pearl and gem ; and I say 
 to myself : Herein be some treasure , and this be a tide of war . so I 
 open the ambry , and within it be even more gloriously work than 
 without ; and there be nought therein , save a little flask of crystal 
 do about with band of gold set with great and goodly gem . so I 
 take the say flask and go my way hastily to my own chamber , and 
 there I look at the say flask and take out the stopple ; and there 
 be a liquor therein , white like to water , but of a spicy smell , 
 sweet , fresh , and enheartene . so I yet think this be some great 
 treasure , and that much hang upon it , could I find out unto what use 
 it might be put . and I say : to - morrow we will put it to the proof . 
 then I put the said flask under my pillow , and lay my sword by my 
 side and sleep , and be not ill - content so far . 

 but on the morrow , when I meet my fellow , they ask I how I have 
 speed , and I tell they , well , and that we would talk the matter over 
 under our tree of counsel . so we go down into the hall , where we 
 meet the witch - lady ; and I look for it that she would be angry and 
 fierce with I ; but it go far otherwise ; for she be blithe and 
 buxom , and abound in endearment more than I could away with . but 
 this I note , that her eye wander , and her speech falter at 
 while , and ever she seem to be seek somewhat ; and withal that 
 her caress hand be seek if they could aught stow away in 
 the bosom of my coat . but all be nought , for as we come to the door 
 of the hall I give Baudoin the flask to guard until we should come to 
 our apple - tree of rede . wherefore the she - wolf go red and white by 
 turn , and fume , and fret her bedizenment with unrestful hand , 
 and when she should let we go our way , she linger and look back 
 oft , and be loth to depart ere she have get what she lack , and 
 that , forsooth , be the said flasket . 

 but when we be without the house , I bid our fellow go with I to 
 another place than the wonte apple - tree of rede , and they understand 
 my word , and I lead they to a little grassy plain without the orchard , 
 where be no covert for a wide space about it , nought but the one 
 linden - tree under which now we sit . there I tell they all the tale 
 of the last night and of the flasket , and put before they all that 
 be in my mind to do that evening at the banquet , and they both of 
 they yeasaid it . but what it be , that shall ye hear anon when we 
 carry the matter through ; but I bade Baudoin still carry the 
 flasket till the evening . 

 thereafter we spake of other matter ; but soon we have good cause to 
 rejoice that we have not talk our talk under the apple - tree   whereas 
 I doubt not that the witch would spy upon we there , for not long 
 have we be at our talk ere , look that way , we see the evil 
 creature by the hedge of the orchard and gaze over at we . 

 we arise then , and come to she as if nought have happen ; and she 
 bid we walk the garden with she , and we yeasaid it , and go with 
 she , and pace about amidst the flower and lie on the blossom 
 grass . Forsooth , both to she and to we the time hang heavy on hand . 
 and meseeme that the sleekness and fairness of her body be worsen 
 since yesterday , and she be pale and haggard , and her eye be 
 wandering and afraid . 

 now she bid we come a little far into the garden and eat a 
 morsel at noon ; and we arise , and she bring we to where be vine 
 trellis all about and overhead , so that it be like a fair green 
 cloister ; and there be a board lay and spread with many dainty of 
 meat and drink . and she bid we sit . verily we have but little 
 stomach to that dinner ; and I say to myself , Poison ! poison ! and 
 even so my fellow deem , as afterwards they tell I . and I see 
 Baudoin loosen his sword in the sheath , and I know that his mind be 
 to smite at once if he see aught amiss . and I , who sit next to the 
 witch , lay my hand on a little dagger which I wear at my girdle . 
 she also see this , and turn as pale as death , and sit tremble 
 before we ; and whatso we eat or drink at that board under the 
 rustle vine - leaf , she give unto we with her own hand ; and then we 
 wotte full surely that she have mean our death there and then , but 
 be cow by the fierce eye of Baudoin and the threat of my hand . 

 Withal it seem that she might not bear it to sit there long amongst 
 we . she rise up and smile on we as ghastly as a corpse , and give we 
 leave , and go hurry into the house . and right glad we be to 
 be at rest from she . yet as we ourselves durst not go far away from 
 the house , lest some new thing might happen , neither could she leave 
 we quite alone , but thrice again that afternoon at some turn of the 
 garden , or orchard , or meadow , we come upon her wan face and eye 
 full of all hate and star pride , and she enforce she to smile 
 upon we , and turn away with some idle word . 

 at last the sun begin to sink , and we go to the perron of the 
 house , and find she stand to meet we in her wonte way . but when 
 we come up she give no hand to any one of we but go up the stair 
 before we , and we follow with no word speak . 

 there be the hall with the lordly service on the board , and the wax- 
 candle light all about , and the great vault of stone fair and 
 stately over it . we go to the dais and the board and sit down , the 
 witch - wife in her gold and ivory chair at the board 's end , and I at 
 her right hand and look down the hall , my two fellow face I , 
 with their back to the clear of the hall . 

 there we sit , and the meat and drink be before we as dainty as 
 ever erst ; but we put forth no hand to they , but sit star at each 
 other for some two minute it might be , and the witch look from one 
 to the other of we , and quake that her hand shake like palsy . 

 then I rise up and put my hand to my bosom   for Baudoin have give I 
 the flasket ere we come to the perron : I spake in a loud voice , and 
 it sound wild and hard in the goodly hall : my lady , I say , thou 
 art look but pale now , and sick and downcast . drink now to I out 
 of this precious flasket , and thou shalt be whole and well . 

 and therewith I hold the flasket aloft ; but her face change 
 horribly ; she spring up in her chair and reach out her arm to 
 clutch at the flasket , scream like an eagle therewith . but I 
 thrust she back into the chair with my left hand ; and therewith arise 
 Baudoin and Arthur , and catch she by the shoulder , and bind she 
 fast to the chair with cord that they have get thereto . but when 
 she get her breath she yell out : ah , now shall all tumble 
 together , my proudful house and I under it ! Loose I , traitor ! 
 loose I , fool ! and give I one draught of the water of might , and 
 then shall I tell you all , and ye shall go free with your thrall if 
 ye will . ah ! ye will not loose I ? ye will not ? well then , at least 
 ye , the fool , shall be under it , and they also , the she - traitor , 
 the scourged and torment fool that might not save themselves from 
 I . o loose I ! loose I ! thou in whose arm I have lie so many a 
 night , and give I to drink of the proud water of might ! 

 so she yell ; and now have all the fairness go from her body : 
 flaggy and yellow be her limb , and she look all over as she 
 face , a lump of stupid and cruel pride , and her word lose meaning 
 and change into mere bestial howling . but for I , since she so 
 desire that water , I know that it be good for we to drink , and I 
 take out the stopple and drank , and it be as if fire run through all 
 my vein , and I feel my strength three - fold straightway , and most 
 wondrous clear be my sight grow therewith ; and I raise my eye now 
 and look down the hall , and lo , there be Aurea , chain by the 
 ankle to the third pillar from the dai ; and over against she , 
 Viridis ; and next , to the fourth pillar , Atra . then I cry in a 
 loud voice that ring through the witch 's hall : lo what I see ! and I 
 run round the head of the board , and thrust and drag Baudoin and 
 Arthur along with I , cry out : come , come ! they be find ! they 
 be here ! and I come to my sweetling , and find she clothe but in her 
 white smock , which be fleck with blood all about , and her face be 
 wan and pin , and the tear begin to run when she see I , but no 
 word come from her lip though the kissing of they be sweet . 

 then I turn about to my two fellow , and they stand bewilder , not 
 know what be toward ; and I come to they and make they drink of 
 the flasket , and their eye be open and the strength of giant 
 come to they , and they run each to his sweetling ; but Baudoin , before 
 ever he kiss Aurea , catch hold of the chain that bind she to the 
 pillar , and by main force drag it out . Wise be that , meseeme , 
 for word be again come into the witch 's howl , and I hear she : 
 ah , long may ye be play with the chain , long ! for now the house 
 rumbleth toward its fall . ah , the bitch be loose ! Woe 's I ! to 
 die alone ! and once more she howl wordless , as both I and Arthur 
 have our love in our arm , and fall to follow Baudoin out on to 
 the perron and down into the fresh fragrant garden wherein now be 
 the moon begin to cast shadow . 

 stand we then aloof from the house , and the rumble whereof the evil 
 hag have howl wax into a thunder , and under our very eye the 
 great white wall and gold - adorn roof fall together , and a great 
 cloud of dust rise under the clear moonlit sky . 

 we look and wonder , and our love also , but no word they spake ; 
 but ere the other two have time to grieve thereat , I give Viridis to 
 drink of the water of might , and she fall to sweet speech 
 straightway , of such sort and such wise as I will not tell you . then 
 I do the same by Aurea and Atra , and forthwith the speech flow 
 from they to their friend . 

 full happy be we then in the early night - season , for the water of 
 might gave they strength also , as to we , and heal all the stripe 
 and wound their body have suffer of the foul witch , and make 
 their eye bright , and their cheek full and firm , and their lip 
 most sweet , and their hand strong and delicious . 

 now when we have stand gaze toward the melting of the beauteous 
 palace for a little , we take our darling in our arm again , whereas 
 the chain would have hinder their walking , and go down to the 
 lip of the water whereas lie the Sending Boat , so that we might be 
 anigh our ferry in case of need ; for we know not what might betide 
 the isle now its mistress have perish . then we fall to and saw 
 off the chain from the dear ankle with our sword , and take 
 Birdalone 's lending from the mail . and Aurea have her gown again , 
 and Viridis her smock , and my green surcoat over it , and Atra wear 
 the battle - coat of the Black Squire . as for their bare foot   for 
 Atra would not have hers dight prouder than her sister ' , we so clad 
 they with kiss that they be not ill - cover belike . 

 so gat we aboard our ferry , and do blood - offering to the wight 
 thereof , and so speed merrily and lovingly over the wide lake back on 
 our homeward road . and we say : this hath the dear Birdalone do 
 for we . 

 and now , my Viridis , I will that thou fill up the tale by tell to 
 Birdalone , as ye tell we , how it fare with you three and the evil 
 one from the time that ye speed Birdalone on her way till the moment 
 when mine eye first behold you make fast to the pillar of the 
 palace which have crumble into dust . 



 CHAPTER X. how it fare with the three lady after the ESCAPE of 
 birdalone 



 Viridis take up the word without more ado , and say : I will do my 
 good herein , and ye , sister , must set I right if I err . when we 
 have see the last of you , dear Birdalone , that early morning , we 
 turn back again to the house as speedily and as covertly as we 
 might , lest the witch might espy our disarray and question we 
 thereover . then we go to the wonder - coffer , and gat thereout 
 raiment for that which we have give away , which be easy for we to 
 do , whereas the witch - mistress be so slothful that she have give to 
 we the word of might wherewith to compel the coffer to yield , so 
 that we might do all the service thereof , and she not to move hand or 
 foot in the matter . so when we be clothe , and the time be come , we 
 go into the hall , by no mean well assure of our mistress . 

 when we come before she , she look on we in surly wise , as she will not 
 be , and say nought for a while ; she star on we and knit she 
 brow , as if she strove to call to mind something that run to and fro 
 in her memory ; and I note that , and for my part I tremble before 
 she . but she spake at last : Meseemeth as if there be a woman in the 
 isle besides you three ; some misdoer that I be minded to punish . 
 tell I , you ! be there not a naked one who come into this hall a 
 while ago , one whom I threaten with pining ? Atra , who be the 
 bold of we , bow the knee before she , and say : Nay , our lady , 
 since when do stranger woman come naked into thine hall , and dare 
 thee there ? 

 say the witch : yet have I an image of a naked woman stand down 
 there before I ; and if I have it in mine eye , so should ye . tell I 
 therefore , and beware , for we be not bid to hold our hand from 
 you if we take you in misdeed . 

 if I quake before , now much more I quake , till my leg well - nigh 
 fail I for fear ; but Atra say : great lady , this image will 
 belike be of that one whom a while ago ye have strip and tie to a 
 pillar here , and torment while ye feast . 

 the lady look on her hard , and again seem strive to gather up 
 the thrum of some memory , and then her face become smooth again , and 
 she spake lightly : all that may well be ; so do ye go about your due 
 service , and trouble our rest here no long ; for we love not to look 
 on folk who be not wholly our own to pine or to spare , to slay or let 
 live , as we will ; and we would that the wind and the wave would 
 send we some such now ; for it be like to live all alone to have but 
 such as you with we , and none to cower before we and entreat we of 
 mercy . so begone , I bid you . 

 thus for that time be we save from the witch 's cruelty ; but our 
 time come before long . the day wear heavily , nor keep we count of 
 they lest we should lose heart for the weariness of waiting . but on 
 a day as we stand on the step of the perron and serve my lady with 
 dainty , of a hot afternoon , come two great white dove a - flying , 
 who pitch down right before our mistress 's foot ; and each have a 
 gold ring about his neck , and a scroll tie thereto , and the witch 
 bade we take the dove and take off the scroll and give they unto 
 she ; and she look on the gold ring which the dove bore , and for a 
 moment on the scroll , and then she say : take ye the dove and 
 cherish they , lest we have need of they ; take also the two scroll 
 and keep they till to - morrow morning , and then give they into our 
 hand . and look ye to this , that if ye give they not unto Us it will 
 be treason against we , and we shall have a case against you , and your 
 body will be Ours . 

 then she rise up slowly , and bid I to she that she might lean upon 
 my shoulder and be help upstairs , so slothful a beast as she be ; 
 and as we go up I hear she say softly to herself : weary on it , 
 now must I drink a sup of the Water of Might , that I may remember and 
 do and desire . but dear be my sister , and without doubt she hath 
 matter of import to tell I by these dove . 

 so when we be together alone I tell the other hereof , and we 
 talk it over ; and they deem the tiding ill , even as I do ; for 
 we might not doubt but that the dove be a sending from the witch- 
 sister who dwell at the House under the Wood ; and sore we misdoubte 
 that they be speed to our mistress to tell she of thee , Birdalone , 
 and mayhappen of the Quest , so wise as we know she be . as to the 
 two scroll , forsooth , they be open , and not seal ; but when we 
 look on they we could make nought of it ; for though they be writ 
 fairly in Latin script , so that we read they , yet of the word no 
 whit might we understand , so we fear the bad . but what might we 
 do ? we have but two choice , either to cast ourselves into the water , 
 or abide what should befall ; and this last one we choose because of 
 the hope of deliverance . 

 next morning , therefore , we come before our mistress in the hall , and 
 we find she pace up and down before the dais ; though she will not be 
 at that hour to be sit in her throne of gold and ivory , lie 
 back on the cushion half asleep . 

 so Atra go up to she , and knelt before she and give she the 
 scroll , and she look on her grimly , and smile evilly , and say : 
 Kneel there yet ; and ye other kneel also , till I see what befitteth 
 you . so do we , and indeed I be fain to kneel , for I might scarce 
 stand up for terror ; and all of we , our heart die within we . 

 but the witch read those scroll to herself , sit in her throne , 
 and spake not a long while ; then she say : come hither , and grovel 
 before we , and hearken ! even so we do ; and she say again : our 
 sister , who hath be so kind unto you , and save you from so many 
 pain , here telleth Us , by the message of the two dove , that ye have 
 betray Us and she , and have steal her thrall and her Sending Boat , 
 and send she an errand for our destruction ; and therewith she 
 delivereth you into our hand , and ye be ours henceforward ; nor be 
 it to be think that ye may escape we . now , for your treason , some 
 would slay you outright here and now , but we will be merciful , and 
 let you live , and do no more than chastise you sharply now ; and 
 thereafter shall ye be our very thrall to do as we will with : 
 thereafter , that be to say , when they whom ye have send our sister 's 
 thrall to fetch have come hither   as belike I may scarce stay they , 
 and I have foil they and use they , and send they away empty . now 
 I tell you , that meanwhile of their come shall ye suffer such 
 thing as we will ; and when they be here we will not forbid you to be 
 anigh they ; but we shall see that there will be little joy to you in 
 that nighness . yea , ye shall know now to what market ye have bring 
 your ware , and what the price of treason be therein . 

 verily then we suffer at her hand what she would , whereof it would 
 shame I to tell more as at this present ; and thereafter do she 
 chain we to those three pillar of the hall whereas ye find we 
 chain ; and we be feed as dog be , and serve as dog , but we 
 endure all for the sake of hope ; and when we durst , and deem the 
 witch would not hear we , we spake together and enheartene each 
 other . 

 but on the fourth day of our torment come the witch to we , and give 
 we to drink a certain red water from out of a leaden flasket ; and 
 when I drink I deem it be poison , and be glad , if gladness might 
 be in I at such a tide ; and when I have drunk I feel an icy chill go 
 through all my body , and all thing swam before my eye , and deadly 
 sickness come over I . but that pass away from I presently , and I 
 feel helpless and yet not feeble ; all sound hear I clear than 
 ever yet in my life ; also I see the hall , every arch and pillar and 
 fret , and the gleam on the pavement from the bright sun that might 
 not enter ; and the witch I see walk up and down the hall by the 
 dais ; but my sister I see not when I look across to their pillar . 
 moreover , I might not see myself when I reach out my hand or my 
 foot , though I see the chain which make my ankle fast to the pillar ; 
 and withal , when I set my hand on my face , or any other part of my 
 body , or what else I might touch , I feel there what I look to feel , 
 be it flesh or linen , or the cold iron of my fetter , or the 
 polished face of the marble pillar . 

 now I know scarce if I be alive or dead , or if I be but begin 
 to be dead ; but there come upon I the desire of life , and I strove 
 to cry out to the sister , but though I form the word in my mouth , 
 and do with my throat as when one cry out aloud , yet no sound of a 
 voice come from I , and more helpless do I feel than erst . 

 but even therewith I see the witch come toward I , and therewith all 
 my body feel such fear of she that I know I be not dead . then she 
 come before I and say : o shadow of a thrall , whom none can see but 
 they unto whom wisdom hath give eye to see wonder withal , now have 
 I tiding for thee and thy sister , to wit , that your lover and 
 seeker be at hand ; and presently I shall bring they into this hall , 
 and they shall be so nigh unto you that ye might touch they if I do 
 not forbid it ; but they shall not see you , but shall wonder where I 
 have hide you , and shall go seek you to - day and many day , and 
 shall find you not at all . so make ye the most of the sight of they , 
 for in they henceforward ye have no other part or lot . 

 Therewith she spit out at I , and go over to my sister , and say 
 word of like import to those which she have say unto I . and 
 presently she go out of the hall ; and not long afterwards I hear 
 voice speak on the perron , and know one for the voice of the 
 witch , and the other for the voice of my lord Baudoin ; and then again 
 wear a little while , and I see the witch come through the great door 
 of the hall lead Sir Arthur by the hand , as if she be his dear 
 friend , and Baudoin and Hugh , my man , follow they . and the say 
 witch be clothe full fair , and have lay by her sloth and stupid pride , 
 as meseeme ; and her limb be grow rounder and sleeker , and her 
 skin fairer , so that to they that know she not she might well seem to 
 be a goodly woman . 

 now they sit to meat as my man hath tell you , and then depart from 
 the hall , and the witch also . but after a while she come back again 
 and loose we , and grimly bid we go with she , and need must we , 
 though we could not so much as see our own foot upon the floor . and 
 she set we to task about the house , and stand by while we toil for 
 she , and mock we not without stripe , and in all way be as rough 
 and cruel and hard with we as she have be smooth and debonair to our 
 lord ; but after noon she bring we back and chain we to our 
 pillar again . and when the evening come and the banquet be , it be 
 we who be the unseen player of the string - play ; and we might play 
 no other melody than what the witch bid we ; else belike , could we 
 have hold converse , we might have play such tune as would have 
 smite the heart of our love , and tell they that we be anigh . 
 to make a short story of it , thus do she day by day , and no comfort 
 or converse might we sister have of each other , or of aught else 
 save the sight of our beloved one , and a glimmer of hope therewith . 
 and , forsooth , for as grievously as my heart be wring by the 
 yearning of I for my love , yet be it a joy unto I to think that he 
 go there desire I , and that whom he desire be not the poor 
 wretched creature chain there in her nakedness , with her body 
 spoil by torment and misery , but the glad maiden whom he have so 
 often call fair and lovesome . 

 so pass the day , and at last hope have grow so pale and wan , that 
 she be no more to be see by we than we be by our lord ; and now 
 it seem to I that death be come , so feeble and wretche as I 
 grow . but the witch would not let we die , but sustain we from time 
 to time with some little draught of a witch - drink that revive we . 

 so wear the time till that evening , when come hope together with the 
 fulfilment of hope , so that one minute we durst hope for deliverance , 
 and the next we be deliver . 

 nor be there more to tell , Birdalone , my dear , save that we come 
 safely to the Isle of the Young and the Old in the full morning - tide ; 
 and as our ferry draw nigh the green shore , there be the two 
 youngling whereof thou didst tell we await our landing , and when 
 we step ashore they come to we bear cake and fruit in a fair 
 basket , and they make much of we and we of they . and so we come to 
 the old man , who be exceed fain of we , and grand and courteous , 
 till he become a little drunk , and then he be somewhat over - kind to 
 we woman . nevertheless , there in that pleasant isle we rest we for 
 three day , that we might somewhat calm and refresh our spirit with 
 what be small and of little account . and when we depart , the old 
 man follow we down to the strand , and lament our departure , as he 
 have do with our lord erewhile ; only this time yet great be his 
 lamentation , and need must we kiss he , each one of we , or never have 
 he be do . so he turn up landward , bewail the miss of we , 
 but presently , before we have see the last of he , be cheerful again 
 and sing . 

 so we go on our way ; and we also , we maiden , in our turn , see 
 those woeful image of the Isle of Queens and the Isle of king ; and 
 we come to the Isle of Nothing , and abode warily by our ferry , and so 
 come away safe , and thus , as thou wott , home to the castle to hear 
 evil tiding of thee . now be this all my tale . 

 birdalone sit shyly and hush when all be do ; and then all they 
 do somewhat to comfort she , each after their own fashion ; and now 
 sorrow for the slain man be make soft and sweet for they , 
 whereas they have to lose not two fellow , but one only . yet , despite 
 of all , trouble and care be on Birdalone 's soul betwixt the joy of 
 love and be belove , and the pain and fear of rob a friend 
 of her love . for Atra 's face , which she might not hate , and scarce 
 might love , be a threat to her day by day . 



 CHAPTER XI . BIRDALONE and the black SQUIRE TALK together in the hall 
 of the CASTLE . 



 now within a few day be the body of Baudoin lay in earth in the 
 chapel of the castle ; and in the solemn of fashion be the burial 
 do . when it be over , the two knight and Sir Aymeris turn they 
 heartily to dighte the war against the Red Hold , and less than a 
 month thereafter be the hosting at the Castle of the Quest , and if 
 the host be not very many   for it go not above sixteen hundred 
 of man all tell , yet the man be of the choicest , both of knight 
 and sergeant and archer . there then they hold a mote without the 
 castle , whereas Arthur the Black Squire be choose for captain , and 
 in three day they be to depart for the Red Hold . 

 now this while Birdalone have see but little of Arthur , who be ever 
 busy about many matter , and never have she have any privy talk with 
 he , though sore she long for it ; yet indeed it be more by she 
 will than his that so it be . but when it be come to the very last 
 day before the departure , she say that she must need see he before 
 he go , and he perchance never to come back again . so when man be 
 quiet after dinner she go into the hall and find he there , pace 
 up and down the floor . for indeed she have send a word to he by 
 Leonard the priest that he should be there . 

 so she go up to he , and all simply she take he by the hand and 
 lead he into a shot - window and set he down by she ; and he , all 
 tremble for love and fear of she , might not forbear , but kiss she 
 face and her mouth many time ; and she grow as hot as fire , and 
 somewhat she weep . 

 then she spake after a while : dear friend , I have it in my mind to 
 say to thee many thing that meseem be sage , but now neither will 
 the thought of they come into my mind , nor the word into my mouth . 
 and this be a short hour . and therewith she fall to kiss he , 
 till he be well - nigh beside himself betwixt desire and joy and the 
 grief of departure , and the hardness of the case . 

 but at last she forbore and say : will it not be when thou art go 
 to - morrow as it be when ye be away upon the Quest , and I know not 
 how to bear myself , so heavy lie all the world and its doing and its 
 fashion upon I ? it will be hard to I , he say ; evil and grim will 
 be the day . she say : and yet , even now in these last day , when I 
 see thee oft , every day my soul be wear with grief , and I know not 
 what to do with myself . I shall come back , he say , and bear my love 
 with I , and then belike we shall seek some remedy . she be silent a 
 while , and then she say : meanwhile of thy coming , and I see thee 
 not at all for many day , how will it be with my grief then ? Quoth 
 he : More than enough of grief no soul may bear ; for either death 
 come , or else some dullness of the pain , and then by little and 
 little the pain weareth . then she say : and how would it be if thou 
 come not back and I see thee never again , or if when thou come back 
 thou find I not , for that I be either dead or go away out of thy 
 reach ? he say : I know not how it would be . when thou say thou 
 shalt die , dost thou wholly believe it in thy sense or thy body 
 otherwise than Holy Church would ? I will tell thee , she say , that 
 now I be sit by thee and see thy face and hear thy voice , 
 it be that only which I believe in ; for I may think of nought else of 
 either grief or joy . yea , when I weep e'en now , it be not for 
 sorrow that I weep , but for I can not rightly tell what . and she take 
 his hand and look fondly upon he . 

 but presently she look on his hand , and say : and now meseemeth 
 that we twain be grow to be such close friend that I may ask thee 
 what I will , and thou be neither angry , nor wonder thereat . I see on 
 thy finger here the ring that I bring with I from the Isle of 
 Increase , and which thereafter thou hadst of I when I give thee back 
 also the shoon which be lend unto I . tell I how thou hadst it 
 back from Atra , as I suppose thou gav it unto she . but how now 
 art thou angry ? for I see the blood come up in thy face . Nay , 
 belove , say he , I be not angry , but whenso I hear of Atra , or think 
 of she closely , shame come on I and confusion , and maybe fear . but 
 now will I answer thee . for even in those hour which we wear on the 
 Isle of the Young and the Old , when all we should have be so happy 
 together , she divine somewhat of my case , or indeed , why do I not 
 say it out , all thereof . and she spake to I such word   for she be 
 both tender and wise and strong of heart   that I cower before she 
 and her grief and pain ; and she give I back the said ring , which 
 forsooth I give to she in the Sending Boat in the first hour that the 
 Isle of Increase lie astern of we . and I wear it now as a token of 
 my grief for her grief . see now , love , since I have answer thee 
 this question without anger or amaze , thou need not fear to ask I 
 any other ; for this of all thing lie close to my heart . 

 birdalone droop her head , and she spake in a low voice : lo now ! 
 the shadow of parting and the shadow of death could not come between 
 our present joy ; but this shadow of the third one cometh between we 
 and be present between we . Woe 's I ! how little do I think of this 
 when thou wert away and I be sick of longing for the sight of thee , 
 and deem that that would heal it all . 

 he spake not , but take her hand and hold it ; and presently she look 
 up again and say : thou art good , and wilt not be angry if I ask 
 thee something else ; this it be : why wert thou so grim with I that 
 other day when ye find I in that evil plight in tow of the Red 
 Tyrant , so that I deem that thou of all other hadst cast I off ? 
 that be bad to I than the witch 's stripe , and I keep think to 
 myself : how simple be my trouble once , and now how tangle and 
 weary ! 

 then he might not refrain he , but throw himself upon she , and 
 clip she and kiss she all he might , and she feel all the 
 sweetness of love , and lack nought of kindness and love to he . 
 and thereafter they sit still awhile , and he say , as if her question 
 have but that moment leave her lip : this , forsooth , be the cause 
 that I look grim on thee : first , that from the time I first see 
 thee and hear thy tale , and of thy deed , I have deem thee wise 
 above the wisdom of woman . but this go forth of thee to the Black 
 Valley , whereof come the slaying of Baudoin , seem unto I a mere 
 folly , till again I have hear thy tale of that also ; and then the 
 tale and thy speech overcome I . but again , though I be grieve and 
 disappointed hereat , belike that have pass from I speedily , but 
 then there be this also which would not let my soul rest , to wit , 
 that I fear concern that slain knight whose head the Red One have 
 hang about thy neck ; for how else , methought , might he have be so 
 wroth with he and thee ; and meseeme , moreover , that thou wert kind 
 in thine heart to the dead man , even when we be come to thee ; and 
 then , seest thou , my desire for thee and the trouble of Baudoin 's 
 slaying , and the black trouble aforesaid . Lo now , I have tell thee 
 this . when wilt thou cease to be angry with I ? 

 she say : I cease to be grieve with thine anger when thine anger 
 die ; yet strange , meseemeth , that thou shouldst trust I so little 
 when thou lov I so much ! 

 and she lean against he and caress he gently , and again be he 
 at point to take she in his arm , when lo ! the sound of man come 
 unto the screen of the hall ; so then those two stand up and go to 
 meet they , and there be the speech of their sundering do . yet 
 belike for a little while both those twain be happy . 



 CHAPTER XII . the KNIGHTS and their fellows betake they to the 
 assaulting of the RED hold 



 on the morrow , when the day be yet young , the knight be ready for 
 departure , and in the very gate they bid farewell to the lady , who 
 kiss they kindly one and all , and Viridis weep sore ; and Atra 
 constrain herself to do even as the other do ; but pale she be 
 and quake when she kiss Arthur and watch he get a - horseback . 

 but the knight bid their lady be of good cheer , for that they 
 would send they tiding of how they speed every seven day at least , 
 whereas it be no long way thence to the Red Hold , save there be 
 battle on the road , and they deem their host which should beset the 
 Hold would be enough to clear all the way behind it . for that same 
 cause withal they have Sir Aymeris with they , nor leave a many man 
 behind they , and they under the rule of three squire , whereof two 
 be but young , and the third , who be make the captain of the 
 castle , be an old and wise man of war , who have to name Geoffrey of 
 Lea . there , withal , be the priest Sir Leonard , who go about now 
 much hush and abash , and seem to fear to give a word to 
 birdalone ; albeit she deem of he that his thought of she be the 
 same as erst they have be . 

 so now when the knight be depart , and all the host be go out 
 of sight , it be heavy time indeed in the Castle of the Quest till 
 they should hear tiding of they again . both Aurea and Atra keep 
 much to themselves , and do I know not what to wear away the time ; 
 for now it be not to be look for that they should venture out - a- 
 gate . but as for Viridis , she wax of well cheer after a while , 
 but whatever betid she would not sunder herself from Birdalone ; nay , 
 not for an hour ; and Birdalone take all her kindness kindly , though 
 forsooth it be somewhat of a pain unto she ; it shall be tell 
 wherefore ere long . 

 Withal , as if to wear the time , Birdalone betake she diligently to 
 her needlework , and fall to the cunningest of broidery ; so that 
 Viridis and the other wonder at she , for when they be do it 
 seem indeed that the flower and creature and knot have grow of 
 themselves upon the cloth , such wondrous work it be . 

 moreover , to his great joy , the very first day of the departure of 
 the host she call Sir Leonard unto she , and pray to go on again 
 with the learn her fair scribe - craft ; and therein also be she 
 diligent hour of every day ; and Viridis would sit beside she 
 wonder at the deftness of her finger , and cry out for joy as 
 the page grow fair and well - learn under they . 

 thus wear a week , and at the end thereof come a messenger from the 
 host and tell how they have come before the Red Hold and have summon 
 they thereof to yield , which they have utterly deny to do , but 
 defy the host ; wherefore the host have now beset the Hold , and more 
 folk be daily flock unto they ; but that the say hold would be 
 hard to win by plain assault , whereas it be both strong and well- 
 man ; but few of the host have be slay or hurt as yet , and of the 
 chieftain not one . 

 right glad be they of the castle because of these tiding ; though , 
 forsooth , the man - at - arm know well enough that the time would soon 
 come when some fierce assault would be make , and then , forsooth , 
 would be sore peril of life and limb unto the chieftain . 



 CHAPTER XIII . birdalone bethink she to fulfil the promise make unto 
 ATRA 



 again wear a week , and once more come the messenger , and do they of 
 the castle to wit that there have be nought more do at the Red 
 Hold , save skirmishing at the barrier , wherein few be hurt on 
 either side ; and also that the engine for batter the wall be 
 now well - nigh all dight , and they would begin to play upon the Hold , 
 and in especial one which hight Wall - wolf , which have be set up by 
 the craft of Greenford . 

 this tiding also be deem good by all , save it might be by Atra , 
 who , as Birdalone deem , pined and fret herself at the delay , and 
 would fain that , one way or other , all be over . Atra spake but 
 little to Birdalone , but watch she closely now ; oft would she gaze 
 on she wistfully , as if she would that Birdalone would speak unto 
 she ; and Birdalone note that , but she might not pluck up heart 
 thereto . 

 wear a third week , and again come the messenger , and tell how three 
 day ago , whenas Wall - wolf have sorely batter one of the great 
 tower which hight the poison - jar , and overthrow a pan of the wall 
 there beside , they have try an assault on the breach , and hard have 
 be the battle there , and in the end , after fierce give and take , 
 they of the Hold have do so valiantly that they have thrust back the 
 assailant , and that in the hot brunt the Black Squire have be 
 hurt in the shoulder by a spear - thrust , but not very grievously ; but 
 withal that he send , in so many word , forbid the lady to make 
 any account of so small a matter . and , quoth the sergeant , most like 
 my lord will wear his armour in four day ' time ; also now we have 
 rear another great slinger , which we call stone - fretter , and soon , 
 without doubt , we shall be stand victorious within that den of 
 thief . 

 now though these tiding be not so altogether ill , yet be those 
 lady sore troubled thereby , and especially Atra , who swoon 
 outright when she have hear the last word thereof . 

 as for Birdalone , she make as little semblance of her trouble as she 
 might , but when all be quiet again she go to find Viridis , and 
 bring she to her chamber and spake to she , say : Viridis , my 
 sister , thou hast be piteous kind unto I from the first minute 
 that thou saw I naked and helpless , and flee from evil unto 
 bad evil ; nowise might thou have do well by I hadst thou 
 be verily my sister of blood ; and I know it that thou wouldst be 
 loth to part from I . 

 Viridis weep and say : why dost thou speak of parting from I , when 
 thou know it would break my heart ? 

 say birdalone : to say it as short as may be , because the parting 
 must now come to pass . Viridis wax pale and then red , and she 
 stamp her foot and say : it be unkind of thee to grieve I thus , 
 and thou doest wrong herein . 

 Hearken , dear sister , say birdalone : thou know , for thou thyself 
 wast the first to tell I thereof , that I be the supplanter in our 
 fellowship , and that I have undo Atra 's hope . this I do not of 
 mine own will , but it come unto I ; yet of mine own will I can do the 
 good I may to amend it ; and this be the good , that I depart hence 
 before the Red Hold be take and my lord come back ; for if they come 
 back and I see my lord Arthur , so fair and beauteous as he be , before 
 I , never shall I be able to go away from he . and lo thou , I have 
 promise Atra by all the kindness she do I when we be come to the 
 Wailing Tower , and I naked and quaking and half - dead with terror , 
 that if occasion serve I would do my utmost to help she , even if it 
 be to my own grief . now behold this that now be , be the occasion , 
 and there will not be another ; for when my love come home hither and 
 beholdeth I , think thou how all the desire which have be gather 
 in his heart this while will blossom and break forth toward I ; and 
 mayhappen he will make but little semblance of it before other folk , 
 for proud and high of heart be he ; but he will seek occasion to find 
 I alone , and then shall I be with he as the lark in the talon of 
 the sparrow - hawk , and he will do his pleasure of I , and that with 
 all the good - will of my heart . and then shall I be forswear to Atra , 
 and she will hate I , as now she doth not , and then be all the 
 fellowship riven , and that by my deed . 

 yet be Viridis wrath , and she say : Meseemeth this be fool 's talk . 
 will not the fellowship be all the more riven if thou depart and we 
 see thee no more ? 

 o nay , say Birdalone ; for when I be go thy love shall be no less 
 for I , though as now thou art angry ; and Atra will love I for that 
 I shall have hold to my promise to mine own scathe ; and thy man and 
 Aurea will lay it to I that I have do valiantly and knightly . and 
 Arthur , how can he choose but love I ; and maybe we shall yet meet 
 again . 

 and therewithal she do at last bow down her head and fall to 
 weeping , and Viridis be move by her tear and fall to kiss and 
 caress she . 

 after a little birdalone lift up her head and spake again : 
 moreover , how can I dare to abide he ? didst thou not see how grim he 
 be to I when they deliver I and bring I back ? and he with his 
 own lip tell I so much , that it be because he doubt that I have 
 do amiss ; and now if I do amiss again , even if it be at his 
 bidding , will it not be so that he will speedily weary of I , and 
 curse I and cast I off ? what sayest thou , Viridis mine ? 

 what be to say , say Viridis , save that thou hast break my heart ? 
 but thou mayst heal it if thou wilt take thy word back , and tell I 
 that thou wilt not sunder thee from we . 

 but birdalone brake out weeping and lament aloud , and she cry 
 out : Nay , nay , it may not be ; I must depart , and Atra hath smite 
 I amidst of my friend . and Viridis know not what to say or to do . 

 at last come Birdalone to herself again , and she look sweetly on 
 Viridis and smile on she from out her tear , and say : Thou seest , 
 sister , how little a loss thou wilt have of I , a mere wild woman . 
 and now nought availeth either I or thee but I must begone , and that 
 speedily . let it be to - morrow then . and when the messenger come at 
 the end of this week , send word by he of what I have do ; and look 
 thou to it but both our lord will praise I for the deed . 

 say Viridis : but whither wilt thou , or what wilt thou do ? to 
 Greenford first , say Birdalone , and after whither the Good Lord 
 shall lead I ; and as for what I will do , I be now deft in two 
 craft , script and broidery to wit ; and , wheresoever I be , folk shall 
 pay I to work herein for they , whereby I shall earn my bread . 
 Hearken also , my sister , canst thou give I any deal of money ? for 
 though I wot little of such matter , yet I wot that I shall need the 
 same . and I ask this whereas , as e'en now I say , I deem our lord 
 shall praise my deed , and that , therefore , they would not that I 
 should depart hence as an outcast , wherefore they shall not begrudge 
 it to I . moreover , for the same cause I would thee speak to the old 
 squire Geoffrey of Lea , and tell he that I have an errand to 
 Greenford , and crave of he that he lend I one of the two 
 youngling , Arnold or Anselm , and two or three man - at - arm to bring 
 I safely thither ; since now , forsooth , I need no more adventure on 
 the road . 

 she smile as she spake ; and now all the passion of anguish seem to 
 have leave she for that while ; but Viridis cast her arm about her 
 neck and weep upon her bosom , and say : Woe 's I ! for I see that 
 thou wilt go whatsoever I may say or do ; I strove to be angry with 
 thee , but I might not , and now I see that thou constrainest I as 
 thou dost all else . I will go now straightway and do thine errand . 

 thus then they part for that time ; but it be not till the day 
 after the morrow that Birdalone be alboun . Viridis tell of she 
 departure both to Aurea and Atra ; and Aurea lament it , but would 
 not do aught to stay she ; for she be waxen weary and listless since 
 the death of her man . as for Atra , she spake but little concern 
 it , but to Viridis praise Birdalone 's valiance and kindness . yet 
 unto herself she say : verily she understand my word that I spake to 
 she about the occasion of her helping . yet woe 's I ! for she shall 
 carry his love with her whithersoever she wendeth ; and a happy woman 
 be she . 

 but when Geoffrey the squire know that the lady , all three , be at 
 one with Birdalone as to her departure , he doubt nothing , but bade 
 Arnold , his mate , take four good man with he , and bring the Lady 
 birdalone unto Greenford and do she bid there . albeit , he deem 
 no less but they would bring she back again . 



 CHAPTER XIV . birdalone leave the CASTLE of the quest 



 on the morrow morn , then , Birdalone spake farewell both to Aurea and 
 Atra ; but as for Viridis , she send her word that she have no heart 
 thereto , and yet she send she a word of comfort , to wit , that she 
 deem that they would one day meet again . Aurea , in her parting 
 word , part praise she , part chid she ; say that she do well and 
 kindly and valiantly , as she will not be . yet , say she , when all be 
 say , thou might have abide this tangle and trouble , which at the 
 bad have not be so evil as death between we . yea , sister , say 
 Birdalone , but might not death have come of my abiding ? 

 as she spake , in come Atra , with her head somewhat droop , meek and 
 humble , her cheek red , her hand tremble ; and she say : wilt thou 
 take now my word of farewell and blessing , and the kiss of peace 
 betwixt we , and bear away the memory of our kindness together ? 

 birdalone stand up proud and straight , and be somewhat pale as she 
 suffer Atra to kiss her cheek and mouth , and say : now hast thou 
 forgive I that weird drag I in betwixt thy love and thy 
 goodhap ; and I have forgive thee that I be lead away by weird into 
 the waste and the wilderness of love . farewell . Therewith she go 
 her way to the gate , and the other follow she not . 

 without abode her Arnold and the four man - at - arm , and her palfrey 
 and a sumpter - horse bear two goodly coffer , wherein Viridis have 
 let load raiment and other having for she ; and Arnold come up to her 
 smile , and say : my lady Viridis hath give I a pouch wherein be 
 money to bear for thee to Greenford and hand over to thee there when 
 we be safe ; and she hath bid I to be in all wise obedient unto 
 thee , lady , which need not , whereas now and from hence forth be I 
 by mine own will thy very servant to do thy pleasure always and 
 everywhere . 

 she thank he and smile on he kindly , so that his heart beat fast 
 for joy and love of she ; and therewith she gat into the saddle and 
 they ride their way together , and Birdalone look back never till 
 the Castle of the Quest be shut from their eye by the ness of the 
 little hill . 


 here end the Fifth Part of the Water of the Wondrous Isles , which be 
 call the Tale of the Quest 's Ending , and begin the Sixth Part of 
 the said tale , which be call the Days of Absence . 




 the sixth part : the day of ABSENCE 




 CHAPTER I. birdalone rid to GREENFORD and there takes leave of 
 ARNOLD and his MEN 



 on the road to Greenford nought befell to tell of ; they come thither 
 when the sun be at point to set , for they have ride diligently all 
 day . 

 as they ride the street of the good town , they note of they , that 
 though it be evening wherein folk do much disport they abroad , there 
 be woman and child enough in the street or stand at their 
 door , but of carle very few , and they for the more part grey - head . 

 now do Arnold bring Birdalone to the town hall , wherein yet sit the 
 deputy of the burgrave , who himself be in the leaguer at the Red 
 Hold ; this man , who be old and wise and nothing feeble of body , make 
 much of Birdalone and her folk , and be glad of they when he know 
 that they have the seal and let - pass of Geoffrey of Lea ; wherefore he 
 give they to eat and drink , and lodge they in his own house , and 
 make they the good of cheer . 

 but betime on the morrow do Birdalone send back Arnold and the four 
 man - at - arm , with no tale but that such be her will ; and bidding 
 farewell to the said Arnold , she suffer he to kiss her hand , and 
 give he a ring from off her finger , so that he go on his way 
 rejoice . 

 so soon as she see he and his man well on the road , she go to the 
 old man , the vice - ruler of the town , who be of the aldermen thereof , 
 and do he to wit that she would wage two or three carle who could 
 deal with horse and beast , and withal handle weapon if need be , 
 to be both as servant and guard for she , as she have errand in that 
 country - side , and belike might well have to go from town to town 
 thereabout . he take she ask kindly , but say it be none so easy 
 to find man who for any wage would fare forth of Greenford at that 
 stour , whereas well - nigh all their fighting - man be lie before the 
 Red Hold as now . Howsoever , ere noontide he bring before her a man 
 of over three score , but yet wayworthy , and two stout young man , his 
 son , and tell she that these man be trusty and would go with she 
 to the world 's end if need be . 

 she take these man readily , and agree with they for a good wage ; and 
 whereas each one have bow and arrow and short sword , she have but to 
 buy for they jack , sallet , and buckler , and they be well armed 
 as for their condition . Withal she buy they three good horse and 
 another sumpt - horse ; which last be load with sundry ware that 
 she deem that she need , and with victual . then she take leave of 
 the alderman , thank he much for his good - will , and so depart 
 from Greenford at all adventure , when the day be yet young . 

 the alderman have ask her whither away , and she have tell he that 
 she be boun for Mostwyke first , and thereafter for shifford - on - the- 
 Strand ; whereas she have hear talk of these two town as be on one 
 and the same highway , and Mostwyke about a score of mile from 
 Greenford ; but when she be well out - a - gate she come to a little 
 road on the right hand which turn clean away from Mostwyke , and she 
 take the said road ; and when she have follow it some three mile , 
 she ask the old carle whither it lead . he look on she and smile 
 somewhat , and she on he in turn ; and she say : wonder not , my 
 friend , that I be not clear about my way , for I shall tell the sooth 
 that I be a damsel adventurous , and be but seek some place where I 
 may dwell and earn my livelihood till well day come ; and this be 
 the whole truth , and thou shalt know it at once , to wit , that I be 
 indeed flee , and be fain to hide the footstep of I , and I bid 
 you three to help I therein . but ye must know that I be flee , 
 not from my foe , but from my friend ; and , if ye will , as we go by 
 the way , I will tell you all the story of I , and we will be friend 
 while we be together , yea , and thereafter if it may be . 

 now she say this because she have look carefully on these man , and 
 herseeme that they be good man and true , and not dull of wit . 
 Forsooth the old man , who hight Gerard of the Clee , be no weakling , 
 and be nought loathly to look on , and his two son be goodly and 
 great of fashion , clear - eye , and well - carven of visage ; they hight 
 Robert and Giles . 

 now spake old Gerard : Lady , I thank thee heartily of thy much grace 
 unto I ; now would I get off my nag and kneel to thee in the highway 
 therefor , but that I see that thou wert fain to make as much way as 
 may be to - day ; wherefore , by thy leave , I will tarry my homaging till 
 we rest our horse by the wayside . she laugh , and praise his 
 wisdom ; and the young man look on she and worship she in their 
 heart . Forsooth , the fellowship of these good and true folk be 
 soft and sweet to she , and soothe the trouble of her spirit . and 
 she enforce herself to talk cheerfully with they , and ask they 
 many thing , and learn much of they . 

 but now go on Gerard to say : Lady , if thou wilt hide thy way from 
 whomsoever it may be , thou hast happen on no ill way ; for though 
 this road be good to ride , it be but a byway through the sheep - walk 
 that folk may drive their wain hereby in the wet season of winter 
 and spring ; and for a great way we shall come to but little save the 
 cot of the sheep - carle ; scarce a hamlet or two for the space of two 
 day ' riding ; and on the third day a little town , hight Upham , where 
 be but few folk save at the midsummer wool - fair , which be now go 
 by . 

 now there be a highway cometh into this road from out of the tilled 
 country and Appleham , a good town , and goeth through it toward the 
 tillage , and the City of the Bridges and the liberty thereof ; and 
 all the land be much builded and plentiful ; but , if thou wilt , we 
 will not take either highway , but wend over the downland which lieth 
 north - east of Upham , and though it be roadless , yet be it not ill- 
 go , and I know it well and its watering - place , little dale and 
 water therein all run north - east , wherein be certain little 
 thorp here and there , which shall refresh we mightily . over that 
 downland we may wend a four day , and then the land will swell up 
 high , and from the end of that high land we shall behold below we a 
 fair land of tillage , well water and woode , and much build ; and 
 in the midst thereof a great city with wall and tower , and a great 
 white castle and a minster , and lovely house a many . in that city 
 mayst thou dwell and earn thy livelihood if thou canst do aught of 
 craft . and if thou mayst not , then may we find somewhat to swink at 
 for a wage , and so maintain thee and we . but the say city be call 
 the City of the five Crafts , and the land round about it be the frank 
 thereof ; and oftenest , frank and city and all , it be call the five 
 craft all simply . now what sayest thou hereof , my lady ? 

 she say : I say that we will go thither , and that I thank thee and 
 thy son of thy good - will , and so may God do to I as I reward you 
 well therefor . but tell I , good Gerard , how it be that thou art so 
 willing to leave kith and kin to follow a gangrel wife along the 
 way ? say Gerard : Dame , I think that the face and body of thee 
 might lead any man that yet have manhood in he to follow thee , even 
 if he leave house and all to go with thee . but as for we , we have no 
 long a house or gear , whereas they of the Red Hold lift all my 
 bestial , and burn my house and all that be therein a month ago . 
 Yea , say Birdalone , and how befalleth it , then , that ye be not 
 before the Red Hold to avenge thee ? Dame , say he , when the muster 
 be I be deem somewhat over old , wherefore the sheriff take I 
 not , but suffer my son also to abide behind to earn a living for 
 I ; may God be good to he therefor , and St. Leonard ! but as to my 
 kindre , I must tell thee that I be not kinne hereabout , but in a 
 good town hight Utterhay , and that when our alderman send for I to 
 bring I to thee , I be more than half - minded to get I back thither . 
 now sooth it be that the good way thither , though it may be indeed 
 the safe rather than the short , lie through the five craft ; 
 for the road go thence to Utterhay a three score mile or so , 
 make the long of it , as it skirteth ever some way off a perilous 
 forest , a place of sore dread and devilish , which hight Evilshaw , on 
 the edge whereof lieth Utterhay , a merry cheaping - stead and a 
 plenteous , and the home of my kindre . wherefore now be the City of 
 the five craft handy to we ; because when thou hast do with we , as 
 I hope it may be long first , then be we other nigh home , and may 
 all simply wend our way thither . 

 birdalone thank he again full heartily ; but therewithal as they 
 ride along there seem to stir in she some memory of the early of 
 her day in the witch 's house , and she begin to have a longing to 
 betake she to Utterhay and the skirt of Evilshaw . 



 CHAPTER II . of birdalone and her fellowship , their faring over the 
 downland 



 thus ride they along and loiter not , though they talk blithely 
 together ; and Birdalone wonder to herself that she might so much as 
 hold up her head for bitter thought of the day and the longing but 
 late pass away , but so it be , that it be only now and again that 
 they sting she into despair and silence , and for the most part she 
 hearken to the talk of the old man and the lad about the day of 
 Greenford and the alarm of lifting and unpeace , and the way of the 
 chapman and the craftsman . 

 an hour after noon they rest in a little dale of the downland where 
 be a pool and three thorn - bush thereby ; and when they have light 
 down , the old man kneel before Birdalone and take her hand , and swore 
 himself her man to do she will , whatso it be ; and then he stand up 
 and bade his son do likewise ; so they two knelt before she in turn , 
 somewhat shy and abash , for all that they be such stout , bold 
 fellow , and find it hard to take her hand , and then when they have 
 it in theirs , hard to let it go again . 

 a score of mile and five they ride that day , and have no roof over 
 they at night save the naked heaven , but to Birdalone that be but 
 little scathe : they make a shift to have some fire by they , and the 
 three man sit long about it that even while Birdalone tell they 
 somewhat of her life ; and as she tell of the House under the Wood and 
 the Great Water , Gerard have some inkling of whereabouts it be ; but 
 be nought so sure , because , as abovesaid in this tale , seldom do 
 any from the world of man venture in Evilshaw , or know of the Great 
 Water from its bank that give unto the forest . 

 in like wise they ride the next day , and come at eventide to a thorp 
 in a fair little dale of the downland , and there they gueste with 
 the shepherd - folk , who wonder much at the beauty of Birdalone , so 
 that at first they scarce durst venture to draw nigh unto she until 
 Gerard and his son have have some familiar converse with they ; then 
 indeed they exceed in kindness toward they , in their rough upland 
 fashion , but ever find it hard to keep their eye off Birdalone , and 
 that the more after they have hear the full sweetness of her voice ; 
 whereas she sing to they certain song which she have learn in the 
 Castle of the Quest , though it make her heart sore ; but she deem 
 she must need pay that kindly folk for their guestful and blithe 
 way . and thereafter they sing to the pipe and the harp their own 
 downland song ; and this she find strange , that whereas her eye 
 be dry when she be sing the song of love of the knighthood , 
 the wildness of the shepherd - music draw the tear from she , would 
 she , would she not . Homelike and dear seem the green willowy dale 
 to she , and in the night ere she sleep , and she lie quiet amidst of 
 the peaceful people , she could not choose but weep again , for pity of 
 the bitter - sweet of her own love , and for pity of the wide world 
 withal , and all the way of its many folk that lie so new before she . 



 CHAPTER III . they come to the city of the five CRAFTS , and birdalone 
 MEETS with the POOR - WIFE 



 they make not so much way that they come to the five craft on the 
 fourth day , but lie under the bare heaven in a dale below the big 
 swell of the downland , whereof Gerard spake . but betime in the 
 morning Birdalone arise and stir up her man , and they gat to 
 horse , and ride the hill before they till they come on to the crest 
 thereof . then Birdalone cry aloud with joy to see the lovely land 
 before she , and the white wall and the tower of the great city , 
 whereas Greenford be but small beside it . 

 so they ride down into the frank , and enter the gate of the city a 
 little after noon , and again be birdalone in all amaze at the going 
 to and fro in the street and the thronging of the market , and the 
 diver folk , as chapman and man - at - arm , and craftsman and lord , who 
 use the said city ; and to say sooth , somewhat her heart sink within 
 she , and it seem to she that it would be hard and troublous to have 
 to deal with so much folk , and that they must need go past she on 
 the right hand and the left without heed her life . 

 Howsoever , Gerard , who know the city , bring she to a fair hostel , 
 where she be well lodge , she and her man . Straightway , then , 
 before she go out into the street again , she fall to get 
 together what she have of fine broider work and of fair script , and 
 to finish what she have unfinishe . and she send forth Gerard and 
 his son to find out where be the market for such good , and if she 
 would have leave to sell the same therein , or anywhere in the town ; 
 and Gerard find the hall of the embroiderer , and therein the master 
 of the craft , and he receive the carle courteously when he hear 
 that there be fine work come to town , and do he to wit that none 
 in any such craft might have freedom of the market save by leave of 
 the guild of the craft ; but , say he , the guild be open - handed and 
 courteous , and be nowise will not to refuse the said leave , be the 
 work good and true ; and he bid Gerard withal tell his mistress that 
 she be good to bring sampling of her work to the Guildhall so soon 
 as she might . so the very next day go Birdalone thither , and find 
 the master a well - look tall man of some five - and - forty winter , who 
 look on she from the first as if he deem it be no ill way of 
 wear the time . to this man she show her work , and though he 
 find it not easy to take his eye off Birdalone herself , yet when he 
 look at her handiwork , he find it well than very good , and he 
 say to she : Damsel , here be what will be seek for at a great 
 price by the great lord and lady of the land , and the rich 
 burgess , and especially by the high prelate ; and so much of it as 
 thou hast a mind to do be so much coin gold unto thee ; and now I 
 see thee what thou art , I be fain that thou gather good to thee . 
 but as diligent as thou mayst be , thou hast but one pair of hand , 
 wonderful soothly , and yet but one pair . he break off at that word , 
 for he be verily star at her hand , and long to see more of 
 her arm than the wrist only , so that he scarce know what he be 
 say . then he turn red and say : soothly I wot that no other 
 hand save thine may do such needlework , or make the draught for 
 they . but thou wilt need woman - servant to help thee , both in 
 dighte the house for thee   for this big old carle here will be 
 scarce meet thereto   and as apprentice to help thee about the work 
 itself ; and if thou wilt , I shall seek the good one out for thee . 
 moreover I must tell thee , that though I know for sure how that no 
 woman in the world may work such needlework as thine , yet while 
 there cometh hither a woman of middle age , a woman wear by trouble , 
 pious , meek , and kind ; and by St. Lucia ! now I look on thee again , 
 she might be somewhat like unto thee , be she young and fresh- 
 look and strong as thou art . now this woman I say , and thereat I 
 marvel , doeth needlework that be somewhat after the manner of thine , 
 and which seem to we excellent till I have see thine . good 
 livelihood she earneth thereby , and be diligent therein ; but she hath 
 no heart to get apprentice , or be make one of our guild , both of 
 which be lawful to she as to thee , lovely damsel . but now I shall 
 counsel she to be make of our guild along with thee , if thou wilt 
 have it so , and then may ye both have three apprentice each , and may 
 make in our city a goodly school , so that our guild shall be 
 glorify thereby , for there will be none such work in the world . 
 how sayest thou ? 

 she thank he much , and yeasaid he , and think in her heart that 
 such work which would keep her hand and her head both busy , would 
 solace the grief of her heart , and wear away the time , that she might 
 live till hope might peradventure arise in she . 

 then say the master : there be one thing else , that is , thy 
 dwelling - place ; and if thou wilt I shall hire thee a house in the 
 street of the Broiderers , a goodly one : sooth to say , that same be 
 mine own , so thou mayst deem that I tell thee hereof to mine own 
 gain ; and that may be   and he redden therewith ; but there be this 
 in it , that if thou lack money I shall let thee live therein 
 without price till thou shalt have earn more than enough to pay I . 

 birdalone thank he well , but she do he to wit that she be 
 nowise penniless ; and presently she depart well pleased , though she 
 deem that the say master be well - nigh more friendly than might be 
 look for . and the next day he come to she in the hostelry , and 
 without more ado bring she to the house in the street of the 
 Broiderers , and she find it fair and well plenishe , and so she fall 
 to work to get all thing ready . 

 now the next week be the day appoint when she should be receive 
 into the broiderer ' guild , and the day before come the master 
 aforesaid to see Birdalone . sooth to say , he have not fail to come 
 to see she every day , on one pretence or another , since the first day 
 they have meet , but ever he do to she with all honour and simply . but 
 on this day he bring with he the woman skilful of her hand , to 
 show she unto Birdalone , who receive she gladly , and thereafter 
 Master Jacobus leave they alone together . 

 the say woman look worn and aged indeed , but be not of more than 
 five - and - forty winter even by seem , after the first look at she ; 
 she be somewhat tall and well - knit , her face well shapen , and her 
 hair yet goodly . there be a kind look in the eye of she , as if she 
 might love anyone with whom she live that would be kind to she . 
 Meek , or rather over - meek , of mien she be , and it seem of she that 
 she have be sore scared and oppress one while or another . 

 so when Master Jacobus be go , Birdalone set she down on the settle 
 beside she , and spake to her full sweetly and kindly , and the woman 
 spake little in turn save answer simply to her question . 
 birdalone ask where she be kinne , and she answer : in Utterhay . 
 then say birdalone : within these last few day I have hear that 
 town name twice or thrice , and never before , as meseemeth ; and yet , 
 hear the name from thy mouth , it seemeth to stir something in I , 
 as if I have be there one time and long to be there again . be 
 there aught in the place whereof folk tell wide about , so that I 
 might have hear it tell of and not note it at the time ? Nay , lady , 
 say the dame , save perchance that it be on the verge of a very great 
 and very evil wood , otherwise it be once a merry town and of much 
 resort from the country - side . 

 birdalone look on she , and see that the tear be come from her 
 eye and run down her cheek as she spake ; so she say to she : 
 why dost thou weep , mother ? be there aught I may do to assuage thy 
 grief ? say the dame : thou art so kind to I , and thy voice be so 
 dear and sweet , that I can not choose but weep . Meseems it be because 
 love of thee hath take mine heart , and therewith be blend memory 
 of past sorrow of mine . Thou askest I if thou mayest do aught to 
 assuage my grief ; dear lady , I be not grieve now , that have go by ; 
 nay , now I be more than not grieve , I be make happy , because I be 
 with thee . but since thou art so debonair with I , I will ask thee 
 to do somewhat for I ; and that be , to tell I of thy life go by ; I 
 mean , sweet young damsel , of thy life when thou wert a little child . 

 then Birdalone kiss she and say : it go to my heart that thou 
 lov I ; for soon as I set eye on thee my heart go out to thee ; 
 and now belike we shall be dear friend ; and that be a thing that 
 shall avail I much , to have a friend who be so much old than I , so 
 that nought can come between we , of the love of man and other grief . 
 Yea , now , say the dame , smile somewhat sadly ; now do I see the 
 water stand in thine eye , and thy voice quaver . be it so , thou 
 lovely kind damsel , that thou hast be grieve by love of a man ? 
 who then may prevail in love if thou prevail not ? and she fall to 
 fondle Birdalone 's hand ; but birdalone say : it be over - long to 
 tell of all my life , mother , though I be so young ; but now I will do 
 as thou badest I , and tell thee somewhat of my day when I be 
 little . 

 and therewith she fall to tell she of her day in the House under 
 the Wood , and the witch and her surliness and grimness , and of her 
 love of the wild thing , and how she wax there . and she spake a 
 long while , for the memory of those day seem to lead she along , as 
 though she verily be alive now in they ; and the woman sit before 
 she , gaze on her lovingly , till Birdalone stay her tale at last 
 and say : now have I tell thee more than enough of a simple matter , 
 and a life that be as that of a wild creature of the wood . now 
 shalt thou , mother , tell I somewhat of thee , and what be thy grief 
 of Utterhay : for thou shalt find that the telling thereof shall 
 solace thee . ah ! so think young folk , say the woman sadly , because 
 there be many day leave for they to hope in . but though the telling 
 of my sorrow be a fresh sorrow to I , yet shalt thou hear it . it be
