 but of the loss of my babe ; but she be of all babe the fairest and 
 the sweetest . 

 then she fall to tell Birdalone all that concern the witch at 
 Utterhay and the poor - wife that ye have hear in the beginning of 
 this book , until the time when she leave the house to buy meat for the 
 witch ; for she herself be the said poor - wife . and then she tell how 
 she come back again and find her guest go and the child withal ; 
 and though she have weep for love of Birdalone , she weep not at 
 telling of this grief , but tell it as a tale which have befall some 
 other one . and she say : and so when I have do run up and down 
 like a wild thing , and ask of the neighbour with lack of breath 
 and fierceness of speech who have take my child away from I ; and 
 when I have go up to the wood and even some way into it , and when I 
 have wander up and down again , and night be fall , I come back at 
 last again to my poor house so weary with my woe , that I scarce know 
 what have befall I . and there upon the board lie the victual and 
 drink which I have bring , and the money which the witch have give 
 unto I ; and despite of grief , hunger flame up in I at the sight , 
 and I throw myself on to it and eat and drank , and so come to myself , 
 that be , to my grief . but the next day I run about hither and 
 thither , and weary folk with my asking and my woe ; but it be all 
 of none avail . the child be go away from I . there be little 
 more to tell of I , sweet lady . if I be to live , need must I take 
 the poor price of my little one , to wit , the witch 's money , and deal 
 with folk for my livelihood ; wherefore I buy I cloth and silk , 
 have now the wherewithal , and set to work on broidery , for even 
 then be I a cunning needle - woman . so be God and the saint good 
 to I , and incline the folk to I , that they be good and piteous , 
 and I lack not work nor due livelihood ; but after a while I weary 
 of Utterhay , where my dear child should have be run about 
 before my foot ; and have by this time get a little money 
 together , and be exceed deft in my craft , I come on hither to 
 live , and , praise be to St. Ursula ! I have find it easy to live : 
 and praise be to all - hallows withal that I have find thee , who art 
 so kind and lovely ; and thou by seem of the very age my child 
 should be if she be live : or how old art thou , dear lady ? 

 birdalone lay her hand on her breast , and she be turn pale , but 
 she say in a low voice : I deem that I be of twenty summer . 

 then they both sit silent , till Birdalone might master the fluttering 
 of her heart , and she say : now meseem I have a memory even early 
 than those I tell thee erst . a woman take I out of a basket and set 
 I on the back of an ass , and I look about , and I be in a grassy 
 lawn of the wood ; and I see a squirrel run up a tree - trunk before 
 I , and wind round the tree and hide he ; and then I stretch out my 
 hand and cry out to he ; and then come the woman unto I , and give 
 I wood - strawberry to eat out of her hand . 

 Brake out the poor - wife thereat , pale and trembling : tell I now , my 
 child , hast thou any memory of what the woman be who set thee on the 
 ass and give thee the strawberry ? birdalone look on she , and 
 scan her face closely , and then shake her head , and say : nay , it 
 be not thou , mother . Nay , surely ; nay , surely , say the woman ; but 
 think again . Said Birdalone , speak slowly : be it my mistress 
 then ? she be a tall woman , somewhat thin and bony , with goodly red 
 hair and white - skin , but thin - lipped . Quoth the poor - wife : no , 
 no ; it be of no use ; nought such be she . then Birdalone look up 
 and say eagerly : yea , but it be her other shape belike : therein 
 be she a tall woman , dark - haired , hook - nosed , and hawk - eye , as if 
 of thirty summer ; a stark woman . Hast thou see such ? dost thou 
 remember she ? 

 the woman spring up and cry out , and be like to have fall , but 
 birdalone arise and hold she in her arm and comfort she , and set 
 she in her seat again and knelt before she ; and presently the poor- 
 wife come to herself and say : my child , thou sayest do I remember 
 she ; how shall I ever forget she ? she be the thief who steal my 
 child . 

 Therewith she slide from off her seat , and kneel by Birdalone , and 
 stoop low down on the floor as if the tall maiden be but a little 
 one , and she fall to kiss she and pat she , her face and her 
 hand , and all about ; and say , sob and yet smile : suffer I a 
 little , my child , mine own lovely child ! for in good sooth I be thy 
 mother , and it be long since I have see thee : but hearken , when I 
 come quite to myself I shall pray thee not to leave I yet awhile , 
 and I shall pray thee to love I . 

 birdalone clip and kiss she , and say : I love thee dearly , and 
 never , never shall I leave thee . 

 then they stand up , and the mother take Birdalone by the shoulder , 
 and hold she a little aloof , and devour she with her eye ; and she 
 say : yea , thou hast grow tall , and belike wilt grow no tall : 
 and how fair and lovely thou hast grow ; and thou that wert bear in a 
 poor man 's house ! no wonder that any should covet thee . and I , I 
 wonder if ever I be as fair as thou art ; forsooth many call I 
 fair for a little while ; and now behold I ! Nay , child and darling , 
 let not thy face grow downcast , for now shall I know nought more of 
 fear and grief ; and be it not like that I shall grow fair of flesh , 
 and shapely , in the happy day we shall dwell together ? and 
 therewith she take she to her arm , and it seem as if she might 
 never have enough of clip and embrace she ; and she would look 
 at Birdalone 's hand and her foot and her arm , and stroke they and 
 caress they ; and she wonder at her body , as if she have be a young 
 mother eat up with the love of her firstborn . and as for 
 Birdalone , she be as glad of her mother as might be ; and yet in her 
 heart she wonder if perchance one of the fellowship might stray 
 that way , and be partaker in her joy of this newfound dear friend ; 
 and she say , might it be Viridis ; but in her inmost heart , though 
 she tell it not to herself , she long that the Black Squire might 
 find she out at last . 



 CHAPTER IV . of the love of GERARD 's son and of jacobu for 
 birdalone 



 now dwelt Birdalone in rest and peace when she have be take into 
 the guild along with her mother , and they have take the due 
 apprentice to they ; and they begin to gather much of good to they , 
 for of fine broidery there be little do in the five craft , and 
 none at all that could be put beside their work , either for beauty of 
 the draught of it , or for skill of handiwork . she declare unto all 
 folk how that the poor - wife   who have to name Audrey   be her very 
 mother , from whom she have be steal in her young day ; but she 
 tell none any tale of how she be steal . and the twain dwelt 
 together in the great loving - kindness ; and it be with Audrey as 
 she have forecast , that now her day be happy , and she live in all 
 ease and content , that the goodliness of her youth come back to she , 
 and she become a fair woman as for her year ; and therewith it grow 
 to be clear that the two be so much alike one to the other , that 
 all might see that they be mother and daughter . 

 Gerard and his two son she maintain yet as her man ; and not only 
 be they of much use to she in fetching and carry , but also true 
 it be that her beauty be so manifest , that she while need a stout 
 lad weapone at she back when she be in the street or amidst the 
 throng of the market ; and many be they , and while of the high , 
 who crave love of she , some with honour , and some with lack of it . 

 of these , forsooth , be but two that anywise trouble she ; and the 
 most trouble be this , that she might not fail to see that the love 
 of she have enter into the heart of the two Gerardsons , Robert and 
 gile ; so that time be when she deem she must even send they 
 away , but when it come to the point she have not the heart thereto ; 
 though none other remedy there seem , so sorely as their soul be 
 wound by long for she . it be not to be say that they ever 
 spake to she thereof , or wittingly weary she with sign of love ; 
 but they could not so easily cover it up but that it be ever before 
 her eye . but she suffer it all for friendship 's sake and for 
 their true service , and in all friendliness do what she might to 
 solace their grief . Forsooth so good and true she find that father- 
 kind , and the young man so goodly and kind , that she say to herself , 
 have she not another man lie in her heart , she might well have 
 choose one of those twain for her very speech - friend and true lover . 

 the second wooer that trouble she be the master , Jacobus , who , when 
 but three month be wear of her dwelling in her house , do all 
 openly crave her love and offer her marriage , he be a man 
 unwedded . Sore be her heart that she must need gainsay he , so 
 kind and courteous as he have be to she at their first come 
 together ; though this indeed be sooth , that straightway , so soon as 
 he see she , he fall into the captivity of her love . Howsoever , 
 gainsay he she need must , and he take the naysay so hardly that he 
 be scarce like a man before she , and weep and pray and lament 
 many time over , till she weary of it , and well - nigh fall to 
 loathe he . so that it come to this at last , that one day she 
 spake to he and say that she might no long bear it , but must seek 
 another house and leave his . there then be the to - do , for he fall 
 on his knee before she , and kiss her foot , would she , would she 
 not , and cry out in his grief , till at last for pure weariness of 
 his folly she give way unto he , and say that she would still abide 
 there ; whereon he rise up from she and go away with all the grief 
 run off he for that time , and as glad a man to look on as you might 
 see on a summer 's day . 

 but the next morning he come unto she again , and she think all be 
 begin afresh , make he no glad countenance ; but he stand up before 
 she and spake friendly , and say how that she be in the right of it , 
 and that if they both dwelt in one house together they be like to 
 have but a weary time of it , both she and he . but , say he , I will 
 not that thou shouldst depart out of this house , for a goodly one it 
 be , and full meet for thee ; it be for I to depart , and not for thee . 
 I tell thee , forsooth , that I have from the first mean this house as 
 a gift from I to thee . and therewith he draw from his pouch a 
 scroll , which be a deed of gift of the said house , duly seal and 
 attest , and he give it into her hand ; but she be sore moved 
 thereat , and at the demeanour of he that morning , and she let the 
 scroll fall to the floor and weep for pity of he , and reach out 
 both her hand , and he kiss they , and then her lip also , and 
 sithence he sit down beside she . but she say : alas ! that thou wilt 
 give I what I may not take , and wouldst have of I what I may not 
 give . 

 but now he wax hotter , and say : this once I command thee to do my 
 will , and take my gift . it will be nought to my gain if thou take it 
 not ; for I may not live in this house when thou art go from it ; and 
 I swear by all - hallow that I will not let any have it to hire , nor 
 will I sell it , since thou hast make it holy by dwell therein . 

 yet be she sore move by his generous fashion , and she say : I will 
 take thy gift then , and live here in honour of thee and thy 
 friendship ; for well I wot thou hadst no mind to buy I with thy 
 gift . 

 so she spake , and he stand up stark and stern , and so depart , and 
 kiss she not again ; though meseem she would have suffer he have 
 he offer it . Nay , belike have he at that moment press his wooing 
 somewhat masterfully , it be not so sure but she might have yeasaid 
 it , and suffer he to we d she and lead she to bed ; though it would 
 have go ill both with he and with she thereafter . 

 Thenceforth dwelt Birdalone with her mother and her maiden and her 
 man in that house , and it become famous in the five craft because of 
 her beauty and her wisdom , which minishe not , but wax day by day ; 
 but therewithal as the time wear , wax her longing and sadness . but 
 all this she hide in her own heart , and be debonair to all about she , 
 and so good to poor folk that none have a word save of blessing on her 
 beauty and her wisdom . 



 CHAPTER V. of the death of AUDREY , mother to birdalone . SHE be 
 warn in a dream to seek the black SQUIRE , and be MINDED to DEPART 
 the city of the five CRAFTS , and seek again the castle of the quest 



 thus dwelt birdalone in the five craft in such rest and peace as her 
 heart would let she ; and dear and good friend she have about she ; her 
 mother first , whose love and desire for love of she make all thing 
 soft and dear unto she . Gerard and the Gerardsons be next , who 
 be ever faithful and true unto she , and deft both of hand and of 
 mind , so that they work many thing for her avail . then come the 
 master , Jacobus , who hold himself unwedded for her sake , and though 
 he no long dwelt in the same house with she , might scarce endure to 
 miss the sight of she for two day run : a dear friend she deem 
 he , as forsooth he be , though while he torment and weary she , 
 and belike have weary she more , but for the sorrow which lie on her 
 own heart , whereof it come that she might not think of any man as of 
 one who might be a lover , and so feel safe even with so kind a friend 
 and so stubborn in his love as be this one . moreover he never again 
 crave love of she in so many word , but only in his going and 
 coming so do , that it be clear how he have she , and the love of 
 she , ever in his heart . 

 wear thus a five year ; and then come a sickness on the city , and 
 many die thereof ; and the say sickness enter into Birdalone 's 
 house , and slay Audrey her mother , but spar all else therein . 
 thereby at the first be Birdalone so overwhelmed that she might heed 
 nought , neither her craft nor her friend , nor the day to come on 
 the earth for she . and moreover when she come more to herself , which 
 be not for many day , and ask why her friend Jacobus have not be 
 to see she the last day , she be tell that he also be dead of the 
 pestilence ; and she sorrow for he sorely , for she love he much , 
 though not in the way he would . 

 and now do the city and land of the five craft begin to look 
 unfriendly to Birdalone , and she fall to think that she must need 
 depart thence , as she well might do , whereas she have foison of good : 
 and at first it be in her mind to go with Gerard and his son unto 
 Utterhay ; but then she deem the thought of her mother , and how she 
 would be ever think of the loss and the gain , and the loss once 
 more stand in the way ; and she turn one thing and another over in 
 her mind , and might not face it . 

 on a night , as she sleep , come to her dream of her day in the House 
 under the wood   as very seldom betid , and the witch - wife be 
 speak to she in friendly fashion   as for she   and blame she for 
 flee away , and be taunt she with the failure of her love , and

 Squire , and what a loss she have of he ; and Birdalone be hearken

 neither fearful nor irksome , and forsooth nought save a mouthpiece 
 for word that both grieve Birdalone and yet be an eager pleasure 
 unto she . but in the midst thereof , and ere the dream have time to 
 change , Birdalone awake , and it be an early morning of later spring , 
 and the sky be clear blue and the sun shin bright , and the bird 
 singing in the garden of the house , and in the street be the sound 
 of the early market - folk pass through the street with their ware ; 
 and all be fresh and lovely . 

 she awake sobbing , and the pillow be wet with her tear , and yet she 
 feel as if something strange and joyous be go to betide she , and 
 for joy of the love of life the heart beat fast in her bosom . 

 she arise all darle naked as she be , and go to the window and 
 look out on the beauty of the spring , while the sound of the 
 market - wain bring to her mind the thought of the mead , and the 
 stream of the river , and the woodside beyond the city ; and she fall 
 a - longing for they , as a while she kneel on the window - seat , half 
 dream and asleep again , till the sun come round that way , and its 
 beam fall upon her bosom and her arm ; and she stand up and look 
 on the fairness of her body , and a great desire take hold of her 
 heart that it might be love as it deserve by he whom she desire . 
 and thus she stand there till she become ashamed , and hasten to do 
 on her raiment ; but even as she be about it , it come upon she that 
 what she have will to do be to seek to the Castle of the Quest , and 
 find out where be her love if there he be not , and so to seek he 
 the world over till she find he . and such a flood of joy possess 
 she when she think this , and so eager to begone she be , that she 
 deem every minute waste till she be on the road . 

 nevertheless , in a while , when her mind be steady , she know that 
 she have somewhat to do ere she might be go , and that here , as oft , 
 it would be more haste less speed . 

 so she abode a little , and then come into her hall duly dight , and 
 find Gerard and his son there to serve she ; and she brake she fast ,

 they of this and that , and Gerard answer lightly again ; but the two 
 gerardson look at one another , as though they would speak and ask 
 a question from time to time , but forbore because they durst not . 
 but Gerard look on they , and deem he wotte what be in their 
 mind ; so at last he spake : our lady , both I , and meseemeth my son 
 also , deem that there be some tiding toward which be great unto 
 thee ; for thine eye sparkle , and the red burn in thy cheek , and 
 thine hand may not be quiet , nor thy foot abide in one place ; 
 wherefore I see that thou hast something in thy mind which strive to 
 be forth of it . now thou wilt pardon we , our dear lady , that we ask 
 concern this , because it be in our love for thee that we speak , 
 lest there be some change toward which shall be a grief to some of 
 we . 

 my man , say Birdalone , flush red , sooth it be that there be a 
 change at hand , and I shall tell you straightway what it be . year 
 ago I tell you that I be flee from my friend ; now the change 
 hath betid that I would seek they again ; and need must I leave the 
 five craft behind to do so . and moreover there be this ill word to 
 be say , which I will say at once , to wit , that when I be but a 
 little way go from the five craft I must wend the other deal of my 
 journey birdalone , as my name be . 

 all those three sit silent and aghast at that word , and the young man 
 grow pale ; but after a while spake Gerard : our lady most well- 
 beloved , this word which thou hast speak , to wit , that thou needest 
 we no long , I have look to hear any time this five year ; and 
 praise be to the saint that it hath come late and not soon . now 
 there be no more to be say but that thou tell we what be thy will 
 that we should do . 

 birdalone hang her head awhile for sorrow of sunder from these 
 man ; then she look up and say : it seemeth , my friend , as if ye 
 deem I have do you a wrong in sunder our fellowship ; but all I 
 may say hereon be to pray you to pardon I , that I need must go 
 alone on my quest . and now what I would have you do , be first of all 
 to fetch hither a notary and scrivener , that he may draw up a deed of 
 gift to you , Gerard and Gerardsons , of this house and all that be 
 therein , save what money I may need for my journey , and gift such 
 as I shall bid you to be give to my workwomen . Ye must need yeasay 
 this , or ye be forsworn of your behest to do my will . but 
 furthermore , I will have you to let the workwoman of mine   and the 
 head one ruling   to hire the aforesaid house , if so they will ; for 
 now be they skilled , and may well earn good livelihood by the work . 
 but the next work be simple ; it be to furnish for I the array of a 
 young man , with such armour as I may easily bear , to dight I for my 
 road . Forsooth ye wot that not unseldom do woman use the custom of 
 go array like man , when they would journey with hidden head ; and 
 ye may happen upon such gear as hath be make for such a woman 
 rather than any man ; but thou shalt get I also a short bow and a 
 quiver of arrow , for verily these be my proper weapon that I can 
 deal with deftly . now my last command be that , when all be do , 
 maybe to - morrow , or maybe the next day , ye bring I out of the city 
 and the frank of the Five craft , and bring I somewhat on my way 
 over the down , for loth be I to part from you ere need must . then 
 they knelt before she and kiss her hand , and they be full of 
 grief ; but they see that so it have to be . 

 thereafter Gerard spake with his son apart , and in a while come to 
 Birdalone , and say : our lady , we will do your will in all wise ; but 
 we shall tell thee , that the five craft will look but strange to we 
 when thou art go , and that we have a mind to betake we to Utterhay 
 and the land of our kindre . wherefore we pray thee to give this 
 house that hath be so dear to we unto thy workwoman and her mate ; 
 for we need it not , nor the hire thereof , but shall do well enough 
 with what money or good thou mayst give we . be this accord to thy 
 will , or have I speak rashly ? 

 she say : Ye be good and ungreedy , and I bless you for it ; be it as 
 ye will ; and this the more , as I be fain that ye go to Utterhay ; 
 for while I have deem that I myself be draw thitherward , 
 wherefore it may be that we shall meet again in that place . 

 and when she have so speak , she might not refrain her tear ; and the 
 Gerardsons turn away , for they be ashamed , both that they should 
 see her weep , or she they . but at last she call to they and say : 
 now make we the speedy end we may of this , for sorry work be the 
 tarrying of farewell ; so I pray you , my friend , to go about the work 
 I have bid you . 

 so all be do as she would , and the day after the morrow be 
 birdalone abide the coming of Gerard and his son with the horse ; 
 and despite of the sundering of friend and the peril that belike 
 lie before she , the world seem fair to she , and life begin 
 anew . and she make no doubt that she would soon be at the Castle of 
 the Quest , and there find all thing much as she have leave they ; and 
 there at least would be the welcome of her dear friend Viridis . 



 CHAPTER VI . of the sundering of birdalone from GERARD and his son 



 presently be the horse come with Gerard and his son , and 
 birdalone gat to horse amongst they . she be arm in a light 
 hauberk , and over it a long and loose surcoat that come down beneath 
 the knee of she ; and a sallet she have upon her head , wide but light , 
 so that not very much of her face be to be see . she have make up 
 her mind to this tale upon the road , when she be among folk , that 
 she be under a vow not to do off her helm for a seven day ' space . 
 Withal she have cover up the lovely shapeliness of her leg with 
 long boot of deer - leather , and her surcoat be wide - sleeve ; she be 
 well hide , and whereas she be a tall and strong woman , she might 
 well pass for a young man , slender and fair - faced . she be girt with 
 a good sword , and Gerard have get she a strong horseman 's bow and a 
 quiver full of arrow , wherewith , as aforesaid , she know well how to 
 deal ; wherefore she be by no mean without defence . 

 so they go their way through the street and out - a - gate ; and it 
 must be say , that be not Birdalone 's thought turn toward the 
 Castle of the Quest , and what she should meet there , her heart have 
 be somewhat sore at leave the city which have cherish she so 
 well these year past ; nay , as it be , the shadow of the southern 
 gate , as she past thereunder , smite somewhat cold upon she , and she 
 silently bade farewell to the City of the five Crafts with some 
 sorrow , though with no fear . 

 forth they ride then through the frank and up on to the shepherd 
 country , and whereas their horse be of the good , and they have no 
 sumpt - beast with they till they come to Upham , where they must 
 need have victual , they make but five day of it to the place where 
 the road turn aside from the country of Mostwyke . there then they 
 draw rein , and Birdalone light down from her horse , and they all , 
 and they lie upon the grass and eat and drink together . 

 but when they be do , spake Birdalone and say : dear friend , 
 this be the hour and the place when we must need part ; for ye shall 
 go back again to five craft , and do what I have bid of you , and 
 do your will , and wend your way with your livelihood unto Utterhay . 
 but as for I , I must go my way first unto Greenford , and thence to 
 seek my friend from whom erst I be flee when ye first become my 
 friend . now perchance ye will say that I have take you up in my 
 need , and cast you aside at my pleasure ; but I may only say that 
 there be at present two deal of my life , and of one of they have ye 
 be partaker , and of the other ye may not be . Forsooth that be a 
 grief unto I , as I suppose unto you be it a great one . but unto 
 I also be it heavy but that my heart tell I it shall not ever 
 be so ; for as I say to you some day agone , I have a hope that we 
 shall yet meet again , be it in Utterhay or in some other place . and 
 now I pray you to pardon I wherein I may have do amiss unto you , 
 and begrudge it not that there be other , who indeed be first- 
 comer in regard to you , and whom I love well than you ; for of your 
 truth and your good - will and loving - kindness will I bear witness 
 wheresoever I may be . 

 then spake Gerard : do ye speak , my son ; for I have no grudge 
 against she , nor aught to bewail I as to she , save , it may be , that 
 I be now so well on in year that it may well befall that I shall not 
 live till the time of the meeting in Utterhay . but I will pray thee 
 this , dear lady , that if thou come to the place where I lie dead thou 
 wilt kiss my burial - stone , and sing due mass for I . Nay , she 
 say , but this be the bad shall betide betwixt we . 

 then spake Robert Gerardson : I be not deft of speech , but this 
 parting make I bold to say this : that from the time when first I 
 set eye on thee I have love thee in such wise that never mayst thou 
 love I as much as I love thee , if thou hast anywhere , as I deem thou 
 hast , a lover of thy body , whom thou lovest . now I have see that 
 for a long while thou hast know this , and hast ever because of it 
 be as meek and kind with I as thou mightest be . and this hath 
 partly grieve I the more , because it hath eke my longing for thee ; 
 and yet it hath comfort I the more , because it hath make I deem 
 well of thee , and deem thee worthy of worship and holy ; 
 therefore have thou all my blessing for it . and now I know that thou 
 sunderest from we that thou mayst go seek thy very bodily lover ; and 
 I say , that if the sundering have be for any light cause , grieve 
 at heart should I have be ; but since it be even so , once more I 
 bless thee , and ever shall I be happy in the thought of thee ; and if 
 ever we meet again , still shalt thou find I as now I be in heart and 
 in soul . 

 she turn to he , not dry - eye , and say : I know that what thou 
 sayest be sooth ; and thou hast guess right as to my going ; and I 
 take thy blessing with love and joy . 

 then be they silent ; but Giles Gerardson be struggle with word , 
 for he be slow to speech ; at last he say : I say much as saith my 
 brother : but see thou , our lady , how ill it have go if thou hadst 
 love one of we with an equal love ; woe worth the strife then ! but 
 now I will crave this of thee , that thou kiss I on the lip , now 
 whena we part ; and again , that thou wilt do as much when first we 
 meet again hereafter . and I tell thee right out , that if thou 
 gainsay this , I shall deem it unfriendly in thee , and that those 
 lovely word which thou didst speak e'en now be but word alone , 
 and that thou art not as true as I have deem thee . 

 she laugh amidst her tear , and say : dear lad , doom I not till I 
 have be find guilty ! I shall nowise naysay thee this , for I love 
 thee , and now and ever shalt thou be unto I as a brother , thou and 
 Robert also ; for even so have ye do by I . but thou wottest , dear 
 lad , that while and again must sister sunder from brother , and even 
 so it have to be now . 

 then they sit silent all four ; and thereafter Birdalone arise and do 
 off her sallet , and kiss and embrace Gerard and his son , and bade 
 they farewell , and she and the young man weep . then she arm 
 herself and gat to horse , and go her way towards Greenford , have 
 nought with she but the raiment and arm that her body bore , and her 
 horse , and some gold piece and gem in a little pouch . so ride she ; 
 and the other turn back sadly toward the five Crafts . 



 CHAPTER VII . BIRDALONE COMETH to GREENFORD , and hear of the wasting 
 of the CASTLE of the quest 



 now come birdalone rid into Greenford an hour before sunset on a 
 day of the latter end of May ; and she have no doubt but to go straight 
 to the hostelry , and that the less as she have not abide there 
 before , as hath be tell . to they that serve she she tell the tale 
 of her vow , that she might not do off her sallet that seven day ; and 
 some trowe she , and some deem she a woman , but whereas she seem 
 by her raiment to be of condition none meddle with she . moreover , 
 as she tell her intent to ride on betime in the morning , it matter 
 the less unto they : withal she give out that she come from foreign 
 part , as sooth it be . 

 in the evening she sit in the hall , and with she be three chapman 
 travel with their ware , and two good man of the town sitting ; 
 and they be talk together , and be courteous and blithe , and 
 amidst their talk they throw many a glance at the slim and fair young 
 squire , as Birdalone seem , and be fain to speak unto he , but 
 refrain they for courtesy 's sake . for her part , Birdalone long 
 sore to ask they somewhat of the Castle of the Quest , but the word 
 clave to her throat for very fear ; and she sit restless and ill at 
 ease . however at last say a townsman to a chapman : art thou for 
 the Red Hold , Master Peter , when thou art do here ? birdalone 
 turn very pale at that word ; and Master Peter spake : Yea , surely , 
 neighbour , if the folk leave aught in my pack for other to buy . he 
 spake in a jovial voice , as if he be merry , and the other all 
 laugh together , as though they be well pleased and in good 
 contentment . and now , deem Birdalone , would be her time to speak 
 if she would learn aught ; so she constrain herself at last , and 
 spake , though in a quavering voice : meseem then , master , this good 
 town be thrive as now ? this I ask because I be a stranger in these 
 part this long while , and now I be come back hither fain be I to 
 find the land in good peace ; for I may chance to take up my abode 
 hereby . 

 the goodman turn to she and smile kindly when they hear the 
 sweetness of her voice ; and one of they say : Sir of the sallet , ye 
 shall be content with the peace in this land , and the thriving of its 
 folk ; the very villein hereabout live as well as franklin in most 
 land , and the yeoman and vavassour be clothe as if they be knight 
 of a good lord 's household . Forsooth their house be both goodly 
 and easy to enter ; and well be that , whereas there lack never good 
 meat and drink on the board therein . and moreover their woman be 
 for ever seek whatso be fair and goodly , whatso be far - fetched and 
 dear - buy , whereof we chapman also thrive , as thou mayst well deem . 
 ah ! it be a goodly land now ! 

 the other nod and smile . but birdalone spake , harden her 
 heart thereto for very need : belike then there be a change of day 
 here , for when I last know of the land there be little peace 
 therein . and that will not be so long agone , say a townsman , 
 smile , for I doubt we should see no grey hair in thine head if thy 
 sallet be off it . birdalone redden : it will be some five year 
 agone , say she . yea , yea , say the townsman , we be begin to 
 end the unpeace then , and it be the dark hour before the dawn ; 
 for five year agone we and the good knight of the Castle of the 
 Quest be lie before the wall of the Red Hold . Forsooth we 
 clear out that den of devil then and there . what betid unto it 
 after ye win it ? say Birdalone , and she tremble withal . say the 
 townsman : hear ye never of the Black Squire , a very valiant knight , 
 since thou sayest that thou hast know this country - side ? she bow 
 a yeasay , for this time she find it hard to speak . 

 well , say the townsman , we hold garrison in the Red Hold for some 
 three month , and thereafter we crave of he to come and be our 
 captain therein ; for , even after the Hold be win , there be yet a 
 sort of runagate that haunt the country - side , man who have no craft 
 save lifting and slay . and forsooth we know this Lord Arthur for 
 the keen and deftest of man - at - arm ; so he yeasaid our asking , and 
 do all he might herein , and forsooth that be all there be to do ; 
 for he be ever in the saddle , and at the work . Forsooth he be not 
 a merry man , save when he be at his busy ; and little he spake in 
 hall or chamber , else have he be well beloved . but at least by no 
 man well might the land have be serve . 

 there be silence a little , and Birdalone wax deadly pale ; then she 
 strove with herself and say : Thou sayest he be and he be ; be he 
 dead then ? say the townsman : not to our knowledge . when he have 
 bring the land into good peace , which be some three year and a 
 half agone , he go his way from the Red Hold all alone , and we see 
 he no more . but some folk deem that he hath enter into religion . 

 Birdalone 's heart sicken , and she think to herself that now all 
 be to begin again ; yet she feel that the bad be over since he be 
 not dead , and she be able to think what she should do . so she say : 
 mayhappen he hath go back to the Castle of the Quest ? Nay , nay , 
 say the townsman , that may not be ; for waste be that house now ; 
 there be none dwelleth there , save , it may be , now and again a 
 wandering carle or carline abideth there a day or two . say 
 birdalone : how hath that befall ? or where be go Sir Hugh , the 
 Green Knight ? say the townsman : we know the Green Knight well ; 
 frank and free and joyous be he ; all man love he ; and his lady and 
 speech - friend , none ever see a lovelier , and as kind as be he . but 
 we might not keep they with we ; they be go into their own country . 
 Sir Hugh leave the Castle of the Quest some three month after the 
 Black Squire come to we for captain , and he give over the castle to 
 Sir Geoffrey of Lea , an old and wise man of war . but not many month 
 thereafter we hear that he also have depart , leave it ungarnishe 
 of man ; and we deem that the cause thereof be that something uncouth 
 be see and hear therein , which folk may not endure . be it not so , 
 my master ? 

 they all yeasaid that , and the talk go on to other matter . as for 
 Birdalone , though her hope to come amongst friend be so utterly 
 overthrow , yet she see not what to do save to go her way to the 
 Castle of the Quest , and see if perchance she might find any tiding 
 there . and she say to herself , that if the bad come to the bad , 
 she would herself dwell there as an hermit of love ; or , maybe , to 
 face those uncouth thing and see if any tiding might be compel 
 out of they . 



 CHAPTER VIII . birdalone COMETH to the castle of the QUEST , heareth 
 the TALE THEREOF from LEONARD , and DEPARTETH THENCE by the SENDING 
 BOAT 



 she arise betime on the morrow , and be out of Greenford so soon as 
 the gate be open , and at first make all speed that she might 
 toward the Castle of the Quest ; and nothing hinder she , for the 
 land be verily in good peace , and she might have come there if she 
 would before sunset , for all whom she meet further she . but as the 
 day wan her courage wan with it , so that at last she stay some 
 six mile short of the house , and crave shelter at a yeoman 's stead 
 there , which be grant she with all kindness ; and they make much of 
 she , and she tell they her vow of the sallet , and they deem nought 
 save that she be a young man . 

 she depart early in the morning with their God - speed , and while the 
 day be yet young come into the meadow before the castle , and see 
 the tower thereof rise up before she : then she check her horse , 
 and ride on no fast than a foot 's pace ; yet as slow as she might 
 ride , need must she get to the gate while the day be yet young . 

 so come Birdalone by that bower wherein she have sleep that first 
 night she come to the castle ; and she rein up to look on it ; and as 
 she sit there gaze , come a man out from it clothe as a man of 
 religion ; and her heart beat quick , and she be like to fall from her 
 horse , for there come into her mind what the townsman have say , that 
 the Black Squire have go into religion . but the hermit come towards 
 she with a cup of water in his hand , and he cast his hood aback from 
 he , and she see at once that it be Leonard the priest , and though 
 it be not the friend whom she seek , yet be she glad that it be a 
 friend ; but he come and stand by she , and say : hail , wayfarer ! wilt 
 thou drink of our well and rest thee a while ? so she take the cup 
 and drink of the water , look kindly on he , while he wonder at 
 the beauty of her hand , and misdoubte he . then she give he back 
 the cup and light down off her horse , and take the sallet from her 
 head , and spake : I may not pass by a friend without a word ; think if 
 thou hast not see I before ? 

 then he know she , and might not refrain he , but cast his arm about 
 she and kiss she , weeping ; and she say : it be sweet to I to find 
 a friend after what I have be tell of yonder house . Yea , say he , 
 and art thou go up thither ? certe , say she , and why not ? say 
 he : they be go , and all go ! how and whither ? say she . but I 
 must full certainly go thither at once ; I will go afoot with thee ; do 
 thou tether my horse till thou comest back . 

 he say : but wilt not thou come back ? I know not , she say : I know 
 nought save that I would go thither ; let it be enough that I suffer 
 thee to go with I , and on the way thou shalt tell I what thou canst 
 of the tale . 

 then go Leonard and tether the horse , and they go together 
 afoot to the gate ; and Birdalone tell what she have hear of Arthur 
 and Hugh ; and Leonard say : this be true , and there be not much else 
 to be say . when the Black Squire come back from the leaguer of the 
 Red Hold , and have hear before of thy departure , he be heavy of mood 
 and few - speak , and wander about as one who might find no rest ; yet 
 be he not stern with Atra , who for her part be no less heavy- 
 hearte : soothly a sad company we be , and it be somewhat well 
 when my Lord Arthur go his way from we ; and indeed eager he be to 
 be go ; and it could be see of he that he be fain of the toil and 
 peril which they of Greenford offer he . then in some four month 
 spake my lord Hugh that he also would be go to a place where be 
 both a land and folk that would look friendly on he ; so he go with 
 my lady Viridis and my lady Aurea , and they have Atra also with they ; 
 and I also they would have have , but my heart fail I to leave the 
 place where I have be so glad and so sorry with thee ; death have be 
 well ; wherefore in yonder bower as in an hermitage I serve God and 
 abide my time . but though I wot nought of where be go the Black 
 Squire , I know whereto those four be go , and it be but a seven 
 day ' ride hence , and the land be goodly and peaceable , and if they 
 be not dead , most like they be there yet . how sayest thou then , thou 
 dearest and kind , wilt thou thither to they ? for if so , I may 
 well lead thee thither . 

 birdalone shake her head . Nay , she say , I deem that I be draw 
 elsewhither , but soon I shall tell thee . Lo now the gate . but ere 
 we enter , tell I of Sir Geoffrey of Lea , and why it be that they 
 might not abide the uncouth thing , or if there be any such . spake 
 Leonard : thing uncouth there be , and I be call upon to lay 
 they , and I do as biddeth Holy Church in all wise , but prevail not 
 against they , and still be they see and hear , till folk might 
 endure it no long . 

 and what like be these thing ? say Birdalone , and be they yet 
 see and hear ? say Leonard : strange it be , but last night I go 
 into the great hall where they mostly betid , and lay I down there , 
 as while I do , for I fear they not , and would see if they yet 
 appear ; but all night come nothing at all . as to the likeness of 
 they . . . then he stop , but say presently : hard it be to tell 
 thee of they , but need must I. there be two of these thing ; and 
 one be an image of a tall woman of middle - age , red - haired , white- 
 skin , and meagre , and while she have a twiggen rod in her hand , 
 and while a naked short sword , and while nought at all . but the 
 voice of she be curse and blaspheming and ill - saying . 

 say birdalone : this be then a fetch of my witch - mistress of whom I 
 tell thee erst , and the image of she ; what be the other ? say 
 Leonard : I be fain not to tell thee . yet need must thou , say 
 Birdalone . dear lady , say Leonard , the other be an image of thee , 
 and even most like unto thee ; but while clothe in a scanty grey coat 
 and barefoot , and while clothe in a fair green gown goodly broider , 
 and broidered shoon ; and while all mother - naked . 

 and what voice cometh from mine image ? say Birdalone , smile , yet 
 somewhat pale withal . say Leonard : one while a voice of sweet 
 singing , as of a bird in the brake , and that be when thou art clothe ; 
 and again , when thou art naked , a voice of shrieking and wail , as 
 of one endure torment . 

 Spake Birdalone : and when do these wonder begin ? say he : not 
 till after Sir Hugh and thy she - friend be go hence . 

 Pondered Birdalone a little ; then she say : I see herein the malice 
 of my witch - mistress ; she would not send these fetch while Hugh be 
 here , lest he should turn to seek I with all his might . but when 
 they depart , she would have the castle waste , and then she send 
 they , wotte that thereby she would rid she of Sir Geoffrey of Lea ; 
 while , on the other hand , I be nought so much unto he that he would 
 spend all his life seek I . but now I deem I know so much of she 
 that I may bid thee to look on she as dead if these fetch come not 
 again within a little while . then mayst thou send and do Sir 
 Geoffrey to wit thereof , and belike he will come back again ; and fain 
 be I thereof for it will be merry if the Castle of the Quest be 
 dwelt in once more . 

 Yea , verily , say Leonard ; but far merry yet wert thou to dwell 
 there . Nay , she say , but now I see that it be not fat for I . 
 let we go in , for I would get to what I would do . 

 so therewith they pass under the shadow of the archway , and 
 birdalone stay not but go straightway into the hall , and through 
 it ; and the priest , who lag somewhat behind her speedy foot , cry 
 out unto she : whither wilt thou ? what chamber wilt thou visit first ? 
 but she stay not , and spake to he over her shoulder as she go : 
 follow I if thou wilt ; I have but one place only to come to ere I 
 leave the Castle of the Quest , save I must need turn back on my 
 footstep . 

 then Leonard come up with she , and she go her way out of the hall , 
 and out on to the waterswale of the castle , and so to the little 
 haven of the water - gate . there Birdalone look about she eagerly ; 
 then she turn to Leonard and point with her finger and say : lo 
 thou ! there yet lieth my ferry of old time , the Sending Boat ; now wot 
 I wherefore I be draw hither . and her eye glitter and her body 
 quiver as she spake . 

 Yea forsooth , say Leonard , there it lieth ; who of all folk in the 
 castle have durst to touch it ? but what hath it to do with thee , O 
 kind lady ? 

 friend , she say , if this day weareth , and I be yet within these 
 wall , then meseemeth there must I abide for evermore ; and there 
 perchance shall I meet that seem of myself , maybe for this night , 
 maybe for ever , till I die here in this castle void of all that I 
 love , and I over - young for it , friend . and I know now that there be 
 hope within I ; for I bethink I of a dear friend over yonder water 
 of whom I have never tell any , nor will tell thee now , save this , 
 that she be the wisdom of my life . 

 wherefore now I will try this ferry and wot if the wight thereof will 
 yet obey the voice of the speaker of the spell , who have shed of her 
 blood to pay therefor . put not forth a hand therefore nor speak a 
 word to let I , but take this farewell of I , with my pity and such 
 love as I may give thee , and let I go , and think kindly of I . 

 then she go up to he , and lay her hand upon his shoulder , and 
 kiss he , and turn about without more ado and step into the 
 boat ; then she sit down and strip her arm of its sleeve , and draw 
 forth a knife and let blood of her arm , and then arise and smear 
 stem and stern therewith , and then sit down with her face to the 
 stern and sing : 


 the red raven - wine now 
 Hast thou drunk , stern and bow ; 
 wake then and awake 
 and the Northward way take : 
 the way of the Wenders forth over the flood , 
 for the will of the Senders be blent with the blood . 


 then she abode a little , while Leonard stand star on her 
 speechless with grief and blind with his bitter tear , till the 
 boat begin to move under she , and presently glide out of the little 
 haven into the wide lake ; then she turn her face back unto he and 
 wave her hand , and he kneel down and bless she , weeping . and so 
 she vanish away from before he . 



 CHAPTER IX . BIRDALONE FINDETH the isle of nothing GREATLY BETTERED , 
 and be KINDLY ENTREATED there 



 now it be scarce noon when she depart , and the dark night come 
 upon she in the midst of the water ; and she fall asleep in the boat 
 ere the night have grow very old , and wake up in the morning , not 
 exceed early , maybe about six o'clock ; then she look ahead and 
 think presently to see the ill - favour blotch of the Isle of 
 nothing on the bosom of the blue water , whereas it be a fair and 
 cloudless morning of latter May . sure enough she see land ahead , and 
 it lie low down on the water , but she deem from the first that it 
 be green of hue , and as she near it she see that it be verily as 
 green as emerald . thereat she be a little troubled , because she 
 think that mayhappen the Sending Boat have go astray , and that if 
 the wight thereof be not wend the old road , maybe he be not 
 make for the old haven . for now indeed she tell herself right out 
 that her will be to go back again to the House under the Wood , and 
 see what might betide there , and if she and the wood - mother together 
 might not overcome the witch . 

 but whatever might happen nought could she do but sit in her place 
 and wend as the Sending Boat would ; and in an hour 's space she be 
 right under the lee of the land , and she see that it be shapen even 
 as the Isle of Nothing have be aforetime . but this make her wonder , 
 that now the grass grow thick down to the lip of the water , and all 
 about from the water up be many little slim tree , and some of they 
 with the May - tide blossom yet on they , as though it be a fair and 
 great orchard that she be near ; and moreover , beyond all that she 
 see the thatched roof of house rise up . 

 presently then the Sending Boat have bring she to the land , and she 
 step ashore , but be wary , and gat her bow bent and set an arrow 
 thereto she begin to go up from the water . yet she think within 
 herself , it will be nought ill if I be come amongst folk , so long as 
 they be peaceful , or else how might I live the journey out to all the 
 isle and so home to the House under the Wood ? 

 so she turn her face to where she have see those roof , which now 
 she see no long because of the thick leaf of the little tree , 
 and so go along a narrow path , which grow to be more and more 
 closely beset with tree , and be now no long apple and pear and 
 quince and medlar , but a young - grow thicket of woodland tree , as 
 oak and hornbeam and beech and holly . 

 at last as she go she hear voice before she , so she steal warily 
 to the edge of the copse , finger on shaft ; and presently could see 
 clear of the sapling and out on to a wide space of greensward , 
 beyond which be a homestead of many house and bower , like unto 
 that of a good yeoman in peaceful land , save that the main building 
 be long , though it be low . but amidst the say greensward be a 
 goodly flock of sheep that have be but of late wash for the 
 shearing , and along with the sheep four folk , two carle and two 
 quean , all of they in their first youth , not one by seem of over 
 a score and two of summer . these folk be clothe but simply , man and 
 woman , in short coat of white woollen   but the woman 's coat a 
 little long than the man 's , without shoon or hosen ; they have 
 garland of green leaf on their head , and be wholly unarmed , 
 save that one of the man bear an ashen wand in his hand . as for 
 their body , they be goodly of fashion , tan indeed by the sun 's 
 burning , but all sweet of flesh be they , shapely and trim , clean- 
 make , and light and slim . 

 Birdalone 's heart yearn toward they , and she step straightway 
 from out of the cover of the coppice , and the sun flame from her 
 sallet and glitter in the ring of her hauberk , so that the folk 
 might not fail to see she ; the sheep flee bundle from her past 
 their keeper , who stand firm , but seem somewhat scared , and move 
 not toward Birdalone . she give they the sele of the day and stand 
 still herself ; but the man with the ash - wand say : hail , thou man ; 
 but we would have thee come no nearer a while , though thy voice be 
 sweet : for we know what thing they be which thou bearest , and that 
 thou art a warrior . wilt thou hurt we ? 

 birdalone laugh as sweetly as the blackbird sing , and she do off 
 her sallet and shake the plenteous hair down over she , and then draw 
 forth her sword and dagger and cast they to earth , and lay her bow 
 and quiver of arrow upon they , and say : now will I come to you , or 
 ye shall come to I , whereas I be unweapone , and no warrior , but a 
 woman , and ye be four to one , and two of you carle ; wherefore now 
 ye may bind I or slay I if you will ; but in any case I pray you 
 first to give I a mouthful of meat . 

 when she have do her speech , she go up to the fairest of the woman 
 and kiss she ; but the two carle make no more ado but come to 
 Birdalone and kiss she one after other , and that as man who need 
 nought to compel they therein , and each thereafter take a hand of she 
 and hold it and caress it . but the other woman have run into the 
 house as soon as Birdalone speak , and come back again with a treen 
 bowl full of milk and a little loaf , not white but brown ; and there 
 blunder about her leg as she come a little lad of some three 
 winter old , naked and brown , who be shy of the gleaming new - comer , 
 and hide he behind the woman one while , and the other while come 
 forth to see the new thing . but the woman say : dear woman , here be 
 for thee some of the ewe ' milk , and a bite of bread , and a little 
 deal of cheese ; the say milk be yet warm , so that it be not yet 
 clottere ; but if thou wilt come with we thou mayest speedily drink 
 cow ' milk , and we be now at point to go milk they . 

 birdalone thank she with a heart full of content , and be not ill- 
 pleased to get her hand free from the two carle ; so she sit down 
 and eat her breakfast while they talk with she , and tell she of 
 diverse work of theirs ; as to how their tree be wax , and new 
 tillage they have do the past spring , and how it befell to the kine 
 and the goat ; of their child also they spake , and how there be 
 already four thereof , and one of the woman , the meat - bringer , already 
 quicken with child once more . so that ere we die , quoth the carle 
 who be speak , we look to see many grandchild , and shall have 
 some stout carle and quean here . and by that time will some of the 
 tree be well grow , so that we may fall timber and make we some keel 
 that will wend the lake , and help we a - fishing ; or we may go to other 
 land ; or while folk may come to we , even as thou hast , thou dear- 
 handed , sweet - voice woman . but wilt thou abide here ever ? 

 Yea , say the other , but that be look forward a long while , that 
 building of ship . what be near and well to think of be , that 
 these apple and pear tree be so well fruit , small as they be , that 
 this harvest we shall be able to make we cider and perry ; yea , and no 
 little deal thereof . but art thou mind to abide with we ever ? 
 that be dear to we ; and belike thou would bear us child , thou 
 also . 

 then spake the meat - fetch woman and laugh withal : Nay , thou 
 also lookest aloof a pretty deal ; whereas what be now to do be to go 
 milk the kine , and to take this guest with we , so that she may drink 
 somewhat well than ewe ' milk though the cider be not ready to 
 hand . but tell I , our dear guest , art thou verily go to abide 
 with we a long while ? that be sweet to we , and we will do all we 
 may to pleasure thee . 

 Nay , say Birdalone , it will no well be but that I depart on the 
 morrow ; and all thank do I give you for your kindness . 

 the woman kiss she , and she arise , and all they go together to 
 the milking of the kine some half mile inland ; and they pass 
 through much of orchard , and some deal of tillage , wherein the wheat 
 be already grow high ; and so come they to a wide meadow through 
 which run a little stream , and therein be a goodly herd of kine . so 
 they fall to the milking , and make Birdalone drink of the sweet cow ' 
 milk , and then go and lie down under the shade of the little young 
 tree , and talk and be merry together . but the man be both of 
 they somewhat willing at first to kiss Birdalone and toy with she , 
 but when she let they know that she desire it not they refrain 
 they without grudge . 

 all this while of their talk they ask Birdalone nought of whence 
 and whither , and she would not ask they , lest it might stir their 
 asking , and then she would have to tell they some deal of her story ; 
 and tell it be now become unto her somewhat weary work . 

 in a while they arise all , and the man and one woman go their way 
 to deal with the acre - land , but the meat - fetcher go back with 
 birdalone into the house ; and she show she all that be therein , 
 which be for the more part , forsooth , the four babe aforesaid . the 
 other come back in the eventide , bear with they foison of blue 
 hare - bell , and tell joyously how they have find they anigh the 
 coppice edge in such a place : and thereafter they be merry , and 
 sing and talk the evening away , and show Birdalone at last to a 
 fair little chamber wherein be a bed of dry grass , where she lay 
 down and sleep in all content . 



 CHAPTER X. of BIRDALONE 's flitting from the isle of nothing 



 on the morrow birdalone arise betime , and would not tarry despite 
 all the kindness of that folk and the change which have come over the 
 Isle of nothing ; so the friend see she down to the boat all 
 together , and bear down with they a deal of bread and cheese and late 
 apple of the last year , for her provision on the road , and a pail of 
 milk withal ; and man and woman they kiss she at departure , and the 
 meat - fetcher say : if by any mean thou mayst find a keel which will 
 carry thee hither , at some time , I would thou wouldst come ; for even 
 if thou be old , and we pass away , yet here shall be our child or 
 our grandchild to welcome thee ; and we will tell they the tale of 
 thee that they remember it and long for thee . 

 then Birdalone kiss she again , and make much of she , and so step 
 into the boat , and fall to her sacrifice to the wight thereof ; and 
 those other star at she and wonder , and spake nought unto she 
 till she be go glide over the face of the water ; but as they 
 walk back to the house , they spake amongst themselves that this 
 must be some goddess   for of Holy Church they know nought   who have 
 come to visit they in her loveliness ; and in after time , when this 
 folk wax a many , and till all the isle and make ship and spread 
 to other land and become great , they yet have a memory of Birdalone 
 as their own very lady and goddess , who have come from the fertile and 
 wise land to bless they , when first they begin to engender on that 
 isle , and have break bread with they , and sleep under their roof , and 
 then depart in a wonderful fashion , as might be look for of a 
 goddess . 

 but as for Birdalone , she come not back ever , nor see that folk 
 again , and now she speed over the water toward the Isle of king . 



 CHAPTER XI . come to the isle of king birdalone findeth THERE A 
 SCORE and two of fair damsel who would FAIN have she company 



 Birdalone come ashore at the say isle at the day - dawn , and see but 
 little change in the isle when it grow light , and still the castle 
 stand look down awfully on to the meadow . but when she have set 
 foot on the land , she handle her bow lest the bad might befall , 
 and look about she , deeming that this time she would not go she 
 way to the dread show that be array in the castle , if forsooth 
 those dead folk yet abode there . 

 so now as she look across the meadow , she see one with light and 
 flutter raiment come forth from the tree , and look toward she 
 whereas she stand flash and gleam in the sun like an image of 
 the God of Love turn warrior . now Birdalone deem for sure that 
 this be a woman ; she see she come a little nigh to she , and then 
 stand look at she under the sharp of her hand ; then she turn 
 about and run back to the brake whence she come ; and presently 
 birdalone hear the sound of voice come thence , and in a little 
 while thereafter come forth from the say brake a rout of woman   one 
 score and two as they be tell thereafter   and walk over the 
 meadow straight unto she . she stand where she be , so as to be nigh 
 unto her ferry in case they will her unpeace ; for though they be 
 weaponless by seem , they be a many . 

 when they be come near they stand about she in a half ring , 
 whisper and laugh each to each . birdalone see that they be 
 all young , and that none of they might be call ungoodly , and some 
 be full fair . they be bright and fine of array . Most bore gold 
 and gem on finger and neck and arm ; they be clothe in light , or it 
 may be say wanton raiment of diverse colour , which have only this of 
 their fashion in common , that they none of they hide over - much of 
 their bare body ; for either the silk slip from the shoulder of 
 she , or dance away from her flank ; and she whose foot be shod , 
 spar not to show knee and some deal of thigh ; and she whose gown 
 reach unsheared from neck to heel , wear it of a web so thin and 
 fine that it hide but little betwixt heel and neck . 

 birdalone stand gaze on they and wonder , and she have a mind to 
 think that they be some show send by her old mistress the witch for 
 her undoing , and she loosen her sword in its sheath and nock an 
 arrow . 

 but then run forward two of the damsel and knelt before she , and 
 each take an hand of she and fall to kiss it , and she feel their 
 hand that they be firm and their lip that they be soft and 
 warm , and they be doubtless alive and real . then spake one of they 
 and say : hail our lord ! how can word say how we rejoice in thy 
 come this happy morn ! now do all we give ourselves to thee as thy 
 slave to do as thou wilt with . yet we pray thee be merciful to we 
 and our longing . 

 Therewith all the sort of they kneel down on the grass before 
 birdalone and join their hand as pray to she . and birdalone 
 be full ill at ease , and wotte not where she be . but she say : 
 Hail ! and good day and fulfilment of wish unto you , fair damsel ! 
 but tell I , be this the Isle of king , as I deem ; for strange it 
 be for I to see ye womenfolk here ? 

 say she who have speak afore : yea verily this be the Isle of king ; 
 but long ago be the king dead , and yet they sit dead in the great 
 hall of the castle yonder , as thou mayst see if thou , who art a man 
 and a valiant warrior , durst follow up yon mountain path thereto ; but 
 we , weak woman and little - hearted , durst not go anigh it ; and we 
 tremble when while a - night cometh down thence the sound of clash 
 sword and clatter shield , and the cry of man in battle . but , 
 praise be to the God of Love , nought cometh down from thence unto we . 
 therefore do we live peaceful life and pleasant here , lack nought 
 but thee , lord ; and lo now thou hast come unto we , and we be happy 
 in our inmost heart . 

 now be Birdalone perplex and know not what to do ; but at last she 
 say : gentle maiden , I pray you pardon I , but I must depart 
 straightway ; for I have an errand , and life or death lieth on it . in 
 all else than my abiding here may ye have your will . 

 Therewith do she move a little way toward her ferry ; but forthwith 
 all they brake out weeping and wail and lament , and some of 
 they come up to Birdalone and cast themselves down before she , and 
 clasp her knee , and take hold of her skirt , and besought she 
 piteously to abide with they . but she put they aside as well as she 
 might , and step aboard the Sending Boat , and stand amidst it 
 wait on their departure ; but they go not , and stand along on the 
 lip of the land cry out and beseech with much clamour . 

 then Birdalone wax somewhat wrath at their noise and tumult , and 
 she draw forth her knife and bar her arm and let blood from it . 
 but when they see the whiteness and roundness of it , and how fine and 
 sleek it be , straightway they change their tune , and cry out : a 
 woman , a woman , a fool of a woman ! and they laugh in scorn and 
 mockery . and the speaker of they say : now there be but one thing 
 for thee to do , and that be to come forth from thy boat and strip off 
 thy steal raiment , and we shall make thee as fine as ourselves , and 
 thou shalt come with we , and with we abide the coming of our lord . 
 Nay , thou art so fair and lovely , that thou shalt be the Lady and 
 Queen of we , and we will do after thy command , and thou mayst 
 chastise we if we fail therein . but now if thou wilt not come forth 
 of the boat uncompelle , we shall pluck thee forth of it . 

 and therewith she set her foot on the gunwale of the boat , and two or 
 three other do the like . but now have Birdalone her sword naked in 
 her hand , and she wax as red as blood , and cry out : Forbear I 
 bid you ! yea verily I be a woman ; but I will not take this offer 
 either , whereas I have an errand , as I tell you . and so stern it be , 
 that if ye now let my departure I will not spare to smite with this 
 sword whoso first cometh aboard my ferry , and though I be not a man , 
 yet shall ye find that in this matter I shall be little bad , 
 whereas I be armed and ye be naked . 

 then they draw back and stand gibe and jeer at she ; but she 
 heed it no whit , but reddened stem and stern of the Sending Boat , 
 and sing she spell , and forth glide the ferry , while the damsel 
 stand and star astonish . as for Birdalone , as she speed on she 
 way she might not refrain her laughter . thus she wend the wet 
 highway . 



 CHAPTER XII . birdalone COMETH again to the ISLE of QUEENS , and 
 FINDETH a perilous adventure therein 



 it be not yet daybreak when Birdalone come ashore again , and the 
 moon be down , and it be dark ; wherefore she durst not go up on the 
 land , but lie down in the ferry and fall asleep there . when she wake 
 again it be broad daylight , the sun be up , and a little ripple be 
 run over the face of the water . she step ashore straightway , 
 and look up the land and to the right hand and the left , and see at 
 once that it be indeed the Isle of Queens , and the house stand trim 
 and lovely as of old time ; then she long somewhat to tread the 
 green meadow a little , for yet young be the day , and she see nought 
 stirring save the throstle and a few small beast . however , she say 
 to herself that she would go nowhere nigh to the goodly house wherein 
 abode those image of death . yet her body long so sore for the 
 springtide freshness of the grass , and be so bewooed of the flowery 
 scent thereof , that though she durst not go unarmed , she do off her 
 footgear and go steal softly barefoot and with naked leg over 
 the embroidered greensward , say aloud to herself : if run for the 
 ferry I need must , light shall I run so dight . 

 nonetheless , she have go but a little way ere a terror take hold of 
 she , though she see no child of Adam anigh , and she turn and run 
 back swiftly to her old place and sit down under a twisted oak - tree 
 hard by the Sending Boat , and abode there pant and quaking , and 
 scarce dare to look up from the grass for a while . then her heart 
 come back to she , and she laugh , and say to herself : I be a fool , 
 for I need fear nought on this Isle of Queens save woman like myself . 

 yet she sit there a little while long without stir ; then she 
 stand up and look keenly around , and , as aforesaid , exceed far- 
 sight she be ; but still she see neither man nor maid nor suckle 
 child . 

 then her eye seek the lip of the lake , and rest on a little 
 bight some stone 's throw ahead of the Sending Boat , where , a little 
 back from the water , slim willow make a veil betwixt the water of 
 the meadow ; and she look , and see how pleasant a place it be for 
 a one to stand and look on the ripple just leave , while the water 
 drip from the clear body on to the grass . and her bare foot fall 
 to tell she clothe side of the sweet coolness of the water , and 
 wait for no naysay , but lightly bear she toward the willowy bight . 
 and when she be there , she do off her sallet and ungirt she , and 
 lay her sword on the grass , and do off her surcoat and hauberk , and 
 so be a woman again in one white coat above her smock . then she 
 look heedfully betwixt the willow - bough , and see no more than 
 before , nought but a little whitethorn brake , now white indeed with 
 blossom , some fifty yard landward from where she stand . so she 
 laugh , and do off her other raiment , and slide swiftly into the 
 water , that embrace her body in all its fresh kindness ; and as for 
 Birdalone , she reward it well for its past toil by sporting and 
 swim to her full . 

 then she come forth from the water , and clothe herself in no great 
 haste , and do on her hauberk and sallet and sword , and so go back 
 to her place , and sit down and begin to do on her foot - gear . 

 but as she look up from her work a moment , lo ! a tall man come 
 toward she , and just about the willow whereby she have bath . her 
 heart beat quick and her face change , yet she hasten , and be shod 
 and stand up in knightly array by then he stay his step some five 
 pace from she , and give she the sele of the day in courteous wise ; 
 and she strove to think that he have not see she , or at least note 
 her otherwise dight ; yet her heart misgave she . 

 he be a grizzle - haired man of over fifty summer by seem , but 
 goodly enough and well - knit ; he be clothe in a green coat more than a 
 little worn , but make after the fashion of knighthood ; he have nought 
 on his head but an oak - chaplet , and no weapon but a short sword by 
 his side and a stout staff in his hand . 

 she give back his greeting in a quavering voice ; and he say : 
 welcome again , young man . art thou come to dwell with we ? truly 
 thou art trim now , but ere some few month thine attire will be not 
 so much fair than ours , and thine hauberk will be rust , for here 
 be no joyous tilting nor deed of arm , and no kind lady to give 
 the award of honour , so that if we fight amongst ourselves it will be 
 because we have fall out , and spitefully . yet   and he laugh , 
 mockingly , as she think   thou mayst bring we luck , and draw some 
 fair damsel unto we , for that be what we await in this isle , which 
 be barren of their fair body , despite of its deceitful name . 

 Thereat Birdalone redden , deeming that he divine her womanhood , 
 but she enforce she to speak hardily , and as manly as she might , and 
 say : yea , fair sir , and if I be the God of Love , as thou deemest , 
 and not merely a poor squire   Louis Delahaye , at thy service , how 
 many damsel shall I send thee if there must need be one to each man 
 of you ? Quoth he : Thou must make up the tale to a score or more , or 
 some of we must lack . sooth to say , at this time thou needest not 
 haste overmuch for all the tale , whereas there be but one other of 
 the company near at hand , a mere foolish young man ; the other be 
 go to the leeward side of the isle , to fetch we venison and fish , 
 both of which be more plenty there than here ; wherefore be we two 
 somewhat lonesome in this stead , all the more as we be over - nigh to 
 the sorcery in the great house , which we durst not enter ; for though 
 nought cometh out thence down unto we , yet hear we a - night - tide , 
 first song , and then cry and shriek , come out therefrom . 

 then he stay his speech , and draw a little nigh to Birdalone , and 
 then grin , and say : Forsooth we can spare he , we twain . and he 
 look on her hard , and the colour come into her cheek , and she 
 laugh uneasily , as a dainty lady when she heareth some unmeet tale . 

 but again the old carle draw nigh to she , and say : Thou seemest 
 to have a good bow and store of arrow ; if thou wouldst lend they to 
 I for a little , and come with I into the wood hard by , I might 
 shoot thee some venison with little toil to thee ; whereas , forsooth , 
 thou look scarce like one who be meet for over - much toil . again 
 she redden , and spake nought this time ; and he say : deem not 
 there be no deer this end of the isle because I say that the other 
 be go to fetch home venison ; only the deer be tame there and 
 more , and we have but evil shooting - gear , whereas thou art well find 
 therein . wilt thou not come ? we shall have merry feast after the 
 hunt . 

 now have Birdalone come to her wit again , and she answer like a 
 merry youth , with a flavour of mockery in her speech : Fair sir , thou 
 shalt not deem that I need much help in slay the dun deer ; for I 
 do thee to wit that I shoot not ill in the bow ; neither be I heavy- 
 footed . but I will not hunt in your park to - day , for I have an 
 errand which calleth I away , so that I shall depart hence presently . 
 besides , wise elder , there be thine errand to see to ; and if I be the 
 God of Love , as thou sayest , I must not keep thee and thy valiant 
 fellow languish mateless ; so with thy leave I will now depart , 
 that I may send you a score of fair damsel for your company . 

 and she turn about and make a step toward her boat ; but the carle 
 draw nearer , laugh ; and he say : truly sayest thou that thou art 
 not heavy - footed , for never see I foot light or fair than glide 
 over the meadow e'en now ; nor a fair body than come like rosy- 
 tint pearl fresh out of the water while I lie hide in yonder 
 thorn - brake that while . wherefore trouble not thyself to bring any 
 more damsel than thyself , fairest Goddess of Love , for thou art 
 enough for I . 

 and therewith he run forward , and stretch out a hand to she ; but in 
 that nick of time have she her sword naked in her hand , and the carle 
 draw back before the glitter thereof , and cry out : Ho , ho ! be it 
 to be battle , my mistress ? Deemest thou that thou wilt slay I as 
 lightly as the dun deer , and thou with thy bow unstrung at thy back ? 
 now shall I show thee a trick of fence ; but fear not that I shall 
 hurt thee to spoil thee . 

 he advance on she with his staff aloft , and her heart fail she , 
 and she quake , and lightly he beat down her guard and do the sword 
 out of her hand ; and again he turn on she to take she , but she 
 spring aside and run from he , but run landward perforce , as he be 
 betwixt she and the boat ; and he follow heavily , and have nought to 
 do in the race . 

 but she have not go a two - score yard ere she hear a great shout , 
 and another man come run over the meadow ; a slim young man be 
 this , and bad of attire than the old carle , for so tattered be his 
 raiment that he be half naked ; but he be goodly of fashion , fresh- 
 coloured and black - haired . birdalone stay her foot when she see 
 he , for though she doubt not to outrun he , yet whither should she 
 run , since her ferry be behind she ? 

 so the young man come up to she , and the old carle meet he all 
 panting , and the young man say : how now , Antony ! what battle be 
 this ? and wherefore art thou chase this fair knight ? and thou , 
 fair sir , why fleest thou this grey dastard ? 

 Said Antony : thou art but a young fool , Otter , this be no man , but a 
 woman , and I have take she , and she be mine . 

 well , say Otter , I say she be as much mine as thine ; nay , more , if 
 she will give herself unto I . but if she will not , she shall go 
 whither she will in thy despite . or art thou a woman ? 

 yea , yea , say Birdalone ; and I pray thee , by thy mother 's head , 
 suffer I to depart ; for heavy and full of need be the errand that I 
 be about . 

 go thou shalt then , say Otter ; lead back to thy place , and I will 
 walk with thee . so do they : and Birdalone go beside the young 
 man quaking ; but he put out no hand unto she ; and sooth to say , she 
 deem that she have seldom see so fair a young man , but it be 
 Arthur or Hugh . 

 now he , as Antony , be girt with a short sword , but he let it be in 
 its sheath ; and as they go , Antony draw his blade again , and hove 
 it up to smite Otter , but as it befell Birdalone see he , and turn 
 round sharp upon he and gat hold of his wrist , and therewith Otter 
 turn also , and catch the old carle by the nape as he turn away , 
 and put a foot before his and shove mightily , so that he go 
 nosele to the earth . 

 then turn Otter about again , laugh , and he say to Birdalone : 
 by Saint Giles ! thou art well - nigh too valiant for a woman , and I 
 would that we two might be together ; and then between we we might 
 achieve the adventure of the dead lady up yonder . she hang her 
 head , and say : Fair sir , it may not anywise be ; yet I thank thee , I 
 thank thee . 

 so come they to the water - side and the Sending Boat , and Birdalone 
 stay her foot there , and the young man say : what be this keel , 
 that seemeth unto I as if it be a ferry for malefactor wend to 
 a death of torment , so grey and bleared and water - log and sun- 
 bleach as it be , and smear over with stain of I know not what ? 

 say birdalone : such as it be , it be my ferry over the water to 
 where I would be . strange ! say Otter ; to my mind it be like to our 
 fortune on this isle , we who be once knight and merry squire and 
 be now as gangrel man , and of ill condition , think of nought 
 save our first desire , even those which we share with the wolf and 
 the kite . 

 she say : but art thou of evil condition , thou who hast just 
 deliver I from trouble ? he smile grimly : Damsel , say he , I 
 have not deliver thee yet from I , though I have from he . but 
 tell I , art thou a sorceress ? not a black one , say Birdalone ; but 
 I will tell thee at once that I have be breed by a witch most 
 mighty , and some deal of lore have I learn . and therewith she tell 
 he of the Sending Boat , and how she would have to speed it on the 
 way . 

 he look on she a little and then turn away , and see her sword 
 lie on the grass ; so he go to it and pick it up and bring it 
 to she , and say : thou mayst yet need this keen friend . so she take 
 it and thrust it back into the scabbard , quake somewhat because of 
 he ; so feeble and frail as she feel before he . then he say : if 
 thou deem thou hast somewhat to reward I for , I have a boon to 
 ask of thee , and grant that , we shall be quit again . yea , she 
 say faintly , and what be the boon ? he say : art thou press to 
 depart now , this minute ? Nay , say Birdalone , not for an hour if 
 there be no peril here from other man , and . . . and . . . and if I 
 be true to thee and will let thee go ? say he , laugh ; hah ! be that 
 not thy word ? fear not , I swear by thine eye that thou shalt depart 
 whenso thou wilt . now then , the boon I crave be , that thou wilt sit 
 down here beside I and tell I the tale of thy life that have be . 
 say she : it weary I to think thereof ; yet hast thou a right to 
 crave somewhat of I , and this be not hard to grant . 

 and she sit down by he ; but he say : do this also for I , take off 
 thine headpiece , since now that we know thee for a woman it serveth 
 thee nought . so do she , and begin her tale straightway , and tell 
 he all thereof , save as to the wood - wife , and he sit hearken and 
 watch her face ; and when she have make an end , he say : now shall 
 I ask none other boon of thee , though I long sore for it ; but good it 
 be that we sunder straightway , else maybe I might yet be for 
 hinder thee . 

 Therewith he stand up , and Birdalone also , and he look on she 
 eagerly , and say : I be now to bid thee farewell , and it be most 
 like that I shall never see thee again , wherefore I will ask thee yet 
 to let one thing come from thy mouth ; for I deem thee the dearest of 
 all woman I have ever see . what shall I say ? say Birdalone , 
 smile on he kindly ; must thou need put the word in my mouth ? 
 Thou hast be friendly with I here when need be to I of 
 friendliness ; wherefore I say , I would I might see thee again , and 
 thou well bestead than now thou art . 

 the young man 's face brighten , and he say : Spake I not that thou 
 wert the dearest of all ? this be even the word I would have put in 
 thy mouth . but now see thou , one goeth on from one thing to another , 
 and I must now ask thee , be there aught which thou hast a mind to 
 give I ere I depart , some keepsake which I durst not ask for ? 

 she flush red and say : I will with a good heart give thee my bow 
 and arrow for a keepsake ; whereas the old carle tell I that ye be 
 ill furnish of shooting - gear .
