 and she would have take her bow from her back , but he laugh aloud , 
 and say : Nay , nay , I will not have that ; for there be those who 
 gird they to a sword and know not how to use it , but few will cumber 
 their shoulder with bow and quiver who can not shoot therewith ; I 
 deem it like that thou art a fell bowman . keep thy bow therefore , 
 and if thou wilt go without any other gift , even so be it . 

 and he make as if he would turn away ; but she put forth both her 
 hand and take his in they , and lift up her face and kiss he 
 kindly , and then turn away to her ferry ; while Otter stand still 
 and say in a merry voice : now be it well than well , for thou art 
 in all way what I would have thee , and there be nought like unto 
 thee . and therewith he turn away and depart ere Birdalone have 
 step into the Sending Boat , and she blushing like a rose the 
 while . then she do due sacrifice to the wight of the witch - ferry , 
 and speed on her way without any hindrance . 



 CHAPTER XIII . come to the ISLE of the YOUNG and the OLD , birdalone 
 findeth it people with child 



 Midst all this have wear some hour , but yet it be barely noon ; 
 wherefore it be yet dark by then Birdalone make the Isle of the 
 Young and the Old ; so she step out of the boat , and lie down on 
 the grass and abode the dawn sleep . and she awake with the 
 clatter of shrill voice , and she rise up and look , and lo a 
 multitude of child all about she , both man and woman child , 
 and , as it seem , from five year old upward to fifteen . they cry 
 and crow merrily when they see she stand up , and press on she to 
 see she the nearer and to touch her hand or her raiment . they be 
 but little clad , and the young one not at all , but be goodly 
 youngling and merry . so great be the noise they raise , that loud 
 be the thunder which have not be hush thereby ; and birdalone 
 stand look on they , smile , and know not what to do . Anon she 
 turn to a tall thin lad of some fifteen winter , and say unto he : 
 Wilt thou now take I unto the house , and the place where dwelleth 
 the old man ? Quoth he : I neither know of an old man , nor rightly 
 what it mean , the word . be not I old enough for thee ? I be the 
 old of these here . but belike thou art hungry ; wherefore if thou 
 come to the place where we sleep a - night , and where we shelter we 
 from the storm and the rain when need be , I will give thee to eat ; 
 for we have both bread and milk and cheese , and raisin of the sun . 

 so he take her hand and lead she along , and ask she by the way 
 concern her armour and weapon , and of the fashion of battle , and 
 she tell he thereof what she would . 

 thus come they to the place where erst have be the cot under the 
 ruin of the great ancient house ; but now be go all that ruin and 
 the great grey wall , though the cot be leave ; and all about it be 
 low bower build of small wood and thatch undeftly . but the lad 
 smile when he see it , as if the sight thereof make he happy ; and he 
 say : all these have we make since I have dwelt here , and no other 
 home have I know . 

 and he lead she into the cot , and set she down to eat and to drink , 
 and through the open door she could see the child swarm , and 
 they that be nigh thrust each other this way and that to 
 catch a sight of she . 

 now she say : fair child , how gatt thou this victual if there be 
 no old folk to help you ? say he : we dig the ground and sow it , 
 and the wheat come up , and we reap it in harvest , and make bread of 
 it ; and we have goat and kine , and we milk they , and turn the milk 
 with a little blue flower , which be fair to see . and there be in 
 this isle little hill where the grape grow plenty ; and some we eat 
 and some we dry for store . Lo thou , such be our way for victual . 
 but tell I , say he , thou sayest old , and I know not the word ; art 
 thou old ? she laugh : not very , say she , yet old than thou . 

 say the lad : thou art fair and dear to look on , and thy voice be 
 sweet ; wilt thou not abide with we , and teach we what it be to be 
 old ? Nay , say she , I may not , for I have an errand which driveth I 
 on ; wherefore I must be go within this hour . 

 Forsooth , she be grow eager now to be do with her journey and 
 come to the House under the Wood , whatever should befall she there . 
 moreover she deem it would not be restful to she to abide among all 
 these restless child , with their ceaseless cry and yelping : if 
 rest she might , she would rest , she deem , in the Isle of Increase 
 Unsought , if there be no ill thing abide there . 

 wherefore now she arise , when she have sit hearken the sound of the 
 lad 's prattle for a while , for as to the sense thereof she might not 
 heed it over - much . the youngling would not leave she , but lead she , 
 hold her hand , down to her ferry again ; she kiss he in thank 
 for his meat , and he redden thereat but say nought . all the whole 
 rout of little one have follow she down to the water , and now they 
 stand , as thick as bee on a honeycomb , on the bank , to watch she 
 departure . but if they be keen to see her doing before , how much 
 keener be they when it come to the baring of her arm and the 
 smearing of the Sending Boat . to be short , so keen be they , and 
 push and shove each other so sturdily , that more than one or two 
 fall into the water , and Birdalone be fright lest they should 
 drown ; but they swam like duck , and get on to the land when they 
 would , which be not so very soon , for some of they hang unto the 
 gunwale of the boat , and hove their face up to look over into it , 
 and leave not hold till the ferry be fairly under weigh and beginning 
 to quicken its speed . 

 so leave Birdalone the isle , and nought befell she on the way to the 
 Isle of Increase Unsought . 



 CHAPTER XIV . the SENDING BOAT DISAPPEARETH from the ISLE of increase 
 UNSOUGHT , and birdalone seeketh to escape THENCE by SWIMMING 



 it be as before that birdalone come to the shore of the isle while 
 it be yet night ; but the wizard keel be so loathsome to she , that 
 she step out of it and lay she down on the land for what be leave 
 of the night ; yet hard she find her bed , and neither grassy nor 
 flowery . 

 for all that , she sleep , for she be weary , and it be broad day and 
 not very early when she awake . she stand up tremble , for she 
 forebode evil , so near as she be to the dwelling of her old 
 mistress ; and she look up to where in time past be the fair and 
 wicked house , and see that all be change indeed ; for no long be 
 the isle goodly with meadow and orchard and garden , but be waste and 
 bare , and nought grow on it save thin and wiry grass , already seed 
 even ere June be bear , and here and there hard and ugly herb , with 
 scarce aught that might be call a flower amongst they . tree there 
 be yet , but the most of they stark dead , and the good die fast . 
 no beast she see , nor fowl ; nothing but lizard and beetle , and now 
 and again a dry grey adder coil up about a sun - burn stone . but 
 of great carrion fly , green and blue , be there a many , and while 
 they buzz about her head till she sicken with loathing of they . 
 all this she find on her way as she go up toward the place where 
 erst be the great perron . but when she come to the top there be no 
 sign either of the stair or the house , or aught that ever be 
 build ; there be nought but the bare bent top , ungrassed , parch 
 by wind , scorch by sun , wash by rain . 

 she wander about the isle , to place where she have not be 
 herself , but which she deem she might have know by the telling of 
 the Green Knight 's tale , have there be no change since those day ; 
 but now be all change , and the whole isle be a mere waste , and 
 withal poisonous of aspect to her mind , as if many corps lay 
 underneath the wretched stone of it . nevertheless , though it seem 
 so evil unto Birdalone , she linger on it , wander about till she 
 be to - weary , for she have no will to depart at such time as she 
 would be like to come to her old abiding - place by night and cloud ; 
 wherefore she dally with the time , and come not back to the haven 
 of her ferry till it be nigh sunset , and the westere sun be in 
 her eye when she come there ; and she say to herself that this be 
 the cause why she might not see the Sending Boat . 

 so she clear her eye and look on the thin grass awhile , and then 
 down over the edge of the land , and still she see not her boat . she 
 turn pale , and a pang of anguish go to her heart ; but she walk 
 a little east , deeming that perchance she have err as to the place 
 of the haven on that dull and empty shore ; but yet there be no boat . 
 then she turn back wild with terror , and seek where erst she have 
 miss it , and find neither boat nor the world 's end . and she 
 deem that there might be some devilish malice of the wight of the 
 Sending Boat , to torment she with fear , and she walk along the 
 land 's edge up and down , and down and up , far each time , and 
 still there be no boat . 

 then she stand still and strove to think , and might not , nor might 
 she do aught , but spread abroad her hand and moan in her agony ; 
 for now indeed she feel herself in the trap ; and she say that all 
 her past life of hope and desire and love and honour be all for 
 nought , and that she be but bear to die miserably in that foul ruin 
 of an isle envenom with the memory of bygone cruelty and shame . 

 but in a little while she come somewhat to herself , and she say : at 
 least this hideous land shall not mock my die anguish ; I will give 
 myself to the water and let it do with I as it will . 

 Therewith she cast off her helm and hauberk first , and her weapon , 
 and her pouch with the treasure that could buy nought for she now , 
 and thereafter all her raiment , till she be as naked as when she 
 first come aland there that other time . again she moan , and put up 
 her hand to her bosom and feel a little gold box lie there betwixt 
 the fragrant hill of her breast , which hang to a thin golden thread 
 about her neck ; and a thought come into her mind , and she stoop 
 adown and draw from her pouch flint and fire - steel , and then open 
 the say golden box and draw thence the tress which Habundia the 
 wood - wife have give to she those year agone , and all tremble she 
 draw two hair from it , as erst she do on the Isle of Nothing , and 
 strike fire and kindle tinder and burn the said hair , and then 
 hang the golden box with the tress therein about her neck again ; and 
 she say : o wood - mother , if only thou couldst know of I and see I , 
 thou wouldst help I ! 

 thereafter she seek along the bank for bread which she have take 
 from her store that morning , and she find it , and compel herself 
 to eat of it for the strengthening of her body , and then she stand 
 and abode tiding ; and by then the sun have just sink below the rim of 
 the lake , and the star begin to twinkle , for the night be 
 cloudless , and exceed fair , and very warm . 

 no visible token come to she , but her heart grow strong , and she 
 seem to see herself yet alive and in hope on the other side of the 
 water ; and she say : who wotteth what Weird may do , or where the 
 water may bear I ? and there be no swimmer strong than I. 

 so then without more ado Birdalone slip into the water , which lay 
 before she as calm and plain as a great sheet of glass , and fall to 
 row with her arm and her leg as though she be but swim 
 from Green Eyot to the mainland , as so oft she have do in the other 
 day . 



 CHAPTER XV . BIRDALONE lacketh little of drowning , but COMETH 
 LATTERLY to the GREEN eyot 



 on swam Birdalone , not as one who have a mind to drown she for the 
 forgetting of trouble , but both strongly and wisely ; and she turn 
 over on to she back , and look on the star above she , and steer 
 herself by they thitherward whereas she deem be the land under the 
 wood . when she have be go from the evil isle for an hour or so , 
 there rise a fair little wind behind she , which help she forward , 
 but scarce raise the water more than a little ripple . 

 still she swam on , and it be some three hour she begin to weary , 
 and then she float on her back and let the wind and water have its 
 way with she ; and now the night be as dark as it would be ere dawn . 

 thus it go for another hour , that while she swam on and while she 
 float ; and now her heart begin to fail she , and the great water be 
 no long unto she a wet highway , but a terrible gulf over which she 
 hang faint . 

 nevertheless she do not give up do what she might : she float 
 supine a long while , and then , when she have gather a little 
 strength , turn over again and strike out , still steer she by the 
 star . but she have scarce make three stroke ere her arm meet 
 something hard and rough ; and at first in her forlornness she deem 
 she have happen on some dread water monster , and for terror of it 
 she sink down into the deep , but come up presently blind and 
 breathless , and spread abroad her arm , and again they come on the 
 thing aforesaid , and this time find that it be nought alive , but 
 the bole of a tree sit high out of the water . so she clomb up on 
 to it with what might she have leave , and sit she down , and see in the 
 dim light that it be big , and that there be a fork betwixt two 
 limb reach up into the air , and she thrust herself in between 
 these two limb and embrace one of they , so that she might scarce 
 tumble off ; and a great content and happiness come over she that she 
 have thus escape from the death of the deep ; but therewithal 
 weariness overcome she , and she sleep , whether she would or not ; and 
 the bole go on over the water no slow than might have be 
 look for , whether it be by the pushing on of the south wind , or 
 by the hand of Weird that would not have she die . 

 long she slumber , for when she awake it be broad day and the sun 
 be shin high in the heaven , and she clear her eye and look 
 around , and see before she the land , but yet blue in the offing . and 
 the tree - bole be yet speed on towards the shore , as if it be 
 be draw there by some bidding of might . 

 now indeed grow Birdalone happy , and she think if any have help 
 she it must have be the wood - mother once again ; and she say to 
 herself that she should soon meet with that helper ; nor heed she 
 that she be naked and unfurnished of any good , whereas she deem 
 indeed that it be but to ask and have of her friend . 

 for a while indeed she know not whither she be wend , and if she 
 face be verily turn toward the land under the wood ; but as the 
 morning wear the blue distance begin to grow green , and then she see 
 that a great wood be indeed before she , and thereafter , as it 
 clear yet more , she know the land she be near for the meadow 
 of the House under the Wood , and it be not long thence ere she see 
 clear and close Green Eyot and Rocky Eyot , though the house be yet 
 hide from she by the green shore of the first of those two isle . 

 shortly to tell it , her tree - bole float with she past the outer 
 ness of Green Eyot , and come ashore in that same sandy bight where 
 erst she be wonte to make her body ready for the water . she 
 step ashore all glad to feel the firm warm sand underneath her 
 foot - sol , and as one drunk with joy she be when the tall flowery 
 grass of the latter May be caress her leg as they shake the 
 seed - dust off the bent , and smite the fragrance out of the blossom ; 
 and she might scarce at first lift her eye from their familiar 
 loveliness . glad she be indeed , but exceed wear and weary with 
 the long voyage , and all the longing and fear and hope which have 
 encompass she that while . she lift up her eye but once , and see 
 the witch 's house standing where it be will not , but no shape of man 
 move about it ; then she turn aside to a little brake of thorn and 
 eglantine in the meadow hard by , and lay she down on the grass in 
 the shade thereof , and almost before her head touch the ground she 
 fall asleep , and sleep there long and peacefully . 



 CHAPTER XVI . BIRDALONE FINDETH HER WITCH - MISTRESS dead 



 it be some while after noon when she waken , and the sun be 
 shin bright and hot . somewhat she feel the burden of fear upon 
 she , even before she be fully come to herself , and know not what it 
 be that she fear ; but when she call to mind that it be even the 
 meeting with her old mistress , her flesh quake indeed with the 
 memory of bygone anguish , but valiantly she arise and face the 
 dwelling of the witch despite her naked helplessness . as she go 
 she look up unto it , and see no smoke come from the chimney , but 
 marvel little thereat since it be not yet cook - time and the 
 weather hot . she draw nigh , and see someone sit on the bench 
 without the door whereas the witch be wonte ; and her heart beat 
 quick , for she see presently that it be none other than her 
 mistress . moreover , near to she stand three of the milch - kine lowing 
 uneasily and as in reproach , even as such beast use when their 
 udder be full and they desire to be milk . 

 birdalone stay a minute , and her leg nigh fail she for fear , and 
 then because of the very fear she hasten on till she come within 
 ten pace of the say witch ; and sore she miss her bow and arrow , 
 and the cutting blade of her feigned squirehood , lest the carline 
 should arise and come rag and shriek at she . 

 then spake Birdalone in no feeble voice , and say : Dame , I be come 
 back unto thee , as thou seest , in even such plight as I flee from 
 thee ; and I have a mind to dwell in this land : what sayest thou ? 
 the witch neither move nor spake at her word ; and the kine , who have 
 hold silence when she first come up , and have turn from she , fall to 
 their peevish lowing again . 

 birdalone draw a step nigh , and say : dost thou hear I , dame , or 
 art thou exceed wroth with I , and art ponder what vengeance 
 thou wilt take on I ? still no answer come from the carline , and the 
 kine keep on lowing now and again . once more birdalone draw nigh , 
 and spake loudly and say : tell I at least , be it peace between we 
 or unpeace ? 

 but now when she look she see that the eye of the witch be open 
 and star , and her lip white , and her hand hard writhen ; and she 
 cry out and say : be she dead ? or will she waken presently and 
 beat I ? surely she be dead . and she put forth her hand and touch 
 her face , and it be stone - cold ; and she find that she be dead 
 beyond any question . 

 then be a great weight lift off her heart , and she turn about 
 and look on the meadow and up to the tree of the wood and down to 
 the ripple stream before she , and fair and sweet and joyous be 
 they get unto she ; and she look at the kine who be draw up 
 towards she , and she laugh merrily , and go to the out - house hard 
 by and take forth a milking - pail and a stool and fall to milk they 
 one after the other , and the beast go off down the meadow lowing 
 in a change voice , for joy to wit , this time . but Birdalone kneel 
 down and drink a long draught of the sweet warm milk , and then arise 
 and go swiftly into the house , and see nought change or worsen 
 so far as she could see . there be her own bed in the corner , and 
 the mistress 's , great and much fair , over against it ; and the 
 hutch by the door wherein the victual be keep : she open it now , 
 and find three loaf there on the shelf , and a meal - tub down below , 
 and she take a loaf and break it and fall to eat it as she walk 
 about the chamber . there be her bow stand in a nook beside the 
 hutch , and the quiver of arrow hang on the wall above it . there 
 be the settle lie athwart from the hearth ; and she smile , and 
 fit her wrist to the back of the carven bear which make its 
 elbow , whereto the witch be will not to tie they when she chastise she . 

 then she go to the coffer that stand against the wall behind it , 
 and throw up the lid of one of they , and find therein a smock or two 
 of her own , yellow by the lapse of time , and her old grey coat , 
 rag as it be when last she wear it , and now somewhat moth - eat 
 withal ; and she draw forth both smock and coat and lay they on the 
 settle . then she open another coffer , and therein be gay and 
 gaudy gown and gear of the witch 's wear ; but lie amongst they , as 
 if the witch have wear they also , her green gown and shoon which she 
 own hand have broider . but she say : Nay , ye have be in ill 
 company , I will wear you not , though ye be goodly , at least not till 
 ye have be fumigate and hallow for I . 

 Therewith she turn back to the settle and do on her her old smock 
 and her ragged grey coat , and say : to - day at least will these be 
 good enough for to - day 's work . and she knit her brow withal , and 
 walk with a firm step out - a - door and stand a while gaze on the 
 dead corpse of her enemy ; and she think how that here be that 
 which once be so great a thing unto she for the shaping of her life- 
 day , and which so oft come to her wake thought after she have 
 escape from her hand ,   though , as aforesaid , she seldom dream of 
 her a - night - time , and moreover an hour ago she yet fear it so sore 
 that she scarce might stand for the fear of it ; and now it be nought 
 but a carven log unto she . 

 but she tell herself that the work be to be do ; so she drag the 
 body away thence , and across the brook , and a little way into the 
 meadow , and then she go back and fetch mattock and spade from the 
 outhouse , where she know they lay , and so fall to dig a grave for 
 the corpse of her dead terror . but howso hard she might toil , she 
 be not through with the work ere night begin to fall on she , and she 
 have no mind to go on with her digging by night . wherefore she go 
 back into the house , and lighted candle , whereof be no lack , and 
 make her supper of the bread and the milk ; and then sit ponder on 
 her life that have be till the passion arise in her bosom , and the 
 tear burst out , and long she weep for desire of other and pity for 
 herself . then she go to the bed she have be erst will not to , and 
 lay she down and fall asleep . 

 and her mistress walk not , nor meddle with her peace ; nor do 
 birdalone so much as dream of she , but of her mother and Master 
 Jacobus in the fair city of the five Crafts ; and in her sleep she 
 weep for thinking of they . 



 CHAPTER XVII . BIRDALONE LAYETH to EARTH the body of the WITCH , and 
 findeth the SENDING BOAT BROKEN UP 



 when morning be , Birdalone awake , and feel a weight upon her heart , 
 and call to mind the task which lie before she . so she arise and 
 clothe herself , and go straight to the grave begin , and toil hard 
 till she have digge it out deep , and sithence she drag the witch 
 thereinto and heap the earth upon she . then she bath she in the 
 nighest pool of the brook , and go back into the house and make she 
 breakfast on the bread and milk , and it be then about mid - morning . 
 thereafter she go about the house , and see to the baking of bread , 
 and so out to the meadow to see to the kine and the goat , and then 
 store the milk for make butter and cheese , and do in all wise as 
 if she be to dwell long in that stead ; but thereafter she rest 
 her body , while her thought go wide about . but she say to 
 herself that she would not go up to the Oak of Tryst to meet the 
 wood - mother that day , but would abide the night , in case aught befell 
 that she should tell she . 

 but when the sun be get low she rouse herself and go out , and 
 walk about the meadow , and hearken to the bird ' song , and 
 watch the kine and the goat as they feed down the pasture ; and now 
 a soft content come over she , that all this be free unto she to hold 
 in peace , and to take her pleasure in , as much as one lone child of 
 Adam might do . 

 at last she wander down to the sandy bight of the lake and stand 
 gaze on Green Eyot , where the osier and willow be grow wild 
 and long in all these year , and she say that she would swim over to 
 it on the morrow . but now her foot take her eastward thence toward 
 the haven of the Sending Boat amongst the alder ; for in her heart 
 she would fain know if there be any tiding for she . 

 so she go softly along the path by the water , where she have speed so 
 swiftly that last time , and come at last to the creek - side , and 
 look down on to the water somewhat timorously . there then she see 
 what she deem be the very boat itself lie as she have know it ; 
 but when she look again she see that it lie from stem to stern all 
 loose stave with the water betwixt , and the thwart and rib all 
 sunder and undo , so that never again might it float upon the 
 wave . then she say in a soft voice : art thou dead then , as thy 
 mistress be dead ? be it not so that thou wert at the point of death , 
 and she also , when thou failedst I at the Isle of Increase Unsought ? 
 no voice come to she as she spake ; and she say again : must I then 
 bury thee as I have bury thy mistress ? Nay , that will I not until 
 thou compell I ; belike in a short while little of the stave of 
 thee shall be leave now that the life be out of thee . let thy ghost 
 and hers foregather if ye will . 

 as she spake the last word , she see a stir about the stern which lay 
 furth in up the creek , and while she quake with fail heart , 
 lo ! a big serpent , mouldy and hairy , grey and brown - flecked , come 
 forth from under the stern and go into the water and up the bank 
 and so into the dusk of the alder - wood . birdalone stand awhile pale 
 and heartsick for fear , and when her foot feel life in they , she 
 turn and steal away back again into the merry green mead and the 
 low beam of the sun , ponder whether this evil creature be the 
 fetch of the wight who drave the ferry under the blood of the sender . 

 so she hasten back again to the house , and light a fire on the 
 hearth , and fall to cook she somewhat of grout to her supper ; and 
 she watch the fire , think withal : now if some poor soul be 
 abroad , they may see the smoke and seek hither , and I may comfort 
 they with food and shelter and converse ; or when night darken , they 
 may see the litten window and come to I ; wherefore shall the fire 
 burn yet and the candle be light , for as warm as be the evening , 
 even as if it be yule - tide and the snow deep without , and the wind 
 howl in the woodland tree . and therewith she weep for longing of 
 they that she love . 

 but in a little she dry her tear , and reproach herself for her 
 much softness ; and she eat her supper when she have light a candle 
  for it be now dark , and again sit look at the hearth , till she 
 say : now be I get soft again , and who know but my softness may 
 tempt the ghost to come in to I . I will give my hand somewhat to 
 do . 

 Therewith her eye catch sight of the rent and rag of her old grey 
 gown , and she smile somewhat ruefully as she call to mind her 
 gallant knight 's array , which lie now on the shore of the evil and 
 ruin isle ; and her goodly attire of the day of the five craft ; 
 and the rich raiment wherein her friend of the Castle of the Quest 
 have clothe she . then she arise and seek needle and thread and some 
 remnant of green cloth , and do off the ragged coat and fall to 
 patch and mend it , and so sit at her work in smock - sewing till 
 the night be old and she be weary and sleep overcome she , and she 
 lie down in her bed and sleep dreamlessly till the sun be high next 
 morning . 



 CHAPTER XVIII . the wood - MOTHER COMETH to birdalone and heareth she 
 STORY 



 now Birdalone arise and bath she and break she fast , and then go 
 about her work with the beast and the dairy ; but all that time 
 seem long to she till she have bow in hand and quiver on back , and 
 be wend her way to the Oak of Tryst ; and swift be her foot and 
 her heart beat quick with hope of pleasure . 

 Forsooth no long tarrying have she , for scarce have she set she down 
 beneath the oak , ere the wood - mother come forth from the thicket even 
 as the first time when Birdalone see she , and presently she have she 
 arm about Birdalone and be kiss and clip she . then they sit 
 down together in the shade of the great tree , and the wood - mother 
 make much of her friend with few word and those but simple , while 
 birdalone weep for joy . 

 at last spake birdalone : wood - mother , my dear , I look in thy face , 
 and I see thee that thou art nowise change , so that thou call to 
 my mind the birdalone that meet thee here when she be stray from 
 the House of Captivity like to a bird with a string to its leg . 

 Habundia smile on she and say : so it be that now thou look 
 old than I. Rounder and fuller be thy body , and thy limb great 
 and fairer , and thy flesh sleeker ; lovely art thou in all wise , and 
 such as I have think of thee during these year , save that thy face 
 be grow wise and sad than might be look for . Mother , she 
 say , I be grow old than I should be by the tale of the year , for 
 I have have joy and grief , and grief and joy , and grief again ; and now 
 that the year have wear , the grief abideth and the joy hath 
 depart , save this joy of thee and the day of the meeting I have so 
 often think of . 

 say the wood - wife : be I to hear the story of thee , I deem it most 
 like that I would fain buy thy joy with thy grief , both that which 
 have be and that which be to come . and now I will ask thee right 
 out to tell I all thy tale , as much as thou canst ; and all thou 
 canst tell to I , who be thine other self : and I wot moreover that 
 thou hast not tell of I to any whom thou hast meet in the world since 
 we be last together : be it not so ? in faith and in troth so it 
 be , say Birdalone . say Habundia , after she have look hard on 
 birdalone a while : now there be this I find in thee , that though 
 thou call I wood - mother still , thou art not my daughter as thou 
 wert erewhile , nor I thy mother ; and I know not whether to be glad or 
 sorry thereof , since thou art even as much my friend as ever thou 
 wert . but much do I rejoice herein that thou hast not tell any one 
 soul of I . 

 say birdalone : I must tell thee that part of the tale I shall tell 
 thee be how I have find my mother in the flesh , and love she 
 sorely ; and then I lose she again , for she be dead . 

 Quoth the wood - wife , smile on her lovingly : then should I be even 
 more thy mother than erst I be : there will be something else in thy 
 tale , sweetle . 

 then Birdalone flush very red , and she smile piteously in 
 Habundia 's face ; but then she put up her hand to hide the change 
 therein which the anguish of longing wrought , and her shoulder shake 
 and her bosom heave , and she weep bitterly ; but the wood - wife still 
 look on her smiling , and say softly at last : Yea , how sweet it 
 be to be grieve with thy pain . 

 but in a while birdalone grow calm again and the very smile blossom 
 out in her face , and they kiss together . then Habundia rise up and 
 look on she , and say at last and laugh out withal : one thing I 
 must need say , that thou hast not fetch thee raiment of price from 
 the knighthood and the king ' house ; or have I not see thy grey 
 coat of old time , while thou wert live amidst the witch 's cruelty ? 
 Yea forsooth , say Birdalone ; thou need not to ask this . verily 
 not , say Habundia , nor why thou art not clothe in the fair green gown 
 which thou didst broider ; for while I have see the witch flaunt 
 it on the wooden ugly body of she , and thou wouldst not wear it after 
 she have curse it with her foulness . be it not so ? yea , it be even 
 so , say Birdalone ; dost thou love I the less therefor ? Habundia 
 laugh again : be I a man of Adam 's son , say she , I might make 
 thee many word on the seemliness of thy short coat , and the kindness 
 of it , that it will be for ever slip off one or other of thy 
 shoulder . but now be I at least enough thy mother , and thou art 
 dwelling even so much in my house , that the next time we meet   and 
 that shall be to - morrow   I shall fetch thee raiment which shall make 
 we forget that thou camest back again to this land as naked as thou 
 didst depart thence . 

 birdalone redden and hang down her head , but the wood - mother sit 
 down beside she and kiss she and say : but now forget all save thy 
 tale , and tell all as closely as thou mayest , for I would lose nought 
 thereof . Yea , say Birdalone ; and where shall I begin ? say 
 Habundia : I know nought thereof save the beginning , that thou fledst 
 away naked and escape the witch ; and the ending , to wit , that the 
 Sending Boat fail thee at the last of the Wonder Isles , and that 
 thou calledst on I not wholly in vain , whereas the witch be dead , 
 and therefore there be nought to stay I from send thee one of my 
 tree and the wight thereof   whom belike I may show to thee one day 
 to save thee from the bottom of the deep water . 

 at that word Birdalone throw herself on the wood - wife and clip and 
 kiss she , and thank she for the helping with all the dear 
 word she might . but the wood - mother laugh for joy , and stroke 
 her cheek and say : now I deem thee my daughter again , whereas thou 
 thank I with such sweet passion for do to thee as a kind 
 mother need must without any thought thereof . and I bid thee , my 
 dear , never again to go so far from I as that I may not easily help 
 thee and comfort thee from out of my realm wherein I be mighty . and 
 now tell I all in thy dear speech . 

 Therewith Birdalone begin her story without more ado , even as ye have 
 hear it afore . yea and many more thing than we can set down do 
 she tell , for full fill she be with the wisdom of the wood . and 
 between while the wood - mother feed she with dainty meat and drink , 
 such as Birdalone have never erst taste the like of . and by then she 
 have get so far as her flight from the Isle of Increase Unsought , the 
 sun be set and the twilight begin . and the wood - wife say : now 
 shalt thou go home to thine house ; and have no fear of witch or evil 
 thing , for I be not far from thee and will watch over thee . Sweet be 
 thy tale , my daughter , and dear be thy she - friend ; and if ever it 
 may be that I may do they any pleasure , fain be I ; and that 
 especially to thy Viridis , who meseemeth be both sweet and wise even 
 as thou thyself art . Nay , dost thou begrudge my love she ? Nay , 
 nay , say Birdalone , laughing ; but I rejoice in it . and hereafter 
 when I tell thee how sorely they pay for help I , I will bid thee 
 to love they yet more than now thou dost . therewith they part , and 
 birdalone come to her house ; and on the way she make as it be a 
 feign tale in mockery of her old trouble , that there would be the 
 witch - mistress await she to whip she . so that when she come to 
 the door she be half fright with her own mock , lest the witch 
 might now at last have take to walk . 

 but all be quiet when she enter with the last of the twilight , and 
 she rest that night in all peace , as in the good of her day in the 
 Five Crafts . 



 CHAPTER XIX . HABUNDIA HIDETH BIRDALONE 's NAKEDNESS with faery 
 raiment 



 next morning Birdalone tarry about the house as little a while as 
 she might , and then go hasten up to the wood ; and when she come 
 within sight of the Trysting Tree , lo ! there be Habundia before she , 
 and the hand of her busy turn over goodly raiment , so that it be 
 well - nigh as if the day have go back to the time of the Captivity , 
 and the sitter under the oak be Birdalone herself deal with her 
 half - finish gown . 

 joyously they meet and embrace each other , and then spake the wood- 
 wife : now , thou darling of the world , I have be no bad than my 
 word , and if thou durst wear web of the Faery thou shalt presently be 
 clothe as goodly as ever thou wert down there amongst the knighthood ; 
 and then thy tale , my dear , and , if it may be , the wisdom of the 
 barren wood - wife set thereto . 

 and therewith she lay on Birdalone 's outstretche arm the raiment 
 she have bring with she , and it be as if the sunbeam have thrust 
 through the close leafage of the oak , and make its shadow nought a 
 space about Birdalone , so gleam and glow in shifty brightness the 
 broidery of the gown ; and Birdalone let it fall to earth , and pass 
 over her hand and arm the fine smock sew in yellow and white 
 silk , so that the web thereof seem of mingle cream and curd ; and 
 she look on the shoon that lie beside the gown , that be do so 
 nicely and finely that the work be as the feather - robe of a 
 beauteous bird , whereof one scarce can say whether it be bright or 
 grey , thousand - hue or all simple of colour . birdalone quiver for 
 joy of the fair thing , and crow in her speech as she kneel before 
 Habundia to thank she : then in a twinkling have she do off her 
 beggar 's raiment , and then the smock clung about her darling 
 nakedness , and next the gown be shimmer all over she , and the 
 golden girdle embrace her loin as though it love they worthily ; 
 and Birdalone look to the wood round about she and laugh , while 
 Habundia lie in her place and smile upon she with gentle loving- 
 kindness . 

 but in a little while be birdalone sober ; for the thought of how 
 fair she should look to the eye of her beloved when she be show 
 unto he on the day of day , thrust her light and eager pleasure 
 aside ; and she take up her shoe from the ground   for she have not 
 do they on , and sit down beside the wood - wife and fall a - toy 
 with the marvel of they ; and thus without more ado begin her tale 
 again , whereas she have leave it last even , when she have tell of how 
 the Sending Boat be speed she over the water toward the Isle of 
 the Young and the Old . 



 CHAPTER XX . birdalone telleth habundia of her love for ARTHUR , and 
 GETTETH from her promise of help therein 



 long they sit there that day , and until the sun be down , and by then 
 have Birdalone little to tell of her story , for she be get therein 
 to the day of the five craft . many time have she weep and turn 
 to Habundia for solace as she tell , not without shame , but without 
 any covering up , all the tale of her love for Arthur the Black 
 Squire , and how she be surprise by the love of he , and of his 
 wisdom and grace and loveliness . and the wood - mother be ever as 
 sweet and kind unto she as could be ; yet might another than a lover 
 have see that much of all this be strange unto she , and she look 
 upon Birdalone as a child who have break her toy , and be hard to 
 comfort for the loss of it , though there be a many more in the world . 
 but when it grow dusk as aforesaid , and it be time to part , she 
 spake to Birdalone , and say : true it be , my child , that thou hast 
 live long in these six year time ; neither do I wonder at the 
 increase of thy beauty , and the majesty thereof ; for fair be the life 
 thou hast live , although thou hast be grieve and torment by it 
 at while . and now I know what it be for which thou long ; and 
 herein again will I play the mother unto thee , and seek about to 
 fetch thee that thou wouldst have ; so be not over - anxious or 
 trouble ; and thou mayest be good herein , as my fair child should be ; 
 for this I have note in thee , that love be not so tyrannous a master 
 but that his servant may while think of other matter , and so 
 solace their soul , that they may live despite of all . 

 now be Birdalone arise , and stand before her friend confused and 
 blushing . but Habundia put she two hand on her shoulder and kiss 
 she , and say : go home now and sleep , and come again to - morrow and 
 let we hear the last of thy tale ; and when that be do , maybe I 
 shall be able to do something for thine avail . 

 so they part , and on the morrow birdalone come again and tell the 
 remnant of her story , which be not so long now that the Black Squire 
 be out of it . and when she have do , Habundia keep silence awhile , 
 and then she say : one thing I will tell thee , that whereas erewhile 
 it be but seldom indeed that any son of Adam might be see in the 
 woodland here , of late , that is , within the last three year , there 
 be many such amongst we ; and to our deeming they be evil beast , more 
 pitiless and greedy than any bear ; and but that we have nought to do 
 with they , for they fear we and flee from we , we should have 
 destroy they one and all . and now that I have hear all thy story , 
 it seemeth unto I not so unlike but these may be the remnant of the 
 band of the Red Hold , and that they have drift hither flee 
 before the might of thy friend of the knighthood . wherefore now , 
 trust I that I will look into this , but I must need be away from 
 here for a little ; so hold thy soul in patience though hear thou 
 nought of I , and dwell quietly at home for seven day ' space , and 
 then come hither and find I , farewell now , my child ! 

 so they kiss and depart ; and Birdalone go home to the house , 
 and wear the day thereafter do what be needful about the stead , 
 and wander through the meadow , and swim the water about 
 Green Eyot ; and the day be not unrestful unto she . 



 CHAPTER XXI . how the wood - WIFE enter the COT , and a wonder that 
 BEFELL THEREON 



 but when it be the sixth day since those two have meet , Birdalone 
 arise in the morning and stand in the door of the house , and she 
 look toward the bent which go up to the wood and see one come 
 down it , and know it for Habundia clothe in her huntress ' raiment and 
 bear something over the left arm , for her bow be in her right 
 hand . so Birdalone run to meet she , and embrace and kiss she , and 
 be merry over she , and say : dear mother , thou farest far from thy 
 fastness to - day . say Habundia : there be nought in the meadow now 
 save the neat and the goat and thou ; of none of that folk be I 
 afraid . but mayhappen thou shalt be afraid to come with I into the 
 depth of the wildwood , for thither would I lead thee . I will be 
 afraid of nought with thee beside I , say Birdalone . but come now 
 and look upon the house that I have win for I . and she take she 
 hand and lead she along ; and the wood - wife say no more till they be 
 across the brook and standing by the porch . 

 then say birdalone : Thou hast a green gown over thine arm ; be that 
 also for I ? Yea , certe , say Habundia ; the old rag which thou hast 
 on thee , and which thou lov so sore , be not fine enough for my 
 company ; and the glitter - gown I give thee may be too fine for the 
 thorn and the briar , and moreover thou mayst be over - easily see if 
 thou bear that broider sunshine mid the bough . wherefore go in 
 now and do on this other coat , though the faery have make it , and 
 then come out to I with thy bow and thy quiver , and I shall find 
 thee sandal - shoon and girdle withal . 

 Nay , wood - mother , say Birdalone , hallow my house by enter it , and 
 eat a morsel with I and drink the wine of the horn folk ere we go 
 our way . 

 Habundia shake her head and knit she brow somewhat as she look 
 hard on the house ; then she say : I know not , Adam 's daughter ; I 
 have little to do with house , and doubt if a house be safe for I . 
 and this one that the witch build ! and belike she bury some human 
 be at one of its four corner . tell I , fair child , saw thou 
 ever here at night - tide the shape of a youngling crown with a 
 garland stray about the house ? 

 Nay , never at all , say Birdalone . say the wood - wife : then maybe 
 thou hast hallow it with the wisdom and love of thee , and I may 
 venture ; and moreover I note that it be all build of tree and the 
 grass of the earth ; and thou art free to use they by my leave . but 
 if aught befall of my come under thy roof , hee it not too much , 
 but think , whatsoever my aspect may be , I be thy wood - mother and 
 wisdom - mother that loveth thee . and I bid thee also wish with all 
 thy might that my aspect may not change to thee . also , if I eat , 
 thou wert well not to sign the meat as Adam 's son be will not . lead in 
 then ; for now be I grow wilful , and will enter whatever betide . 

 birdalone marvel at those word , but she fall to wish strongly 
 that her friend might not lose her lovely youthful shape either then 
 or ever , and she take her hand , which tremble somewhat , and lead she 
 over the threshold ; and when they be under the roof herseeme that 
 the wood - mother dwindle in a wondrous way , though her face be as 
 sweet and her limb as shapely as ever ; and she laugh shrilly yet 
 sweetly , and spake in a thin clear voice : birdalone , my dear , wish 
 strongly , wish strongly ! though thou shalt see nothing bad of I 
 than this . and she be scarce three foot high , but as pretty as a 
 picture . 

 thereat indeed be birdalone affright , but she wish all she 
 might , and stoop down to kiss this little creature ; and therewith 
 again the wood - wife seem to wax again as great and tall as ever she 
 be , and her voice come full and strong again , as she laugh and 
 say : now be it all over for this time , and I see how well thou 
 lov I ; and I pray thee love I no less for this wonder thou hast 
 see in I . but now it be well that I never go under a roof 
 again . and she take her arm about Birdalone and clip she 
 lovingly ; and glad be birdalone to feel her so strong and solid 
 again . 

 then they sit to the board and eat a simple meal of bread and cheese 
 and wood - berry , and drink milk withal ; and the wood - mother be 
 merry , and the smile dance over her face as she look on Birdalone 
 with all love - kindness , so that Birdalone wonder what be toward ; 
 but so light - hearted and happy she grow , that she deem it might be 
 nought save good . 

 but when they have eat , then Birdalone do off her old coat , which 
 she say be meet enough for her daily toil , and do on the fair 
 green hunting - gown and the sandal - shoon , and girt she with the fair 
 girdle which Habundia have fetch she , and draw up the lap of she 
 gown therethrough till her leg be all free of the skirt . and 
 Habundia look on she , and laugh and say : now be these white 
 and smooth leg as bad as the gleam - gown for the lie hid ; but it 
 may no well be , and thou must draw thy skirt down and stumble , if 
 need must be , when we come to the ambushment . 

 birdalone redden as she laugh at the word , and take down her bow 
 and hang her quiver at she back and thrust her sharp knife into her 
 girdle , and forth they go both of they , and be presently past the 
 bent which go up from the meadow and in amongst Habundia 's tree . 



 CHAPTER XXII . BIRDALONE WENDETH the wildwood in fellowship with 
 habundia 



 now as they go their way lightly through the wood , spake Habundia 
 and say : Birdalone , my child , fair be the gold ring with the 
 sapphire stone that the third finger of thy right hand beareth ; 
 seldom have I see so fair a stone as that deep blue one ; hangeth any 
 tale thereby ? say birdalone : do I not tell thee thereof , wood- 
 mother , how that my beloved who be lose give it unto I the very last 
 time I see he , woe worth the while ? Nay , say Habundia , I mind not 
 the tale . but deem thou he would know it again if he see it ? 
 yea , surely , say Birdalone , hang her head ; for when first he give 
 it , the gift be not to I , but to another woman . and she hold she 
 peace , and go on with hang head and all the glee fade out of 
 she a while . 

 at last she turn to Habundia , and say : I have now bethought I to 
 ask thee whither we be go and on what errand ; for at first I be 
 so glad at heart , I know not why , and it be so merry to be wend 
 the wood with thee freely , that I have no thought in I as to whither 
 and wherefore . but now wilt thou tell I ? 

 say the wood - wife : how if I be to tell thee we be go a- 
 hunting ? birdalone say : then I should ask thee what like the 
 quarry be . and suppose it be man ? say the wood - wife . birdalone 
 turn somewhat pale . my mother , she say , if we be go against 
 some of those man of the Red Bands , I be not happy over it . I be no 
 warrior , and fear stroke . say Habundia , laugh : yet art thou a 
 fall archer ; and thou mayest shoot from an ambush of the thick 
 leaf , since June be in to - day . but neither would I slay or hurt 
 any man , say Birdalone , but it be to save I from present death . 

 Habundia look on she with a sly smile and say : well maybe though 
 we take cover and get within wind of our quarry thou shalt not need 
 to speed an arrow to he . have patience therefore . for this be a 
 strange beast which I have mark down ; he be not ill to look on , and 
 his voice , which we may well hearken , for while he singeth , be 
 rather sweet than surly . what mean thou , mother ? say Birdalone , 
 grow red and then paler yet ; what man be it ? since thy call he 
 a beast be a jest , be it not ? 

 Nay , say Habundia , I neither name he nor know he ; only I deem he 
 by no mean to be one of the Red Band . for the rest , he may be a man 
 in a beast 's skin , or a beast in a man 's skin , for aught I know ; 
 whereas he seem , so far as I have see he , to be not wholly man- 
 like or wholly beast - like . but now let we hold our peace of he till 
 we be come nigh to his haunt . 

 so they go on their way , and Birdalone say but little , while the 
 wood - wife be of many word and gay . they make all diligence , for 
 birdalone be not soon weary , and moreover as now she be anxious 
 and eager to see what would befall , which she might not but deem 
 would be something great . 

 they go without stay till past noon , when they be come to a 
 little shady dale wherethrough run a clear stream ; there they rest 
 and bath they , and thereafter sit under the bough and eat the 
 dainty meat which the wood - wife provide , howsoever she come by it ; 
 and when they have rest a while , the wood - wife turn the talk once 
 more unto Arthur the Black Squire , and would have Birdalone tell she 
 all nicely what manner of man he be ; and Birdalone be nothing loth 
 thereto ; for have she she will she have talk of he day - long . 



 CHAPTER XXIII . the wood - wife bringeth birdalone to the SIGHT of 
 arthur in the wildwood 



 now they go on again , no less speedily than before , and rest but 
 little , until it be hard on an hour before sunset . and now Habundia 
 begin to go warily , as if they be come anigh to their journey 's end 
 and the thing that they seek . they be come by now to a long bent 
 of the forest well grow with big - bole oak - tree , not very close 
 together , so that short fine greensward be all underneath they ; and 
 Habundia go heedfully from bole to bole , as if she would be ready 
 to cover herself if need be ; and Birdalone go after she , and be 
 now flush of face , and her eye glitter , and her heart beat fast , 
 and her leg tremble under she , as she go run from tree to 
 tree . 

 so come they nigh to the crown of the bent , and before they be the 
 oak - tree sparser and small as they go down the further side , 
 which seem by their sudden shortening to be steep than the hither 
 side ; and betwixt they show the topmost of thorn and whitebeam and 
 logwood , intertwine with eglantine and honeysuckle and the new 
 shoot of the traveller 's joy . there the wood - wife put forth her 
 hand to bid Birdalone stay , who come up to her friend and stand 
 before her eager and quivering : and anon come the sound of a man 's 
 voice singing , though they could hear no word in it as yet amidst 
 the rustle of the tree and the tumult of song which the blackbird 
 and throstle raise in the dale below they . 

 then spake the wood - wife softly : Hearken , we be right and the time 
 be good , our beast be give tongue : now below we be the bent - side 
 steep , and goeth down into a very little dale with a clear stream 
 run amidst ; and therein be the very lair of the thing that we be 
 hunting . wherefore now let we slip warily down between the bush 
 till we get close to the bottom , and then belike we shall see the 
 very creature quite close , and we shall then consider and think what 
 we shall do with he . 

 birdalone have no voice wherewith to answer she , but she steal quietly 
 along by her side till they come to the bank of the dale and plunge 
 into the thicket that flourish there , and fall to thread it , 
 make they as small as might be . but ere they have go but a little 
 way the wordless song of what be below have cease , and they hear 
 the sweet tingle of the string - play , and the wood - wife stay she to 
 hearken , and the smile go ripple over her face and she beat time 
 with her finger ; but Birdalone , she star wildly before she , and 
 would have scramble down the bank straightway at all hazard , for 
 that string - play be a melody of the Castle of the Quest , but 
 Habundia withhold she by the arm . and then suddenly the music die , 
 and there come up a voice of wail and lament , and Birdalone put 
 her hand and hold the palm tight against her ear , and be at point 
 to cry out aloud herself ; but Habundia draw a hand of she down and 
 whisper into her ear : child , child , make thyself strong and 
 forbear , and then perchance joy may come to thee ; hold thy peace and 
 come softly along with I ! 

 so Birdalone forbore , and strove with her passion , though the sob 
 rent her bosom for a while ; and by then the loud lamenting wan and 
 be do , and the sound of sobbing come up from below , as it have be 
 an echo of Birdalone 's grief . 

 then Habundia draw she on again till they see the level of the dale 
 and its stream piecemeal betwixt the leaf , and they have a glimpse 
 of a man on the hither side of the stream ; and again they go lower , 
 till they be well - nigh on a level with the greensward of the dale ; 
 and as Birdalone kneel with head bent low , and her hand cover her 
 eye , the wood - wife put away from before she the thick leaf of a 
 hazel - bush , and whispering say : child , child ! look forth now and 
 see what be before thee , and see if thou know he , or if he be 
 strange to thee , and thy mother hath do nought for thee when all be 
 say . 

 birdalone look up , pale and wild - eye , and into the dale , and see a 
 man sit on the grass by the stream - side with his head bow down 
 on to his knee and his face cover with his hand ; he be clothe but 
 in two or three deerskin hang about he , with a strip of skin for a 
 girdle , wherein be thrust a short sword ; his brown hair hang down 
 long and shaggy over his face . close by his side lie a little harp , 
 and far off a short spear roughly heft with an ash - staff . he 
 be beat the earth with his foot and writhe he about over they . 
 and Birdalone look , and her breath well - nigh fail she . for 
 presently he sit more quietly , and lift up his head , and she see 
 his face that it be Arthur , her beloved ; and now she durst not move 
 lest he should spring up and flee away ; and the mingled pain and 
 longing within she be sweet indeed , but well - nigh deadly . 

 now his hand seek round to his harp , and he take it in his arm and 
 fondle it as it be , and his finger go among the string , and 
 anon the voice of it come forth , and it be nought change from the 
 last time it spake , and birdalone hearken breathlessly , till the 
 melody die again and Arthur look about he and raise his face as 
 a dog when it fare to howl . 

 then Birdalone give a great cry , and leapt forth out of the thicket 
 and stand on the greensward with nought betwixt they two , and she 
 stretch out her arm to her beloved and cry out : o ! no , no , no ! 
 do it not , I beseech thee , lest I deem that thou art all change , and 
 that the man and the dear heart belove of thee have go out of thee 
 and leave thee but a beast in a man 's shape ! 

 he leap up as she spake , and thrust forward his head and look 
 fierce at she , and cry out : what ! art thou come again ? this be 
 the second time I have see thee , thou image of she that hath 
 torment I so long ; of she that leave I in my most need and hide 
 herself away from I . hah ! a man , sayest thou ? do I not strive 
 with it , and hold my manhood so long as I might ; and at last it might 
 no long be , and I become a beast and a man - slayer ? but what avail 
 it to talk with thee , since thou art but the image of she that hath 
 waste my life . yet perchance of the image I may make an end since I 
 may not lay hand on the very destroyer herself ; and , woe 's me , how I 
 love she ! yea , and do still ; but not thee , o false image ! 

 and forthwith he draw the blade from his girdle and spring forward at 
 Birdalone ; and she cower and cringe , but move not else . but 
 therewithal the wood - wife come leap through the bush , and she 
 nock an arrow on her bended bow , and threaten he therewith , and 
 cry out : thou man - beast , I will slay thee if thou hurt my child 
 and my dear ; so forbear ! Nay , I tell thee more , unless thou make she 
 as glad at the sight of thee as I mean she to be , I will in the long 
 run slay thee ; so look to it . 

 he laugh and say : what ! there be another image of the love that 
 waste I , be there ! nay , but by the Hallows , this new - comer be the 
 first one , and the one who chatter at I be the second . or be it 
 this , that all woman now have the semblance of the evil one that have 
 undo I , and there be nought else leave ? 

 and he stand star at Birdalone and move not a while ; and she 
 stand with her hand before her face cringe before he . then he 
 raise his arm and cast the weapon far into the bush of the bank- 
 side , and then come forward and stand before Birdalone , and draw down 
 her hand from her face and star in the eye of she , hold she by 
 the two arm ; and he say : Thou hast forget now , belike , how fair 
 a life we two might have live if thou hadst not flee from I and 
 spoil I . 

 and thou ! by the look of thee , for thou art sleek and fair , though 
 this moment thou art pale for fear of I , thou hast live a happy 
 life through all these year , with many a merry thing to think of : 
 and dost thou deem that my life be happy , or that I think of any 
 merry thing , or of anything save my sorrow ? dost thou doubt it ? go 
 ask the good spear of Greenford , or the Riders of the Red Hold , and 
 the field of the slaughter ! if there be little joy there , less be 
 there elsewhere . 

 he leave go of her therewith and stand tremble before she , and she 
 bow down and put palm to palm and hold they out to he as one who 
 pray ; and she know not what she do . 

 then he cry out with a lamentable cry and say : o woe 's I ! for I 
 have fright she and scar the wit out of she , so that she know 
 not who I be nor what I would ; and I would pray to she and beseech 
 she to pity I , and not depart from I again or mock I with image 
 of herself . 

 then he go down on his knee to she , and he also join his hand 
 to pray to she ; but it seem as if she be stricken to stone , so 
 wholly she move not . but for he , he sink his forehead to earth ,
