 he , and he be dead ! he be dead , and I have slay he , because I be 
 a timorous fool and fear he ; and he be come to his right mind 
 and know I for what I be ! 

 but Habundia come and stand over they , and draw up Birdalone , and 
 say : Nay , nay , be comfort ! for now he be thus , and the strength 
 be go out of he for a while , we may deal with he . Abide , and I 
 will fetch the blood - staunch herb and the sleepy herb , and then we 
 will heal he , and he will come to his right mind and be a man again . 

 Therewith she hasten away and be go but a little ; and meanwhile 
 birdalone kneel down by her love and wipe the blood from he , and 
 caress his sword - harden hand and moan over he . but when the 
 wood - wife come back she put Birdalone aside once more , and kneel down 
 by the squire and raise his head , and lay the blood - stauncher to 
 his mouth and his heart , and mutter word over he , while Birdalone 
 look over her shoulder with her pale face ; then the she - leech 
 fetched water from the stream in a cup which she draw from her 
 wallet , and she wash his face , and he come somewhat to himself , so 
 that she might give he drink of the water ; and yet more he come to 
 himself . so then she take the sleepy herb and bruise it in her 
 hand and put in his mouth and again say word over he , and 
 presently his head fall back and his eye close and he sleep 
 peacefully . 

 she stand up then and turn to Birdalone and say : now , my child , 
 have we do all that we may do , save that we shall bring he to a 
 place where the dew and the sun shall not torment he and sicken he ; 
 for he shall lie thus till the sun come up to - morrow , or long ; and 
 fear nor , for when he awaketh he shall be in his right mind , and 
 shall know thee and love thee . this I swear to thee by the earth and 
 the sun and the woodland . 

 Said Birdalone , tremble yet : o mother , but may I kiss he and 
 caress he ? yea , surely , say the wood - mother , smile in her face , 
 but be not too long over it , for lo ! the last of the sun , and it be 
 well that he be under cover ere the twilight fall . 

 birdalone kneel down by her love quietly at that word , and fall to 
 kiss he softly , and lay she cheek to his , and call he gentle 
 name such as none can tell again without shame , till the wood - wife 
 lay her hand on her shoulder and say kindly and sweetly : rise up 
 now , for thou must make it enough for this present ; thou shalt have 
 time enough hereafter for more and much more . 

 so Birdalone arise and say : how shall we bear he to his place ? 
 shall I not take he by the shoulder and thou by the leg ? for I be 
 strong than thou after all these year . 

 laugh the wood - wife : Nay , little one , say she ; thou know I 
 not utterly as yet . Thou shalt not bear he at all , nor any part of 
 he ; I be strong enough for more than that ; see thou ! and she 
 stoop down and take he up in her arm as if he be a little 
 child , and step off lightly with he ; but look back over her 
 shoulder and say to Birdalone : but thou may walk by I and hold 
 a hand of he as we go , though it will hinder I somewhat ; but I know 
 thine heart and would pleasure thee , my child . 

 birdalone run up to she and thank she and kiss she , and take 
 Arthur 's left hand , while Habundia bear he on down the dale and out 
 of it , and still along the stream till they come to a place where it 
 be narrow on either side thereof , and a sheer rock come down so near 
 to the water that there be but a strip of greensward three yard 
 wide betwixt water and rock ; and in the face of the rock be a cave 
 wide enough for a man to enter by stoop somewhat . therein the 
 wood - wife lightly bore Arthur , and Birdalone follow ; and they find 
 the cave dry and roomy within ; there be a bed therein of dry heather 
 and bracken , and thereon Habundia lay her burden , and say : now , my 
 child , there be nought to do but abide till he come to himself 
 again , which may be some time to - morrow ; and be of good cheer , for he 
 will come to his right self , but he will be weak and humble ; but I 
 shall have meat and drink ready for he . now if thou wilt be rule 
 by I , thou wilt keep out of the way when he awaken ; moreover , be 
 thou not scared if I meet his awakening with another shape than that 
 which thou hast know of I ; for sure it be that it will trouble his 
 wit over - much if again he seeth the two of we alike . but fear not ; 
 for thy sake , my child , I will take no ugly shape , though it may well 
 be less beauteous than thine . 

 I will do what thou wilt , mother , say Birdalone , for I see that thou 
 art help I all thou mayest ; yet I beseech thee let I sit by he 
 till the time of his awakening draweth nigh . 

 the wood - wife smile and nod yeasay on she , and they sit down , 
 both of they , beside the sleep man , and the day die into the 
 night as they sit hearken to the ripple of the brook and the song 
 of the nightingale . 



 CHAPTER XXIV . the wood - MOTHER CHANGETH she form to that of a woman 
 STRICKEN in YEARS 



 when the morrow come , there yet lie Arthur sleep peacefully , and 
 birdalone awake from the slumber which have at last fall on she , and 
 look about she and see not Habundia in the cave ; so she arise and 
 bent over Arthur and kiss he , and so go forth and stand in the 
 door and look about she . and she be still dim - eyed with she just 
 depart slumber and the brightness of the morning sunlight , and she 
 scarce know whether it be a part of a dream , or a sight that be 
 verily before she , that she seem to see one come across the brook 
 toward she , step heedfully from stone to stone thereof : a woman 
 stricken in year , but slim and trim and upright , clothe in a gown of 
 green cloth , with a tippet of some white fur . when she be come on 
 to the greensward she spake to Birdalone in a sweet voice , but thin 
 with eld , and give she the sele of the day ; and Birdalone be 
 somewhat afraid to see a newcomer , but she greet she , draw back 
 a little from she shyly . but the old woman say : what maketh thee 
 here , my daughter ? dost thou not know that this be my land and my 
 house , and that I be say not to be unmighty in these wood ? 

 I pray thee pardon I if I have do amiss , say Birdalone ; but here 
 have I a sick friend , a young man , and I would pray thee suffer he 
 to abide here in this cave a little long ; for there hath be also 
 another friend , a woman , but she hath go out while I sleep , belike 
 to gather simple , for she be wise in leechcraft , and be tend the 
 sick man . I pray thee humbly to suffer we lest we lose our friend . 

 as she spake , she hear the carline chuckle softly , and at last she 
 say : why , Birdalone , my dear , dost thou not know I after all these 
 year ? look on I again , look ! and thou shalt see that I be not so 
 much change from what thou sawest I last night . I be still thine 
 image , my dear , only I be the image of what thou wert , and now I the 
 image of what thou shalt be when two score year and ten of happy 
 life have wear for thee . tell I , be I now aught like to thy mother 
 in the flesh ? 

 how hast thou fright I , mother , say Birdalone ; I think that my 
 friend have forsake I , and that perchance the new - comer be another 
 witch like unto the old one , and that I be never to be at rest and 
 happy . but as to my mother in the flesh , nay , thou art not now 
 wholly like unto she ; and sooth to say I shall be fainer when thou 
 hast thine own shape of I young back again , for I love thee not so 
 much as now thou art . 

 the wood - wife laugh : well , she say , thou shalt not see over - much 
 of I in this shape ; and that the less because of something I shall 
 now tell thee , to wit , that I have be think the matter over , and 
 I would have thee leave we twain together alone before the young man 
 awaketh . I would have thee get thee home and abide he there ; it 
 shall not be long I promise thee ; and this also , that he shall come 
 home to thee sound in body and whole in limb . 

 Birdalone 's countenance fall , and she say : why this second mind , 
 mother ? why , I pray thee ? say Habundia : I fear for thy love lest 
 he be not strong enough to open his eye upon thy face ; but after he 
 hath be a day in the wood , and I have speak to he diversely and 
 cheer he with the hope of meet thee , he may well be strong 
 enough to seek thee for a mile 's length , and find thine house first 
 and then thee . so now wilt thou obey I ? Nay , if thou must need 
 weep , I will be go into the thicket till thou hast do , thou 
 wilful ! birdalone smile through her tear , and say : I pray thee 
 pardon my wilfulness , mother , and I will depart without turn back 
 into the cave . Nay , say Habundia , there be no need for so much 
 haste as that : I will in now , and do my leechdom with the sick man . 
 but do thou go across the stream , thou barefoot , and thou wilt find 
 on the other side , by the foot of the quicken - tree yonder , honeycomb 
 and white bread and a bicker of wild goat ' milk . bathe thee then if 
 thou wilt , and bring those matter over hither ; and then shalt thou 
 go in and kiss thy mate 's sick face with thy fresh one , and 
 thereafter shall we sit here by the ripple of the water and break our 
 fast ; and lastly , thou shalt go in and kiss again and then take to 
 the road . but tell I , deemest thou surely that thou canst find it 
 again ? yea , surely , mother , say Birdalone ; I be wood - woman enough 
 for that ; and now I will do all thy will . and therewith she step 
 out lightly on to the greensward and seek up the stream till she 
 find a smooth - ground pool meet for her bath , and when that be 
 do , she fetch the victual and come back to the wood - wife ; then 
 they two sit down together , and eat and drank while the water ripple 
 at their foot . but when they be do , birdalone gat she into the 
 cave again , and kiss the sleep man fondly , and come forth 
 lightly and stand a moment before the wood - wife , and say : tell I 
 this at least , mother , when shall he be there ? to - morrow quoth the 
 wood - wife ; and , for my part , I would keep thee within door and abide 
 he there , lest there be trouble ; for he may not yet be as strong as 
 the strong . birdalone hang down her head and answer not , but 
 say presently : Farewell , wood - mother , and be thou blessed . then 
 she take up her bow and betook she lightly to the woodland way , and 
 the wood - wife stand look at she till the thicket have hide she , 
 and then turn back and go into the cave . 



 CHAPTER XXV . the wood - wife HEALETH and TENDETH the black SQUIRE 



 she stand over Arthur for a minute or two , and then stoop down and 
 whisper a word in his ear , and presently he stir on the bed and 
 half open his eye , but straightway turn on his side , as if to 
 gather sleep to he , but she take he by the shoulder and say in a 
 clear voice : Nay , knight , nay ; hast thou not sleep enough ? be there 
 nought for thee to do ? he sit up in the bed and rub his eye , and 
 his face be come to its wholesome colour , and his eye look out 
 quietly and calmly as he look about the cave and see the wood - wife 
 stand by he ; and he spake in a voice which be somewhat weak , but 
 wherein be no passion of rage or woodness : where be I then ? and who 
 art thou , dame ? she say : thou art in a cave of the woodland , and I 
 be for one thing thy leech , and meseemeth thou desir to eat and to 
 drink . he smile and nod his head ; and she fetch he the milk , 
 and he drink a long draught , and sigh thereafter , as one who be 
 pleased ; and she smile on he , and fetch he the bread and the 
 honey , and he eat and drink again , and then lie down and fall fast 
 asleep . and she suffer his slumber for two hour or so , and then 
 awake he again ; and again he ask where he be and what be she , 
 but she say as before . and say she : the next thing thou hast to 
 do be to arise , as thou well mayest , and take this raiment , which be 
 fair and clean , and go wash thee in the brook and come back to I ; 
 and then we will talk , and thou shalt tell I of how it be with 
 thee , and peradventure I may tell thee somewhat of how it shall be 
 with thee . as she speak she go to a coffer which stand in a nook 
 of the cave , and draw forth from it a shirt and hosen and shoon , and 
 a surcoat and hood of fine black cloth , and a gilded girdle and a 
 fair sword , red - sheathe , and say : these may serve thy turn for the 
 present , so take they and don they , and thou shalt look like a squire 
 at least , if not a knight . 

 so he arise as one in a dream and go out ; but as he pass by she 
 she see something gleam on his breast , and note that it be 
 Birdalone 's fair sapphire ring which hang about his neck ; so she 
 smile , and say under her breath : Crafty be my dear daughter ! but 
 that shall save I some word at least . and she abide his return . 

 Anon he cometh back clothe in the fair raiment , with the sword by his 
 side ; and the wood - wife smite her palm together and cry out : now 
 indeed thou art fair and well - liking , and a fair lady might well take 
 pleasure in behold thee . 

 but his brow be knit , and he look sullen and angry , and he say : 
 what be all this play ? and where gatt thou this ring which I find 
 e'en now about my neck ? and who art thou , and why have I be 
 bring hither ? 

 his eye look fiercely on she as he spake , hold out his palm 
 with the ring lie thereon . but the wood - wife answer : many 
 question , fair youth ! but I will tell thee : the play be for thine 
 healing and pleasure , whereas both sick hast thou be and sorry . as 
 to the ring , it be thou hast get it and not i. but I will tell thee 
 this , that I have see it on the finger of a fair damsel who haunteth 
 the woodland not far hence . as to what I be , that be a long tale 
 to tell if I tell it all ; but believe this meanwhile , that I be the 
 lady and mistress of hereabout , and be not without power over my 
 folk and my land . and as to why thou wert bring hither , I bring 
 thee because I have no well house handy for a sick man to lie in . 

 then Arthur stand a long while consider the ring that lie on his 
 palm , and at last he put his hand on the wood - wife 's shoulder , and 
 look into her face beseechingly , and say : o mother , if thou be 
 mighty be merciful withal , and have pity on I ! Thou callest I a 
 youth , and so I may be in regard to thee ; but I tell thee it be five 
 long year and there hath be no other thought in my heart but what 
 be loathsome to I , and it hath wear and waste my youth , so that it 
 waneth and withereth and be nought . o , if thou be mighty , bring I 
 to she that I may see she at least one time before I die . and 
 therewith he fall down on his knee before she , and kiss the hem of 
 her gown , and weep . but she draw he up and look on he with the 
 merry countenance of a kind old woman , and say : Nay , nay , I be not 
 so hard to be win to thy help that thou needest pray so sore and 
 weep : here need we tarry no long , and if thou wilt come with I we 
 shall go seek the damsel who bear this ring , though how it should 
 come to thee why should I know ? neither do I know if the said ring- 
 bearer be the one woman whom thou needest . but I will tell thee at 
 once that she be a dear friend of mine . 

 then Arthur throw his arm about she , and kiss her cheek and 
 bless she , while she laugh on he and say : Nay , fair sir , if 
 thou wilt do so much with the withered branch , what wilt thou with 
 the blossom of the tree ? and he be abash before she , but hope 
 make his heart to dance . 

 so the wood - wife take up her bow , sling her quiver at she back , and 
 girt her short sword to she , and then lead he forth , and so into the 
 thicket out of the dale and forth into the oaken bent , and lightly 
 she lead he thereafter through the woodland . 



 CHAPTER XXVI . the black squire TELLETH the wood - wife of his doing 
 since BIRDALONE go from the castle of the quest 



 as they go Habundia say to Arthur : now shalt thou talk and tell 
 for the shortening of the way , and let we know somewhat of thy story . 
 but first I must tell thee , for thou mayest not know it , so witless 
 as then thou wast , that yesterday we find thee down in the dale 
 yonder , play the string - play sweetly indeed , but otherwise dight 
 like a half beast more than a man , so that we wonder at thee and 
 pity thee . 

 Arthur knit his brow as if he strove with some memory and might not 
 master it ; then he say : Thou sayest we , who then be the other ? 
 say Habundia : I have a dear friend with I . Quoth he : and do she 
 pity I also ? yea , say the wood - wife , else scarce have she be a 
 friend to I . o let we on swiftly , say Arthur , so long as the time 
 may be ! and they quicken their pace and eat up the way speedily . 

 presently spake the wood - wife again : now for the tale of thee , fair 
 sir ; yet will I shorten it somewhat by tell thee that I know thy 
 name , that thou art Arthur the Black Squire of the Castle of the 
 Quest . he star at that word , and say : how knew thou this ? how 
 couldst thou guess it , who hast never see I erst ? a friend tell 
 I , say she ; too long it be as now to tell thee thereof . rather 
 do thou tell I how thou didst fare when ye find thy friend go 
 from the castle that time ye come home from the winning of the Red 
 Hold . 

 Arthur star astonished , and say : what be it ? dost thou verily 
 know my love ? or art thou a sorceress and know somewhat of I by 
 spell - work ? I be somewhat more than a sorceress , may - happen , say 
 the wood - wife ; but heed it not , since I be thy friend to - day , but 
 tell I what I ask , that I may have all the tale of thee ; it will 
 serve for the shortening of the way . Said Arthur : and who but I 
 needeth it as short as may be ? so stand we not loiter here , and I 
 will talk as we wend on speedily . 

 on they speed therefore , and say Arthur : how do I fare ? as one 
 stun , mother , and know not what have happen ; and when I hear 
 their babble of how she have do wrong here and right there , I be 
 drive half mad by it , so that I hasten back to the Red Hold , and 
 become the captain of Greenford , to hunt down their scattered foeman ; 
 for I say to myself that need must I rage and slay , and that be 
 worse amongst my friend than mine unfriend . what then ? that 
 business come to an end ; though all the ill man be not slain , but 
 all be drive away from the part of Greenford ; and sooth to say 
 they durst not come anywhere nigh where they hear of I . then 
 become each day like every other , and the thought of my hope and my 
 despair eat mine heart out , and I be of no avail unto any . now it 
 so happen , amidst my many battle and chase , I have hunt the 
 band of the Red Hold into the northw march of the woodland ; and 
 I note that even they , howsoever hard bestead , and the bad of man 
 to boot , would scarce at the first be drive into the thicket 
 thereof , though at last , whether or no they have make covenant with 
 the devil , there I know not , they have betake they to the depth of 
 the wood and have bear off woman from the dwelling and get child 
 on they , and be like to breed an evil folk . that then I note that 
 this Evilshaw be a dwelling loathe and desert , and little like it 
 be that any would meddle with I there . three year have wear since 
 I be cast away at the Castle of the Quest by she that love I , who 
 must need sacrifice both she and I to the busy devil of folly ; and 
 I also deem that if I seek for she I should not find she ; and yet 
 more forsooth , that if I find she she would be as hard unto I as 
 when she flee from I . and as for I , I be get hard and crab , 
 and no man , if his heart would let he , would have aught to say to 
 I . so I gat I away from the Red Hold , as I have from the Castle of 
 the Quest , and I give out that I would enter into religion , and 
 forbid any man to follow I . neither do any desire it . first of 
 all I set I down at the very outskirt of the woodland , and raise 
 I a bower there , rude and ill - shapen . few folk come anigh I , and 
 yet some few , charcoal - burner , and hunter of the edge of the wood , 
 and suchlike . these deem I a holy man , whereas I be but surly . 
 somewhat also they fear I , whereas in some of their hunting or 
 going and coming after prey I have put forth all my strength , eke 
 out by the lore of knighthood , which be strange to they . one man 
 there be of they who be fashion of the minstrel craft by nature , 
 and who forgather with I specially , till we become friend , and he 
 be a solace to I , with his tale and his song of a rougher people 
 than I have be will not to deal with . but when I have be in that place 
 for two year he die of a sickness , and I be leave lonely , and my 
 soreness of heart fall upon I till I scarce know what next I should 
 do . so I fare away yet deep into the wildwood , take with I the 
 harp which my friend have give I before he die . it be summer , and 
 I wander about ever deep into the wood , until belike I have scarce 
 be able to win out of it if I have try . at last , when the autumn 
 come , I build myself again some sort of a bower in a clearing of the 
 wood wherein be water , and the resort of plenteous venison . 

 what befell next ? my mind be not over - clear concern it all , for I 
 be now become more of a beast than a man . but this I know , that 
 some man of the band whom I have chase happen on I . they know I 
 not for their old foeman , but of their kind it be to torment and 
 slay any man whom they might lightly overcome . yet be not the 
 battle so over - light but that I slew and hurt diver of they ere they 
 get I under and strip I and bind my hand and torment I , 
 after the manner that the devil shall do with they when they shall 
 go to their reward . yet somehow I live , though they deem I dead , 
 and I crawl away thence when they be go ; and somehow I be 
 heal of my body , but I be confused of my wit thereafter , and now 
 can call to mind but little of what befell I as I stray from place 
 to place , save that I remember I be hapless and heart - sore ever : 
 and also meseemeth that I see vision at while , and those who have 
 be in my life before these thing , their image would come before 
 I to mock I as I sit sing while and while play the string- 
 play   for my harp I bear ever with I ; and while I bewail I , and 
 call for help on they that would not or might not help I . and now 
 I may not even tell the year of my abiding in the desert , how many 
 they be . but I pray thee let we on more swiftly yet . 

 say the wood - wife : Thou hast tell I but little of thy life , black 
 Squire , but it be enough maybe ; and I see that thou mayst not tell I 
 more because thou hast thy mind set on what may betide thee when this 
 day be over . but thou must know that thou hast come into the wood of 
 Evilshaw , wherein , besides those savage man who quell thee and 
 their like , there be uncouth thing no few , and wight that be not of 
 the race of Adam ; wherefore no great marvel be it that thou saw 
 vision , and image of they that be not by thee . Yea , say he , but 
 one vision have I that confuse and overcome I more than all other , 
 and meseemeth that come to I not long ago . for first I see the 
 shape of she that my soul desireth ever , and it weep and lament for 
 I ; and then for a little I seem as if I be come forth from my 
 confusion of wit ; when lo ! there issue from the thicket another 
 image of my beloved and blame I and threaten I . God wot good 
 cause there be of the blame . but tell I , mother , since thou 
 call thyself wise , what may this portend ? 

 the wood - wife laugh : since I be wise , say she , I will foretell 
 thee good day . and now we will talk no more of thee or thy love or 
 thy sorrow , but since thou wilt so fiercely devour the way , I will 
 tell thee a tale or two of this wood and its wight to save we from 
 over - much weariness . 

 so do she , talk and tell as they go ; and she go on a pace 
 before he , and howsoever long or hardly he might stride he might not 
 overgo she . and so fast they go , that they be within a little 
 way of the Oak of Tryst a good while before the sun have set , though 
 they have set out from the cave three hour after the hour when 
 Birdalone and the wood - wife have leave the House under the Wood on the 
 yesterday . they have come to a steep rock that rise up from a water 's 
 side , and the wood - wife bade stay , whether Arthur would or no , and 
 she make he eat and drink , bring the victual and wine from out of 
 a cleft in the said rock . and she hold he there till the night be 
 come and there be a glimmer of the rise moon in the east , and he 
 be ill at ease and restless ; but still she hold he there till the 
 moon rise high and shine upon they , and the shadow of the oak - bough 
 lie black all around . 

 then she bid he arise , and let he on to the Oak of Tryst , yea and 
 somewhat beyond it toward the great water . then she spake to he : 
 Black Squire , I be now come home , and will lead thee no far ; I 
 be deeming that we should have sleep in the wood a good way from 
 this , and then would I have bring thee on thy way to - morrow 
 morning ; but the eagerness of thine heart hath make thy foot so 
 speedy , that we be here somewhat rathe , and yet I be not ill - please 
 therewith . then she turn he about and say : look down the bent 
 and tell I what thou seest . he say : I see the bole of goodly 
 tree , and betwixt they the gleaming of a great water . she say : go 
 thitherward then while the moon be yet at she bright , and thou 
 shalt presently come to wide mead lie along the water , and a 
 stream run through they . enter then into the mead and look 
 about thee , and thou shalt see a little house   there be none other 
 nigh   stand just across the said stream ; go up thither boldly and 
 crave gueste from whomsoever thou shalt find there , and maybe 
 thing shall go after thy mind . More than this I may not do for 
 thee . farewell then , and if thou wilt thou mayst meet I again ; that 
 be to say , that which be verily I : but it be like that this shape 
 which hath be stride on with thee daylong thou shalt not see any 
 more . 

 he look on her wondering , for she seem to grow goodly and stately 
 before his eye . but even as he stretch forth his hand to take 
 her , she turn about suddenly and fare into the wood out of his 
 sight , wend full as swiftly as might have be look for . then 
 he draw his sword and turn his face from the wood , and go down 
 toward the water . 



 CHAPTER XXVII . SIR ARTHUR COMETH to the HOUSE under the wood 



 so come Arthur into the meadow , and go eagerly but warily over the 
 dewy grass . and here and there a cow rise before he and go 
 bundle down the mead a little way , and the owl cry out from 
 behind he , and a fox bark from the thicket 's edge . then he find 
 himself on the stream - side , and he stay and look from side to 
 side , and lo ! on the other side of the stream a little house that 
 look familiar to he as a yeoman 's dwelling in the build land , 
 and the thatch thereon shine under the moon and its window be 
 yellow with candle - light ; and so homely it seem to he , that he 
 thrust his sword into the sheath and lightly cross the brook , and 
 come to the door and lay his hand upon the latch and lift it and 
 shove the door , and all be open before he . 

 his eye , come from the night , dazzle with the bright light of the 
 candle , but he see a fair woman rise up in her place , and he say : 
 may a traveller in the woodland be welcome here to - night , deal 
 with all in all honour ? 

 but the woman come toward he hold out her two hand , and ere he 
 could cry out that he know she , she have throw herself upon he , and 
 have cast her arm about he and be kiss his face , and murmur : 
 o welcome indeed ! welcome , welcome , and welcome ! and so sore do his 
 past grief and his desire move he , that he be weak before she , and 
 hold down his hand and let she do . and both those be breathless 
 with wonder and joy and longing ; and they stand aloof a little in a 
 while and look on each other , she with heave bosom and streaming 
 eye , and he with arm stretch forth and lip that strove with his 
 heart 's word and might not utter they ; but once more she give 
 herself to he , and he take she in his arm strongly now , so that she 
 be frail and weak before he , and he lay his cheek to her cheek and 
 his lip to her lip , and kiss her eye and her shoulder and 
 murmur over she . and then again they stand apart , and she take he 
 by the hand and lead he to the settle , and set he down by she , and 
 herself by he ; and a while they say nought . then she spake as one 
 who have come to herself and be calm , though her heart be aflame for 
 love : tell I , love , when thine hand be on the latch didst thou 
 look to find I here in this house ? for thine hand it be that wake 
 I ; I hear not thy foot before the threshold , for I be weary and 
 slumber . alas ! that I lose the sound of thy foot ! he spake , and 
 his voice sound false unto he , as if it come from another 's mouth : 
 I wot not ; the woman that lead I nearby seem to bid I hope . then 
 he say : Nay , the sooth be that I should have die if I have not 
 find thee here ; I have be sick so long with hop . 

 again be they silent till she say : I would that I have hear thee 
 cross the brook . but the wood - wife bid I look for thee no 
 early than to - morrow ; else have I time enough ; and I would have make 
 the house trim with the new green bough , and dighte our bed with 
 rise bloom ; and I would have do on I my shin gown that the 
 wood - wife give I . for indeed she be but clothe in her scanty smock 
 and nought else . 

 but he lay his head on her bosom and kiss she all about , and say : 
 Nay , my own love , it be well , it be well . and she murmur over 
 he : o friend , my dear , think not that I have will to hide I from 
 thee . all that be here of I be thine , and thine , and thine . 

 and she take his hand and they arise together , and she say : O 
 friend , I flee from thee once and leave thee lonely of I because I 
 deem need drave I to it ; and I fear the strife of friend , and 
 confusion and tangle . now if thou wilt avenge thee on I thou 
 mayest , for I be in thy power . yet will I ask thee what need will 
 drive thee to leave I lonely ? 

 he say : the need of death . but she say : mayhappen we shall lie 
 together then , as here to - night we shall lie . 



 CHAPTER XXVIII . fair day in the HOUSE of LOVE 



 on the morrow it be sweet time betwixt those twain , and what be 
 hard and fierce of their love they seem to have put behind they . a 
 dear joy it be to Birdalone that day to busy herself about the 
 housekeeping , and to provide whatsoever seem now , or have seem to 
 she in her early day , to be dainty of their meadow and woodland 
 husbandry , as cream and junket and wood - fruit and honey , and fine 
 bread make for that very occasion . 

 Withal she be careful as a mother with a child that he should not 
 over - weary himself with the sun of the early summer , but rather to 
 follow the brook up into the wood and lie adown in the flecked shadow 
 and rest he wholly , as if there be nought for he to do but to 
 take in rest all that be do for his service , both by the earth and 
 by the hand and nimble foot of Birdalone . and as she be wilful in 
 other way of her cherishing , so also in this , that for nought in 
 that daylight would she go anywise disarray , nay not so much as to 
 go barefoot , though he pray she thereof sorely , and tell she that 
 fair and sweet she be in her smock alone than in any other 
 raiment . for in the morning she go in her woodland green let down 
 to her heel , and when the day wear towards evening , and the wind 
 come cool from over the Great Water , then she do on her wonder- 
 raiment which the wood - wife have give she , and lead Arthur over the 
 meadow here and there , and go gleam by the side of the black- 
 clothe man along the water 's lip . and they look forth on to Green 
 Eyot and Rock Eyot , and stand by the shallow bight where she have 
 bath those time ; and they go along to the dismal creek where the 
 Sending Boat be will not to lie , and where yet lie the scattered stave 
 of it ; and then along the meadow - land they go from end to end , 
 rest oft on the flowery grass , till the dew begin to fall and the 
 moon cast shadow on the greensward . then home they fare to the 
 house ; and again on the way must birdalone feign for their disport 
 that the witch be come back again , and be await she to play the 
 tyrant with she ; and Arthur fall in with her game , and kiss she and 
 clip she , and then draw his sword and say : by all - hallow I 
 shall smite off her head if she but lie a finger on thee . 

 so they play like two happy child till they come to the door of 
 the house , and Birdalone shove it open , and they two look in 
 together and see nought worse therein save the strange shadow that 
 the moon cast from the settle on to the floor . then Birdalone draw 
 in her love , and go about light the candle and quicken a 
 little cooking fire on the hearth , till the yellow light chase the 
 moon away from the bed of their desire . 



 CHAPTER XXIX . those TWAIN will seek the wisdom of the wood - wife 



 again next day be their life such as it have be the day before ; and 
 as they lie in cool shadow of a great oak , Birdalone fall to tell 
 Arthur all the whole story of her dealing with the wood - wife , and 
 how that she have so love she and holpen she , that through her love 
 and her help she have escape the witch and her snare , who would have 
 turn she into a half - devil for the undoing of manfolk . and how 
 that the say wood - wife have never appear to she but as an image and 
 double of herself , save on the time when she play the leech to he . 
 then she tell he how all have go when the wood - wife have seek he 
 out for the fulfilment of their love , and of the dreadful day when 
 they have come upon he out of his wit and but little manlike . 

 then she ask , would he , within the next day or two , that they 
 should go see the wood - wife together and thank she for her help , and 
 bring he within the ring of her love and guard ; and he yeasaid it 
 with a good will . 

 after this she would have he tell she of how thing have go with

 Quest and find she go . so he tell she somewhat , and of his dole 
 and misery , and his dealing with the foeman of Greenford ; but yet 
 scantly , and as one compel ; and at last he say : 

 dear love , since thou art cossette I with all solace of caress , 
 I pray thee remember my trouble and grief , how sore they be , and do 
 with I as with a sick man get well , as I wot surely thou 
 would do ; and do thou that which be at this present the softest 
 and merriest to I , and that forsooth be , that thou shouldest talk 
 and tell , and I should hearken the sweetness of the music , and only 
 here and there put in a word to rest thee and make thy tale the 
 sweeter . 

 she laugh with love on he , and without more ado fall to tell

 Captivity , both when she be but a bairn , and when she be grow to 
 be a young woman ; and long be she about the tale , nor be it all 
 do in one day ; and a multitude of thing she tell he which be not 
 set down in this book . 

 in the evening when they be go again to and fro the mead , it 
 be other talk they fall on , to wit , of their fellow of the Quest , 
 both of Sir Hugh and the three lovely lady : and now be Arthur 
 nought but kind when he spake of Atra , nor spake Birdalone otherwise ; 
 but she say : I shall now say a hard word , yet must thou bear it , my 
 loveling , since we twain be now become one , and have but one joy 
 together and one sorrow . Deemest thou that Atra be yet alive ? Sooth 
 it be , say Arthur , it may well be that I have slay she . and what 
 may we do by she if ever we fall in with her alive ? say Birdalone . 
 I wot not , say Arthur ; some would say that we have do penance for 
 our fault , both thou and I ; and what other penance may we do , save 
 sunder from each other ? and by God above I will not . by thine 
 head and thine hand I will not , say Birdalone . 

 so say they ; but therewith their eye tell tale of the fair eve and 
 the lovely meadow , and the house , the shrine of the dear white bed 
 no less sweet to they than erst ; but then presently Birdalone stay 
 her love , and take her arm about he , and each feel the sweetness of 
 the other 's body , and joy blossom anew in their heart . then fall 
 Arthur to telling of the deed and the kindness of Baudoin , whom 
 never again they should see on the earth ; and they turn back home 
 to the house , and on the way spake birdalone : this be what I would 
 we should do : whereas I have seek thee and thou I , and we have 
 find each other , whereas ye seek I when I go astray in the 
 Black Valley of the Greywethers , and before , when ye three seek 
 your own love , now I would that we should seek our fellow and have 
 joy in they , and thole sorrow with they as in day go by . 

 Spake Arthur : Dear be the rest with thee in this wilderness ; yet 
 be it a deed of fame , and would bring about a day of joy , might we 
 find our friend again , and knit up the link of the fellowship once 
 more . but thou the wise and valiant ! belike thou hast in thine head 
 some device whereby this might be set about . 

 birdalone say : Simple be my device , to wit , that we ask one who be 
 wise than I. let we tarry not , but go to - morrow and see the wood- 
 wife and talk with she concern it . then she smile upon he and 
 say : but when thou seest she , wilt thou be aghast if she come 
 before we in my shape of what I be five year agone , or six ? 

 Nay , nay , he say , thou art not so terrible as that ; not very far do 
 I run from thee now . and therewith they kiss and embrace , and so 
 enter the House of Love . 



 CHAPTER XXX . they have speech with habundia concern the GREEN 
 KNIGHT and his fellows 



 when the morrow be they arise and go their way toward the wood , 
 and Birdalone in her hunter 's coat , quiver at back and bow in hand . 
 they come to the Oak of Tryst , and Birdalone be at point to call on 
 the wood - wife by the burning of a hair of her , when she come lightly 
 from out the thicket , clothe as Birdalone , and her very image . she 
 stand before they with a glad countenance , and say : welcome to the 
 seeker and finder . but Arthur step forth and knelt before she , 
 and take she right hand and kiss it , and say : here I swear 
 allegiance to thee , o Lady of the Woods , to do thy will in all 
 thing , and give thee thank from my heart more than my tongue can 
 say . 

 Quoth the wood - wife : I take thine allegiance , fair young man , and 
 mine help shalt thou have henceforward . then she smile and her eye 
 dance for merriment , and she say : yet thy thank meseemeth for 
 this while be more due to the wise carline who bring thee through 
 the wood two day ago , and only leave thee when the way be easy and 
 clear to thee . 

 Lady , say Arthur , I know now how great be thy might , and that thou 
 canst take more shape than this only ; and humbly I thank thee that 
 for we thou hast take the shape that I love the good of all on the 
 earth . 

 say the wood - wife : stand up , Black Squire , and consider a little 
 what thou wouldst have I do for thee , while I have speech with mine 
 image yonder . and therewith she come up to Birdalone , and draw she a 
 little apart , and fall to stroke her cheek and pat her hand

 have I do for thee what I promise , and art thou wholly happy now ? 
 o yea , say birdalone ; if nought else befell we in this life but to 
 dwell together betwixt the woodland and the water , and to see thee 
 oft , full happy should we be . 

 nevertheless , say Habundia , art thou not come hither to ask somewhat 
 of I , that ye may be happy ? so it be , wise mother , say 
 birdalone ; grudge not against I therefor , for more than one thing 
 drive I thereto . I will not grudge , say the wood - wife ; but now I 
 will ask thy mate if he have think what it be that he will have of 
 I . and she turn to Arthur , who come forth and say : Lady , I have 
 hear thee , and herein would we have thee help we : there be erst 
 six fellow of we , three carie and three quean , to whom be add 
 this sweetle here ; but one of they , to wit the Golden Knight , be 
 slain , and for the rest . . . yea , I know , say the wood - wife ; my 
 child here hath tell I all ; and now ye wot not where they be or if 
 they be yet alive , all or any of they . now be it not so that ye 
 would seek these friend , if it be but to greet they but once , and 
 that ye would ask of the wise wood - wife help to find they ? be there 
 any more of the tale ? Nay , lady , say Arthur . 

 say she : well then , that help shall ye have , be it but for the 
 sake of that little Viridis whereof my child hath tell I . wherefore 
 abide tiding of I for a fourteen day , and seek not to I ere then ; 
 and meantime fear not , nor doubt I , for many messenger I have , and 
 ever may I do somewhat if the end of the tale be to be tell in these 
 woodland : and I deem these friend will not be hard to draw hither , 
 for it be most like that they be think of you and long for you , 
 as ye for they . and now I will depart on my business , which be 
 your , and do ye be happy to - day in the woodland , and to - morrow in 
 the meadow and by the water ; and let no trouble weigh down your 
 happy day . 

 Therewith she flit away from they , when she have kiss they both . 
 but when she be go they fare away together deep into the wood , 
 and be exceed merry disport they , and on their return they 
 gat they venison for their meat , and so come back to the House of 
 Love when the moon be up and shin brightly . 



 CHAPTER XXXI . HABUNDIA COMETH with tiding of those DEAR friend 



 wear the day thenceforth merrily ; and one day it be delight in the 
 wide mead , and another they go a long way west along the water- 
 side , and so into another meadow - plain , small than their home- 
 plain , which Birdalone have never erst come into ; and three eyot lay 
 off it , green and tree - beset , whereto they swam out together . then 
 they go into the wood thereby in the heat of the afternoon , and so 
 wear the day , that they deem themselves belate , and lie there 
 under a thorn - bush the night through . 

 another day birdalone take her mate over on to Green Eyot and Rock 
 Eyot , and show he all the place she be use to haunt . and they 
 have their fishing - gear with they , and angle off the eyot a good 
 part of the day , and have good catch , and swam back therewith merrily . 
 and Birdalone laugh , and say that it seem to she as if once 
 again she be ransom her skin of the witch - wife by that noble 
 catch . 

 diver time also they fare into the wood , and thrice they lie out 
 the night there in some woodlawn where be water ; and on one of these 
 time it happe that Arthur awake in the grey dawn , and lie open - eye 
 but not move for a little ; and therewith he deem he see the gleam 
 of war - gear in the thicket . so he keep as still as he might , but gat 
 his sword out of its sheath without noise , and then leapt up 
 suddenly , and spring thitherward whereas he have see that token , and 
 again see armour gleam and hear some man crash through the 
 underwood , for all be go in one moment . so he wake up Birdalone ,

 thereabout heedfully , arrow on string , but find nought fiercer than 
 a great sow and her farrow . so come the full day , and they gat they 
 back to their meadow and their house ; but thereafter be they 
 wary in go about the woodland . 

 in all joyance then wear the day till the fifteenth , and in the 
 morning early they go their way to the Oak of Tryst , and have no 
 need to call Habundia to they , for presently she come forth out of

 hand . but she cast it down and run up to Birdalone , and kiss she 
 and clip she , and then she take a hand of Arthur and a hand of 
 Birdalone , and hold they both and say : my child , and thou dear 
 knight , have ye still a longing to fall in with those friend of 
 your , and to run all risk of whatsoever contention and strife there 
 may be betwixt you thereafter ? yea , certe , say Arthur ; and even so 
 say Birdalone . well be that then , say the wood - wife ; but now and 
 for this time , ere I help you , I shall put a price upon my help , and 
 this be the price , that ye swear to I never wholly to sunder from 
 I ; that once in the year at least , as long as ye be alive and 
 wayworthy , ye come into the Forest of Evilshaw , and summon I by the 
 burning of a hair of mine , that we may meet and be merry for a while , 
 and part with the hope of meet once more at least . and if ye will 
 not pay the price , go in peace , and ye shall yet have my help in all 
 other matter that may seem good unto you , but not in this of join 
 your fellowship together . how sayest thou , Birdalone , my child ? how 
 sayest thou , Black Squire , whom , as meseemeth , I have deliver from 
 a fate bad than death , and have bring out of wretchedness into 
 bliss ? 

 Spake Birdalone : have I dare , I would have bid thee to swear to 
 I even such an oath , to wit , that thou wouldst never wholly sunder 
 thee from I . how then may I not swear this that thou bidd I , 
 and that with all joy and trustiness ? 

 Spake Arthur : Lady , have I no will to swear oath for thy sake , yet 
 with a good will would I swear it for my true - love 's sake who loveth 
 thee . yet verily of mine own will would I swear it joyfully , be it 
 for nought else save to pleasure thee , who hast do so kindly by I , 
 and hath give I back my manhood and my love , which else I have 
 miserably lose . 

 spake the wood - wife : it be well again . join hand then , and swear 
 as I have bid you by the love ye bear each other . 

 even so they do , and then the wood - wife kiss they both and say : 
 now do I deem you earth 's very child and mine , and this desire of 
 yours be good , and it shall be do if I may bring it about ; yet 
 therein the valiance and wisdom of you both may well be try . for 
 this have I find out by my messenger and other , that your friend 
 be alive , all of they ; and they have think of you in their inmost 
 heart , and have long determine that they must need go seek you if 
 they be to live life happy and worthy . furthermore , their quest 
 hath draw they hither to Evilshaw   nor say I that I have be 
 nothing therein , and they be even now in the wood . but ye shall 
 know that peril encompass they ; for they fare but a few , and of 
 those few be there two traitor who be minded to deliver they to the 
 man of the Red Company , unto whom three woman as fair as your she- 
 friend be a prize indeed . wherefore the Red Folk be dog 
 they , and will fall upon they when they find the occasion . but I 
 shall see to it that the occasion shall be in time and place where 
 they shall not be unholpen . now what ye have to do for your part , 
 be to waylay the waylayer , and keep watch and ward anigh the road 
 they must need take , and to fall on when need be . but this again I 
 shall see to , that your onset fail not . 

 but now ye may say : since thou art mighty , why shouldst not thou 
 thyself take our friend out of the hand of these accurse , as thou 
 couldst well do , and we to take no part therein ? my friend , this 
 might indeed well be ; but thou , Birdalone , hast tell I the whole 
 tale , and how that there be wrong to be forgive which can not be 
 make right , and past kindness to be quicken again , and coldness to 
 be kindle into love , and estrangement into familiar friendship ; and 
 meseem that the sight of your body and your hand make manifest to 
 the eye of they may do somewhat herein . yet if otherwise ye think , 
 then so let it be , and go ye back to the House under the Wood , and in 
 three day ' time I will bring you your friend all safe and sound . 

 now they both say that they would not for aught that they should 
 have no hand in the deliverance of they ; so the wood - wife say : come 
 with I , and I shall lead you to the place of your ambush . 

 then all they go on together , and fare a long way west , and toward 
 the place where erst they two have find Arthur ; and at last , two 
 hour before sunset , they come to where be a glade or way between 
 the thicket , which be as it be a little beat by the going of 
 man - folk . and the wood - wife do they to wit , that the evil folk 
 aforesaid have so use it and beat it , that it might just look as if 
 folk be will not to pass that way , whereas it be not very far from 
 their chief haunt and stronghold . a little on the north side of 
 this half - blind way , and some ten yard through the thicket , the 
 ground fall away into a little dale , the bottom whereof be plain and 
 well grassed , and water by a brook . 

 Thither the wood - wife bring the twain ; and when they all stand 
 together on the brook - side , she say to they : dear friend , this be 
 your woodland house for this time , and I rede you go not forth of it , 
 lest ye happen upon any of those evil man ; for nought have ye to fear 
 from any save they . here amidst these big stone , which make , see 
 ye , as it be a cavern , have I stow victual for you ; and armour 
 therewithal , because , though both of you be in a manner armed , yet 
 who knoweth where a shaft draw at a venture may reach . 

 and from the say stone she draw forth two very fair armour , helm 
 and hauberk , and leg and arm ward ; and they be all of green , and 
 shone but little , but be fashion as no smith of man - folk could 
 have do the like . 

 this be thine , Sir Arthur , say the wood - wife , and thou wilt wear it 
 like as it be silk ; and this thine , my child , and thou art strong 
 enough to bear such light gear . and I charge you both to do on this 
 gear presently , nor do it off till ye have achieve the adventure . 
 and now this be the last word : here be a horn of oliphant which thou 
 shalt wear about thy neck , Birdalone ; and if thou be sore bestead , or 
 thy heart faileth thee , blow in it , yet not before the onfall ; and 
 then , whether thou blow much or little , thou shalt be well holpen . 

 now be not downcast if nought befall to - night or to - morrow , or even 
 the day after ; but if the third day be tidingless , then at sunset 
 burn a hair of my head , Birdalone , and I will come to you . and now 
 farewell ! for I have yet to do in this matter . 

 with that she kiss Birdalone fondly and embrace Arthur , and go 
 her way ; and those twain abode in the dale , and sleep and watch by 
 turn , and all be tidingless till the morrow 's dawn ; neither be 
 there aught to tell of on that day and the night that end it . 



 CHAPTER XXXII . of the fight in the forest and the RESCUE of those 
 friend from the MEN of the RED COMPANY 



 Light be grow on the dawn of the next day , and the colour of 
 thing could be see , when Birdalone , who be hold this last watch

 the going of the wild thing anear they in the wood . 

 so she do off her helm to hear the well , and stand thus a little ;

 amidst it two shriek as of woman .

 before he on the saddle : curse the fool ! 

 laugh out they who be about he , and one young man cast a jeer at

 even therewith the seven who have pass on have turn back and be

 but as she run it come into her mind in a twinkling that her sword

 unwitting . 

 yet be not the wood - wife 's promise unavaile , for even while the

 but Arthur , when he see Birdalone on her foot , and unhurt by seem ,

 CHAPTER XXXIII . VIRIDIS TELLETH the TALE of their seeking 



 now come they back to where be the three other , and Viridis be

 now to all of they save Atra this appearance seem to be the image

 gaze the image kindly and sweetly on they , so that they behold it

 good speed they make , so that ere the night have fall on they ,

 here end the Sixth Part of the Water of the Wondrous Isles , which be 
 call the Days of Absence , and begin the Seventh Part , which be
